The Four Types of Precedence and Posteriority
Enumeration of four types of precedence and posteriority with terminological clarification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four distinct meanings of precedence and posteriority used in kalam and the proper terminological distinction between 'essence' and 'causality'.

Core Idea
Precedence and posteriority are not one-dimensional; they have four distinct meanings.

Theologians use the terms 'precedence' and 'posteriority' in multiple senses. In this passage, the author lists four: temporal, spatial (rank), virtue, and essence (which really means causality). He then clarifies a subtle point about the final cause versus efficient and formal causes, which is crucial for understanding the relationship between God and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the original Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Let the author's words settle in your mind before analyzing.
The passage uses technical terms; don't worry if not everything is clear yet.
2
Identify the four types of precedence
The author lists four types: temporal, spatial (also called rank), virtue, and essence (which he later corrects to causality). Find each in the text and note the examples given.
The example for temporal is parent-child; for spatial is imam-ma'mum; for virtue is scholar-ignorant; for essence is cause-effect.
3
Understand the critique of 'essence'
The author points out that using 'essence' for precedence is imprecise because 'essence' is broader than 'causality'. He argues that the correct term should be 'precedence by causality'.
This is a terminological correction important for precision in theological arguments.
4
Distinguish types of causes
The author then distinguishes the final cause (which precedes only in the mind, not in existence) from efficient and formal causes (which are concurrent in existence with their effects). Grasp this distinction.
This has implications for understanding God's relationship to the world: God as efficient cause is concurrent, not separate in time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقال القوم: التقدم والتأخر يطلق ويراد به التقدم بالزمان كتقدم الوالد على الولد، ويطلق ويراد به التقدم بالمكان والتأخر به كتقدم الإمام على المأموم، ويسمى هذا القسم متقدما بالرتبة، وقد يطلق ويراد به الفضيلة كتقدم العالم على الجاهل، وقد يطلق ويراد به التقدم بالذات والتأخر بها كتقدم العلة على المعلول، وهؤلاء قصدوا في إطلاق لفظ الذات، فإنهم أرادوا به العلية، والذات أعم من العلية فكان من حقهم أن يقولوا التقدم بالعلية، ثم العلية الغابية تتقدم على المعلول في الذهن والتصور لا في الوجود، فهي متأخرة في الوجود متقدمة في الذهن، بخلاف العلة الفاعلية والعلل الصورية فإنها لا تكون مقارنة في الوجود.
The people said: Precedence and posteriority are used, and intended by them: precedence in time, like the precedence of the parent over the child; and they are used, and intended by them: precedence in place and posteriority in it, like the precedence of the imam over the ma'mum; and this division is called precedence in rank; and they are used, and intended by them: virtue, like the precedence of the scholar over the ignorant; and they are used, and intended by them: precedence by essence and posteriority by it, like the precedence of the cause over the effect. And these intended in the use of the word 'essence' only the causality, but essence is more general than causality, so it was proper for them to say precedence by causality. Then the final cause precedes the effect in the mind and conception, not in existence; so it is posterior in existence and prior in the mind, unlike the efficient cause and formal causes, for they are not concurrent in existence.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage classifies the four meanings of precedence and posteriority. It begins by listing them: (1) temporal precedence, (2) spatial precedence (also called rank precedence), (3) precedence by virtue, and (4) precedence by essence, which really means precedence by causality. The author then critiques those who use 'essence' for causality, since essence is broader. He then distinguishes between different types of causes: the final cause (al-illa al-gha'iyya) is prior only in the mind, not in existence, whereas the efficient cause (al-illa al-fa'iliyya) and formal causes (al-illal al-suwariyya) are concurrent in existence with their effects. This distinction is crucial for later arguments about the relationship between God and the world.

Key Terms

التقدم بالمكان
التقدم بالرتبة
التقدم بالفضيلة
التقدم بالذات
العلية
العلة الغابية
العلة الفاعلية
العلة الصورية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'precedence by essence' is the same as 'precedence by causality', or that all causes precede their effects in the same way.
Correction: The author clarifies that 'essence' is broader than 'causality', so the correct term is 'causality'. Moreover, the final cause precedes only in the mind, not in existence, unlike efficient and formal causes which are concurrent.

Checkpoint
Which of the following types of precedence does the author say is only in the mind and not in existence?
A) Temporal precedence
B) Precedence by rank
C) Precedence of the final cause
D) Precedence of the efficient cause

Key Takeaway
Precedence and posteriority have four distinct senses; among them, 'precedence by causality' is the precise term, and the final cause precedes only in the mind, while efficient and formal causes are concurrent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You learned that 'precedence by essence' is imprecise; the correct term is 'precedence by causality'. Think of something you've held as essential to your identity—like your role, status, or a belief. Could it be that you've been confusing what is essentially you with what causes you to be that way? Are you attributing to your essence what is actually an effect of something deeper? What would change if you rephrased 'I am essentially X' to 'I am caused by X'? Commit to examining one such belief today.
?
The final cause precedes only in the mind, not in existence. Consider a goal or intention you cherish—something you think about constantly. Do you treat that goal as if it already exists, letting it dominate your thoughts while actual duties lag? How might this misalignment between mental precedence and existential reality be affecting your peace or productivity? What concrete step can you take today to bring your mental priority into harmony with what actually exists?
?
The passage distinguishes four types of precedence. Which type do you most often use to order your daily priorities—temporal, spatial, by virtue, or by causality? For example, do you put tasks that are 'more important' (virtue) before urgent ones (temporal)? Is your reasoning consistent, or do you mix them up? Name one area in your life where you are misapplying the type of precedence, leading to imbalance. Write down one change you will make before tomorrow.

The Fifth Type and the Limits of Induction
The author critiques the inductive limitation of precedence divisions and proposes a sixth type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the classification of precedence is not exhaustively proven and how a sixth type can be rationally conceived.

Core Idea
Classifications of precedence are based on induction, not demonstration.

In this passage, the author challenges the established four- or five-fold classification of precedence. He argues that the only evidence for the limit is induction, not logical necessity. He even suggests a sixth type: precedence in existence without essential causation, time, or place. This opens the door to further rational investigation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, noting the mention of the fifth type and the critique.
Pay attention to the example: the one and the two.
2
Understand the critique
The author states that those who limit the divisions to four or five have no proof except induction. What does this imply about the certainty of the classification?
Induction is not a demonstrative proof.
3
Analyze the sixth type
Consider the proposed sixth type: precedence in existence without essential causation. How does it differ from the previous types? Why does the author consider it rationally necessary?
Reflect on the possibility of two things: self-existent and derived existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من زاد قسما خامسًا وهو التقدم بالطبع؛ كتقدم الواحد على الاثنين، ولو طالبهم مطالب لِمَ حصرتم الأقسام في أربعة أو خمسة لم يجدوا على الحصر دليلا سوى الاستقراء، حتى لو قيل لهم: ما الذي أنكرتم على من زاد قسما سادسا وهو التقدم والتأخر في الوجود من غير التفات إلى الإيجاب بالذات ولا التفات إلى الزمان والمكان والتقدم للواحد على الاثنين شديد الشبه بهذا القبيل، فإن الواحد ليس بعلة يلزم منه وجود الاثنين ضرورة، بل يمكن أن يتصور شيئان: أحدهما وجوده بذاته، والثاني وجوده مستفاد من غيره، ثم بعد ذلك يقع البحث في أنه يستفيد وجوده منه اختيارًا أو طبعا أو ذاتا، هذا معقول بالضرورة.
And among them are those who added a fifth division, which is precedence by nature (tab'), like the precedence of the one over the two. If a questioner were to ask them: Why have you limited the divisions to four or five? They would find no proof for the limitation except induction. So much so that if it were said to them: What do you deny about one who adds a sixth division, which is precedence and posteriority in existence without regard to essential entailment and without regard to time and place? And the precedence of the one over the two is very similar to this kind. For the one is not a cause from which the existence of the two necessarily follows, but rather it is possible to conceive two things: one whose existence is by itself, and the other whose existence is derived from another. Then after that, research occurs as to whether it derives its existence from it by choice, by nature, or by essence. This is rationally necessary.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage introduces a fifth division of precedence: precedence by nature (bi al-tab'), exemplified by the one before the two. The author then criticizes those who limit the divisions to four or five, noting that they have no proof other than induction (istiqra'). He goes further, proposing a possible sixth division: precedence in existence without regard to essential causation, time, or place. The example of the one and the two is similar to this sixth type, because the one is not a cause that necessitates the two, but two things can be conceived purely in terms of existence: one self-existent, the other derived. The subsequent investigation concerns whether the derivation is by choice, nature, or essence. The author affirms that this is rationally necessary (ma'qul bi al-darura).

Key Terms
التقدم بالطبع
الاستقراء
التقدم في الوجود
الإيجاب بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The classification of precedence into four or five types is exhaustive and proven by logical demonstration.
Correction: The author shows that the limitation is only supported by induction, not demonstrative proof, and a sixth type can be rationally conceived.

Checkpoint
What is the only proof the author says the scholars have for limiting the divisions of precedence to four or five?
A) Revelation
B) Consensus
C) Induction
D) Logical demonstration

Key Takeaway
The classification of precedence is not closed; reason can conceive additional types beyond those based on induction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you accepted a classification or ruling simply because it was commonly listed (like the four or five types), without checking whether it is based on induction or proof? How might your reliance on unverified categories be affecting your understanding of aqeeda?
?
The author shows that reason can conceive beyond inductive categories. Is there an area of your faith where you have allowed inherited categories to limit your rational reflection? What would it mean for you to take the author's critique seriously and ask: 'Could there be a sixth type?' in your own theological thinking?
?
The passage presents the author's willingness to challenge a standard classification. Are you willing to challenge commonly accepted divisions in your own study of aqeeda, even if it makes you uncomfortable? What personal barrier holds you back from doing so?

The Two Methods of the Mutakallimun and the First Proof through Accidents
Author introduces two methods and explains the first method's four-step proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main methods of the mutakallimun for proving the world's temporality and the four-step proof using accidents.

Core Idea
The mutakallimun prove the world's temporality through a four-step chain involving accidents and substances.

In this passage, the author outlines the two methods of the mutakallimun for addressing the question of whether the world is temporally originated or preeternal. He then elaborates on the first method, which is a foundational proof used by the majority. This proof proceeds in four logical steps, each building on the previous one, culminating in the conclusion that the world must have a beginning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two methods
Read the passage and note the two methods: establishing huduth and invalidating qidam.
2
List the four steps
Find and list the four steps: establishing accidents, their temporality, inseparability from substances, and impossibility of infinite events.
3
Understand the logical chain
See how each step depends on the previous one and leads to the conclusion.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand the principle: whatever is not preceded by events is temporally originated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : للمتكلمين طريقان في المسألة : أحدهما : إثبات حدث العالم. والثاني : إبطال القول بالقدم. أما الأول فقد سلك عامتهم طريق الإثبات بإثبات الأعراض أولا وإثبات حدثها ثانيا، وبيان استحالة خلو الجواهر عنها ثالثًا، وبيان استحالة حوادث لا أول لها، رابعا، ويترتب على هذه الأصول أن ما لا يسبقه الحوادث فهو حادث، وقد أوردوا هذه الطريقة في كتبهم أحسن إيراد.
So we say: for the mutakallimun there are two methods concerning the question: one: establishing the temporal origination (huduth) of the world; and the second: invalidating the claim of preeternity (qidam). As for the first, the majority of them have followed a method of proof by first establishing accidents (a'rad), second establishing their temporal origination, third demonstrating the impossibility of substances (jawhar) being devoid of them, and fourth demonstrating the impossibility of events that have no beginning. And it follows from these principles that whatever is not preceded by events is temporally originated. And they have presented this method in their books in the best possible way.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage introduces the two main methods of the mutakallimun on the question of the world's temporality. The first method is establishing the world's temporal origination (huduth) via accidents. The second method is invalidating the claim of preeternity (qidam). The author then elaborates on the first method: the majority of mutakallimun follow a four-step proof: first, establishing the existence of accidents; second, proving that accidents are temporally originated; third, demonstrating that substances cannot be devoid of accidents; and fourth, demonstrating the impossibility of an infinite chain of events. From these principles, it follows that whatever is preceded by events is itself temporally originated. The author notes that they have presented this method excellently in their books.

Key Terms
حدث
عرض
جوهر
حادث
قدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that establishing the temporality of accidents is sufficient without needing to prove the impossibility of an infinite chain.
Correction: The author shows the four-fold structure is necessary because the temporality of accidents alone does not guarantee a beginning; an infinite chain of accidents is possible unless proven impossible.

Checkpoint
How many steps are in the first method of the mutakallimun to prove the temporality of the world?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun's proof of the world's temporality relies on a four-step argument about accidents and substances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that everything that is not preceded by events is temporally originated. If the world itself is temporal, then you too are a temporal being. How does this certainty about your own temporality change the way you value your time and your life's purpose starting today?
?
The proof shows that accidents (temporary attributes) constantly come into existence. Think about your own attachments—your wealth, health, relationships—all are accidents. Are you living as if these are permanent? What is one concrete step you will take today to detach your heart from something you treat as if it will never fade?
?
The mutakallimun reasoned that an infinite chain of events is impossible. Every action, every decision you make is an event. Can you justify how you spend your moments if each one is temporally originated and leads to either accountability or heedlessness? What will you do differently in the next hour to ensure your events are oriented toward what lasts?

Al-Ash'ari's Argument from Assembly and Separation
Al-Ash'ari argues that preeternal substances require a cause for assembly or separation, proving their temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ash'ari's logical argument that preeternal substances require an external cause for their states, thereby proving their temporality.

Core Idea
Essences do not change; changing states demand an external cause.

Al-Ash'ari presents a rigorous logical argument to demonstrate that the world cannot be preeternal. By examining substances and their possible states of assembly or separation, he shows that change in state implies an external cause, which must be temporal, thus the substances themselves must be temporal. This passage articulates the core of that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Pay attention to the logical flow from assumption to conclusion.
2
Understand the enumeration of states
Al-Ash'ari lists all possible states for preeternal substances: assembled, separated, neither, both, or some mixture. Notice he reduces these to the essential fact that substances are subject to assembly and separation.
The reduction is key: substances must be either assembled or separated, or subject to change between these states.
3
Grasp the premise about essences
The crucial premise: a substance's essence does not change, but we observe it changing from assembly to separation. This indicates the change is not from the essence itself.
This premise is the foundation for the need for an external cause.
4
Follow the conclusion
Since change occurs, there must be an external 'assembler and divider' (jami' farq). This cause is temporal, so what is preceded by temporal events must itself be temporal.
Thus the assumption of preeternity is invalidated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني فقد سلك شيخنا أبو الحسن الأشعري رضي الله عنه طريق الإبطال وقال : لو قدرنا قدم الجواهر لم يخل من أحد أمرين؛ إما أن تكون مجتمعة أو مفترقة، أو لا مجتمعة ولا مفترقة أو مجتمعة ومفترقة معا، أو بعضها مجتمع وبعضها مفترق وبالجملة ليست تخلو عن اجتماع وافتراق، أو جواز طريان الاجتماع والافتراق وتبدل أحدهما بالثاني، وهي بذواتها لا تجتمع ولا تفترق؛ لأن حكم الذات لا يتبدل وهي قد تبدلت فإذا لا بد من جامع فارق، فيترتب على هذه الأصول أن ما لا يسبق الحادث فهو حادث.
As for the second, our teacher Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, may Allah be pleased with him, followed a method of invalidation and said: if we were to suppose the preeternity of substances, they would not be free from one of two states; either they would be assembled (mujtami'a) or separated (muftariqa), or neither assembled nor separated, or both assembled and separated together, or some assembled and some separated. In summary, they are not free from assembly and separation, or the possibility of the occurrence of assembly and separation and the replacement of one by the other. And they, by their essences, do not assemble nor separate; because the judgment of the essence does not change, yet they have changed. So there must be an assembler and a divider (jami' farq). So it follows from these principles that whatever is not preceded by an event is temporally originated.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage presents the second method employed by Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari to invalidate the claim of preeternity. Al-Ash'ari argues that if substances were preeternal, they would necessarily be either assembled or separated, or some combination. Since their essences do not change, but they do change (e.g., from assembly to separation), there must be an external cause that assembles and separates them. This cause is temporal, so the substances are preceded by events, therefore they must be temporally originated. Thus the argument concludes that whatever is not preceded by an event is temporally originated, establishing the world's temporality.

Key Terms
قدم
اجتماع
افتراق
جامع فارق
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the argument from assembly/separation applies only to composite bodies, not to substances themselves.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari's argument is directed at substances (jawāhir) themselves, showing that even they require a cause for their states, thus they are temporal.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari's argument, why must preeternal substances have a cause for their assembly or separation?
A) Because substances cannot exist without accidents.
B) Because their essences change, requiring an external agent.
C) Because they are created by God.
D) Because they are infinitely many.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's argument shows that because substances undergo change (assembly/separation) while their essences do not, an external cause must exist, proving they are temporally originated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have experienced changes in your life—shifts in belief, character, or state. What external cause do you attribute those changes to? Can you truly claim you are self-sufficient in shaping your own soul?
?
If essences do not change by themselves, then your own essence (your inner self) cannot change without an external agent. Are you actively seeking that external agent (Allah) to transform you, or are you relying on your own efforts?
?
Al-Ash'ari's argument proves that any change requires a changer. When you look at your own spiritual stagnation, do you acknowledge that you need a Divine assembler to reorder your heart? What have you done today to invite that change?

Proof That an Infinite Body Is Impossible
Geometric proof that infinite body is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that an actually infinite body cannot exist, and why the same reasoning applies to numbers and individuals.

Core Idea
An infinite body leads to logical contradiction via superimposition of lines.

The existence of an infinite body is not merely empirically unlikely; it is rationally impossible. This lesson presents a classic kalam proof using two lines of different lengths. By reasoning about what happens when the shorter line is superimposed on the longer, we uncover a contradiction that demonstrates the impossibility of any actual infinite in the physical world.

Guided Path
1
Visualize the two lines
Imagine two lines starting from the same point, one longer than the other by a fixed length (e.g., a cubit). Both are assumed to extend infinitely.
This is a thought experiment, not a physical drawing.
2
Consider the superposition
If the shorter line is superimposed on the longer, either it covers the entire longer line (making them equal) or it falls short. Both options lead to contradictions if the lines are infinite.
The proof examines both cases.
3
Analyze the two cases
Case 1: The shorter equals the longer → impossible because they are unequal by definition. Case 2: The shorter falls short by a finite amount → then both lines are finite, contradicting the assumption of infinity.
The key is that finite difference makes both finite.
4
Extend the proof to numbers and individuals
The same logical structure applies to any supposed infinite collection: it leads to contradictions of size and magnitude, showing that actual infinites cannot exist.
This reinforces the conclusion that the physical world must be finite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على خط هو أنقص من الأول بذراع، ونصل بين النقطتين بحيث يطبق الخط الأصغر على الخط الأطول، فإن كان كل واحد من الخطين تمادى إلى غير نهاية، فيكون الخط الأصغر مساويا للخط الأطول، وهو محال، وإن قصر الأقصر عن الأطول بمتناه فقد تناهى الأقصر وتناهى الأطول؛ إذ قصر عنه الأقصر بمتناه وزاد الأطول عليه بمتناه، وما زاد على الشيء بمتناه كان متناهيا. وعلى كل حال فإن كان أحدهما أكبر من الثاني كان فيما لا يتناهى ما هو أصغر وأكبر وأكثر وأقل وذلك محال، فعلم أن جسما لا يتناهى محال وجوده، وإن بعدا لا يتناهى في خلا أو ملا محال، ويمكن أن ينقل هذا البرهان بعينه إلى أعداد وأشخاص لا تتناهى حتى يتبين أن فرض ذلك محال.
On a line that is shorter than the first by a cubit, and we connect the two points such that the smaller line is superimposed on the longer line. If each of the two lines extends indefinitely, then the smaller line would equal the longer line, which is impossible. And if the shorter falls short of the longer by a finite amount, then both the shorter and longer are finite, because the shorter falls short of it by a finite amount and the longer exceeds it by a finite amount, and what exceeds something by a finite amount is finite. In any case, if one is greater than the other, then in what is infinite there would be something smaller and larger, more and less, which is impossible. So it is known that an infinite body is impossible in existence, and that an infinite distance in void or plenum is impossible. And this same proof can be transferred to infinite numbers and individuals until it becomes clear that assuming that is impossible.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage presents a geometric proof (burhān) against the existence of an actually infinite body. The author imagines two lines, one shorter than the other by a fixed length. If both lines extend infinitely, the shorter line would either equal the longer (if the difference is absorbed in infinity) or remain shorter by a finite amount, making both finite. This contradiction proves that infinite bodies cannot exist. The proof is then extended to numbers and individuals, showing that any actual infinite leads to absurdity. This is a classic kalam argument for the finitude of the world.

Key Terms
جسم لا يتناهى
برهان التطابق
خلا
ملا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof relies on empirical observation about physical lines.
Correction: It is a purely rational demonstration that does not depend on any empirical facts about actual lines.

Checkpoint
What does the proof show about an infinite body?
A) It is possible if finite differences are ignored.
B) It is impossible because it leads to contradictions.
C) It is possible only in void.
D) It is impossible only for distances, not for numbers.

Key Takeaway
An infinite body is impossible because assuming it leads to logical contradictions, whether through geometric superposition or numerical reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the universe cannot be infinite, then it has a beginning and an end. How does this change the way you view your own life and its fleeting nature? What would you do differently today if you truly believed your time is finite and limited?
?
The proof shows that assuming infinity leads to logical contradiction. Are there areas in your life where you assume infinite time, infinite chances, or infinite mercy without accountability? What is one step you can take today to recognize God's command as urgent and not something to postpone?
?
This rational demonstration points to the impossibility of an infinite body, which implies a Creator who brought everything into existence from nothing. Do you live with a sense of dependence on the Creator every moment, or have you become comfortable with the illusion of self-sufficiency? How can you actively cultivate reliance on God in your daily decisions?

The Three Rational Judgments and the Contingency Proof
Divides existents into necessary, impossible, and possible, and proves world is contingent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational judgments (necessary, impossible, possible) and how they are applied to prove that the world is contingent and requires a cause.

Core Idea
The three rational judgments are the building blocks for proving the existence of a necessary creator.

This lesson introduces the foundational classification of all intellectual concepts into necessary, impossible, and possible. By defining each category, we establish the framework for the contingency argument. The passage then applies this framework to the world, showing that because its parts change, it must be contingent and thus requires an external cause.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three rational judgments
Read the passage and note that all conceivable things fall into exactly three categories: necessary, impossible, and possible.
This is the starting point for all rational discourse in kalam.
2
Define each category
Focus on the definition of each: necessary is that whose non-existence would lead to absurdity; impossible is that whose existence would lead to absurdity; possible is neither necessary in existence nor non-existence.
These definitions are logical, not physical.
3
Apply the division to the world
The world, with its changing parts, cannot be necessary (since necessary does not change) nor impossible (since it exists), so it must be possible.
This is the first step of the contingency proof.
4
Recognize the beginning of the syllogism
The passage then states: every changing thing is possible of existence in itself, and every possible thing has its existence from another. This is the core of the proof.
The text cuts off, but the logical structure is clear.
5
Reflect on the incomplete proof
Notice that the conclusion—that the world has a cause—is not fully written here, but the premises are sufficient to draw it.
This shows how the rational division is used in a larger argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول: القسمة العقلية حصرت المعلومات في ثلاثة أقسام؛ واجب ومستحيل وجايز؛ فالواجب هو ضروري الوجود بحيث لو قدر عدمه لزم منه محال، والمستحيل هو ضروري العدم بحيث لو قدر وجوده لزم منه محال، والجايز ما لا ضروري في وجوده ولا عدمه والعالم بما فيه من الجواهر العقلية والأجسام الحسية والأعراض القايمة بها قدرناه متناهيا وغير متناه، وكذلك لو قدرنا أنه شخص واحد أو أشخاص وأنواع كثيرة؛ إما أن يكون ضروري الوجود أو ضروري العدم وذلك محال؛ لأن أجزاءه متغيرة الأحوال عيانا، وضروري الوجود على كل حال لا يتغير بحال. فوجه تركيب البرهان منه أن نقول: كل متغير أو متكثر فهو ممكن الوجود باعتبار ذاته، وكل ممكن الوجود باعتبار ذاته فوجوده بإيجاد غيره، فكل متغير أو
We say: The rational division has encompassed known things into three categories: Necessary (wājib), Impossible (mustaḥīl), and Possible (jāʾiz). The necessary is that whose existence is necessary such that if its non-existence were supposed, an impossibility would follow. The impossible is that whose non-existence is necessary such that if its existence were supposed, an impossibility would follow. The possible is that which is neither necessary in existence nor non-existence. And the world with what it contains of intellectual substances, sensible bodies, and accidents subsisting in them—if we suppose it finite and infinite, similarly if we suppose it one individual or many individuals and many types—either it would be necessary of existence or necessary of non-existence, and that is impossible, because its parts are observed to be changing states, and the necessary of existence does not change in any state. So the way to construct the proof from this is to say: Everything changing or multiple is possible of existence in its essence, and everything possible of existence in its essence has its existence from another. So every changing or...
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage presents the foundational trichotomy of wājib, mustaḥīl, and jāʾiz. It defines each: the necessary cannot not exist, the impossible cannot exist, the possible can be or not be. Applying this to the world, since the world has parts that change, it cannot be necessary (unchanging) nor impossible (since it exists), so it is possible. The syllogism then formalizes: every changing thing is possible; every possible thing needs a cause; therefore the world needs a cause. The argument is cut off but the structure is clear.

Misconception: Some mistakenly think 'possible' means 'unreal' or 'not actual', but in kalam it means 'contingent'—capable of existing or not existing, needing a cause.
Correction: The passage defines possible as that which is neither necessary in existence nor non-existence. It does not imply non-existence; many possible things actually exist.

A) It is too large.
B) Its parts change.
C) It is finite.
D) It is infinite.

The world's changing nature proves it is contingent and therefore depends on a necessary cause.

?
If all possible beings require a cause, then you are a possible being. Are you living in awareness that your very existence is sustained by the Necessary every moment? What would it look like to thank Him for that sustenance today?

The Three Rational Divisions: Definitions
Sanusi defines the three rational divisions with their logical conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definitions of the necessary, impossible, and contingent as used in rational theology.

Core Idea
These three categories exhaust all that can be rationally conceived.

Before we can reason about God and creation, we need a shared vocabulary. This lesson introduces the three fundamental rational judgments that every theologian must know. The author defines them with clarity, grounding all future arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions carefully
Read the Arabic definitions once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical conditions used.
Each definition uses the phrase 'such that if... an impossibility would follow.'
2
Identify the three categories
Find the three categories and notice how each is defined by a condition of necessity or impossibility.
The contingent is defined negatively as not necessary in existence or non-existence.
3
Reflect on the logical nature
Ask yourself: what makes these three categories exhaustive? The answer lies in the law of excluded middle for necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : القسمة العقلية حصرت المعلومات في ثلاثة أقسام ; واجب ومستحيل وجايز ; فالواجب هو ضروري الوجود بحيث لو قدر عدمه لزم منه محال، والمستحيل هو ضروري العدم بحيث لو قدر وجوده لزم منه محال، والجايز ما لا ضروري في وجوده ولا عدمه والعالم بما فيه من الجواهر العقلية والأجسام الحسية والأعراض القائمة بها قدرناه متناهيا وغير متناه ، وكذلك لو قدرنا أنه شخص واحد أو أشخاص وأنواع كثيرة; إما أن يكون ضروري الوجود أو ضروري العدم وذلك محال ; لأن أجزاءه متغيرة الأحوال عيانًا، وضروري الوجود على كل حال لا يتغير بحال.
So we say: The rational division has restricted information to three divisions: necessary (wājib), impossible (mustaḥīl), and contingent (jā’iz). The necessary is that whose existence is necessary such that if its non-existence were supposed, an impossibility would follow. The impossible is that whose non-existence is necessary such that if its existence were supposed, an impossibility would follow. The contingent is that which is not necessary in its existence nor its non-existence. And the world, with what it contains of intellectual substances, sensible bodies, and the accidents subsisting in them, we suppose it to be finite and infinite; likewise, we suppose it to be a single individual or individuals and many types; it must be either necessary of existence or impossible of existence, and that is impossible because its parts are observably changing in states, and that which is necessary of existence in every state does not change in any state.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification followed by an immediate application. The author first defines the three rational judgments that exhaust all intelligible beings: the necessary (wājib) is that whose non-existence involves a logical contradiction; the impossible (mustaḥīl) is that whose existence involves a contradiction; the contingent (jā’iz) is that which is neither necessary nor impossible in itself. Then he applies this to the world: regardless of how we imagine it (finite or infinite, one or many), it cannot be necessary or impossible because its parts are observed to change. Since a necessary being cannot change, the world must be contingent. This sets up the need for a proof of contingency.

Key Terms
واجب
مستحيل
جايز
ضروري الوجود
ضروري العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that contingent means something that exists but could have not existed.
Correction: The passage defines contingent as that which is not necessary in its existence nor its non-existence, so it may or may not exist; it is purely possible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of the 'contingent' (jā’iz)?
A) That whose existence is necessary and non-existence is impossible.
B) That which is not necessary in its existence nor its non-existence.
C) That whose non-existence is necessary.
D) That which is necessary in its existence but contingent in its non-existence.

Key Takeaway
The three rational divisions—necessary, impossible, contingent—exhaust all that can be rationally conceived, each defined by a logical condition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as though God—the Necessary Being—is truly necessary for your every moment, or do you treat Him as optional, like a contingent addition to your life? What would change if you consciously depended on Him as the only One whose existence cannot be absent?
?
Consider something you deeply desire but that is ultimately contingent—health, wealth, a relationship. If it were taken away, would your trust in the Necessary Being remain unshaken? Are you anchored in what is necessary or attached to what is contingent?
?
When you face the impossibility of something that cannot happen by divine decree (like a miracle being a true contradiction), do you submit your intellect to what the Necessary Being has determined, or do you let your desires try to force the impossible? How can you train your heart to rest in the necessary order of reality?

The World's Contingency from Change
Sanusi applies the three divisions to the world, arguing for its contingency from change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author proves that the world cannot be necessary or impossible, and therefore must be contingent.

Core Idea
The world’s observed change shows it cannot be necessary; only the contingent changes.

Having established the three categories, the author now applies them to the world. By observing that everything in the world changes, he demonstrates that the world cannot be necessary or impossible. This sets the stage for proving the existence of a necessary creator.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise: change is observed
The author states that the parts of the world are observably changing. This is the key observation.
Change is taken as an undeniable empirical fact.
2
Recall the definition of necessary
A necessary being cannot change because it is necessary in every state. Compare with the world's changing parts.
If something is necessary, it must be immutable.
3
Conclude the world is contingent
Since the world exists but changes, it cannot be necessary or impossible, so it must be contingent.
This establishes contingency, which then requires a cause.

Source Text

The necessary is that whose existence is necessary such that if its non-existence were supposed, an impossibility would follow. The impossible is that whose non-existence is necessary such that if its existence were supposed, an impossibility would follow. The contingent is that which is not necessary in its existence nor its non-existence. And the world, with what it contains of intellectual substances, sensible bodies, and the accidents subsisting in them, we suppose it to be finite and infinite; likewise, we suppose it to be a single individual or individuals and many types; it must be either necessary of existence or impossible of existence, and that is impossible because its parts are observably changing in states, and that which is necessary of existence in every state does not change in any state.

This passage is a definition and classification followed by an immediate application. The author first defines the three rational judgments that exhaust all intelligible beings: the necessary (wājib) is that whose non-existence involves a logical contradiction; the impossible (mustaḥīl) is that whose existence involves a contradiction; the contingent (jā’iz) is that which is neither necessary nor impossible in itself. Then he applies this to the world: regardless of how we imagine it (finite or infinite, one or many), it cannot be necessary or impossible because its parts are observed to change. Refutation of Essential Necessitation
Shahrastani enumerates three arguments refuting essential necessitation and concludes with volitional creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why essential necessitation is rationally impossible and why the world must be created by God's will, not by His essence alone.

Core Idea
Essential necessitation cannot explain why this specific world exists rather than another.

Shahrastani presents three powerful arguments to dismantle the doctrine of essential necessitation, which claims that God's essence necessarily produces the universe. This passage is a cornerstone of Ash'arite kalam, showing that without a voluntary choice, the existence of this particular world is inexplicable.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once without analyzing. Let the author's rhythm and argument structure come through.
Notice that the passage begins with a summary statement and then lists three reasons.
2
Identify the three arguments
The passage presents three distinct arguments against essential necessitation. Locate each one and note the key terms: existence (الوجود), preponderator (المرجح), and specification (المخصص).
The first argument uses the commonality of existence; the second uses the need for a specifying factor; the third uses the need for relation.
3
Understand the first argument
Focus on the idea that existence is common to necessary and contingent beings. If essence alone caused existence, there would be no reason for one thing to exist over another.
Shahrastani argues that a specific characteristic must be involved.
4
Understand the second argument
The second argument builds on the first: the possible is equally balanced between existence and non-existence. Only a voluntary specifying factor (mukhassis) can tilt the balance.
This argument directly targets the idea of essential necessitation by showing it cannot account for specification.
5
Understand the third argument and conclusion
The third argument asserts that for one thing to proceed from another, there must be a relation or correspondence. God's essence is absolutely transcendent and unrelated, so essential emanation is unintelligible. The conclusion is that creation must be volitional (al-ijad al-ikhtiyari).
The author also refutes the philosophers' claim about the one giving rise to only one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متكثر فوجوده بإيجاد غيره، وأيضا فإن الكل متركب من الآحاد، والآحاد إذا كان كل واحد منها ممكن الوجود فالكل واجب أن يكون ممكن الوجود، وكل ممكن فهو باعتبار ذاته جايز أن يوجد وجايز أن لا يوجد، وإذا ترجح جانب الوجود على العدم احتاج إلى مرجح والمرجح يستحيل أن يكون مرجحا باعتبار ذاته، ومن حيث وجوده فقط لأمور:

أحدها: أن الوجود من حيث هو وجود أمر يعم الواجب والجايز، وهو فيهما بمعنى واحد لا يختلف، فلو أوجد من حيث إنه وجود أو من حيث إنه ذات لما كان أحد الموجودين أولى بالإيجاد من الثاني، فيتعين أنه يوجد لكونه وجودًا على صفة أو ذاتا على صفة. ويدل على ذلك الأمر الثاني: وهو أن وجوه الجواز قد تطرقت إلى الأفعال، وتلك الوجوه متماثلة، والموجب لا يخصص مثلا عن مثل، فإن نسبة أحد المثلين إليه كنسبته إلى المثل الثاني، فإذا خصص أحد المثلين دون الثاني علم أن الإيجاب الذاتي باطل، فإن منعوا تلك الوجوه فلا شك أن الجايز بنفسه يتساوى طرفاه ونسبة من حيث هو ذات إلى أحد طرفيه كنسبته إلى الطرف الثاني، فإذا تخصص الذات بالوجود دون العدم فلا بد من مخصص وراء كونه ذاتا وبطل الإيجاب بالذات.

والثالث: أن الموجب بالذات ما لم يناسب الموجب بوجه من وجوه المناسبة لم يحصل الموجب، وذلك أنا إذا تصورنا ذاتين أو أمرين معلومين لا اتصال لأحدهما بالآخر ولا مناسبة بينهما ولا تعلق ولا إشعار، بل اختص كل واحد منهما بحقيقته وخاصته لم يقض العقل بصدور أحدهما عن الثاني، وواجب الوجود لذاته ذات قد تعالى وتقدس عن جميع وجوه المناسبات والتعلق والاتصال، بل هو منفرد بحقيقته التي هي له وهي وجوب وجوده ولا صفة له ثانية تزيد على ذاته الواجبة، فلا يلزم أن يوجد عنه شيء بحكم الذات، فإيجاب الذات غير معقول أصلا بعد رفع النسب والعلائق، وعن هذا قلتم: إن الموجب لما كان واحدا استحال أن يصدر عنه شيئان معا، ولما كان عقلا أي: مجردا عن المادة أوجب عقلا بالفعل مجردا عن المادة، ولما اقتضى الإيجاب مناسبة والمناسبة تقتضي المماثلة حتى ينوب أحد المثلين مناب الثاني؛ ثبت لواجب الوجود مثل ثبوت مناب واجب الوجود في الإيجاب، فعرف أن الإيجاب الذاتي باطل، فتعين الإيجاد الاختياري، وذلك ما أردنا أن نورد.
It is multiple so its existence is by causing another. Also, the whole is composed of parts, and if each part is possible of existence, the whole must be possible of existence. Every possible thing, in itself, is admissible of existence and admissible of non-existence. If the side of existence outweighs non-existence, it needs a preponderator, and the preponderator is impossible to be a preponderator by virtue of its essence and by virtue of its existence only, for reasons:

First: Existence qua existence is something common to the necessary and the possible, and it is in both with one meaning without difference. So if it were to bring into existence by virtue of existence or by virtue of essence, then one of the existents would not be more deserving of being brought into existence than the other. Thus it is determined that it brings into existence because it is existence with a characteristic or essence with a characteristic. That is indicated by the second reason: namely, that the aspects of possibility have applied to acts, and those aspects are similar, and the necessitator does not specify one similar from another. For the relationship of one of the two similars to it is like its relationship to the second similar. So if it specifies one of the two similars over the other, it is known that essential necessitation is false. But if they deny those aspects, then there is no doubt that the possible in itself has its two sides equal, and its relationship qua essence to one of its sides is like its relationship to the second side. So if the essence is specified with existence over non-existence, there must be a specifying factor beyond its being an essence, and essential necessitation is invalidated.

Third: The essential necessitator, as long as it does not correspond to the necessitated in any aspect of correspondence, the necessitated does not occur. That is because if we conceive two essences or two known things with no connection of one to the other, no correspondence, no relation, and no indication, but each is particular to its own reality and property, the intellect does not judge that one proceeds from the other. And the Necessary of Existence in essence is an essence that is exalted and transcendent above all aspects of correspondences, relations, and connections. Rather, it is unique in its reality that belongs to it, which is the necessity of its existence, and it has no second attribute additional to its necessary essence. So it does not necessarily follow that anything proceeds from it by virtue of essence. Thus essential necessitation is unintelligible at all after negating relations and connections. And from this you said: since the necessitator is one, it is impossible for two things to proceed from it simultaneously; and since it is an intellect, i.e., abstract from matter, it necessitates an actual intellect abstract from matter; and since necessitation requires correspondence, and correspondence requires similarity so that one of the two similars substitutes for the second, then there would be established for the Necessary of Existence a similar that substitutes for the Necessary of Existence in necessitation. Thus it is known that essential necessitation is false, and so volitional bringing-into-existence is determined. That is what we intended to present.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the doctrine of essential necessitation (al-ijab bi al-dhat), which holds that God's essence necessarily and eternally produces the world. The author presents three arguments to prove that this is impossible and that the world must arise from voluntary choice.

First argument: Existence is a common term applied to both the necessary and the possible. If God brought the world into existence merely by virtue of being existence or essence, there would be no reason for Him to create one thing over another. Therefore, creation must involve a specific characteristic or quality, not just essence.

Second argument: The possible is equally balanced between existence and non-existence. If God's essence alone necessitated creation, it could not specify one side over the other without a additional factor. Such specification requires a voluntary choice, not essential causation.

Third argument: For one thing to proceed from another, there must be some relation or connection. God's essence is absolutely transcendent and has no relation to contingent things. Therefore, essential emanation is unintelligible. The author also notes that if essential necessitation were true, it would require a similarity between cause and effect, leading to multiplicity and anthropomorphism, which is impossible for God.

Conclusion: Essential necessitation is false, and the world must be created by an act of will (al-ijad al-ikhtiyari).

Key Terms
الوجود
الواجب
الجائز
الإيجاب الذاتي
المرجح
المخصص
الإيجاد الاختياري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's eternal essence implies an eternal world necessarily.
Correction: The passage argues that essence alone cannot specify which world exists; therefore, creation requires a voluntary act.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the three arguments presented in this passage?
A) Essential necessitation is true.
B) The world is eternal.
C) God's essence necessitates.
D) Essential necessitation is false.

Key Takeaway
Essential necessitation is rationally false; creation must be volitional.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage argues that God's essence alone cannot necessitate creation because existence is common. In your life, do you ever assume that God's actions toward you are automatic or necessary based on your deeds? How does this lesson call you to see His will as free and personal? What one change in your attitude toward God's decrees will you make starting today?
?
If the Necessary Being is absolutely transcendent with no relation to created things, how does that challenge your sense of closeness to Him? Does it make you feel distant, or does it deepen your awe? Identify a specific moment this week when you will intentionally meditate on God’s transcendence to cultivate humility and reliance on His will.
?
The proof shows that essential necessitation would imply a similarity between cause and effect, leading to absurdity. What in your own thinking might be a 'similarity' you wrongly assume between God and yourself (e.g., expecting God to act like a human would)? How can you actively guard against comparing God to His creation in your worship and daily decisions?

The Principle of Possibility and the Priority of the Necessary Existent
Juwayni defines origination and argues from possibility to the priority of the Necessary Existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Juwayni's definition of origination as existence from non-existence and how the possibility of the world entails that its existence depends on a cause, making the Necessary Existent prior in essence and existence.

Core Idea
The world's possibility proves it needs a cause; that cause is prior in essence, not time.

Juwayni begins by clarifying what it means for something to originate from non-existence. He then builds a powerful argument: since the world is possible in itself, its existence cannot be self-sufficient—it requires a cause. This cause must be the Necessary Existent, which is prior to the world in essence and existence, not merely in time.

Guided Path
1
Understand the clarification of origination
Read the first part where Juwayni explains that 'existence from nothing' is the meaning of origination. He says phrases like 'it was not and then it was' indicate the precedence of non-existence. This removes the illusion of temporal priority.
Notice that Juwayni acknowledges the need for expansive language.
2
Grasp the principle of possibility
Focus on the statement that the world is possible in itself, meaning it is equally capable of existence and non-existence. This is the key premise.
The term 'possible' here means contingent, not impossible.
3
See the implication of existence
Since the world is possible, if it exists, it must be due to a cause. Without that cause, it would be non-existent.
This is the core logical step: possibility alone does not guarantee existence.
4
Conclude the priority of the Necessary Existent
Therefore, the Necessary Existent (the cause) is prior to the world in essence and existence. Without it, the world would not exist.
This priority is essential, not temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فافهم ذلك فيجب أن تزيل هذا الإيهام عن فكرك، فيتصور أن وجود شيء لا من شيء هو المعني بحدوث الشيء عن العدم، فإن قولنا : له أول المعنى بحدوثه، وإن قولنا : لم يكن فكان هو المعنى بسبق العدم ؛ إذ لا بد من عبارة وتوسع في الكلام ليطلق لفظ السبق والتأخر والاستمرار والانقطاع ، وإذا ثبتت هذه القاعدة، فتقول : إذا كان العالم ممكن الوجود باعتبار ذاته، والممكن معناه أنه جايز الوجود وجايز العدم، فيستوي طرفاه أعني : الوجود والعدم باعتبار ذاته ، فإذا وجد فإنما يوجد باعتبار موجده ، ولولا موجده لما استحق إلا العدم، فهو إذا مستحق الوجود والعدم بالاعتبارين المذكورين، فكان واجب الوجود سابقا عليه بالذات والوجود ؛ إذ لولاه لما وجد.
So understand that, and you must remove this illusion from your mind, so that you conceive that the existence of something from nothing is the meaning of the origination (ḥudūth) of a thing from non-existence. For our saying 'it has a beginning' is the meaning of its origination, and our saying 'it was not and then it was' is the meaning of the precedence of non-existence; for there must be expression and expansion in speech to allow the terms of precedence, posteriority, continuity, and discontinuity. And when this principle is established, we say: if the world is possible of existence in consideration of its essence, and the possible means that it is permissible of existence and permissible of non-existence, so its two sides are equal, namely existence and non-existence in consideration of its essence. So if it exists, it only exists by consideration of its cause, and were it not for its cause it would deserve nothing but non-existence. So it is deserving of existence and non-existence by the two mentioned considerations. Thus the Necessary Existent was prior to it in essence and existence, because without It it would not exist.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is a definition and a proof. First, the author commands the reader to remove the previous illusion and conceive 'existence from nothing' as the meaning of origination (ḥudūth). He explains that saying 'it has a beginning' means it originated, and 'it was not and then it was' means non-existence preceded it. This language is necessary but expansive. Then he establishes the principle: if the world is possible in itself (i.e., equally capable of existence and non-existence), then if it exists, it does so only by its cause. Without that cause, it would be nothing. Therefore, the Necessary Existent is prior to the world in essence and existence, because without It the world would not exist. This priority is essential, not temporal.

Key Terms
الإيهام (illusion)
حدوث (origination)
العدم (non-existence)
ممكن الوجود (possible of existence)
جايز الوجود وجايز العدم (permissible of existence and non-existence)
واجب الوجود (Necessary Existent)
سابق بالذات والوجود (prior in essence and existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the priority of God to the world is temporal.
Correction: The priority is essential and existential, not temporal. The Necessary Existent is prior as the cause of existence, not in time.

Checkpoint

B) Because it is necessary
C) Because it is possible in itself
D) Because it is eternal

Key Takeaway
The possibility of the world demands a cause, and that cause—the Necessary Existent—is prior in essence, not merely in time.

The Three Categories and the Attribution of Existence to the Creator
Enumerates three categories and defends the correct attribution of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational categories of existence and why only existence is directly attributed to the Creator, not the accidental necessity.

Core Idea
Existence is from God; necessity is a consequence, not a direct act.

Before we can discuss creation, we need a clear vocabulary. Theologians classify all beings into three categories: the Necessary (God), the Impossible (contradictions), and the Possible (contingent). This passage clarifies that the Possible depends on the Creator for its existence alone, not for the necessity that follows. This distinction guards against a subtle theological error.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the key terms: واجب, ممتنع, ممكن, عرضي, ذاتي.
The passage is dense; go slowly.
2
Identify the three categories
Locate where the author states that the Necessary and Impossible are extremes, and the Possible is a middle. Understand the definitions.
3
Understand the core argument about attribution
The author argues that only existence (الوجود) is attributed to the Creator (الموجد). The necessity that adheres to the possible is an accident (عرضي) and not directly from the Creator. Compare the correct statement 'وجد بإيجاده' vs the incorrect 'وجب وجوده بإيجابه'.
This is the key doctrinal point.
4
Reflect on the challenge
Consider why someone might mistakenly attribute necessity to the Creator. Reflect on how this distinction protects God's transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي نثبته أن الواجب والممتنع طرفان والممكن واسطة؛ إذ ليس بواجب ولا ممتنع فهو جايز الوجود وجايز العدم، والوجود والعدم متقابلان لا واسطة بينهما، والذي يستند إلى الموجد من وجهين الوجود والعدم في الممكن وجوده فقط حتى أن يقال: أوجده أي أعطاه الوجود ثم لزمه الوجوب لزوم العرضيات، فالأمر اللازم العرضي لا يستند إلى الموجد، فأنتم إذا قلتم: وجب وجوده بإيجابه فقد أخذتم العرضي، ونحن إذا قلنا: وجد بإيجاده فقد أخذنا عين المستفاد الذاتي، فاستقام كلامنا لفظا ومعنى وانحرف كلامكم عن سنن الجادة. ولربما نقول: إن الممكن إذا وجد في وقت معين أو على شكل مخصوص يجب وجوده على ذلك الوجه؛ لأن الموجد إذا علم حصوله في ذلك الوقت على ذلك الشكل، وأراد ذلك فهو واجب الوقوع؛ لأن خلاف المعلوم مستحيل الوقوع والحصول، وإذا كان ممكن الجنس جايز الذات، لكن لم يكن وجوب ذلك إلا
And what we affirm is that the Necessary and the Impossible are two extremes, and the Possible is a middle, for it is neither necessary nor impossible; rather it is possible of existence and possible of non-existence. Existence and non-existence are contraries with no middle between them. And what depends on the Creator from the two aspects of existence and non-existence – in the possible – is only its existence, so that it is said: 'He brought it into existence,' i.e., He gave it existence, then necessity adhered to it as accidents adhere. For the necessary accident does not depend on the Creator. So when you say: 'Its existence became necessary by His necessitation,' you have taken the accident. And when we say: 'It existed by His bringing into existence,' we have taken the very essential benefit, so our statement is correct in word and meaning, and your statement deviates from the proper path. And perhaps we say: When the possible exists at a specific time or in a specific form, its existence in that manner becomes necessary, because if the Creator knows its occurrence at that time in that form, and wills that, then it is necessary of occurrence, for the contrary of what is known is impossible of occurrence and attaining. And if it is possible in genus and possible in essence, yet its necessity is only...
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage clarifies the three categories of existence: the Necessary (God), the Impossible (contradictions), and the Possible (contingent beings). The possible is a middle term: it is possible of existence and non-existence. Existence and non-existence are direct opposites with no intermediate. The author argues that only existence is attributed to the Creator; the necessity that follows existence is an accidental property (عرضي) and does not originate from the Creator. Those who say 'its existence became necessary by His necessitation' mistakenly attribute this accident to God, while the correct formulation is 'it existed by His bringing into existence,' which directly attributes existence. The author then adds a nuance: when a possible being exists at a specific time and form due to God's knowledge and will, its occurrence becomes necessary (in the sense that it must happen as known), but this necessity is not directly from the agent but a consequence of divine knowledge and will. The passage ends abruptly, but the argument is that the possible's necessity in specific circumstances does not negate its essential possibility.

Key Terms
واجب
ممتنع
ممكن
عرضي
ذاتي
موجد
وجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The necessitation of a possible being's existence is directly from the Creator.
Correction: The Creator gives existence only; necessity is an accidental property that follows existence independently.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which aspect of a possible being is directly attributed to the Creator?
A) Both existence and necessity
B) Only existence
C) Only necessity
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The Creator grants existence to possible beings; the necessity that follows is not directly from Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The passage warns against saying 'God necessitated existence' and instead insists on 'God brought into existence.' ?
If only existence is from God, then every quality you have—your intelligence, health, opportunities—are mere accidents that adhere. Are you more attached to these accidents or to the existential gift itself? How can you shift your gratitude from the gifts to the Giver of existence?

The Conditional Necessity of the Possible
Explains how a possible being becomes necessary in specific circumstances due to divine knowledge and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a possible being's existence becomes necessary in a specific form or time due to divine knowledge and will.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge and will make the possible necessary, without changing its essence.

Having established that existence alone is directly from the Creator, the author now addresses a nuance: when a possible being exists at a specific time and in a specific form, its occurrence becomes necessary because God knows and wills it. This necessity is not a contradiction to its essential possibility but a consequence of the all-encompassing divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the conditional structure: if the possible exists at a specific time and form, then...
The passage is cut off, but the main point is clear.
2
Identify the condition
The author says: when the possible exists at a specific time and form, its existence becomes necessary in that manner. Why? Because God knows and wills it.
3
Understand the reasoning
The reasoning: if God knows something will occur at a specific time and form, and wills that, then it must happen, because the contrary of what is known is impossible. This does not negate the possible's essential possibility.
This shows that necessity can arise from divine attributes without contradicting the possible's nature.
4
Reflect on the broader implications
Consider how this argument preserves both God's foreknowledge and human free will (or contingency of creation).
The passage ends before full resolution, but the logic is standard in kalam.

Proof of the Impossibility of Actual Infinity
The author presents a proof by contradiction against actual infinity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the impossibility of an actual infinite is proven by contradiction using the method of estimation (tawahhum).

Core Idea
Assuming an actual infinite leads to the part equaling the whole, a contradiction.

In this passage, the author presents a logical proof that an actually infinite quantity cannot exist. He uses the method of 'estimation' (tawahhum) to compare parts of an assumed infinite series, showing that this leads to a contradiction. This argument applies not only to physical bodies but also to motions, individuals, and souls.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author begins with a contradiction involving two magnitudes.
Pay attention to the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the proof structure
The proof is by contradiction: assume an actual infinite, then derive a contradiction. Identify the steps: the assumption, the estimation, the comparison, and the conclusion.
3
Apply the reasoning to different domains
The author generalizes the proof to motions, individuals, and souls. See how the same structure applies.
Note that the proof does not depend on the type of entity.
4
Understand the key term 'tawahhum'
Estimation (tawahhum) is the mental operation of isolating a part from an infinite series. Grasp why this is crucial for the proof.
This is the main tool for generating the contradiction.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The proof shows that actual infinity is impossible in all these cases. Consider why this matters in kalam and theology.
This argument is foundational for arguments about God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يمتدا بل يقصر أحدهما عنه فيكون متناهيا والقدر المفضول كان متناهيا أيضا، فيكون المجموع متناهيًا أيضا وقد فرضه غير متناه هذا خلف، وكذلك إن فرضته في حركات غير متناهية، أو أشخاص غير متناهية متعاقبة أو مجتمعة، أو تفرض نفوسا غير متناهية معا أو مجتمعة في الوجود، فالعبارة عنه أن نقول لك أن تفرض حركة واحدة أو شخصاً واحدًا، وتفصل من الحركات أو الأشخاص مقدارا بالتوهم، وتنسب الحركات الزائدة إلى الحركات الناقصة، وتم البرهان من غير تفاوت بين الصورتين.
They extend but one of them falls short of the other, so it becomes finite, and the deficient quantity is also finite, thus the sum is also finite, but it was assumed infinite — this is a contradiction. Similarly, if you assume it in infinite motions, or infinite individuals successive or simultaneous, or you assume infinite souls together or simultaneous in existence, then the expression for it is that we say to you: you assume one motion or one individual, and you separate a quantity from the motions or individuals by estimation, and you relate the excess motions to the deficient motions, and the proof is completed without difference between the two cases.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a proof by contradiction demonstrating the impossibility of an actual infinite. The author first considers two magnitudes: if one is finite and the other is finite, their sum must be finite. But if the sum was assumed infinite, a contradiction arises. He then applies the same reasoning to infinite motions, individuals, and souls, whether they are successive or simultaneous. The crucial move is to imagine taking one item (e.g., one motion) and subtracting a finite quantity from the infinite series via estimation (توهم). Then comparing the deficient part to the excessive part leads to the conclusion that the less equals the more, which is absurd. The proof concludes that there is no difference between the various cases; the same contradiction holds in all.

Key Terms
متناه (finite)
غير متناه (infinite)
تقدير بالتوهم (estimation)
خلف (contradiction)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof only applies to physical bodies.
Correction: The author extends the proof to motions, individuals, and souls, whether successive or simultaneous.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the proof in this passage?
A) Actual infinites are possible if they are successive.
B) Assuming an actual infinite leads to a logical contradiction.
C) Only bodies can be infinite, not motions.
D) Souls are finite because they are simultaneous.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of an actual infinite is proven by showing that any attempt to conceive it leads to a logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that an actual infinite is impossible, have you considered that your own list of sins and good deeds is finite, and therefore you will be held accountable for each one? How does this realization change how you view your actions today?
?
The proof includes souls: no infinite number of souls can exist together. Does this affect how you view the Day of Judgment, where all souls are gathered? Do you live as if that gathering is a finite reality you must face?
?
If you accept that an actual infinite is impossible, then the universe must have a beginning. That means your own existence is contingent on a Creator. Are you living in light of that contingency, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient?
