The Proof that Past Motions are Finite
First part of proof: from present moment to finitude of past motions and time

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core proof that past events and motions cannot be infinite because they end at the present moment.

Core Idea
The present moment makes the past finite.

This lesson unpacks the first part of Sanusi's proof. He starts with the present cycle and argues that the past, having ended here, must be finite. You will see how each motion has a beginning and an end, and how time as a counter also becomes finite.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key premise
Understand that the past has ended with the present moment; whatever ends with something is finite.
Sanusi uses 'الدورة التي نحن فيها' (the cycle we are in) as the reference point.
2
Follow the logic for each motion
Every motion that exists and ceases has a finite side; it has a last part that is the end of the past and the beginning of what remains. Thus each motion has a first and last.
This shows finitude in essence (ذاتا).
3
See the role of time
Motions are counted by time, and if the counted is finite, the counter (time) must also be finite.
This closes the first part of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
برهان آخر في أن ما لا يتناهى من الحوادث والحركات لا يتحقق في الوجود وذلك أنا نفرض الكلام في الدورة التي نحن فيها، ولا شك أن ما مضى قد انتهى بها، وما انتهى بشيء فقد تناهى ومعنى قولنا: إن ما مضى قد انتهى أنا إذا أردنا قصر النظر على ما نحن فيه وقطعناه عما سيأتي اقتضى ذلك تناهي الحركات الماضية؛ إذ يتناهى الحركات الماضية لم يكن مشروطا فيها لحوق الحركات الآتية، فما من حركة توجد وتعدم إلا وقد انقضت حركات قبلها بلا نهاية، فيوجد أبدا طرف منها متناهيا ويكون ذلك الطرف آخر الماضي وأولا لما بقي: فكل حركة أو كل دورة فقد تحقق لها أول وآخر، وإذا تناهى من طرف فقد تناهى من الطرف الثاني، فالحركات كلها إذا في ذواتها متناهية أولا وآخرا، وهي معدودة بالزمان والزمان هو العاد للحركات، فالمعدود إذا يتناهى أولا وآخرا كذلك يلزم أن يتناهى العاد أولا وآخرا. يبقى أن يُقال: أهي متناهية عددًا أم لا؟ قلنا: وكل عدد موجود فهو قابل للزيادة والنقصان والأكثر والأقل فهو متناه، وكل عدد موجود فهو متناه، وهذا الحكم يجري في كل عدد موجود معًا كالنفوس الإنسانية، أو متعاقبة كالأشخاص الإنسانية وعندهم النفوس غير متناهية عددا وهي قابلة للزيادة والنقصان والأشخاص الإنسانية غير متناهية عددًا وهي أيضا قابلة للزيادة والنقصان فإذا طبقت الأشخاص على النفوس تساوت لا محالة، وإن نقصت الأشخاص عددًا وطبقت الباقي على عدد النفوس فإن كان كل واحد منهما لا يتناهى كان الزائد مثل الناقص، وإن كان يتناهى فقد حصل المقصود، وتطبيق الخط على الخط كتطبيق العدد على العدد وتطبيق النفس على الشخص، والحركات والمتحركات إذا تناهت ذاتا وزمانًا ومكانا وعددًا، وهذا قاطع لا جواب عنه ولا سؤال عليه.
Another proof that what is infinite of events and motions does not occur in existence: that we suppose the discourse in the cycle in which we are, and there is no doubt that what has passed has ended with it, and what ends with something is finite. And the meaning of our statement 'what has passed has ended' is that if we intend to restrict consideration to what we are in and cut it off from what will come, that necessitates the finitude of past motions; since the finitude of past motions is not conditioned by the attachment of future motions. So there is no motion that exists and ceases except that motions before it have passed without end, but there always exists a finite side of it, and that side is the last of the past and the first of what remains. So every motion or every cycle has a first and a last. And if it is finite from one side, it is finite from the other side. Thus all motions are in themselves finite, first and last, and they are counted by time, and time is the counter of motions. So if the counted is finite first and last, likewise it follows that the counter is finite first and last. It remains to be said: Are they finite in number or not? We say: Every existing number is subject to increase and decrease and more and less, so it is finite. And every existing number is finite. And this judgment applies to every number existing together, like human souls, or successive, like human individuals. And according to them, souls are infinite in number and are subject to increase and decrease, and human individuals are infinite in number and are also subject to increase and decrease. So if individuals are applied to souls, they necessarily equal. And if the individuals decrease in number and the remainder is applied to the number of souls, then if each of them is infinite, the greater is like the lesser; and if it is finite, then the goal is attained. And applying line to line is like applying number to number, and applying soul to individual. And motions and moving things are finite in essence, time, place, and number. And this is decisive, with no answer to it and no question against it.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a proof that an infinite regress of past events or motions is impossible. The author begins by stating the proof: consider the current cycle (the present moment). The past has ended with this cycle; whatever ends with something is finite. Thus, past motions are finite. He explains that the finitude of past motions is not dependent on future motions. Every motion that exists has a finite 'side'—an endpoint that is the last of the past and the first of the remaining. Each motion and cycle has a beginning and an end. Since things are finite from one end, they are finite from the other. Therefore, all motions are finite in their essence (first and last). They are counted by time, which is the counter; if the counted is finite, the counter must also be finite. The author then addresses a potential question: 'Are these motions finite in number?' He answers that every existing number is subject to increase and decrease, hence it is finite. This applies to numbers that exist simultaneously (like human souls) or successively (like human individuals). Some (the materialists?) claim souls are infinite and individuals are infinite, both subject to increase/decrease. When individuals are matched to souls, they must be equal. If individuals decrease, matching the remainder to souls shows that if both are infinite, the greater equals the lesser (absurd), and if finite, the goal is reached. He likens this to applying line to line, number to number, soul to individual. The conclusion: motions and moving things are finite in essence, time, place, and number. This argument is decisive and unanswerable.

Key Terms
متناهية
غير متناهية
حركات
دورة
زمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The past could be infinite if time itself is infinite.
Correction: The argument shows that by anchoring at the present, the past must have an endpoint, making it finite. Time, as a counter of events, cannot be infinite if the events it counts are finite.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why must past motions be finite?
A) Because they are not counted by time.
B) Because the past has ended with the present moment.
C) Because motions are infinite in essence.
D) Because future motions limit them.

Key Takeaway
The present moment forces the past to be finite, and this finitude extends to time as the counter of motions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the past is finite and ends at this very moment, then your entire life up to now is a countable number of breaths. Does the thought that each of your past actions is finite and recorded bring you peace or fear? In the next hour, what is one action you can take that you would want to be the 'last of the past' before a positive change?
?
The argument shows that time itself is finite because it counts finite motions. If time is not an endless commodity, how should that urgency reshape your priorities today? Will you let this awareness translate into a concrete decision before you sleep tonight?
?
The proof relies on the present moment as the boundary that makes the past finite. Do you live as though the present moment is a decisive cut-off point, or do you act as if there is always an infinite future to repent? What would it look like to treat this moment as the only moment you are guaranteed?

Refuting Infinite Numbers: Souls and Individuals
Second part: addressing numbers, souls, individuals, and final conclusion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the possibility of infinite numbers, applying the same principle to souls and human individuals.

Core Idea
Every existing number is finite because it can increase and decrease.

After proving that motions are finite, Sanusi anticipates an objection: what about numbers? He shows that any existing number, whether of souls or individuals, must be finite. This lesson walks through the application of line to line and the conclusion that all motions are finite in every respect.

Guided Path
1
Pose the question
Sanusi asks: Are motions finite in number? He then states the principle: every existing number is subject to increase and decrease, hence it is finite.
This covers both simultaneous numbers (souls) and successive numbers (individuals).
2
Apply to souls and individuals
Even if souls and individuals are claimed to be infinite, the fact that they can increase or decrease shows they must be finite. Matching one to the other reveals an absurdity if both are infinite.
The argument uses a one-to-one correspondence.
3
Analogy of line to line
Applying line to line is like applying number to number or soul to individual; this illustrates the impossibility of an infinite number.
4
Final conclusion
Motions and moving things are finite in essence, time, place, and number. This argument is decisive.
Sanusi claims no answer or question can refute it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

?
If the number of your sins is also finite, and every sin can be counted, does that give you hope that Allah's mercy can encompass them all? How does this change the way you think about repentance today versus putting it off?

Critique of the Second Premise: Possibility Does Not Entail Necessity
Refutation of the second premise's contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that 'what is possible must exist' is contradictory, and how its revised formulation leads to the necessity of a cause.

Core Idea
Possibility and necessity cannot be the same thing.

In this passage, the theologian turns to the second premise of the argument for the world's eternity, which states that whatever is possible must exist. He immediately identifies a fundamental contradiction: possibility and necessity are opposites. Yet he anticipates a rephrasing that attempts to salvage the premise by distinguishing essence from cause. This lesson examines that initial critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the key terms: جواز (possibility) and وجوب (necessity).
The original text ends mid-sentence; we have only the beginning of the argument.
2
Identify the contradiction
The theologian states that the second premise is contradictory in wording and meaning. He points out that الجواز (possibility) and الوجوب (necessity) are opposites (متناقضان). Ask yourself: Why can't something be both possible and necessary in the same sense?
In logic, two opposite attributes cannot apply to the same thing in the same respect.
3
Understand the qualification
The theologian then considers a rephrasing: 'what is possible in its essence must exist by another.' This distinction allows that something can be possible in itself but necessary through an external cause. This leads to the idea that the world's existence requires a cause.
The text breaks off before the theologian's full response to this rephrasing, but the implication is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكلام على المقدمة الثانية؛ وهي أن ما يجوز وجوده يجب وجوده. قلنا هذا الكلام أولا متناقض لفظاً ومعنى؛ أما اللفظ فإن الجواز والوجوب متناقضان إلا أن يُقال : إن ما يجوز وجوده في ذاته يجب وجوده بغيره، قيل: وهذا
And as for the discussion of the second premise, which is that what is possible of existence must exist. We say: This statement is contradictory in wording and meaning. As for the wording, possibility and necessity are contradictory, unless it is said: that which is possible in its essence must exist by another. It is said: And this...
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage begins the refutation of the second premise. The theologian points out that the claim 'what is possible must exist' is contradictory in terms because possibility (jawāz) and necessity (wujūb) are opposites. However, one might rephrase it as 'what is possible in itself must exist through another', i.e., through a cause. The theologian then begins to respond to this rephrasing but the text breaks off. The argument is that if the world's existence is necessary through another, then it is contingent in itself and requires a cause, which undermines the claim of eternal existence.

Key Terms
جواز
وجوب
متناقض
بغيره

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if something is possible, it necessarily exists.
Correction: Possibility (jawāz) does not entail existence. Necessity (wujūb) is a stronger modality; something possible can exist or not exist. The premise confuses these two categories.

Checkpoint
What is the theologian's initial objection to the second premise?
A) The premise is unprovable
B) The premise is contradictory because possibility and necessity are opposites
C) The premise leads to atheism
D) The premise is accepted by all

Key Takeaway
The initial objection to the second premise is that it is self-contradictory; when reformulated, it points to the necessity of a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that something could just exist without a cause? Consider the implication that if the universe were merely possible, it would require a cause. Does your belief in God rest on recognizing that your own existence is contingent? If so, how does this affect your daily sense of dependence on Him?
?
The theologian points out that possibility and necessity are contradictory. In your life, do you treat the possible as if it were necessary—acting as if outcomes are guaranteed? How does recognizing contingency change your trust in Allah?
?
If the world's possibility points to the necessity of a cause, what does that cause demand from you? Are you living as if that Cause (Allah) is truly the only Necessary Being?

Objection on Successive Existences and Its Refutation
The author refutes an objection about infinite successive existences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between successive and simultaneous existences in arguments about infinity, and why the objection that an infinite past is possible fails.

Core Idea
Infinite simultaneous existences are impossible, but successive infinities are a different case.

This passage tackles a common imaginative objection: if an infinite future is possible, why not an infinite past? The author clarifies that the proof against an actual infinite only applies to things existing together at one time, not one after another. By the end, you'll see how a simple increase/decrease argument shuts down the confusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Let the author's flow come through.
Note the key terms: موجودات متعاقبة (successive) and موجودات مجتمعة (simultaneous).
2
Identify the objection
The objection says: if an infinite chain is possible at the end (future), it should be possible at the beginning (past). Find where this is stated.
The author says this notion is false according to reason but supported by imagination.
3
See how the author reframes
The author shifts the focus: the proof given earlier was about existents gathered together simultaneously, not successive ones. This is the key move.
4
Understand the refutation
For an actually infinite simultaneous collection, increase and decrease are impossible. If you add or subtract a finite number, the infinite would become more or less—a contradiction. Grasp this argument.
The illustration of adding or subtracting from an actual infinite clarifies why it's impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم، ربما يظن في الموجودات المتعاقبة في الوجود أنها لا تتناهى أولا قبل آخر، وآخرا بعد أول وأنها إذا جازت غير متناهية آخرا جازت غير متناهية أولا، وهذا الظن وإن كان خطأ عند العقل إلا أن الخيال ربما يعين هذا الرأي، لكن الفرض إذا كان في موجودات غير متناهية مجتمعة في الوجود لا متعاقبة أعدادا ومعدودات بالفعل لا بالقوة فيلزمها ضرورة ألا تقبل الزيادة والنقصان، إذ لو قبلت وهي غير متناهية لكان الزايد مثل الناقص والذي يوضحه أن كل جملة موجودة الآحاد بالفعل إذا زيد عليها عدد معلوم، أو نقص منها عدد محصور صار ما لا يتناهى أكثر مما كان أو أنقص.
Yes, it may be thought concerning successive existences that they are infinite: first before last, and last after first, and that if they are permissible as infinite at the end, they are permissible as infinite at the beginning. And this notion, although it is false according to reason, imagination may support this view. However, the hypothesis is about existents that are actually infinite and gathered together in existence, not successive, in numbers and numbered things actually, not potentially. Then it necessarily follows that they do not admit increase and decrease. For if they admitted it while being infinite, the increased would be like the decreased. And that which clarifies it is that every whole whose units are actually existent, if a known number is added to it or a limited number is subtracted from it, the infinite becomes more than it was or less.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation. The objection arises from the imagination: one might think that successive existences (al-mawjūdāt al-mutaʿāqiba) could be infinite, i.e., having no first before a last, or no last after a first, and that if an infinite series is permissible at the end (future), it should also be permissible at the beginning (past). Although reason rejects this, imagination supports it. The author refutes by clarifying that the proof given earlier concerns existents that are simultaneously gathered together (mujtamiʿa fī al-wujūd), not successive ones. For an actually infinite collection of simultaneous existents, increase and decrease are impossible. If such a collection admitted increase while being infinite, the increased would equal the decreased—a contradiction. The author provides an illustration: any whole consisting of actual units, when a finite number is added or subtracted, the resulting infinite would become larger or smaller, which is absurd for an actual infinite.

Key Terms
موجودات متعاقبة
موجودات مجتمعة
غير متناه
زيادة
نقصان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if an infinite future is possible, an infinite past must also be possible.
Correction: The author shows that the proof against an actual infinite only applies to things existing simultaneously, not to successive series. Successive infinities are not the same as a simultaneous infinite collection.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the key distinction that refutes the objection about successive existences?
A) The difference between infinite and finite
B) The difference between simultaneous and successive existences
C) The difference between actual and potential existence
D) The difference between numbers and numbered things

Key Takeaway
The proof against an actual infinite only applies to simultaneously existing things, not to successive series; thus, objections based on successive infinities are invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage shows that imagination can lead us astray even when reason has already settled a matter. Think of a time when you held onto a belief or suspicion simply because it 'felt right' even after learning the truth. What will you do to train your heart to submit to reason in matters of faith?
?
The distinction between simultaneous and successive infinities reveals that not all infinities are the same. In your life, do you treat your relationship with Allah as a 'simultaneous' present surrender or a 'successive' series of future promises? What change must you make today to bring your intention into the present?
?
If an actual infinite cannot be increased or decreased, consider your deeds: every deed is finite and recorded. How does this truth affect how you view the apparent insignificance of a small sin or a small good deed? What one concrete action will you take after this lesson to add to your balance of good deeds?

Why the Number of Souls Cannot Be Actually Infinite
Argument that an actually infinite number of souls cannot admit increase or decrease.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an actually infinite number of souls cannot exist because its increase over time leads to a logical contradiction.

Core Idea
An actual infinite cannot increase, so souls—which increase over time—cannot be infinite.

This passage presents a key argument from Islamic theology against the possibility of an actual infinite number of human souls. The author uses logical reasoning to show that since souls increase over time, they cannot be actually infinite. Let's unpack this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the source passage carefully to get the overall argument. Pay attention to the key terms: souls, infinite, increase.
The Arabic is dense; the translation will help you follow the logic.
2
Identify the opposing view
The passage begins by describing the opposing group’s claim that human souls are actually existing and infinite. Notice how the author sets up the assumption he will refute.
3
Trace the increase argument
The author argues that souls increase over time because new individuals are born every moment. An actually infinite number cannot become larger, so the assumption leads to a contradiction.
This is the core of the proof.
4
Understand the ratio analogy
The author compares past souls to present souls using a ratio of deficient to excessive. Think about why this ratio is impossible for an actual infinite.
This clarifies the logical impossibility.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that such a situation is impossible for an actually existing infinite confined in existence. Consider what this means for the nature of souls.
This argument supports the finitude of created beings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنفوس الإنسانية عند القوم موجودات بالفعل مجتمعة في الوجود، قابلة للزيادة والنقصان فإن الزمان الذي نحن فيه قد اشتمل على أشخاص إنسانية متناهية ولكل شخص نفس، إذا أضفت تلك النفوس إلى ما مضى من الأشخاص التي بقيت نفوسها كانت الأوائل منها أنقص، وهي مع الزيادة أزيد، وكذلك في كل زمان وساعة تتزايد النفوس أبدًا، وما قبل الزيادة في كل وقت يستحيل أن يكون غير متناه ؛ وذلك لأن نسبة ما مضى إلى الحاصل كنسبة الناقص إلى الزايد، وحيث ما تمادى إلى الأول صار أنقص، كما إذا تمادى إلى الآخر صار أزيد ، ومثل هذا مستحيل فيما لا يتناهى موجودًا بالفعل محصورًا في الوجود.
Human souls, according to the [opposing] group, are actually existing, collected in existence, capable of increase and decrease. For the time in which we are has included finite human individuals, and each individual has a soul. If you add those souls to the past individuals whose souls remain, the earliest of them are less, and they are with increase more. Likewise in every time and hour the souls increase forever. And that which is before increase at every time is impossible to be infinite. That is because the ratio of what has passed to what is obtained is like the ratio of the deficient to the excessive. Whenever it extends to the first it becomes less, just as when it extends to the last it becomes more. And such is impossible for an infinite that is actually existing, confined in existence.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is a proof by an increase-based argument. The author considers the view of those who claim that human souls are actually infinite in number, coexisting simultaneously. He argues that if souls were infinite, they would have to admit increase over time, but an actually infinite cannot increase or decrease. The argument proceeds: in each moment there are finite number of people, each with a soul. When you consider all souls from past to present, the number grows over time. A thing that always increases cannot be infinite, because adding to an infinite would make it larger, which is impossible for an infinite. The ratio of past to present souls is like that of a smaller to a larger number. If you go back to the beginning, the number is smaller; if you go to the end, it is larger. Such a relation is impossible for an actually existing infinite that is confined in existence.

Key Terms
نفوس (souls)
غير متناه (infinite)
زيادة (increase)
نقصان (decrease)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an infinite set can increase because we can always add one more, like the set of natural numbers can have new elements added.
Correction: The author shows that an actually existing infinite, unlike a potential infinite, does not admit increase. If souls were actually infinite, they would already be complete, so no addition is possible.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why can't an actually infinite number of souls exist?
A) Because souls are immaterial
B) Because souls increase over time, but an infinite cannot increase
C) Because the past is infinite
D) Because souls are finite in number

Key Takeaway
An actually existing infinite cannot admit increase or decrease, so the number of souls must be finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your soul is one of a finite number created in time. Does this truth make you feel small or significant? How does the fact that your soul is not part of an endless chain change the weight of your choices today?
?
If the number of souls were infinite, your existence might be lost in an endless crowd. Since it is finite, you are known and countable before God. How does this awareness affect your sense of accountability for every moment you are given?
?
The author argues that an infinite cannot increase. Your soul, however, can increase in virtue or decrease in sin. Which direction is your soul trending right now? What will you do before the next 'increase' is accounted for?

Why Simultaneous Infinity Prevents Increase or Decrease
Objection and answer: successive vs simultaneous infinity and increase/decrease.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an actually infinite set of simultaneous beings cannot admit increase or decrease, and how this refutes objections about infinite series.

Core Idea
An actually infinite set cannot be made larger or smaller by any finite amount.

This passage tackles a common objection: if the universe can extend infinitely into the future, why not infinitely into the past? The author distinguishes between temporal succession and simultaneous existence. For beings that coexist at once, infinity precludes any change in number. Let us examine how he argues this point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without worrying about technical details. Then read the English translation. Notice the shift from discussing successive beings to simultaneous ones.
The passage starts with an objection, then refutes it by distinguishing between two types of infinity.
2
Identify the two scenarios
Find where the author talks about 'beings that succeed each other' (successive) and 'beings that are simultaneous'. Mark the contrast.
Successive infinity may be imaginable, but simultaneous infinity is the real focus.
3
Understand the logical proof
The author argues that a simultaneous infinite set cannot admit increase or decrease. The reason is given: if you add or subtract a finite number, the set becomes 'more' or 'less' infinite, which is contradictory.
This is a core argument against actual physical infinity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم، ربما يظن في الموجودات المتعاقبة في الوجود أنها لا تتناهى أولا قبل آخر، وآخرا بعد أول وأنها إذا جازت غير متناهية آخرًا جازت غير متناهية أولا، وهذا الظن وإن كان خطأ عند العقل إلا أن الخيال ربما يعين هذا الرأي، لكن الفرض إذا كان في موجودات غير متناهية مجتمعة في الوجود لا متعاقبة أعدادًا ومعدودات بالفعل لا بالقوة فيلزمها ضرورة ألا تقبل الزيادة والنقصان، إذ لو قبلت وهي غير متناهية لكان الزايد مثل الناقص والذي يوضحه أن كل جملة موجودة الآحاد بالفعل إذا زيد عليها عدد معلوم، أو نقص منها عدد محصور صار ما لا يتناهى أكثر مما كان أو أنقص.
Yes, perhaps one might think about beings that succeed each other in existence that they are infinite, first without last, and last without first, and that if they are allowed to be infinite at the end, they are allowed to be infinite at the beginning. And this opinion, though it is false according to reason, imagination might support this view. But the supposition, if it is about infinite beings that are simultaneous in existence, not successive, in numbers and counted things actually, not potentially, then necessarily they must not admit increase and decrease. For if they admitted it while being infinite, the increased would be like the decreased. And what clarifies it is that every aggregate whose units actually exist, if a known number is added to it or a limited number is subtracted from it, the infinite becomes more than it was or less.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection: one might think that since beings can succeed each other infinitely into the future, they could also be infinite in the past. The author says this is false in reason, though imagination may suggest it. The real focus is on beings that exist simultaneously, not in succession. If such simultaneous beings were infinite, they cannot admit increase or decrease. The reasoning: if you add or subtract a finite number from an infinite set, the result would be greater or lesser, which is impossible for an infinite. So simultaneous infinite must be unchanging in size. The objector's confusion stems from mixing successive and simultaneous existence.

Key Terms
متعاقبة
مجتمعة
زيادة ونقصان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an infinite series can be infinite in one temporal direction (future), it can be infinite in the other (past).
Correction: Successive infinity in one direction does not imply the other direction is also infinite; moreover, the key case is simultaneous infinity, which behaves differently.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the idea that successive beings can be infinite in the past?
A) Because the past has already ended
B) Because reason rejects it, though imagination may allow it
C) Because the past is finite
D) Because succession implies finitude

Key Takeaway
Simultaneous infinite beings cannot be increased or decreased because any finite change would imply a change in infinity, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that adding to an actual infinite is impossible, yet you often act as if your accumulations of wealth, status, or knowledge will satisfy you. When do you feel the emptiness of this pursuit? What finite addition are you chasing right now that you think will finally make you 'enough'?
?
If an infinite being cannot be increased or decreased, what does that say about your attempts to please Allah through deeds? Are you trying to 'add' to His glory, or are you seeking to align yourself with the infinite? Where in your life are you treating your relationship with Allah as a finite transaction rather than an infinite connection?
?
The passage shows that the imagination can accept what reason rejects. Think of a time when your imagination made you doubt a rational truth about Allah. How did you overcome that? What will you do the next time your feelings contradict what your intellect knows?

The Proof of Universal Finitude
Conditional argument proving finitude of all beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that all created beings—from souls to spiritual movers—must be finite, and the distinction between actual and estimative infinity.

Core Idea
If an infinite multitude were actual, it would entail logical contradictions.

This passage presents a sophisticated conditional argument to prove that everything in creation—souls, bodies, celestial motions, and spiritual movers—must be finite. Using logical reasoning, the author shows that an actually infinite number of souls leads to impossibility, and then extends the conclusion to all beings. He also clarifies the meaning of 'infinite number,' distinguishing between actual infinity (impossible) and infinity in estimation (possible in thought).

Guided Path
1
Follow the conditional argument about souls
Read the first part of the passage carefully. The author sets up a conditional: if an infinite number of souls have already entered existence, then consider the remaining souls. They must be either finite or infinite. Follow each case and see the contradiction.
Notice the use of 'if-then' reasoning typical of kalam.
2
Understand the proportion argument
The author then introduces a proportion argument: the remaining souls must have a numerical proportion (e.g., half) relative to the existing infinite. But an actual infinite cannot have a proportion. This proves the total must be finite.
This step reinforces why an actual infinite is impossible.
3
See the extension to all beings
The conclusion that souls are finite is then extended: since each soul is attached to a body, bodies are finite; then elemental mixtures, celestial motions, and spiritual movers are all finite. This shows the universal finitude of creation.
The argument cascades from souls to the entire cosmos.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : إذا دخل ما لا يتناهى في الوجود من النفوس، فالباقي من النفوس التي لم تدخل في الوجود بعد أما إن كانت متناهية أو غير متناهية، فإن لم تكن متناهية فقد تضاعف ما لا يتناهى بما لا يتناهى وذلك محال، وإذا كانت متناهية فإذا زيدت على غير المتناهي صارت الجملة أكثر من المقدار الأول، وقد علم أن غير المتناهي الموجود لا يكون شيء أكثر منه، وهذا أكثر منه فهو خلف، ثم يلزم أن يكون الباقي على نسبة عددية ؛ إذ هي موجودة بالفعل، أما نسبة النصف أو الثلث أو الربع أو غير ذلك من النسب. وإذا كانت الجملة غير متناهية بالفعل استحال تقدير النسبة إليها، فإن ما لا يتناهى لا نصف له ولا ثلث ولا ربع وإذا تحققت النسبة دل ذلك على أن الجملة محصورة معدودة متناهية وليس يلزم على ما ذكرناه كون العدد غير متناه، فإنا لا نسلم ذلك في عدد معلوم موجود. و معنى قولنا : إن العدد لا يتناهى أنه في التقدير الوهمي لا يتناهى ؛ أي الوهم يعجز عن بلوغ حد ونهاية فيه، فيحكم العقل أنه لا نهاية له، وكما أن العدد على الإطلاق معلوم من غير ربط بمعدود، وكذلك النصف والثلث والربع على الإطلاق معلوم من غير نسبة إلى ما لا يتناهى إنما المستحيل إثبات أعداد موجودة غير متناهية، فإن كل موجود معدود فهو محدود ، وإذا تحقق بطلان النفوس غير المتناهية، وكانت كل نفس متعلقة ببدن تحقق بطلان الأبدان غير المتناهية، ثم إذا تناهت الأبدان والنفوس الإنسانية تناهت الامتزاجات العنصرية، فتناهت الحركات الدورية السماوية، فتناهت المتحركات العلوية، فتناهت المحركات الروحانية،
So we say: If an infinite of souls enters into existence, then the remaining souls that have not yet entered into existence are either finite or infinite. If they are not finite, then the infinite is multiplied by the infinite, and that is impossible. And if they are finite, then when added to the infinite, the total becomes greater than the first quantity. And it is known that the existing infinite does not have anything greater than it. This is greater than it, so it is a contradiction. Then it follows that the remainder is in a numerical proportion, since it actually exists, such as a half, a third, a quarter, or other proportions. And if the aggregate is actually infinite, it is impossible to estimate a proportion to it, for the infinite has no half, no third, no quarter. If the proportion is realized, that indicates that the aggregate is confined, counted, finite. And it does not follow from what we have mentioned that number is infinite, for we do not concede that in a known existing number. And the meaning of our saying 'the number is infinite' is that it is infinite in estimative estimation; that is, the estimation is incapable of reaching a limit and an end in it, so reason judges that it has no end. And just as number in absolute terms is known without relation to counted things, likewise half, third, and quarter in absolute terms are known without relation to the infinite. The impossible is only the affirmation of actually existing infinite numbers, for every existing counted thing is limited. And if the impossibility of infinite souls is realized, and each soul is connected to a body, then the impossibility of infinite bodies is realized. Then if bodies and human souls are finite, the elemental mixtures are finite, the celestial rotational motions are finite, the higher moving things are finite, and the spiritual movers are finite.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage contains the core proof and its final conclusion. The author presents a conditional argument: if there are infinitely many souls already in existence, then the remaining souls (not yet created) must be either finite or infinite. If they are infinite, then the total would be infinite multiplied by infinite, which is impossible. If they are finite, then adding them to the infinite set would produce a larger infinite, contradicting the fact that nothing is greater than an infinite. Thus the number of souls must be finite. Furthermore, the remaining souls must have a numerical proportion to the existing ones (e.g., half, third), but an infinite cannot have a proportion. So the total is finite. The author then clarifies that the phrase 'number is infinite' only means in estimation, not in actual existence. Finally, he extends the conclusion: since each soul is attached to a body, bodies are also finite; then elemental mixtures, celestial motions, and spiritual movers are all finite. This demonstrates the finitude of all created beings.

Key Terms
متناه (finite)
غير متناه (infinite)
نسبة (proportion)
تقدير وهمي (estimative estimation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the number 'infinite' in mathematics refers to an actual infinite multitude.
Correction: The author explains that 'infinite number' only means that in estimation (and in reason) no end can be reached; actual infinite numbers cannot exist because every counted thing is limited.

Checkpoint
What does the author mean by 'the number is infinite'?
A) That there is an actually infinite number of things
B) That in estimation the number cannot be reached
C) That the number is finite
D) That the number is uncountable

Key Takeaway
Everything in creation is finite, and the only valid concept of infinity is estimative, not actual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every created thing in your life—your health, wealth, relationships, and even your own soul—is finite and limited, then where is your ultimate security? Are you investing your heart in something that will end?
?
The proof shows that even spiritual movers are finite. Does your worship reflect an awareness that only Allah is truly infinite, or do you treat created things—like scholars, teachers, or even your own deeds—as if they have no end?
?
You live as if you have an infinite number of tomorrows, but this lesson proves that every soul has a finite number. If you truly believed that your time is limited, what would you change today—not in theory, but in action—before your finite hour ends?

Al-Baqillani's Proof: Power Affects an Attribute, Not Existence
Al-Baqillani argues that power's effect is on an attribute, not existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Baqillani's argument that created power only affects an attribute (a state) of an action, not the existence or non-existence of the action itself.

Core Idea
Power shapes the quality of an act, not its being.

This lesson presents one of the most precise arguments in classical kalām: the distinction between voluntary and involuntary motion leads to a necessary conclusion about the scope of created power. al-Baqillani uses this everyday experience to show that power's effect must be on an attribute, not on existence itself, thereby refining our understanding of causality within Ashʿarī theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its full translation to get a sense of the argument's flow and key terms.
Pay attention to the logical structure: empirical observation → two dichotomies → conclusion.
2
Understand the empirical premise
Al-Baqillani begins with a necessary human perception: we clearly distinguish between voluntary and involuntary movements (e.g., a tremor vs. raising a hand). This distinction is not based on the movements themselves (they are identical as motions) but on something extra: one is willed and powered, the other is not.
This empirical starting point is crucial; it grounds the argument in common human experience.
3
Follow the first dichotomy
The argument then considers two possibilities: either power relates to the voluntary movement like knowledge relates to its object (without any effect) or it relates with an effect. The first is rejected because if power had no effect, the distinction we perceive would vanish — both movements would be similar in that regard.
This rejection forces us to accept that power must have an effect.
4
Follow the second dichotomy and conclusion
Since power has an effect, the effect must be either on the existence/coming-into-being of the movement or on an attribute of it. The first option is invalid because if power could affect existence, it would affect every existent (contradicting the opponent's view). Therefore, the effect must be on an attribute, which al-Baqillani calls a 'state' (ḥāl) additional to existence.
This is the key conclusion: power's scope is limited to affecting attributes, not existence itself.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this argument matters: it clarifies the Ashʿarī position that created power does not create or annihilate things (that is reserved for God) but rather modifies their attributes within an already created substrate.
This argument also responds to opponents who claim that power affects existence directly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال القاضي : الإنسان يحس من نفسه تفرقةً ضرورية بين حركتي الضرورية والاختيارية؛ كحركة المرتعش وحركة المختار والتفرقة لم ترجع إلى نفس الحركتين من حيث الحركة؛ لأنهما حركتان متماثلتان بل إلى زايد على كونهما حركة، وهو كون أحدهما مقدورة مرادة، وكون الثانية غير مقدورة ولا مرادة، ثم لم يخل الأمر من أحد حالين؛ إما أن يُقال : تعلقت القدرة بأحديهما تعلق العلم من غير تأثير أصلا، فيؤدي ذلك إلى نفي التفرقة، فإن نفي التأثير كنفي التعلق فيما يرجع إلى ذاتي الحركتين، والإنسان لا يجد التفرقة فيهما وبينهما إلا في أمر زايد على وجودهما وأحوال وجودهما. وإما أن يُقال : تعلقت القدرة بأحديهما تعلق تأثير لم يخل الأمر من أحد أمرين؛ إما أن يرجع التأثير إلى الوجود والحدوث، وإما أن يرجع إلى صفة من صفات الوجود، والأول باطل بما ذكرناه أنه لو أثر في الوجود لأثر في كل موجود فتعين أنه يرجع التأثير إلى صفة أخرى، وهي حال زائدة على الوجود.
Al-Qadi said: A person necessarily perceives in himself a distinction between two movements, the involuntary and the voluntary, like the movement of a person with a tremor and the movement of a person acting voluntarily. The distinction does not refer back to the movements themselves as movements because they are two similar movements, but rather to something added to their being movement, which is that one of them is powered and willed, and the other is neither powered nor willed. Then the matter is not free from one of two states: either it is said that power is related to one of them like the relation of knowledge with no effect at all, which leads to the denial of the distinction, for the negation of effect is like the negation of relation with respect to the essences of the two movements, and a person does not find the distinction in them and between them except in something additional to their existence and the states of their existence. Or it is said that power is related to one of them with a relation of effect; then the matter is not free from one of two things: either the effect refers back to existence and coming-into-being, or it refers back to an attribute of the attributes of existence. The first is invalid due to what we mentioned: if it affected existence, it would affect every existent. So it is determined that the effect refers back to another attribute, which is a state additional to existence.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage contains al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani's proof that created power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) must have an effect on an attribute distinct from existence. The proof begins with an empirical observation: every person necessarily distinguishes between involuntary movement (e.g., a tremor) and voluntary movement. This distinction is not based on the movements themselves, since as movements they are identical, but on an added quality: one is intended and produced by power (maqdūra murāda), the other is not. From this, al-Baqillani considers two possibilities regarding how power relates to the movement: either it relates like knowledge (without affecting the thing), or it relates with an effect. The first option is rejected because it would eliminate the very distinction we experience—if power has no effect, then both movements would be similar in that regard, but we clearly differentiate them. Since the first option fails, the second must be true: power relates with an effect. The effect, however, cannot be on the existence or temporal origination (ḥudūth) of the movement, because if power could affect existence, it would affect every existent (contradicting the opponent's own view). So the effect must be on an attribute (ṣifa) of the movement, which al-Baqillani calls a 'state' (ḥāl) additional to existence. This establishes that created power produces an effect on a quality, not on the very fact of existence.

Key Terms
الحركة الضرورية
الحركة الاختيارية
القدرة
تعلق التأثير
الحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that al-Baqillani's argument proves that created power affects the existence of an action (i.e., brings it into being).
Correction: The argument actually proves the opposite: power does not affect existence; it only affects an attribute (a state) of the action that is already existing.

Checkpoint
According to al-Baqillani, the distinction between voluntary and involuntary movement shows that:
A) Both movements are identical in all respects.
B) Power has no effect on the movement.
C) Power must have an effect on an attribute distinct from existence.
D) Power affects existence itself.

Key Takeaway
Through voluntary and involuntary movements, al-Baqillani shows that power must affect a state (ḥāl) beyond existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own actions, do you genuinely feel the distinction between what you do by choice and what you do out of compulsion? What excuses might you be making that blur that line?
?
If power only affects attributes and not existence itself, what does that mean for how you view your own efforts? Does your sense of pride or shame rely on an exaggerated sense of control?
?
You know that some of your actions are involuntary — like your heartbeat or a startled reaction. Yet you still hold yourself accountable for your voluntary deeds. Have you ever considered that the distinction al-Baqillani proves might be the very basis of your responsibility before God? How does that settle in your heart?

Defining the 'State' (al-Hal) in Actions and the Doctrine of Kasb
Explains the method of defining the state in actions and compares opponent's view to al-Qadi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the 'state' (al-hal) in actions is identified through the classification of movement, how it is attributed to the servant as acquisition (kasb), and how this relates to the justice of reward and punishment according to al-Baqillani.

Core Idea
The state in an action is a rational distinction that determines moral and legal value, not an extra entity.

This passage from al-Baqillani's Nihayat al-Aqdam delves into the intricate concept of 'state' (al-hal) in human actions. By using the example of movement, the author demonstrates how to pinpoint the specific aspect that makes an act distinct, which is then attributed to the servant as acquisition (kasb). This analysis sets the stage for a crucial discussion on justice and the assignment of reward and punishment, comparing the positions of the Mu'tazila and al-Qadi.

Guided Path
1
Understand the method of specifying the state
The passage begins by proposing a method: take a genus like 'movement', analyze its divisions (writing, speech, craft), and identify the differentiating state. Notice how this state is not a separate entity but a distinguishing factor.
This method grounds the discussion in concrete examples.
2
See how the state becomes acquisition (kasb) for the servant
The passage then explains that this state is attributed to the servant as his act and acquisition, and specific action-names are derived from it. Recognize that this establishes human responsibility within a divine framework.
Kasb is the key term linking action to moral accountability.
3
Connect to reward and punishment through command and prohibition
The passage ties the state to whether a command or prohibition attaches, making the act worship or disobedience. This aspect is what merits reward or punishment.
This shows that moral quality is not inherent in the act but in its relation to divine law.
4
Compare the opponent's view with al-Qadi's view on justice
The opponent (Mu'tazila) is criticized for grounding reward/punishment in good/evil as superadded states, which is furthest from justice. Al-Qadi is praised for attributing the act's existence to God and the specific state to the servant, thus achieving true justice.
This contrast clarifies the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb and divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أراد تعيين ذلك الوجه الذي سماه حالا فطريقه أن يجعل حركة مثلا اسم جنس يشمل أنواعا وأصنافا أو اسم نوع يتمايز بالعوارض واللوازم، فإن الحركات تنقسم إلى أقسام؛ فمنها ما هو كتابة ومنها ما هو قول، ومنها ما هو صناعة باليد، وينقسم كل قسم إلى أصناف فكون حركة اليد كتابة وكونها صناعة متمايزان، وهذا التمايز راجع إلى حال في إحدى الحركتين تتميز بها عن الثانية مع اشتراكهما في كونهما حركة. وكذلك الحركة الضرورية والحركة الاختيارية فتضاف تلك الحالة إلى العبد كسبا وفعلا، ويشتق له منها اسم خاص مثل قام وقعد وقايم وقاعد، وكتب وقال، وكاتب وقايل، ثم إذا اتصل به أمر ووقع على وفاق الأمر سمي عبادة وطاعة، فإذا اتصل به نهي ووقع على خلاف الأمر سمي جريمة ومعصية، ويكون ذلك الوجه المكلف به، وهو المقابل بالثواب أو العقاب، كما قال الخصم: إن الفعل يقابل بالثواب أو العقاب لا من حيث إنه موجود، بل من حيث إنه حسن أو قبيح، والقبح والحسن حالتان زائدتان على كونه فعلا وكونه موجودا، وهو أبعد من العدل، والقاضي أقرب إلى العدل، فإنه أضاف إلى العبد ما لم يقابل بثواب أو عقاب، وقابل بالثواب والعقاب ما لم يكن من آثار قدرته، والقاضي عين الجهة التي هي عنده لم تقابل بالجزاء فأثبتها فعلا للرب، وعين الجهة التي هي فعل العبد وكسبه فقابلها بالجزاء وذلك هو العدل.
Whoever wants to specify that aspect which he called 'state' (al-hal), his method is to take 'movement' (al-haraka) for example as a genus name that includes types and kinds, or a species name that is distinguished by accidents and necessary concomitants. For movements are divided into divisions: among them is writing, among them is speech, and among them is manual craft; and each division is divided into kinds. So the hand's movement being writing and its being a craft are two distinct things, and this distinction goes back to a state (al-hal) in one of the two movements by which it is distinguished from the second, despite their sharing in being movement. Likewise, necessary movement and voluntary movement. Then that state is attributed to the servant as acquisition (kasban) and act (fi‘lan), and there is derived for him from it a special name, such as 'he stood' and 'he sat' and 'stander' and 'sitter', and 'he wrote' and 'he said' and 'writer' and 'speaker'. Then, if a command attaches to it and it occurs in accordance with the command, it is called worship and obedience; but if a prohibition attaches to it and it occurs contrary to the command, it is called crime and disobedience. And that aspect is what is imposed (al-mukallaf bihi), and it is what is met with reward or punishment, just as the opponent (al-khasm) said: 'The act is met with reward or punishment not insofar as it exists, but insofar as it is good or evil; and good and evil are two states superadded to its being an act and its being existent.' And he (the opponent) is farthest from justice, while al-Qadi is closer to justice, for he attributed to the servant what is not met with reward or punishment, and met with reward and punishment what is not from the effects of his power. Al-Qadi specified the aspect which, in his view, is not met with recompense, so he affirmed it as an act of the Lord; and he specified the aspect which is the act and acquisition of the servant, so he met it with recompense; and that is justice.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage defines a method for specifying the 'state' (al-hal) in an action, using movement as an example. The author explains that movements are divided into types (writing, speech, manual craft) and further into kinds. The distinction between, say, a hand movement that is writing versus a hand movement that is a craft lies in a 'state' that differentiates them, despite both being movements. This state is then attributed to the servant as acquisition (kasb) and act, and specific names (like 'stood', 'sat', 'writer') are derived. When a command attaches and the act conforms, it becomes worship/obedience; when a prohibition attaches and it violates, it becomes crime/disobedience. This aspect is what the servant is charged with and what merits reward or punishment. The author contrasts the opponent (likely the Mu'tazila) who says reward/punishment is based on goodness and evil as superadded states—which he says is farthest from justice—with al-Qadi (al-Baqillani) who attributes to the servant only what does not merit reward/punishment, and attributes to the Lord the aspect that does merit recompense, thereby achieving justice. The key point is that justice lies in properly assigning the two aspects: one to God (the act in itself) and one to the servant (the specific character that makes it obedience or sin).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the 'state' (al-hal) is a separate accident or entity added to the action.
Correction: The passage indicates that the state is a rational distinction that differentiates actions within a common genus, such as writing vs. craft in hand movements. It is not an extra entity but a distinguishing characteristic that serves as the basis for moral and legal qualification.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the method for specifying the 'state' (al-hal) in an action?
A) By considering the action's existence alone.
B) By taking an example like movement and examining its divisions and distinguishing features.
C) By referring to divine command and prohibition.
D) By focusing on the servant's power alone.

Key Takeaway
The 'state' in an action is the aspect that distinguishes it morally and legally, and justice requires attributing it to the servant as acquisition while attributing the act's existence to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you tend to focus on the outward action or the inner state that makes it obedience? Which one do you think Allah values more, and why does that difference matter for your accountability?
?
Have you ever excused a sin by saying 'it was written' or 'it was from Allah'? How does this lesson's insistence on attributing the specific state (the moral quality) to you challenge that evasion?
?
If the state of an action is what truly deserves reward or punishment, what does that imply about the care you should take in forming your intentions? What is one intention you will consciously set in your next action to align it with obedience?

Actions Have Multiple Rational Aspects
Explains that actions have multiple rational aspects, distinguishing general from specific, and how this relates to divine creation and human acquisition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a single action can be viewed under multiple rational aspects, and how this distinction allows for both divine creation and human acquisition.

Core Idea
An act’s existence comes from God; its specific character comes from humans.

This passage from al-Qadi’s method explains a key concept: every action can be considered under different 'mental considerations' or rational aspects. For example, a movement can be considered simply as existence (a general aspect) or as writing (a specific aspect). This distinction resolves a theological puzzle: how can an act be both created by God and acquired by humans? The answer lies in these multiple aspects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key idea
Read the passage and notice that actions have multiple rational aspects, some general (like existence) and some specific (like being writing).
2
Understand the distribution
See that the act in itself is only possibility; existence comes from the divine agent, while specific attributes come from the human agent.
This is the core of the argument.
3
Distinguish the two relations
Notice the two types of relation: general aspect to divine attribute (ījād) and specific aspect to human attribute (kasb). These have different implications for the agent's knowledge and change.
God knows all aspects; humans need not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يوضح طريقة القاضي ويبين أنه ما خالف الأصحاب تلك المخالفة البعيدة أن الفعل ذو جهات عقلية واعتبارات ذهنية عامة وخاصة؛ كالوجود والحدوث والعرضية، واللونية وكونه حركة أو سكونا، وكون الحركة كتابة أو قولا، وليس الفعل بذاته شيئا من هذه الوجوه، بل هي كلها مستفادة له الفاعل، والذي له بذاته هو الإمكان فقط، وأما وجوده فمستفاد من موجده على الوجه الذي هو به وهو أعم الوجوه، وأما كونه كتابة أو قولا فمستفاد من كاتبه أو قايله وهو أخص الوجوه، فيتميز الوجهان تميزا عقليا لا حسيا وتغاير المتعلقان أحدهما إيجادا وإبداعا، وهو نسبة أعم الوجوه إلى صفة لها عموم التعلق، وسمي الثاني كسبا وفعلا، وهو نسبة أخص الوجوه إلى صفة لها خصوص التعلق، فهو من حيث وجوده يحتاج إلى موجد، ومن حيث الكتابة والقول يحتاج إلى كاتب وقايل والموجد لا تتغير ذاته أو صفته لوجود الموجد، ويشترط كونه عالما بجميع جهات الفعل والمكتسب تتغير ذاته وصفته الحصول الكسب، ولا يشترط كونه عالما بجميع جهات الفعل.
And among what clarifies the method of al-Qadi and shows that he did not oppose the companions in that far opposition is that the act has rational aspects (jihāt ‘aqliyya) and mental considerations, general and specific; like existence, temporality, accidentality, color-ness, its being movement or rest, and the movement being writing or speech. The act in itself is not any of these aspects; rather they are all acquired for it from the agent. That which it has in itself is only possibility (al-imkān). As for its existence, it is acquired from its bringer-into-existence (mūjid) in the manner in which it is, and that is the most general of aspects. As for its being writing or speech, it is acquired from its writer or speaker, and that is the most specific of aspects. So the two aspects are distinguished by a rational distinction, not a sensory one, and the two relata are different: one is bringing-into-existence and originating, which is the relation of the most general aspect to an attribute that has general relation; the second is called acquisition (kasb) and act (fi‘l), which is the relation of the most specific aspect to an attribute that has specific relation. Thus, in terms of its existence, it needs a bringer-into-existence; in terms of writing and speech, it needs a writer and speaker. The bringer-into-existence does not change in essence or attribute due to the existence of the brought-into-existence, and he must be knowing of all aspects of the act. The acquirer (al-muktasib) changes in essence and attribute by obtaining the acquisition, and he need not be knowing of all aspects of the act.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage further clarifies al-Qadi's method and shows that his position is not a far departure from the companions. It explains that an act has multiple rational aspects (jihāt ‘aqliyya) and mental considerations (i‘tibārāt dhihniyya), both general and specific—examples include existence, temporality, accidentality, color, being movement or rest, and being writing or speech. The act in itself is none of these; they are all acquired from the agent. What belongs to the act in itself is only 'possibility' (al-imkān). Its existence comes from its bringer-into-existence (God) in the most general aspect; its specific attribute (e.g., being writing) comes from the human writer or speaker. These two aspects are distinguished rationally, not sensorily. The first relation (most general aspect to divine attribute) is called origination (ījād and ibdā‘), and the second (most specific aspect to human attribute) is called acquisition (kasb) and act (fi‘l). Therefore, in terms of existence, the act needs God; in terms of specific character, it needs the human. God does not change by creating, but the human does change by acquiring. God must know all aspects of the act; the human need not know all aspects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the act is a single simple entity with no internal distinction.
Correction: The act has multiple rational aspects that are distinguished mentally, not as separate parts. This allows it to be both created by God and acquired by humans.

Checkpoint

A) Existence
B) Writing or speech
C) Possibility (al-imkān)
D) Temporality

Key Takeaway
Actions have multiple rational aspects: their existence is from God, their specific character from humans, allowing a synthesis of divine creation and human responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you honestly admit that the sinful specific character (e.g., lie, theft) is your own doing, while its existence is from God? How does this accountability change your repentance?
?
If every action's existence is from God and its specific form from you, where do you place your trust for outcomes? Are you content that God provides the 'that it is' while you must choose the 'what it is'?
?
God knows all aspects of your actions, but you often act in ignorance. Will you begin this day asking Him to guide your specific choices, recognizing that even your best actions are only possible through His giving of existence?

The Mu'tazila's Argument from Self-Awareness
Presents the Mu'tazila's rational argument from self-awareness of voluntary action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila use self-awareness of voluntary action to argue for the causal efficacy of human power, and the disjunction they pose to the Ash'ari position.

Core Idea
Self-awareness of voluntary action proves human power has a real effect.

This lesson explores the Mu'tazila's first rational argument, which begins with a simple observation: we feel that we act according to our intentions. From this self-awareness, they build a case for the causal efficacy of human power — a direct challenge to the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb. The passage presents the core logic of their argument, including a clever disjunction that forces a choice between two interpretations of the perceived difference between voluntary and involuntary motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: the Mu'tazila first appeal to self-awareness, then argue that denying this is to deny necessity, and finally present a disjunction about the difference between voluntary and involuntary motion.
The passage ends mid-sentence but the core argument is complete up to the rejection of kasb.
2
Understand the self-awareness claim
The Mu'tazila claim that a person directly perceives that they act according to their motivations. This is presented as a necessary truth (darura) — denying it is absurd.
This is a foundational epistemological claim.
3
Analyze the inference to causal power
From the self-awareness, they infer that the originated power (al-qudra al-haditha) must have the capacity to bring about the willed action; otherwise, this self-awareness would be an illusion.
The argument hinges on the reliability of immediate perception.
4
Examine the disjunction
The Mu'tazila point out that the Ash'aris agree there is a perceived difference between voluntary and involuntary motion. They then pose a disjunction: either the difference lies in the motions themselves (one by my power, the other by God's) or in a quality of the agent. They argue that if the agent is capable, his power must have an effect.
This forces the Ash'ari to either admit human causal efficacy or adopt an unintelligible notion of kasb.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما شبهة المعتزلة فتنحصر في مسلكين؛ أحدهما مدارك العقل، والثاني مدارك السمع. أما الأول، فقالوا: الإنسان يحس من نفسه وقوع الفعل على حسب الدواعي والصوارف، فإذا أراد الحركة تحرك، وإذا أراد السكون سكن، ومن أنكر ذلك جحد الضرورة، فلولا صلاحية القدرة الحادثة لإيجاد ما أراد لما أحس من نفسه ذلك، قالوا: وأنتم وافقتمونا على إحساس التفرقة بين حركة الضرورة والاختيارية، ولم يخل الحال من أحد أمرين؛ إما أن يرجع إلى نفس الحركتين من حيث إن إحديهما واقعة بقدرته، والثانية واقعة بقدرة غيره، وإما أن يرجع إلى صفة في القادر من حيث إنه قادر على إحديهما غير قادر على الثانية، فإن كان قادرا فلا من تأثير في مقدوره ويجب أن يتعين الأثر في الوجود؛ لأن حصول الفعل بالوجود لا بصفة أخرى تقارن الوجود، وما سميتموه كسبا غير معقول، فإن
As for the doubt (shubha) of the Mu'tazila (Mu'tazila), it is confined to two approaches: one is the rational perception (madarik al-'aql), and the second is the scriptural perception (madarik al-sam'). As for the first, they said: The human being perceives from himself the occurrence of the action according to motivations (dawa'i) and deterrents (sawarif). So when he wants movement, he moves; when he wants stillness, he becomes still. Whoever denies that denies the necessary (darura). Were it not for the aptitude of the originated power (al-qudra al-haditha) to bring about what he wills, he would not perceive that from himself. They said: And you have agreed with us on the perception of the difference between the involuntary movement (harakat al-darura) and the voluntary movement (al-ikhtiyariyya). The matter is not free from two possibilities: either the difference goes back to the two movements themselves, in that one of them occurs by his power and the second occurs by another's power; or it goes back to a quality in the agent, in that he is capable of one of them and not capable of the other. For if he is capable, then there is no effect in his object of power, and the effect must be determined in existence, because the occurrence of the act by existence is not by another quality concomitant with existence. And what you call kasb is unintelligible, for...
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila's first rational argument (madarik al-'aql) for the causal efficacy of human power. The Mu'tazila begin by appealing to immediate self-awareness: a person feels that he acts according to his intentions—if he wants to move, he moves; if he wants to remain still, he does. Denying this is to reject a necessary truth (darura). If the originated power had no capacity to produce what is willed, this self-awareness would be illusory. They then point out that the Ash'aris (the addressees 'you') agree that there is a perceived difference between involuntary motion (like a shiver) and voluntary motion. From this, the Mu'tazila argue that the difference must be either in the motions themselves (one by my power, the other by God's) or in a quality of the agent (capable of one, not the other). If the agent is truly capable, then his power must have an effect (athar) on his object of power, and that effect must be the existence (wujud) of the act. They reject the Ash'ari concept of kasb as unintelligible. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the argument is clear up to that point. Note: The Mu'tazila are using a disjunction to force recognition of human causal efficacy.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha)
مدارك العقل (madarik al-'aql)
ضرورة (darura)
قدرة حادثة (qudra haditha)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Mu'tazila deny divine power entirely.
Correction: The Mu'tazila argue for human power as a created but effective cause, not as an independent power separate from God.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila argument, what does the perception of a difference between voluntary and involuntary motion imply?
A) That voluntary motion occurs by God's power and involuntary motion by human power.
B) That voluntary motion has a different cause than involuntary motion, either in the movement itself or in the agent's capacity.
C) That both types of motion are caused by God alone.
D) That involuntary motion is an illusion.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila ground human causal efficacy in self-awareness, arguing that the perceived difference between voluntary and involuntary motion necessitates a real effect of human power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you genuinely feel that you are the one who chose and executed it, or do you attribute the power entirely to God while acting carelessly? This lesson forces you to confront the reality of your own agency. Are you willing to admit that your power truly affects outcomes, and that you will be held accountable for the effects you produce?
?
Have you ever denied your own responsibility by saying 'God made me do it' or 'it was destined'? The Mu'tazila argue that denying the evident self-awareness is a denial of necessary truth. Examine your heart: do you use divine predestination as an excuse to avoid the weight of your choices? This lesson demands that you accept the reality of your power and its consequences.
?
The Ash'ari concept of kasb (acquisition) is called 'unintelligible' by the Mu'tazila. Do you find the concept of kasb satisfying, or do you feel it is a mere word that doesn't match your inner experience? Reflect on the moment you make a decision: do you experience your power as real or illusory? This lesson pushes you to decide between two worldviews. Which one aligns with your actual consciousness of action?
