Why Existence Is Not the Basis for Distinguishing Movements
The author critiques using existence as the basis for distinguishing two movements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that the distinction between two similar movements is not based on their existence (wujūd) but on inherent qualities or states.

Core Idea
Existence is imperceptible; the felt distinction between acts lies elsewhere.

The author begins by acknowledging that our inner sense (wijdān) does perceive a difference between two movements, such as a human action and a divine action. However, he challenges the claim that this difference is due to one being brought into existence by an originated power. Instead, he argues that the distinction ultimately traces back to either a quality inherent in the locus or a state within the movement itself—not to existence as such. This lesson unpacks that critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the author starts by conceding wijdān but then questions the opponent's conclusion.
2
Identify the two alternatives
The author says the distinction goes back to two possibilities: a quality in the locus or a state in the movement. Consider what these might be.
3
Understand the imperceptibility of ījād
The author asserts that bringing into existence (ījād) is not sensed. This is a key point: we don't perceive existence itself, only attributes.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The passage ends with the claim that we have found a reference other than existence for distinguishing movements. This sets up the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكرتموه من التفرقة بين الحركتين، أما الوجدان فمسلم، لكن لم قلتم: إن أحديهما موجودة بالقدرة الحادثة فإنا قد بينا أنها راجعة إلى أحد أمرين؛ إما إلى صفة في المحل، وإما إلى حال في الحركة، وفصلناهما أحسن تفصيل. وبالجملة الإيجاد غير محسوس ولا يدرك بإحساس النفس ضرورة فقد وجدنا للتفرقة بين الحركتين والحالتين مرجعا ومردا غير الوجود، أليس من أثبت المعدوم شيئًا عندكم ما رد التفرقة إلى العرضية واللونية والحركية في أنها بالقدرة الحادثة فإنها صفات نفسية ثابتة في العدم، ولا إلى الاحتياج إلى المحل فإنها من الصفات التابعة للحدوث، فلذلك نحن لا نردها إلى الوجود، فإنها من آثار القدرة الأزلية، ونردها إلى ما أنتم تقابلونه بالثواب والعقاب حتى ينطبق التكليف على المقدور، والمقدور على الجزاء، والدواعي والصوارف أيضا تتوجه إلى تلك الجهة، فإن الإنسان لا يجد في نفسه داعية الإيجاد، ويجد داعية القيام والقعود والحركة والسكون والمدح والذم وهذه هيئات تحصل في الأفعال وراء الوجود تتميز عن الوجود بالخصوص والعموم، فإن شئت سميتها وجوها واعتبارات، وبالجملة لا تنزل في كونها معقولة عن درجة الاختصاص ببعض الجائزات الذي هو من أثر الإرادة والإحكام، والإتقان الذي من أثر العلم وكون الصيغة أمرا ونهيا ووعدا ووعيدا بالإرادة.
You mentioned the distinction between the two movements. As for inner sense (wijdān), it is conceded, but why do you say that one of them is brought into existence (mawjūda) by the originated power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha)? For we have shown that it goes back to one of two things: either to a quality (ṣifa) in the locus (maḥall), or to a state (ḥāl) in the movement, and we have detailed them in the best detail. In sum: bringing into existence (ījād) is not sensed nor perceived by the sensation of the soul, necessarily. We have found for the distinction between the two movements and the two states a reference and a return other than existence (wujūd). Is it not from those who affirm the non-existent as a thing (al-ma'dūm shay') among you that they trace the distinction to accidentalness (al-ʿaraḍiyya), coloress (al-lawniyya), and movementness (al-ḥarakiyya) in that they are by the originated power, for they are self-attributes (ṣifāt nafsiyya) established in non-existence, and not to the need for a locus, for they are among the attributes dependent on temporality (al-ḥudūth). Therefore we do not trace them back to existence, for they are from the effects of the eternal power (al-qudra al-azaliyya), and we trace them back to that which you oppose with reward and punishment, so that the obligation (taklīf) may match the predestined (al-maqdūr), and the predestined may match the recompense (al-jazā'). The motives (dawāʿī) and deterrents (ṣawārif) also turn to that aspect. For man does not find in himself a motive for bringing into existence, and he finds a motive for standing, sitting, movement, stillness, praise, and blame. These are modalities (hay'āt) that occur in actions beyond existence, distinguished from existence by particularity and generality. If you wish, call them aspects (wujūh) and considerations (iʿtibārāt). In sum, they do not descend in their intelligibility from the degree of particularity (ikhtiṣāṣ) with some possible things (jā'izāt) which is from the effect of will (irāda) and exactitude (iḥkām), and the perfection (itqān) which is from the effect of knowledge (ʿilm), and that the formulation (ṣīgha) becomes command, prohibition, promise, and threat by the will.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a rebuttal against an opponent who distinguishes between two similar movements (one from God, one from human) based on the feeling of their existence. The author concedes that inner sense (wijdān) notices a distinction, but questions why the opponent attributes one to human originated power. The author argues that the distinction actually goes back to either a quality in the locus or a state in the movement—these are pre-conscious attributes. The act of bringing into existence (ījād) is imperceptible; what we perceive are specific attributes like being a movement or a color. The author then references the Mu'tazilite doctrine of 'non-existent as a thing' (ma'dūm shay'), but distances himself: the distinction should not be traced to existence (which is from God's eternal power), but to what is rewarded or punished. Because reward/punishment applies to human acts, which are the specific modalities (hay'āt) beyond existence—like standing, sitting, etc. Man has a motive for these modalities, not for existence itself. So these 'faces' (wujūh) and 'considerations' (iʿtibārāt) are the basis of distinction, and they are intelligible and attached to the act through divine will and knowledge, making the divine command, prohibition, etc., meaningful.

Key Terms
وُجْدَان
إِيجَاد
صِفَة نَفْسِيَّة
حَال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The difference between two similar movements (e.g., human vs. divine) is that one exists and the other does not, or they differ in existence.
Correction: The author shows that existence is not perceivable; rather, the distinction lies in inherent qualities or states.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the main problem with saying one movement exists by originated power?
A) It contradicts inner sense.
B) Bringing into existence is not sensed.
C) The opponent cannot prove it.
D) The distinction is based on modalities.

Key Takeaway
Distinctions between acts are not rooted in existence itself but in attributes that are perceptible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever attributed the difference between two similar actions to something as abstract as 'existence' without realizing that what you actually sense is a quality or state? How does this lesson force you to reconsider what you truly perceive in your actions?
?
This lesson argues that the act of bringing into existence is imperceptible. If you cannot sense existence itself, then what is it that you rely on when you claim that your actions are distinct from another's? Does your faith in divine power rest on something more concrete?
?
The author states that the perceived distinction must be about something other than existence. In your own life, when you feel a difference between a sincere act and a hypocritical one, are you attributing that to the 'existence' of the act or to an inner attribute? How might this lesson change the way you evaluate your own deeds?

The True Basis: Modalities of Reward and Punishment
The author proposes that the distinction between acts is based on modalities (hay'āt) that are the objects of reward and punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the true basis for distinguishing human acts from divine acts lies in specific modalities (hay'āt) that are the subjects of reward and punishment.

Core Idea
Acts are distinguished by their modalities, not by existence; these modalities are the basis for moral accountability.

Having argued that existence is not the distinguishing factor, the author now presents the correct alternative. He references the Mu'tazilite doctrine of 'non-existent as a thing' but redirects the discussion to what truly matters: the modalities (hay'āt) that exist in actions beyond their mere existence. These modalities—such as standing, sitting, praise, blame—are what humans have motives for and are rewarded or punished for. This lesson explains how this view makes sense of divine commands and theodicy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how the author engages with the Mu'tazilite view of non-existent attributes.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite reference
The author mentions those who affirm the non-existent as a thing (ma'dūm shay')—this refers to the Mu'tazilite doctrine that attributes exist in non-existence. But he distances himself.
3
Identify the positive proposal
The author states we trace the distinction not to existence but to what is rewarded/punished. Find the sentence that says this.
4
Grasp the modalities (hay'āt)
The author lists examples like standing, sitting, movement, etc. These are modalities beyond existence. They are the basis for motives and moral evaluation.
5
Connect to divine attributes
The passage ends by linking these modalities to divine will and knowledge, showing how they make commands meaningful.

The Locus of Obligation and Effect of Created Power
The author uses an example to define the locus of obligation and then answers an objection about the effect of human power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the locus of religious obligation is the action that falls under human capacity, not the capacity itself, and that the effect of created power is a real aspect or state of the act.

Core Idea
The command is about performing, not creating.

This passage addresses a central objection in Islamic theology: if God creates all actions, how can humans be held responsible? The author illustrates with a practical example and clarifies that the commanded action is what lies within human power, while its existence as creation belongs to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening example and statement
Understand the example of the person causing damage and the key statement about the locus of obligation.
This sets up the question about human power and responsibility.
2
Grasp the answer about the effect
The author explains that the effect of created power is an aspect or state of the act, similar to how opponents view God's power.
This is the core of the response to the objection.
3
Reflect on the commands
Consider the commands 'do' and 'do not do' – they are about performing the act, not creating it. Worship becomes worship by command, and that is attained by the servant.
This shows how the servant's action is real despite God's creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو فتح بثقاً حتى هلك زرع أو [هد به دار عوتب على ذلك وضمن وغرم، فمورد التكليف ما اندرج تحت القدرة، وما اندرج تحت القدرة غير مورد التكليف (٣). والجواب عن السؤال من حيث التحقيق إنا قد بينا وجه الأثر الحاصل بالقدرة الحادثة وهو وجه أو حال للفعل مثل ما أثبتموه للقادرية الأزلية، فخذوا من العبد ما يشابه فعل الخالق عندكم فلينظر إلى الخطاب بافعل لا تفعل، أخوطب أوجد أو لا توجد أو خوطب اعبد (٤) الله ولا تشرك به شيئًا، فجهة العبادة [التي (0) أخص وصف للفعل صار عبادة بالأمر، وذلك حاصل بتحصيل العبد مضاف إلى قدرته ، فما يضركم إضافة أخرى نعتقدها وهي مثل ما اعتقدتموه تابعا ، فالوجود عندنا كالتابع () أو كالذاتي الذي كان ثابتا ) في العدم ، والفرق بيننا أنا جعلنا الوجود متبوعا وأصلا، وقلنا هو عبارة عن الذات والعين وأضفناه إلى الله سبحانه وتعالى، وجميع ما يلزمه من الصفات، وأضفنا إلى العبد ما لا يجوز إضافته إلى الله تعالى حيث لا يُقال: أطاع الله تعالى وعصى الله تعالى، وصام وصلى وباع واشترى وقام ومشى فلا تتغير صفاته بأفعاله، فلا يعزب عن علمه (١) ذرة من خلقه، بخلاف ما يضاف إلى العبد فإنه يشتق له وصف واسم من كل فعل يباشره، وتتغير ذاته وصفاته بأفعاله، ولا يحيط علما بجميع وجوه اكتسابه وأعماله، وهذا معنى ما قاله الأستاذ أبو إسحاق (١٠) : إن العبد فاعل بمعين أو الرب فاعل بغير معين (١١) (1.)
Or if he opened a breach until crops perished or property was ruined, he is censured, liable, and fined. So the locus of obligation is what falls under capacity, and what falls under capacity is not the locus of obligation (3). The answer to the question from the perspective of verification: we have already shown the aspect of the effect produced by the created power, and it is an aspect or state of the act similar to what you have established for the eternal power. So take from the servant what resembles the act of the Creator in your view. Then consider the address 'Do' and 'Do not do' – is it addressed 'Bring into existence' or 'Do not bring into existence'? Or is it addressed 'Worship (4) God and do not associate anything with Him'? The aspect of worship which is the most specific description of the act becomes worship by the command. That is attained by the servant's acquisition added to his power. So what harm does another addition that we believe cause you? And it is like what you have believed as dependent. So existence in our view is like the dependent, or like the essential which was established in non-existence. The difference between us is that we have made existence followed and fundamental, and we have said it is the same as essence and entity, and we have attributed it to God, exalted and high, and all the attributes that follow from it. And we have attributed to the servant what cannot be attributed to God, exalted, such that it is not said: 'God obeyed and God disobeyed,' 'He fasted and prayed,' 'bought and sold,' 'stood and walked.' So His attributes do not change by His actions. Not a particle of His creation escapes His knowledge (1). Contrary to what is attributed to the servant, for a description and a name are derived for him from every action he performs. His essence and attributes change by his actions. He does not encompass in knowledge all aspects of his acquisition and deeds. This is the meaning of what the master Abu Ishaq (10) said: 'The servant is an agent by a specific [means] or the Lord is an agent without a specific [means]' (11) (1.)
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage provides the author's detailed answer to an objection regarding the effect of human power in actions. The author begins with an example of a person who causes damage (opening a breach) and is held liable, showing that the commanded action falls under the servant's capacity, but the capacity itself is not the locus of obligation. The answer then clarifies that the effect of created power is an aspect or state of the act, similar to how opponents (the Mu'tazila) attribute an effect to God's eternal power. The author challenges them to consider the commands 'do' and 'do not do': the command is about performing the action, not about creating it. Worship becomes worship by the command, and that is something the servant can bring about through his power, so there is no harm in also attributing that effect to God in a different way. The author then contrasts their view: they make existence (the act) attributed to God as the original and follow, and they attribute to the servant only what cannot be attributed to God—like acts of obedience, sin, movement, etc.—because God's attributes are unchanging and He knows all things. The servant's attributes change with his actions, and he does not fully know all aspects of his deeds. The passage concludes with a citation from al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini: 'The servant is an agent by a specific means, or the Lord is an agent without a specific means,' summarizing the distinction.

Key Terms
القدرة الحادثة
التكليف
مورد التكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the author denies any role for human power in actions; that the creature has no effect whatsoever.
Correction: The author affirms an effect of human power, but it is an aspect/state of the act, not the act's existence. The commanded action is genuinely performed by the servant.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the 'locus of obligation' (مورد التكليف)?
A) The capacity itself
B) What falls under capacity
C) The act of creation
D) The divine command

Key Takeaway
The locus of obligation is the action that falls under human capacity, not the capacity itself, and the effect of human power is the aspect of the act that makes it a commanded action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of worship, do you truly feel that your effort and intention matter, even though Allah creates the existence of that act? How does this affect your sincerity and diligence?
?
If the worth of your worship is in its compliance with the command, not in its existence, how can you ensure that your actions are performed exactly as commanded, rather than just going through the motions?
?
Consider a sin you committed. Do you ever subtly blame Allah's decree for it? This lesson teaches that your capacity produces an aspect of the act. Will you now own your sin fully and seek repentance, knowing that you are truly accountable?

Attribution of Actions to God and Servant
The author contrasts divine and human attribution of actions and cites Abu Ishaq's famous distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between attributing actions to God, whose attributes are unchanging, and to the servant, who derives names and descriptions from each action and whose essence changes.

Core Idea
God's attributes are unchanging; the servant's attributes change with each action.

Having established that human power has an effect, the author now contrasts how actions are attributed to God versus to human beings. He emphasizes that God's essence and attributes remain unchanged by His actions, while humans acquire new descriptors from every act they perform. This leads to the profound statement of al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq distinguishing between the servant as an agent by a specific means and God as agent without a specific means.

Guided Path
1
Understand the difference in attribution
Read the part where the author says 'فما يضركم إضافة أخرى' through the contrast between God and servant. Notice what can and cannot be said of God.
The author lists actions that are not attributed to God, like obeying, disobeying, etc.
2
Grasp the consequences
God's attributes do not change; He knows all. The servant's attributes change, and he has limited knowledge.
This highlights the fundamental difference in how actions affect the agent.
3
Learn the summary statement
Memorize and understand the meaning of 'الفاعل بمعين' vs. 'الفاعل بغير معين'.
This concisely captures the distinction: the servant's agency is tied to means, God's is absolute.

أو فتح بثقاً حتى هلك زرع أو [هد به دار عوتب على ذلك وضمن وغرم، فمورد التكليف ما اندرج تحت القدرة، وما اندرج تحت القدرة غير مورد التكليف (٣). والجواب عن السؤال من حيث التحقيق إنا قد بينا وجه الأثر الحاصل بالقدرة الحادثة وهو وجه أو حال للفعل مثل ما أثبتموه للقادرية الأزلية، فخذوا من العبد ما يشابه فعل الخالق عندكم فلينظر إلى الخطاب بافعل لا تفعل، أخوطب أوجد أو لا توجد أو خوطب اعبد (٤) الله ولا تشرك به شيئًا، فجهة العبادة [التي (0) أخص وصف للفعل صار عبادة بالأمر، وذلك حاصل بتحصيل العبد مضاف إلى قدرته ، فما يضركم إضافة أخرى نعتقدها وهي مثل ما اعتقدتموه تابعا ، فالوجود عندنا كالتابع () أو كالذاتي الذي كان ثابتا ) في العدم ، والفرق بيننا أنا جعلنا الوجود متبوعا وأصلا، وقلنا هو عبارة عن الذات والعين وأضفناه إلى الله سبحانه وتعالى، وجميع ما يلزمه من الصفات، وأضفنا إلى العبد ما لا يجوز إضافته إلى الله تعالى حيث لا يُقال: أطاع الله تعالى وعصى الله تعالى، وصام وصلى وباع واشترى وقام ومشى فلا تتغير صفاته بأفعاله، فلا يعزب عن علمه (١) ذرة من خلقه، بخلاف ما يضاف إلى العبد فإنه يشتق له وصف واسم من كل فعل يباشره، وتتغير ذاته وصفاته بأفعاله، ولا يحيط علما بجميع وجوه اكتسابه وأعماله، وهذا معنى ما قاله الأستاذ أبو إسحاق (١٠) : إن العبد فاعل بمعين أو الرب فاعل بغير معين (١١) (1.)
Or if he opened a breach until crops perished or property was ruined, he is censured, liable, and fined. So the locus of obligation is what falls under capacity, and what falls under capacity is not the locus of obligation (3). The answer to the question from the perspective of verification: we have already shown the aspect of the effect produced by the created power, and it is an aspect or state of the act similar to what you have established for the eternal power. So take from the servant what resembles the act of the Creator in your view. Then consider the address 'Do' and 'Do not do' – is it addressed 'Bring into existence' or 'Do not bring into existence'? Or is it addressed 'Worship (4) God and do not associate anything with Him'? The aspect of worship which is the most specific description of the act becomes worship by the command. That is attained by the servant's acquisition added to his power. So what harm does another addition that we believe cause you? And it is like what you have believed as dependent. So existence in our view is like the dependent, or like the essential which was established in non-existence. The difference between us is that we have made existence followed and fundamental, and we have said it is the same as essence and entity, and we have attributed it to God, exalted and high, and all the attributes that follow from it. And we have attributed to the servant what cannot be attributed to God, exalted, such that it is not said: 'God obeyed and God disobeyed,' 'He fasted and prayed,' 'bought and sold,' 'stood and walked.' So His attributes do not change by His actions. Not a particle of His creation escapes His knowledge (1). Contrary to what is attributed to the servant, for a description and a name are derived for him from every action he performs. His essence and attributes change by his actions. He does not encompass in knowledge all aspects of his acquisition and deeds. This is the meaning of what the master Abu Ishaq (10) said: 'The servant is an agent by a specific [means] or the Lord is an agent without a specific [means]' (11) (1.)
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage provides the author's detailed answer to an objection regarding the effect of human power in actions. The author begins with an example of a person who causes damage (opening a breach) and is held liable, showing that the commanded action falls under the servant's capacity, but the capacity itself is not the locus of obligation. The answer then clarifies that the effect of created power is an aspect or state of the act, similar to how opponents (the Mu'tazila) attribute an effect to God's eternal power. The author challenges them to consider the commands 'do' and 'do not do': the command is about performing the action, not about creating it. Worship becomes worship by the command, and that is something the servant can bring about through his power, so there is no harm in also attributing that effect to God in a different way. The author then contrasts their view: they make existence (the act) attributed to God as the original and follow, and they attribute to the servant only what cannot be attributed to God—like acts of obedience, sin, movement, etc.—because God's attributes are unchanging and He knows all things. The servant's attributes change with his actions, and he does not fully know all aspects of his deeds. The passage concludes with a citation from al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini: 'The servant is an agent by a specific means, or the Lord is an agent without a specific means,' summarizing the distinction.

Key Terms
إضافة الأفعال
الفاعل بمعين
الفاعل بغير معين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God is subject to change or that human actions are independent of God's creation.
Correction: God's attributes are unchanging and He knows all; the servant's attributes change with each action, and his agency is tied to specific means.

Checkpoint

A) The servant acts independently of God
B) The servant's agency is tied to particular means or instruments
C) The servant has no power at all
D) The servant is the real creator of his actions

Key Takeaway
God's attributes are unaffected by His actions, while the servant's essence and attributes change with each action, and this distinction is captured by the statement that the servant is an agent by a specific means and God is an agent without a specific means.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that God’s attributes do not change, yet you often act as if He is indifferent to your sins or unaware of your struggles. Are you living as if His omniscience is a distant theory rather than a present reality that demands your honesty? What one thing will you change today because He truly sees you?
?
The passage says the servant's essence and attributes change with every action. Think of your most recent sin: how has it changed you? Are you willing to confess that you are not the same person you were before that act, and to seek the One whose attributes are unchanging to restore you?
?
If you truly believe that every good deed you do is ultimately from God, why do you still take pride in your achievements? Will you today attribute your successes to Him alone and your failures to your own specific means, without excuse?

Al-Ka'bi's Argument on Indistinguishable Substances
Al-Ka'bi argues that indistinguishable self-subsistent entities are impossible, and the author notes the rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ka'bi's argument that two self-subsistent entities without any distinguishing factor are rationally impossible, and the critique that this reasoning merely restates the dispute rather than proving it.

Core Idea
Distinguishing between things requires a real difference; otherwise, their existence is impossible.

Al-Ka'bi, a Mu'tazili theologian, proposed a way to prove the impossibility of multiple eternal self-subsistent beings (like the intellects of philosophers). His argument seems straightforward, but critics argued it begs the question. Let's examine his reasoning and the response.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to al-Ka'bi's supposition and conclusion.
2
Identify al-Ka'bi's supposition and conclusion
Al-Ka'bi supposes two self-subsistent entities with no distinction. What does he conclude? Why is this supposed to be a proof?
Notice that he uses 'لو قدرنا' (if we suppose) to set up a conditional.
3
Understand the opponent's objection and the author's advice
The opponent says al-Ka'bi merely restated the dispute. The author adds that additional conditions are needed for the impossibility to be clear.
Think about why the opponent invokes the existence of intellectual substances as a counterexample.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسلك الكعبي طريقاً آخر وقال : لو قدرنا قائمين بأنفسهما لا يتميز أحدهما عن الآخر بزمان أو بمكان أو حيز، ولا يكون لأحدهما حقيقة خاصة يمتاز أحدهما عن الثاني بها فهو مستحيل عقلا، والخصم ربما يقول : هذا هو نفس النزاع عبرت عنه تعبيراً وصيرته دليلا، فإن جماعة من العقلاء صاروا إلى إثبات جواهر عقلية من عقول مجردة ونفوس مجردة قديمات قائمات بأنفسها، ويمتاز بعضها عن بعض بخواص وحقائق ولم تمنع العقول ثبوتها، ومن أراد أن يسلك هذه الطريقة فلا بد أن يزيد فيها شروطا حتى تتحقق الاستحالة وتتضح للعقل، والله أعلم.
And al-Ka'bi followed another way and said: If we suppose two self-subsistent entities, neither is distinguished from the other by time, place, or location, and neither has a specific essence by which one is distinguished from the second, then it is rationally impossible. But the opponent may say: This is the very dispute; you have expressed it as an argument. Indeed, a group of rational thinkers affirmed the existence of intellectual substances—abstract intellects and abstract souls, eternal and self-subsistent—and they are distinguished from one another by properties and essences, and reason did not prevent their affirmation. Whoever wants to follow this method must add conditions until the impossibility is realized and becomes clear to reason. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents an argument by al-Ka'bi and a critique of it. Al-Ka'bi attempts to show that two self-subsistent entities (قائمات بأنفسهما) cannot be distinct if they share no differentiating factor (time, place, location, or specific essence). He concludes that such a situation is rationally impossible. However, the author notes that the opponent can object that al-Ka'bi has merely restated the dispute itself, not provided a proof. The opponent points out that many rational thinkers (العقلاء) affirm the existence of self-subsistent intellectual substances (جواهر عقلية) such as abstract intellects and souls, which are eternal and yet distinct by properties and essences. Reason does not preclude their existence. Therefore, al-Ka'bi's method requires additional conditions to make the impossibility evident. The author concludes with 'God knows best', acknowledging the complexity of the issue.

قائم بنفسه
حقيقة خاصة
استحالة عقلية
العقلاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Some might think al-Ka'bi's argument is decisive and proves the impossibility of multiple eternal beings.
Correction: The passage shows that the opponent objects that the argument merely restates the dispute, and that without additional conditions, it does not convince those who affirm intellectual substances.

Checkpoint

B) They must have a specific essence to be distinguished, otherwise their existence is rationally impossible.
C) They are identical in every way.
D) They cannot be proven to exist.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ka'bi's argument that indistinguishable self-subsistent entities are impossible is not conclusive; it requires additional conditions to be convincing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Ka'bi's argument shows that without a real distinguishing essence, two self-subsistent entities cannot exist. How often do you rely on external markers (your job, status, appearance) to define your identity rather than the unique essence Allah has given you? What would it take for you to root your self-worth solely in your relationship with Him?
?
The opponent's rebuttal points out that reason accepts the existence of unseen intellectual substances. In your own faith, are there beliefs that your reason struggles to accept but that the intellect of the 'rational thinkers' (العقلاء) affirms? How can you strengthen your trust that your intellect, when guided by revelation, can embrace such truths?
?
This lesson reveals that even a great scholar's argument can be incomplete and require further conditions. Objection and Answer on God's Measure and Contingency
Response to an objection about God's specific measure and the nature of contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the necessity of a specifier for God's measure by distinguishing between necessary rational judgments and estimation of power.

Core Idea
Contingency is known by necessity, not by estimation of power.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection against the need for a specifier for God's attributes and measure, and the author's rejoinder clarifies the nature of contingency. The key is that contingency is a priori rational knowledge, not dependent on our ability to imagine a power that can bring about or prevent the thing. This distinction resolves the objection and refines our understanding of rational judgments.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the entire passage once to grasp the flow. The objection compares God's measure to created measures and raises an analogy with the number of attributes.
2
Identify the author's key distinction
Focus on the answer where the author separates contingency itself from the need for a preponderator. Note that contingency is known necessarily, not through estimation of power.
The author calls this a 'nukta' that many scholars missed.
3
Reflect on the implications for kalam
Consider why this distinction matters for proving God's transcendence and the role of necessary knowledge in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنكرون على من يقول : إن القدر الذي اختص به نهاية وحد. فإن قيل : هو واجب له لذاته ، فلا يحتاج إلى مخصص، والمقادير التي هي في الخلق إنما احتاجت إلى مقدر؛ لأنها جايزة، وذلك لأن الجواز في الجايزات إنما يعرف بتقدير القدرة، فلما كانت المقادير الخلقية مقدورة عرف جوازها واحتاج الجواز إلى مرجح، فإذا لم يكن فوق الباري سبحانه قادر يقدر عليه لم تكن إضافة الجواز وإثبات الاحتياج له، ألسنا اتفقنا على أن الصفات ثمان أفهي واجبة له على هذا العدد أم جايز أن توجد صفة أخرى؟ فإن قلتم : يجب الانحصار في هذا العدد كذلك نقول: الاختصاص بالحد المذكور واجب له؛ إذ لا فرق بين مقدار في الصفات عددًا وبين مقدار في الذات حدا. فإن قلتم : جايز أن توجد صفة أخرى، فما الموجب للانحصار في هذا الحد والعدد فيحتاج إلى مخصص حاصر. والجواب : قلنا : المقادير من حيث إنها مقادير طولا وعرضا وعمقاً لا تختلف شاهدا وغائبا في تطرق الجواز العقلي إليها واستدعاء المخصص، فإنا لو قدرنا مثل ذلك المقدار بعينه في الشاهد تطرق الجواز العقلي إليه واختصاصه به دون مقدار آخر يستدعي المخصص، وتطرق الجواز إلى الجايزات لا يتوقف على تقدير القدرة عليها، بل معرفة ذلك بينة للعقل ضرورية حتى صار كثير من العقلاء إلى أن العقل نفسه عبارة عن علوم ضرورية هي معرفة الجواز في الجايزات، والاستحالة في المستحيلات والوجوب في الواجبات، وتقدير القدرة عليها إنما يحتاج إليه في ترجيح أحد الجايزين على الثاني، لا في تصور نفس الجواز. وهذه نكتة قد أغفلها كثير من أصحاب الكلام.
You object to those who say: 'The measure which is specific to Him has an end and a limit.' For if it is said: He is necessary in Himself, so He does not need a specifier, while the measures that are in creation only need a determiner because they are contingent. That is because contingency in contingent things is known by the estimation of power. So when the created measures are (within) power, their contingency is known, and contingency needs a preponderator. So if there is no power above the Exalted Creator that can determine it, then the attribution of contingency and the proof of need for Him are not established. Did we not agree that the attributes are eight? Are they necessary for Him in this number, or is it possible that another attribute exist? If you say: The limitation to this number is necessary, then we say similarly: The specification to the mentioned limit is necessary for Him, for there is no difference between a measure in attributes in number and a measure in essence in limit. If you say: It is possible that another attribute exists, then what is the cause for the limitation to this limit and number, so that it needs a restricting specifier? The answer: We say: Measures, as measures of length, width, and depth, do not differ, whether witnessed or unseen, in being subject to rational contingency and in requiring a specifier. For if we were to conceive that exact measure in the witnessed realm, rational contingency would apply to it, and its specification to that measure rather than another requires a specifier. And the application of contingency to contingent things does not depend on the estimation of power over them; rather, knowledge of that is evident to the intellect necessarily, so much so that many rationalists have come to hold that the intellect itself consists of necessary knowledges: knowledge of contingency in contingent things, impossibility in impossible things, and necessity in necessary things. The estimation of power over them is only needed for preferring one of two contingents over the other, not for conceiving contingency itself. And this is a point that many of the kalam scholars have neglected.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the necessity of God's specific measure (qadr). The objection begins by asserting that God, being necessary in Himself, does not require a specifier (mukhassis), unlike created measures which are contingent and therefore need a determiner (muqaddir). The rejoinder shifts the discussion to the attributes: if the attributes are limited to eight, then by analogy the measure should also be limited. The question is whether this limitation is necessary or possible. The author's answer establishes that measures, whether witnessed or unseen, are subject to rational contingency and thus require a specifier. Crucially, contingency is not known through the estimation of power (taqdīr al-qudra) but is a necessary knowledge of the intellect. The intellect itself comprises three necessary categories: contingency, impossibility, and necessity. Estimation of power is only relevant for preferring one contingent over another, not for conceiving contingency itself. The author notes that many kalam scholars have missed this subtle point (nukta).

Key Terms
مقادير
جواز
وجوب
استحالة
مخصص
تقدير القدرة
علوم ضرورية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Contingency is known through imagining a power that can bring about or prevent the thing.
Correction: The author states that contingency is a necessary knowledge of the intellect, not dependent on estimating power. Estimation of power is only needed for preferring one contingent over another.

Checkpoint
According to the author, how is contingency known?
A) By estimation of power
B) By empirical observation
C) By necessary knowledge of the intellect
D) By scriptural proof

Key Takeaway
Contingency is a necessary rational judgment, not dependent on estimation of power; this distinction resolves the objection about God's measure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you realize that your own existence is contingent not because you are weak, but because your essence is contingent? How does that affect your reliance on God beyond your own abilities?
?
The author says many kalam scholars neglected the point that contingency is necessary knowledge. Is there a truth in your faith that you have neglected because of a habit of thought? What step will you take to correct it?
?
When you face a situation you deem impossible, do you base that judgment on your own estimation of power or on the necessary truth that only God is necessary? How can you shift your mindset to see contingency as a gateway to trust in the Necessary Being?

The Intellect as Necessary Sciences
Defines the intellect and its role in discerning possibility, impossibility, and necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the intellect as necessary sciences and the distinction between conceptualizing possibility and estimating power.

Core Idea
The intellect knows possibility, impossibility, and necessity — power estimation is only for choosing.

Sanusi defines the intellect as a set of necessary cognitions that distinguish the three rational judgments. He then introduces a critical nuance: the concept of power (qudra) is not part of the intellect's basic operation but is only needed when weighing alternatives. This subtle point was overlooked by many theologians.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the concise definition of the intellect.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three rational judgments
Find the three types of knowledge mentioned: possibility (jawāz), impossibility (istiḥāla), and necessity (wujūb). Note how each is linked to its object.
3
Understand the distinction about power
The passage says estimating power (taqdīr al-qudra) is only needed for preferring one possible over another, not for conceptualizing possibility itself. Grasp why this is a subtle point.
Many scholars missed this nuance.
4
Reflect on the common neglect
Consider why many kalam practitioners neglected this distinction. How might it affect theological reasoning?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العقل نفسه عبارة عن علوم ضرورية هي معرفة الجواز في الجايزات، والاستحالة في المستحيلات والوجوب في الواجبات، وتقدير القدرة عليها إنما يحتاج إليه في ترجيح أحد الجايزين على الثاني، لا في تصور نفس الجواز. وهذه نكتة قد أغفلها كثير من أصحاب الكلام.
The intellect itself consists of necessary sciences: knowledge of possibility regarding possible things, impossibility regarding impossible things, and necessity regarding necessary things. Estimating ability over them is only needed for preferring one possible over another, not for conceptualizing possibility itself. This is a subtle point that many practitioners of kalam have neglected.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage defines the intellect as a set of necessary (a priori) cognitions that distinguish between the three rational judgments: possibility (jawāz), impossibility (istiḥāla), and necessity (wujūb). The author then clarifies that the estimation of power (qudra) to perform an act is only required when choosing between two possible alternatives, not for the mere conceptualization of possibility. Many theologians overlook this distinction, mistakenly requiring power assessment for possibility itself.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The intellect only knows possibility, impossibility, and necessity. Power estimation is a separate step needed when choosing between possibilities, not for conceptualizing possibility itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Where in your life have you confused the two? Do you think you can do something just because it's not impossible? How might this distinction change how you assess your own capabilities and choices before God?
?

?
You likely know that Allah is all-powerful, but this lesson clarifies that power is only relevant in the context of choice among possibilities. When you think about your own actions, do you first affirm that the act is possible (allowed by Allah's will) before considering your ability to do it? What if you reverse the order and start from your perceived ability? How might that lead to a distorted view of your reliance on Allah?

The Affirmers' Rational Proof for States
Shahrastani presents the proof of the affirmers of states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument used by the affirmers of states (ahwal) and why it forces a distinction between shared and specific attributes.

Core Idea
Reason requires that shared attributes and differentiating attributes are distinct, otherwise we fall into sophistry.

Shahrastani begins by presenting the argument of those who affirm the reality of states (ahwal). They appeal to a basic rational judgment: when two things share a property and also differ, the basis of sharing must be different from the basis of difference. This seemingly simple observation becomes the foundation for a key position in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice how the affirmers present their argument as a necessary rational judgment.
The argument is presented as a dilemma with two options.
2
Identify the shared and differentiating attributes
Blackness and whiteness share in being colors and accidents (colority, accidentality) and differ in their specific natures (blackness-ness, whiteness-ness). Identify these terms.
These are examples of what later theologians call 'states'.
3
Understand the dilemma
The affirmers present two possibilities: that by which they share is either different from or the same as that by which they differ. The first (different) is their position; the second (same) is dismissed as sophistry.
Why would sameness be sophistry? Because it would mean black and white are identical in both respects, which is absurd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما أدلة الفريقين؛ فقال المثبتون: العقل يقضي ضرورة أن السواد والبياض يشتركان في قضية وهي اللونية والعرضية ويفترقان في قضية وهي السوادية والبياضية، فما به الاشتراك غير ما به الافتراق أو غيره، فالأول سفسطة، والثاني تسليم المسألة. وقال النفاة: السواد والبياض المعينان قط لا يشتركان في شيء هو لهما، بل يشتركان في شيء هو اللفظ الدال على الجنسية والنوعية والعموم، والاشتراك فيه ليس يرجع إلى صفة هي حال للسواد والبياض، فإن حالتي العرضين يشتركان في الحالية ولا يقتضي ذلك الاشتراك ثبوت حال للحال، فإنه يؤدي إلى التسلسل، فالعموم كالعموم، والخصوص كالخصوص.
As for the proofs of the two schools, the affirmers said: reason necessarily judges that blackness and whiteness share in a matter, which is colority and accidentality, and differ in a matter, which is blackness-ness and whiteness-ness. So that by which they share is either other than that by which they differ, or it is the same. The first is sophistry, and the second is granting the issue. The deniers said: blackness and whiteness specifically never share in anything that belongs to them, but rather they share in something that is the word denoting genus, species, and universality. The sharing in that does not refer to an attribute that is a state for blackness and whiteness, for the two states of the accidents share in state-ness, and that sharing does not necessitate establishing a state for the state, for it leads to infinite regress. Thus the universal is like the universal, and the particular is like the particular.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage presents the arguments of the mutbitun (affirmers of states) and the nafah (deniers). The affirmers argue from the fact that black and white both share in being colors and accidents, and differ in being black and white. That which they share must be different from that which they differ, otherwise it would be sophistry to say they are both the same and different. The deniers respond that they do not share in any real thing; the sharing is only in the word that denotes genus and species. The claim that the two states (of black and white) share in 'state-ness' would require a state for the state, leading to infinite regress. They hold that only the universal word is common, not a real attribute.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the affirmers' argument is merely analogical or linguistic, but it is based on a necessary rational judgment.
Correction: The affirmers claim that reason itself necessitates the distinction, not just convention.

Checkpoint
According to the affirmers, what does reason necessarily judge about blackness and whiteness?
A) They are completely different
B) They share and differ in different respects
C) They are identical
D) They are both accidents of the same type

Key Takeaway
The affirmers establish that shared and specific attributes must be distinct, otherwise contradiction arises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this passage, the affirmers argue that reason necessarily distinguishes between shared and specific attributes. Reflect on a time when you confused a shared attribute (like 'goodness') with a specific one (like 'mercy' or 'justice') in your understanding of God. How might this confusion lead to sophistry in your own faith? Commit to examining your understanding of God's attributes using both reason and revelation.
?
The passage says that if the shared and specific were the same, we fall into sophistry. Consider your own life: are there beliefs or actions where you treat two different things as identical? For example, do you equate 'feeling close to God' with 'being obedient'? Write down one area where you need to distinguish between related but distinct concepts, and how this lesson's logic can help.
?
Reason judges that blackness and whiteness are distinct realities because they differ in specific nature. Apply this to yourself: your identity as a Muslim and your individual personality—are they the same or different? How does recognizing distinct states help you understand your relationship with God? This lesson demands that you use reason to discern differences. Will you take time today to reflect on where you have overlooked essential distinctions in your faith or life?

The Deniers' Objection: Infinite Regress of States
Shahrastani presents the deniers' objection to states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the deniers' objection that affirming states leads to infinite regress, and why they conclude that only the universal word is common.

Core Idea
If sharing required a state, then state-ness would need another state, leading to infinite regress.

After presenting the affirmers' argument, Shahrastani records the response of the deniers (nafah). They reject the notion that blackness and whiteness share in any real attribute; instead, the sharing is merely in the word denoting the genus. They raise a powerful objection: if the two states of black and white share in 'state-ness', that would require a state for that state, and so on infinitely. This reduces the affirmers' position to absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the deniers' objection
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the deniers first deny any real shared attribute, then present the infinite regress argument.
The deniers' argument has two parts: a denial of real sharing and a reductio against the affirmers.
2
Understand the infinite regress argument
If the affirmers grant that both blackness and whiteness have states (e.g., colority and accidentality), then those two states themselves share in being states (state-ness). If sharing implies a real state, then state-ness would require a state, leading to an infinite chain.
This is a classic argument against reifying universals.
3
See the conclusion
The deniers conclude that the universal is merely a word (the genus name), and the particular is just the particular. No real states exist over and above individuals.
Their position is that only the linguistic expression is common.

The First Proof: Proponents and Opponents on Shareable Attributes
Proponents' first proof and opponents' initial reply

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational proof for the existence of states (ahwal) and the opponents' linguistic reply.

Core Idea
The debate over states hinges on whether shared attributes are real or merely verbal.

The proponents of states (muthbitun) begin with a powerful argument from reason: blackness and whiteness clearly share something and differ in something. That shared factor, they claim, must be a real attribute (hal). The opponents (nufat) counter that the sharing is only nominal—a matter of words, not reality. This exchange sets the stage for the entire debate on states.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two positions
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note that the proponents (muthbitun) and opponents (nufat) each give a distinct argument.
The opponents' reply is more subtle than a simple denial.
2
Understand the proponents' dilemma
The proponents argue that the factor of sharing is either other than the factor of difference or the same. If the same, that's sophistry; if other, the opponent concedes a real distinction.
This is a logical move to force the opponent to acknowledge a real attribute.
3
Examine the opponents' reply: verbal sharing and infinite regress
The opponents deny any real shared entity; blackness and whiteness share only in the word 'color'. They further argue that if states share in 'state-ness', that leads to infinite regress.
The infinite regress argument is a key tool for opponents of states.
4
Reflect on what each side assumes about language and reality
Consider how the proponents assume that shared terms like 'color' refer to a real attribute, while the opponents treat them as mere linguistic conventions.
This reflects deeper metaphysical commitments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما أدلة الفريقين؛ فقال المثبتون: العقل يقضي ضرورة أن السواد والبياض يشتركان في قضية وهي اللونية والعرضية ويفترقان في قضية وهي السوادية والبياضية، فما به الاشتراك غير ما به الافتراق أو غيره، فالأول سفسطة، والثاني تسليم المسألة. وقال النفاة: السواد والبياض المعنيان قط لا يشتركان في شيء هو لهما، بل يشتركان في شيء هو اللفظ الدال على الجنسية والنوعية والعموم، والاشتراك فيه ليس يرجع إلى صفة هي حال للسواد والبياض، فإن حالتي العرضين يشتركان في الحالية ولا يقتضي ذلك الاشتراك ثبوت حال للحال، فإنه يؤدي إلى التسلسل، فالعموم كالعموم، والخصوص كالخصوص.
As for the proofs of the two groups: The proponents said: Reason necessarily judges that blackness and whiteness share in a matter, namely color-ness and accident-ness, and differ in a matter, namely blackness-ness and whiteness-ness. So that by which they share is either other than that by which they differ, or the same. The first is sophistry, and the second is granting the issue. The deniers said: The two meanings of blackness and whiteness absolutely do not share in anything that belongs to them; rather they share in something which is the word indicating generality, specificity, and universality. The sharing in it does not refer to an attribute that is a state (hal) for blackness and whiteness. For the two states of the two accidents share in 'state-ness' (al-haliyya), and that sharing does not necessitate the affirmation of a state for the state, as it would lead to infinite regress. So generality is like generality, and specificity is like specificity.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage provides the first proof of the proponents (muthbitun) and the immediate reply of the opponents (nufat). The proponents argue that reason judges necessarily that blackness and whiteness share in being colors and accidents, and differ in being this specific black or white. The factor of sharing must be other than the factor of difference or the same; if the same, that is sophistry; if other, then the issue (the existence of a state) is granted. The opponents reply by denying any real shared entity: blackness and whiteness share only in the word (lafz) that indicates generality and type. The sharing in that word does not imply a real attribute that is a state for blackness and whiteness. They further argue that if states (ahwal) were to share in 'state-ness', that would require a state for the state, leading to infinite regress. Therefore, the generality and specificity are merely verbal.

مثبتون
نفاة
حال
اشتراك
لفظ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the opponents deny any shared feature between black and white, e.g., that they have nothing in common.
Correction: The opponents do not deny that the words 'black' and 'white' share the label 'color'; they deny that this sharing refers to a real attribute (hal) existing in the things themselves.

Checkpoint

A) in a real state (hal) called 'color-ness'
B) only in the word (lafz) indicating generality
C) in their essences (dhat)
D) nonexistent; they share nothing at all

Key Takeaway
The first proof for states is met with a linguistic rebuttal: what appears to be shared reality may be merely shared language.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you describe God as 'All-Knowing and 'All-Powerful', do you take these as mere words, or do you believe there is a real distinction in His essence? This lesson challenges you to decide whether your theological vocabulary corresponds to reality or is just a linguistic convenience.
?
The opponents warn that multiplying states leads to infinite regress. In your own spiritual life, do you overcomplicate your understanding of faith with abstract layers that distance you from simple worship? Are you guilty of intellectualizing what is meant to be lived?
?
This debate forces you to ask: Are you content to let your beliefs about reality remain at the level of language, or are you willing to struggle with the hard question of whether shared attributes are real? What is at stake if you choose wrongly?

Intellectual Universals and the Basis of Demonstration
Proponents defend intellectual universals as basis of demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proponents' argument that universals like sharing and differentiation are intellectual realities beyond words, and why rejecting them undermines all rational inference.

Core Idea
Universals are intellectual realities, not mere words; they are the foundation of demonstration.

The proponents of states respond to the charge that their doctrine relies on verbal distinctions. They argue that the categories of sharing and differentiating are intellectual, not linguistic. To reduce them to mere words is to deny the very possibility of logical definition and demonstrative proof. This lesson unpacks their defense of intellectual universals.

Guided Path
1
Read the proponents' supporting argument
Read the passage carefully. Let the argument unfold in their own words.
2
Identify the key claim
Find the statement that sharing and differentiating are intellectual matters beyond words.
This is the foundational claim.
3
Notice the consequence of denial
The proponents say that denying intellectual universals leads to denying intellectual boundaries and demonstrative proofs.
It's a strong charge.
4
Understand the role of generality
Recognize that intellectual generality in proofs is necessary for reaching knowledge of the intended meaning.
5
Reflect on the necessity of comprehensive definitions
The final point: without comprehensiveness in a definition, you cannot know the defined object.
This ties back to the need for intellectual universals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المثبتون: الاشتراك والافتراق قضية عقلية وراء اللفظ، وإنما صيغ اللفظ على وفق ذلك ومطابقته، ونحن إنما تمسكنا بالقضايا العقلية دون الألفاظ الوضعية، اعتقد أن العموم والخصوص يرجعان إلى اللفظ المجرد فقد أنكر الحدود العقلية للأشياء والأدلة القطعية على المدلولات والأشياء لو كانت تتمايز بذواتها ووجودها بطل القول بالقضايا العقلية وحسم باب الاستدلال بشيء أولي على شيء مكتسب متحصل، وما لم يدرج في الأدلة العقلية عموما عقليا لم يصل إلى العلم بالمدلول قط، وما لم يتحقق في الحد شمولاً بجميع المحدودات لم يصل إلى العلم المحدود. قال النافون: الكلام على المذهب ردًّا وقبولا إنما يصح بعد كون المذهب معقولا، ونحن نعلم بالبديهة أن لا واسطة بين النفي والإثبات، ولا بين العدم والوجود وأنتم اعتقدتم الحال لا موجودة ولا معدومة، وهو متناقض بالبديهة، ثم فرقتم بين الوجود والثبوت، فأطلقتم لفظ الثبوت على الحال، ومنعتم إطلاق لفظ الوجود، فنفس المذهب إذا لم يكن معقولا فكيف يسوغ سماع الكلام والدليل عليه؟ ومن العجايب أن ابن الجباي قال: لا توصف بكونها معلومة أو مجهولة، وغاية الاستدلال إثبات العلم بوجود الشيء، فإذا لم يتصور له وجود ولا تعلق العلم به؛ بطل الاستدلال عليه، وتناقض الكلام فيه.
The proponents said: Sharing and differentiating is an intellectual matter beyond the word; rather, words are formulated in accordance with and in conformity to it. We hold to intellectual judgments, not conventional words. Whoever thinks that generality and particularity refer merely to words has denied the intellectual limits (hudud) of things and the demonstrative proofs of meanings. If things were distinguished by their essences and existence alone, the claim of intellectual judgments would be invalidated and the door of inferring from a priori premises to acquired results would be closed. And unless there is an intellectual generality in the intellectual proofs, one never arrives at knowledge of the meant. And unless there is comprehensiveness of all the defined in the definition, one never arrives at knowledge of the defined. The deniers said: Discussion of a doctrine, whether to reject or accept it, is only valid after the doctrine is understandable. We know by intuition that there is no intermediary between negation and affirmation, nor between non-existence and existence. You have claimed that the state is neither existent nor non-existent, which is contradictory by intuition. Then you distinguished between existence (wujud) and subsistence (thubut), applying the term subsistence to the state, and preventing the term existence. So if the doctrine itself is not understandable, how is it permissible to engage in discussion and proof about it? Among the wonders is that Ibn al-Jubba'i said: It (the state) is not described as known or unknown. The purpose of inference is to establish knowledge of the existence of a thing. If its existence is not conceivable, nor is knowledge attached to it, then inference about it becomes invalid, and discussion about it becomes contradictory.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage contains the proponents' rebuttal to the opponents' verbal-only theory, followed by the opponents' final objection. The proponents argue that universals like sharing and differentiating are intellectual realities, not mere words; words conform to these realities. To deny intellectual universals is to deny logical definitions and the possibility of demonstrative reasoning. Without intellectual generality in premises, one cannot reach knowledge of conclusions. Without comprehensive definitions, knowledge of the defined is impossible. The opponents counter by asserting the principle of excluded middle: there is no third state between existence and non-existence. They accuse the proponents of positing a state that is neither existent nor non-existent, which is intuitively contradictory. They note that the proponents distinguish between existence (wujud) and subsistence (thubut), applying 'subsistence' to states while denying they are 'existent'. If the state is inconceivable, then any argument about it is invalid. Finally, they find it remarkable that Ibn al-Jubba'i says states are not described as known or unknown, because the very aim of inference is to know the existence of something; if states have no existence and cannot be known, inference about them is futile.

Key Terms
قضايا عقلية
حدود عقلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That intellectual universals are merely mental constructs or verbal conventions.
Correction: The proponents insist they are objective intellectual realities that words conform to, and they are essential for demonstration.

Checkpoint
What is the main claim made by the proponents regarding sharing and differentiation?
A) They are purely linguistic.
B) They are intellectual realities beyond words.
C) They are neither intellectual nor linguistic.
D) They are identical with existence.

Key Takeaway
Intellectual universals are objective realities that ground all rational inference and definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The text says words are fashioned to match intellectual realities. Is your inner reality aligned with the words you speak? If not, what will you do to bring your heart's conviction to match your tongue's declaration?

The State Contradicts Excluded Middle
Opponents reject the state as violating excluded middle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponents' final objection that the state (hal) violates the principle of excluded middle and is thus inconceivable, making any argument about it invalid.

Core Idea
If a state is neither existent nor non-existent, it is contradictory by intuition and cannot be discussed.

The deniers of states launch a final attack against the concept of the state. They appeal to the fundamental principle of excluded middle: there is no third state between existence and non-existence. Since the proponents claim the state is neither existent nor non-existent, the deniers argue it is logically impossible. They then point out that the proponents' distinction between existence (wujud) and subsistence (thubut) only masks the problem. Moreover, if the state cannot be known, as Ibn al-Jubba'i suggests, then inference about it is futile.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponents' objection
Read the passage carefully. Note the initial condition for discussing any doctrine.
2
Identify the key intuition
The opponents appeal to the law of excluded middle as known by intuition.
This is a bedrock principle.
3
Find the charge
They accuse the proponents of positing a state that is neither existent nor non-existent.
This is contradictory per excluded middle.
4
Examine the existence/thubut distinction
Notice how the opponents critique the distinction between wujud and thubut.
They see it as an arbitrary verbal maneuver.
5
Reflect on Ibn al-Jubba'i's statement
Consider why saying the state is not known makes inference about it invalid.
The purpose of inference is to know existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المثبتون: الاشتراك والافتراق قضية عقلية وراء اللفظ، وإنما صيغ اللفظ على وفق ذلك ومطابقته، ونحن إنما تمسكنا بالقضايا العقلية دون الألفاظ الوضعية، اعتقد أن العموم والخصوص يرجعان إلى اللفظ المجرد فقد أنكر الحدود العقلية للأشياء والأدلة القطعية على المدلولات والأشياء لو كانت تتمايز بذواتها ووجودها بطل القول بالقضايا العقلية وحسم باب الاستدلال بشيء أولي على شيء مكتسب متحصل، وما لم يدرج في الأدلة العقلية عموما عقليا لم يصل إلى العلم بالمدلول قط، وما لم يتحقق في الحد شمولاً بجميع المحدودات لم يصل إلى العلم المحدود. قال النافون: الكلام على المذهب ردًّا وقبولا إنما يصح بعد كون المذهب معقولا، ونحن نعلم بالبديهة أن لا واسطة بين النفي والإثبات، ولا بين العدم والوجود وأنتم اعتقدتم الحال لا موجودة ولا معدومة، وهو متناقض بالبديهة، ثم فرقتم بين الوجود والثبوت، فأطلقتم لفظ الثبوت على الحال، ومنعتم إطلاق لفظ الوجود، فنفس المذهب إذا لم يكن معقولا فكيف يسوغ سماع الكلام والدليل عليه؟ ومن العجايب أن ابن الجباي قال: لا توصف بكونها معلومة أو مجهولة، وغاية الاستدلال إثبات العلم بوجود الشيء، فإذا لم يتصور له وجود ولا تعلق العلم به؛ بطل الاستدلال عليه، وتناقض الكلام فيه.
The proponents said: Sharing and differentiating is an intellectual matter beyond the word; rather, words are formulated in accordance with and in conformity to it. We hold to intellectual judgments, not conventional words. Whoever thinks that generality and particularity refer merely to words has denied the intellectual limits (hudud) of things and the demonstrative proofs of meanings. If things were distinguished by their essences and existence alone, the claim of intellectual judgments would be invalidated and the door of inferring from a priori premises to acquired results would be closed. And unless there is an intellectual generality in the intellectual proofs, one never arrives at knowledge of the meant. And unless there is comprehensiveness of all the defined in the definition, one never arrives at knowledge of the defined. The deniers said: Discussion of a doctrine, whether to reject or accept it, is only valid after the doctrine is understandable. We know by intuition that there is no intermediary between negation and affirmation, nor between non-existence and existence. You have claimed that the state is neither existent nor non-existent, which is contradictory by intuition. Then you distinguished between existence (wujud) and subsistence (thubut), applying the term subsistence to the state, and preventing the term existence. So if the doctrine itself is not understandable, how is it permissible to engage in discussion and proof about it? Among the wonders is that Ibn al-Jubba'i said: It (the state) is not described as known or unknown. The purpose of inference is to establish knowledge of the existence of a thing. If its existence is not conceivable, nor is knowledge attached to it, then inference about it becomes invalid, and discussion about it becomes contradictory.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage contains the proponents' rebuttal to the opponents' verbal-only theory, followed by the opponents' final objection. The proponents argue that universals like sharing and differentiating are intellectual realities, not mere words; words conform to these realities. To deny intellectual universals is to deny logical definitions and the possibility of demonstrative reasoning. Without intellectual generality in premises, one cannot reach knowledge of conclusions. Without comprehensive definitions, knowledge of the defined is impossible. The opponents counter by asserting the principle of excluded middle: there is no third state between existence and non-existence. They accuse the proponents of positing a state that is neither existent nor non-existent, which is intuitively contradictory. They note that the proponents distinguish between existence (wujud) and subsistence (thubut), applying 'subsistence' to states while denying they are 'existent'. If the state is inconceivable, then any argument about it is invalid. Finally, they find it remarkable that Ibn al-Jubba'i says states are not described as known or unknown, because the very aim of inference is to know the existence of something; if states have no existence and cannot be known, inference about them is futile.

Key Terms
بديهة
وجود
ثبوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the state can be 'subsistent' without being existent, resolving the contradiction.
Correction: The opponents argue that this is a verbal trick; subsistence still implies some kind of existence, and the distinction does not avoid the violation of excluded middle.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the opponents' objection to the state?
A) It is too abstract.
B) It violates the law of excluded middle.
C) It contradicts revelation.
D) It is not mentioned in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The concept of the state is self-contradictory because it violates the intuitive law of excluded middle, making any argument about it invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there beliefs you hold about faith or reality that, when examined logically, reveal a contradiction similar to the one exposed here? Will you have the courage to discard or revise them, or will you cling to them through verbal distinctions?
?
The opponents insist that a doctrine must be conceptually coherent before it can be debated. When you defend your beliefs, do you first ensure they are clearly defined and consistent, or do you defend them reflexively without examination?
?
You have seen how an arbitrary distinction between 'existence' and 'subsistence' was used to avoid a contradiction. Are there areas in your own thinking where you rely on similar word games to avoid facing a truth that would require you to change?

Universals as Rational Realities Beyond Words
Affirmers argue that universals are rational realities beyond mere words.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that for the affirmers, universals (commonality and differentiation) are rational judgments independent of language, and that reducing them to mere words undermines rational inference and definition.

Core Idea
Universals are rational realities, not just linguistic labels.

The affirmers present a key argument for the reality of universals. They begin by asserting that commonality and differentiation are rational judgments that exist beyond the mere utterance. This passage explains why reducing universals to mere words would destroy rational inference and definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, focusing on the key claim about universals being rational judgments beyond words.
2
Identify the conditional reasoning
Notice the 'if... then' argument: if things were self-differentiating by essences alone, rational judgments would be useless.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the conclusion
The passage ends with two 'not lead to' statements that tie rational universals to valid inference and definition.
This shows why universals are necessary for knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المثبتون : الاشتراك والافتراق قضية عقلية وراء اللفظ، وإنما صيغ اللفظ على وفق ذلك ومطابقته، ونحن إنما تمسكنا بالقضايا العقلية دون الألفاظ الوضعية، اعتقد أن العموم والخصوص يرجعان إلى اللفظ المجرد فقد أنكر الحدود العقلية للأشياء والأدلة القطعية على المدلولات والأشياء لو كانت تتمايز بذواتها ووجودها بطل القول بالقضايا العقلية وحسم باب الاستدلال بشيء أول على شيء مكتسب متحصل، وما لم يدرج في الأدلة العقلية عموما عقليا لم يصل إلى العلم بالمدلول قط، وما لم يتحقق في الحد شمولاً بجميع المحدودات لم يصل إلى العلم بالمحدود.
The affirmers said: commonality and differentiation are a rational judgment beyond the utterance, and the utterance is only formulated in accordance with that and conforming to it. We only hold to rational judgments without conventional expressions. Whoever believes that universality and particularity refer to the bare utterance has denied the rational boundaries of things and the decisive proofs for the indicated things. If things were distinguished by their essences and existences, the assertion of rational judgments would be invalid, and the door of inference from a first principle to an acquired result would be closed. And whatever is not included in rational proofs with a rational universal never leads to knowledge of the indicated thing at all, and whatever is not verified in the definition to encompass all the defined things never leads to knowledge of the defined.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a proof by the affirmers (al-muthbitūn) for the reality of universals. They begin by asserting that commonality (ishtirāk) and differentiation (iftirāq) are rational judgments (qaḍiyya ʿaqliyya) that exist beyond the mere utterance (al-lafẓ). The utterance is constructed to match these rational realities. They then argue that anyone who reduces universality and particularity to mere words denies the rational boundaries of things and the definitive proofs for realities. They reason: if things were self-differentiating by their essences alone, rational judgments would be useless and inference from first principles to conclusions would be impossible. The passage concludes that without rational universals in proofs, knowledge of the indicated thing is impossible, and without comprehensiveness in definition, knowledge of the defined is impossible.

Key Terms
اشتراك
افتراق
قضية عقلية
حدود عقلية
العموم والخصوص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Universals are merely linguistic conventions with no reality beyond words.
Correction: Universals are rational realities that exist beyond language and are the basis for language and inference.

Checkpoint
According to the affirmers, what is the consequence of reducing universality and particularity to the bare utterance?
A) It affirms rational boundaries.
B) It denies rational boundaries and decisive proofs.
C) It makes inference easier.
D) It has no consequence.

Key Takeaway
Universals (commonality and differentiation) are rational judgments that ground all valid inference and definition; to reduce them to mere words destroys the possibility of rational knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a theological text, do you treat its meanings as mere products of language or as real rational entities that exist independently? This lesson demands that you commit to the reality of rational judgments. Can you truly say you do? If not, what will you change starting today?
?
This lesson argues that without rational universals, you can never really know anything. Think of a time you dismissed a theological claim as 'just semantics.' Was that dismissal actually a denial of the rational boundaries of things? How will you now engage with arguments that require universal categories?
?
The affirmers here stake the entire possibility of inference and definition on the reality of universals. Is your own faith built on mere utterances, or on rational judgments that conform to reality? This lesson requires you to choose. Which do you rely on when you say 'Allah exists' — is that a rational judgment or a mere convention? Be honest with yourself.
