The Negators' Objection: Why a Middle State is Impossible
Negators' objection: a state between existence and non-existence is unintelligible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the negators' argument that the doctrine of states is unintelligible because it posits a middle state between existence and non-existence, violating the law of excluded middle.

Core Idea
A state that is neither existent nor non-existent is a logical impossibility.

The negators (al-nāfūn) challenge the very intelligibility of the doctrine of states. They argue that a necessary condition for any theological debate is that the doctrine under discussion be conceptually coherent. Using the principle of non-contradiction, they claim that the state, described as neither existent nor non-existent, is self-contradictory and therefore cannot be a subject of rational discourse.

Guided Path
1
Read the negators' objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Notice the structure: a methodological premise, an intuitive fact, a criticism, and a rhetorical conclusion.
The negators use 'by intuition' (bil-badīha) to assert the self-evident nature of their claim.
2
Analyze the logical structure
Identify the key components: the principle that a doctrine must be intelligible, the claim that there is no middle between existence and non-existence, the accusation that the state contradicts this, and the additional criticism about the wujūd/thubūt distinction.
3
Reflect on the challenge
Consider why the negators think the state is unintelligible. Ask yourself: if a state is neither existent nor non-existent, what is its ontological status? Why does this violate intuition?
This objection is a fundamental challenge to the proponents of states.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال النافون : الكلام على المذهب ردًّا وقبولا إنما يصح بعد كون المذهب معقولا، ونحن نعلم بالبديهة أن لا واسطة بين النفي والإثبات، ولا بين العدم والوجود وأنتم اعتقدتم الحال لا موجودة ولا معدومة، وهو متناقض بالبديهة، ثم فرقتم بين الوجود والثبوت، فأطلقتم لفظ الثبوت على الحال، ومنعتم إطلاق لفظ الوجود، فنفس المذهب إذا لم يكن معقولا فكيف يسوغ سماع الكلام والدليل عليه؟
The negators said: Discussion of a doctrine, whether for rejection or acceptance, is only valid after the doctrine is intelligible. We know by intuition that there is no intermediary between negation and affirmation, nor between non-existence and existence, but you have believed in a state neither existent nor non-existent, which is contradictory by intuition. Then you distinguished between existence and thubūt (positive reality), applying the term thubūt to the state and prohibiting the application of the term existence. So if the doctrine itself is not intelligible, how can it be permissible to listen to speech and proof concerning it?
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents the negators' (al-nāfūn) objection against the doctrine of states (al-ḥāl). They begin with a methodological principle: a doctrine must be intelligible before it can be discussed or debated. They then state a self-evident truth: there is no intermediary between negation and affirmation, or between non-existence and existence. Yet the proponents of states claim that the state is neither existent nor non-existent, which the negators consider a direct contradiction of intuition. The negators further criticize the distinction between existence (wujūd) and thubūt (positive reality), where the proponents apply thubūt to states but deny them existence. The objection culminates in a rhetorical question: if the doctrine itself is unintelligible, how can one even discuss it or present proof for it?

Key Terms
الحال
النفي والإثبات
العدم والوجود
الوجود والثبوت
بديهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that a state is a plausible intermediate category between existence and non-existence.
Correction: The negators argue that this is logically impossible because there is no middle between existence and non-existence by intuition.

Checkpoint
According to the negators, what is the status of a state that is neither existent nor non-existent?
A) It is a valid rational concept.
B) It is contradictory by intuition.
C) It is unknown.
D) It is similar to thubūt.

Key Takeaway
The negators' objection is that a state neither existent nor non-existent is logically impossible, making the doctrine of states unintelligible and thus not a valid subject for theological discussion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever believed something about Allah or His attributes that, upon reflection, might be logically impossible? Will you investigate whether your own theological assumptions align with the principle of non-contradiction, even if it means abandoning a comfortable belief?
?
The negators argue that an unintelligible doctrine should not even be discussed. Are there areas in your faith where you rely on terms or concepts that you cannot clearly define or reconcile with reason? Commit to at least one specific concept to study until you can explain it without contradiction.
?
If the doctrine of states is unintelligible, what does that mean for your own understanding of Allah's attributes? Are you willing to accept that human reason has limits and that some truths transcend intuition? Or will you, instead, accept a logical contradiction just because it seems profound? Decide today: will you trust your intellect where it is clear, or will you suspend reason in the name of mystery?

The Negators' Critique of Universals as Mental Concepts
Negators analyze affirmers' definition via two interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the negators' critique of the affirmers' claim that commonality and differentiation are rational judgments, and their distinction between mere mental considerations and real external attributes.

Core Idea
Universals are mere mental concepts, not real attributes of individual things.

In this passage, the negators respond to the affirmers by offering two possible interpretations of what it means to say that commonality and differentiation are 'rational judgments.' They show that either this claim reduces to harmless mental considerations, or it asserts the existence of real universals, which they deny altogether. Their analysis cuts to the heart of the debate over the reality of universals.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Notice the two conditional phrases: 'إن عنيتم به أن...' and 'فإن عنيتم بذلك أن...'.
The structure is a classic 'if... then...' argument.
2
Identify the first interpretation
The negators consider that the affirmers might mean a thing is known from one aspect and unknown from another. They agree this is possible, but argue these aspects are merely mental considerations (اعتبارات ذهنية), not real attributes, like relations (نسب وإضافات).
This interpretation is harmless to the negators.
3
Identify the second interpretation
The negators then consider that the affirmers might mean a single thing has a real shared attribute and a real distinguishing attribute. This is the actual point of contention. The negators reject it as impossible.
This is the core dispute.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The negators argue: a particular, concrete thing cannot share in any respect, and a universal that is truly common has no existence at all. Understand why this challenges the affirmers' position.
If universals have no existence, they cannot be real attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قالوا : ما المعنى بقولكم : الافتراق والاشتراك قضية عقلية؟ إن عنيتم به أن الشيء الواحد يعلم من وجه ويجهل من وجه فالوجوه العقلية اعتبارات ذهنية وتقديرية، ولا يقتضي ذلك صفات ثابتة لذوات، وذلك كالنسب والإضافات مثل القرب والبعد في الجوهرين فإن عنيتم بذلك أن الشيء الواحد تتحقق له صفة يشارك فيها غيره، وصفة يمايز بها غيره فهو نفس المتنازع فيه، فإن الشيء الواحد المعين لا شركة فيه بوجه والشيء المشترك العام لا وجود له البتة.
Then they said: What do you mean by your statement: 'Differentiation and commonality are a rational judgment'? If you mean by it that one thing is known from one aspect and unknown from another, then the rational aspects are mental and estimative considerations, and this does not entail attributes that are fixed in essences, like relations and attributions such as proximity and distance in two substances. But if you mean by it that for one thing there is actually an attribute by which it shares with another and an attribute by which it distinguishes from another, then that is the very point of contention. For the one particular thing has no sharing in any respect, and the common universal thing has no existence at all.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is the negators' response to the affirmers' claim that commonality and differentiation are rational judgments. They offer two possible interpretations of the affirmers' statement. First interpretation: if by 'rational judgment' the affirmers mean that a single thing is known from one aspect and unknown from another, then these aspects are merely mental concepts (iʿtibārāt dhihniyya) and not real attributes fixed in essences. They compare them to relations like proximity and distance in two substances. Second interpretation: if the affirmers mean that a thing truly has a shared attribute and a distinguishing attribute, then that is precisely the matter under dispute. The negators then argue: a particular, concrete thing cannot have any sharing, and a universal that is truly common has no existence whatsoever. Thus, the affirmers' position is either trivial or untenable.

Key Terms
الوجوه العقلية
اعتبارات ذهنية
النسب والإضافات
الشيء المشترك العام
قضية عقلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that universals are real attributes of particulars, existing externally.
Correction: The passage argues that real universals cannot exist; they are merely mental concepts.

Checkpoint
According to the negators, what is the status of a common universal thing?
A) It has existence in the mind only.
B) It has no existence at all.
C) It exists externally.
D) It is a relation.

Key Takeaway
The negators argue that universals are not real; a common universal thing has no existence, and a particular thing cannot share in any respect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you label someone as 'good' or 'bad,' do you treat that label as a real attribute of their soul, or as a mental concept that may not capture their full reality? How might this distinction affect how you judge others and seek Allah's forgiveness for them?
?
The negators argue that a particular thing cannot share in any respect with another. Reflect on your own identity: You are a unique soul before Allah. Are there universal labels (like 'sinner', 'successful') that you have attached to yourself as if they define your essence? Could they be mere mental concepts? How does this realization change your approach to repentance and self-improvement?
?
Consider the first interpretation: rational aspects are mental considerations. When you think about Allah's attributes (like 'Merciful', 'Just'), are you treating them as mental concepts that relate to your understanding, or as real attributes that correspond to His essence? How does this affect the sincerity of your worship and reliance on Him?

The Objection Against the Doctrine of States
Objection against the doctrine of states by al-nufat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key arguments raised by the deniers of states (al-nufat) against the doctrine of al-ahwal.

Core Idea
Denying states preserves the logical division necessary for definition and inference.

The classical kalam debate over the reality of attributes beyond existence and non-existence is captured in the objection of the deniers of states. They argue that affirming states undermines rational inference and definition. This passage presents their case, challenging the proponents' notion of an intermediary between existence and non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how the objectors begin by questioning what is meant by 'commonality and distinction' as rational judgments.
The objectors present two possible interpretations and reject both as problematic.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The objectors consider two meanings: (1) rational aspects as mental considerations, which do not imply real attributes; (2) actual shared and distinctive attributes, which they argue cannot apply to a particular entity or a universal.
3
Understand the core objection
The objectors claim that affirming a state (al-hal) that is neither existent nor non-existent destroys the essential division between existence and non-existence, which is necessary for valid inference and definition.
They argue that a valid speculation must lead to either affirmation or negation, not a middle term.
4
Review their positive alternative
The objectors define reality (haqiqa) as a unique, inseparable property (khasisa) of each essence. They hold that generality and particularity pertain only to expressions, not to essences themselves.
For them, existence and the most specific attribute of a thing are identical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قالوا : ما المعنى بقولكم : الافتراق والاشتراك قضية عقلية؟ إن عنيتم به أن الشيء الواحد يعلم من وجه ويجهل من وجه فالوجوه العقلية اعتبارات ذهنية وتقديرية، ولا يقتضي ذلك صفات ثابتة لذوات، وذلك كالنسب والإضافات مثل القرب والبعد في الجوهرين فإن عنيتم بذلك أن الشيء الواحد تتحقق له صفة يشارك فيها غيره، وصفة يمايز بها غيره فهو نفس المتنازع فيه، فإن الشيء الواحد المعين لا شركة فيه بوجه والشيء المشترك العام لا وجود له البتة. وقولهم : إن نفي الحال يؤدي إلى حسم باب الحد والحقيقة والنظر والاستدلال بالعكس أولي فإن إثبات الحال التي لا توصف بالوجود والعدم وتوصف بالثبوت دون الوجود حسم باب الحد والاستدلال، فإن غاية الناظر أن يأتي في نظره بتقسيم داير بين النفي والإثبات، فينفي أحدهما حتى يتعين الثاني، ومثبت الحال قد أتى بواسطة بين الوجود والعدم، فلم يفد التقسيم والإبطال علما ولا يتضمن النظر حصول معرفة أصلا، ثم الحد والحقيقة على أصل نفاة الأحوال عبارتان عن معنى واحد، فحد الشيء حقيقته وحقيقته ما اختص في ذاته عن سائر الأشياء، ولكل شيء خاصية بها يتميز عن غيره، وخاصيته تلزم ذاته ولا تفارقه، ولا يشترك فيها بوجه وإلا بطل الاختصاص. وأما العموم والخصوص في الاستدلال فقد بينا أنه راجع إلى الأقوال الموضوعة لها ليربط شكلا بشكل ونظيراً بنظير، والذات لا تشتمل على عموم وخصوص البتة، بل وجود الشيء وأخص وصفه واحد. قال المثبتون : نحن لم نثبت واسطة بين النفي والإثبات، فإن الحال ثابتة عندنا، ولولا ذلك لما تكلمنا فيها بالنفي والإثبات، ولم نقل على الإطلاق: إنه شيء ثابت على حياله موجود، فإن الموجود المحدث إما جوهر وإما عرض، وهو ليس أحدهما، بل هو صفة معقولة لهما، فإن الجوهر قد يعلم بجوهريته ولا يعلم بحيزه، وكونه قابلا للعرض والعرض يعلم بعرضيته ولا يخطر بالبال كونه لونًا أو كونا، ثم يعرف كونه لونا بعد ذلك، ولا يعرف كونه سوادًا أو بياضا إلا أن يعرف، والمعلومان إذا تمايزا في الشيء الواحد رجع التمايز إلى الحال، وقد يعلم ضرورة من وجه، ويعلم نظرا من وجه، كمن يعلم كون المتحرك متحركا ضرورة، ثم يعلم بالنظر بعد ذلك كونه متحركا بحركة، ولو كان المعنيان واحدا لما علم أحدهما بالضرورة والثاني بالنظر، ولما سبق أحدهما إلى العقل وتأخر الآخر، ومن أنكر هذا فقد جحد الضرورة.
Then they said: What do you mean by saying that differentiation and commonality are a rational judgment? If you mean that the one thing is known from one aspect and unknown from another, then rational aspects are mental and estimative considerations, and that does not entail attributes subsisting in essences, like relations and additions such as proximity and distance in two substances. If you mean that the one thing has an attribute by which it shares with another and an attribute by which it differs from another, that is the very matter of dispute, for the one particular thing has no sharing in any respect, and the common universal thing has no existence at all. And their statement: Denial of the state (al-hal) leads to closing the door of definition (al-hadd), reality (al-haqiqa), speculation (al-nazar), and inference (al-istidlal); the opposite is more appropriate, for affirming the state which is not described by existence or non-existence but is described by subsistence (al-thubut) without existence closes the door of definition and inference. For the utmost of the speculator is to bring in his speculation a division revolving between negation and affirmation, so he negates one until the other is determined, but the affirmer of the state has introduced an intermediary between existence and non-existence, so the division and nullification do not yield knowledge, and speculation does not entail the attainment of cognition at all. Then definition and reality according to the deniers of states are two expressions for one meaning: the definition of a thing is its reality, and its reality is what is specific in its essence from all other things; and for every thing there is a property (khasisa) by which it is distinguished from others, and its property is inseparable from its essence and does not leave it, and it is not shared in any respect, otherwise specification would be invalid. As for generality and particularity in inference, we have shown that it refers to the expressions posited for it to connect one form with another and one analogue with another; the essence does not include generality or particularity at all, rather the existence of a thing and its most specific attribute are one. The affirmers (al-muthbitun) said: We have not affirmed an intermediary between negation and affirmation, for the state (al-hal) is subsistent (thabita) in our view, and were it not so, we would not speak of it in terms of negation and affirmation, and we do not say absolutely that it is a thing subsistent by itself and existent. For the originated existent is either a substance or an accident, and it is neither, rather it is an intelligible attribute of them. For the substance is sometimes known by its substantiality and not known by its location, and its being receptive of accidents; and the accident is known by its accidentally and it does not occur to mind that it is a color or a locus, then it is later known that it is a color, and it is not known that it is blackness or whiteness unless it is known. And when two known things are distinguished in one thing, the distinction refers back to the state. And something is sometimes known necessarily from one aspect and known by speculation from another, like one who knows that the moving thing is moving necessarily, then later knows by speculation that it is moving with a motion. And if the two meanings were one, one would not be known necessarily and the other by speculation, nor would one precede the intellect and the other follow. And whoever denies this has denied the necessary.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit concerning the doctrine of states (al-ahwal) in kalam. The opponents first ask what is meant by saying that distinction and commonality are a rational judgment. They argue that if it means the same thing is known from one aspect and unknown from another, then rational aspects are merely mental considerations and do not imply real attributes. If it means a thing actually has an attribute that it shares with others and one that distinguishes it, that is exactly the disputed point: a particular entity has no sharing, and a universal common thing has no existence. They then argue that denying the state is better than affirming it, because affirming a state that is neither existent nor non-existent but only subsistent destroys the possibility of definition and inference, since a sound inference divides between existence and non-existence, but the affirmer introduces a middle term. The opponents further explain that definition and reality are the same for the deniers of states: a thing's definition is its reality, which is its specific essence, and each thing has a unique property that is inseparable from it and not shared. They also note that generality and particularity in inference pertain to expressions, not to essences; existence and the most specific attribute are one. The proponents (al-muthbitun) respond by asserting that they do not posit an intermediary between existence and non-existence; rather, the state is subsistent in their view, but not as an existent entity. It is an intelligible attribute of substances and accidents. They provide examples: a substance is known by its substantiality but not by its location or its being receptive of accidents; an accident is known by its accidentally but not by its being a specific color. Distinctions in knowledge of the same thing must refer back to a state. They give the example of motion: one knows necessarily that a moving thing is moving, and later by speculation that it is moving with a motion; if the two meanings were identical, they would not differ in mode of knowledge. Thus, denying states contradicts necessary knowledge.

Key Terms
الحال
النفي والإثبات
الحد
الحقيقة
النفاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The deniers of states reject any real attributes in things.
Correction: The objectors affirm that each thing has a unique, inseparable property (khasisa) that constitutes its reality; they only reject the notion of a state that is neither existent nor non-existent.

Checkpoint
According to the objectors, what is the relation between definition (hadd) and reality (haqiqa)?
A) They are two distinct concepts.
B) They are one and the same meaning.
C) Definition is broader than reality.
D) Reality is unknowable.

Key Takeaway
The deniers of states argue that affirming a state between existence and non-existence undermines the logical division necessary for definition and inference, and they redefine reality as a unique, inseparable property of each essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own theological reflections, do you ever introduce vague ideas or 'middle grounds' to avoid the sharp either-or of truth? How does this passage's demand for clear division challenge you to be more precise in your understanding of God's attributes?
?
The deniers say that the existence of a thing and its most specific attribute are one. Do you live as if your knowledge of God's essence is inseparable from His attributes, or do you functionally separate them in your worship? What would change if you truly integrated them?
?
This lesson presents a logical argument that an intermediary between existence and non-existence destroys inference. Are there areas in your faith where you have settled for ambiguity rather than seeking the clarity that leads to certainty? What step will you take today to resolve that ambiguity?

The Proponents' Defense of States (Ahwal)
Proponents' defense of states and argument from epistemic distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the proponents of states (al-muthbitun) refute the objections and argue that states are necessary for distinguishing real attributes.

Core Idea
States are subsistent but not existent; they explain real distinctions in knowledge.

The proponents respond to the objections by clarifying that they do not posit a middle term between existence and non-existence. Instead, states are subsistent (thabita) attributes that are neither substances nor accidents. They provide concrete examples from substance, accident, and motion to show that distinctions in how we know a thing require states.

Guided Path
1
Read the proponents' response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the proponents directly address the accusation of affirming an intermediary.
They begin by denying that they affirm an intermediary.
2
Understand what a state is not
The proponents clarify that a state is not an existent thing on its own; it is not a substance or an accident, but an intelligible attribute of them.
3
Examine the examples
They give examples: a substance is known by its substantiality but not by its location or its being receptive of accidents; an accident is known by its accidentally but not by being a specific color. This shows that different known aspects refer back to a state.
These examples illustrate that knowledge of one thing can have multiple, distinct aspects.
4
Consider the argument from necessary vs. speculative knowledge
The proponents argue that if the two meanings (e.g., 'moving' and 'motion') were identical, they could not be known one necessarily and the other by speculation, nor could one precede the other in the intellect.
This refutes the objectors by appealing to common human experience.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The doctrine of states posits a third metaphysical realm of entities that are neither existent nor non-existent.
Correction: The proponents clarify that states are not existent entities; they are subsistent (thabita) attributes that are not substances or accidents, and they do not constitute an intermediary realm.

Checkpoint
According to the proponents, what is the status of a state (al-hal)?
A) It is an existent intermediary between existence and non-existence.
B) It is a pure mental consideration with no extra-mental reality.
C) It is subsistent (thabit) but not existent as a substance or accident.
D) It is both existent and non-existent simultaneously.

Key Takeaway
The proponents argue that states are subsistent attributes that account for real epistemic distinctions, and denying them contradicts necessary knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you knew something necessarily (like that a moving object is moving) but then later understood it through reasoning. Did you acknowledge that the object's motion was real even before you analyzed it? How does recognizing distinct states challenge you to accept truths about Allah that you know necessarily but cannot fully analyze?
?
The proponents argue that denying states is equivalent to denying necessary knowledge. Is there a truth about your own soul—like its existence or its attributes—that you have ever doubted because you could not define it? How will you humbly accept the reality of what you know necessarily, even when your intellect cannot fully categorize it?
?
If states are real but not existent, then our knowledge of things is richer than the mere existence of objects. How will you now pay more attention to the distinct attributes of things and people you encounter, seeing them as real distinctions that point to a greater reality? Commit to one concrete way you will apply this discernment in your daily interactions.

Proof that the Effect of a Cause is a State (Hal)
Al-Shahrastani proves the necessity of states via a rational division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why rational division leads to affirming states (ahwal) as attributes of substances, not independent existents.

Core Idea
The cause's effect cannot be itself or an independent essence, so it must be a state.

Al-Shahrastani engages with those who deny accidents but accept that motion causes a substance to be moving. He then asks: what exactly does the cause necessitate? Through a simple division, he shows the only coherent answer is a 'state'—an attribute that is not independently existent but is known along with the substance. This lesson walks through that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read carefully to follow the author's logical structure. Note the key terms.
2
Identify the rational division
Trace the division: cause necessitates either itself or something else; that something else is either independent essence or attribute. See why each branch except attribute is impossible.
This is the core of the proof.
3
Reflect on the nature of states
Notice the author's emphasis that states are not known on their own but only with the substance, like composition between two bodies.
This distinguishes states from classical accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونفاة الأعراض أنكروا أن الحركة عرضاً زائداً على المتحرك، وما أنكروا كونه متحركا، وأنتم معاشر النفاة وافقتمونا على أن الحركة علة لكون الجوهر متحركًا، وكذلك القدرة والعلم وجميع الأعراض والمعاني والعلة توجب المعلول لا محالة، فلا يخلو إما أن توجب ذاتها، وإما شيئًا آخر وراء ذاتها، ويستحيل أن يقال : توجب ذاتها، فإن الشيء الواحد من وجه واحد يستحيل أن يكون موجبًا وموجبًا لنفسه، وإن أوجب أمراً آخر، فذلك الأمر إما ذات على حيالها أو صفة لذات، ويستحيل أن تكون ذاتا على حيالها فإنه يؤدي إلى أن تكون العلة بإيجابها موجدة للذوات، وتلك الذوات أيضا علل وهو محال، فإنه يؤدي إلى التسلسل فيتعين أنه صفة(۱) لذات، وذلك هو الحال التي أثبتناها، فالقسمة العقلية ألجأتنا إلى إثباتها، والضرورة حملتنا (۲) على أن لا نسميها موجودة على حيالها ومعلومة على حيالها، وقد يعلم الشيء مع غيره ولا يعلم على حياله ؛ كالتأليف بين الجوهرين والمماسة، والقرب والبعد، فإن الجوهر الواحد لا يعلم فيه تأليف ولا مماسة ما لم ينضم إليه جوهر آخر، وهذا في الصفات التي هي ذوات وأعراض متصور، فكيف في الصفات التي ليست بذوات، بل هي أحكام الذوات.
And the deniers of accidents denied that motion is an accident additional to the moving body, but they did not deny that it is moving. And you, O company of deniers, agreed with us that motion is a cause for the substance being moving, and likewise power, knowledge, and all accidents and meanings. And the cause necessitates the effect inevitably. So it is either that it necessitates its own essence or something other than its essence. And it is impossible to say: it necessitates its own essence, for the one thing from one aspect cannot be necessitator and necessitated of itself. And if it necessitates another matter, then that matter is either an independent essence or an attribute of an essence. And it is impossible that it be an independent essence, for that would lead to the cause by its necessitation producing essences, and those essences themselves would be causes, which is impossible, for it leads to infinite regress. So it is determined that it is an attribute of an essence, and that is the state (al-hal) which we have affirmed. So the rational division compelled us to affirm them, and necessity carried us not to call them existent on their own and known on their own. And a thing may be known with another but not known on its own, like the composition between two substances and contact, proximity and distance, for the single substance is not known to have composition or contact unless another substance is joined to it. And this is conceivable in attributes that are essences and accidents, so how about attributes that are not essences but rather judgments of essences?
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a proof by rational division: the cause must necessitate either itself or something else. Self-necessitation is impossible (contradiction). If it necessitates something else, that thing is either an independent essence or an attribute. Independent essence leads to infinite regress (causes producing causes). Therefore, it must be an attribute, which al-Shahrastani identifies as the 'state' (hal). He emphasizes that states are not independently existent or known; they are known only along with the substance, like composition or contact between two substances. The argument concludes that attributes that are not essences are judgments of essences—i.e., states.

Key Terms
حال (hal)
علة (cause)
معلول (effect)
تسلسل (infinite regress)
صفة (attribute)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'states' are half-existing entities that can be known independently.
Correction: Al-Shahrastani explicitly says states are not known on their own; they are known only with the substance, similar to how composition is known only when two substances are together.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the effect of a cause cannot be an independent essence?
A) Because it would imply that the cause creates essences, leading to infinite regress.
B) Because independent essences cannot be caused.
C) Because the effect must be identical to the cause.
D) Because the cause and effect are the same.

Key Takeaway
The only coherent way to understand the effect of a cause is as an attribute (state) that is not independently existent but is known only through the substance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own actions, do you treat them as separate entities that you 'produce,' or as states of your soul that reveal who you are? How does al-Shahrastānī's argument challenge you to reconsider the relationship between your will and your deeds?
?
The passage proves that an effect cannot be an independent essence without leading to infinite regress. In your life, do you sometimes treat the fruits of your efforts as if they have a life of their own, forgetting that they depend on you? What would change if you saw them as attributes of your character instead?
?
Al-Shahrastānī says that states are known only with something else—like composition is known only between two substances. Which of your own attributes—such as patience, anger, or humility—do you only recognize in relation to others? How does this lesson urge you to take greater responsibility for those relational states?

States Are Real, Not Mere Mental Aspects
Al-Shahrastani refutes the objection that states reduce to mental considerations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Shahrastani defends the reality of states (ahwal) against the objection that they are nothing more than mental considerations.

Core Idea
States are real attributes, not mere mental fictions.

This passage presents a key objection to the Ash'ari theory of states: that states are just mental aspects. Al-Shahrastani responds by showing that these aspects are not arbitrary but anchored in real distinctions in knowledge. He distinguishes states from mere relations, which are only words.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the objection-and-answer structure.
The objection is introduced by 'وأما قولكم'.
2
Identify the objection and the response
Find where the opponent's claim is stated and where the refutation begins. The response starts with 'فنقول'.
3
Understand the key distinction
Notice how al-Shahrastani insists that mental aspects are not absolute but 'specific to essences' (مختصة بذوات). He equates them with states (أحوال).
He redefines the opponent's terms.
4
Examine the evidence from two knowledges
The author argues that two distinct knowledges (one necessary, one acquired) attach to a single essence, proving multiple aspects. This shows states are real.
This is the core rational proof.
5
Differentiate states from mere relations
The author ends by contrasting states with relations (نسب وإضافات), which are mere words without real knowledge. This reinforces the reality of states.
Relations are not real knowns.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولكم: إنه راجع إلى وجوه واعتبارات عقلية. فنقول: هذه الوجوه والاعتبارات ليست مطلقة مرسلة، بل هي مختصة بذوات ، فالوجوه العقلية لذات واحدة هي بعينها الأحوال فإن تلك الوجوه ليست مجردة قائمة بالمتكلم بل هي حقايق معلومة معقولة، لا أنها موجودة على حيالها ولا معلومة بانفرادها بل هي صفات توصف بها الذوات، فما عبرتم عنه بالوجوه عبرنا عنه بالأحوال فإن المعلومين قد تمايزا وإن كانت الذات متحدة، وتمايز المعلومين يدل على تعدد الوجهين والحالين وذلك معلومان محققان تعلق بهما علمان متمايزان؛ أحدهما ضروري، والثاني مكتسب، وليس ذلك كالنسب والإضافات فإنها ترجع إلى ألفاظ مجردة ليس فيها علم محقق متعلق بمعلوم محقق.
And as for your saying: It reduces to mental aspects and considerations. We say: These aspects and considerations are not absolute and unbound, rather they are specific to essences. So the mental aspects of a single essence are precisely the states (ahwal). For those aspects are not abstract subsisting in the speaker; rather they are real known intelligible things, not that they are existent on their own nor known in isolation, but rather they are attributes by which the essences are described. So what you express as 'aspects' we express as 'states'. For the two known things are distinguished even if the essence is one. And the distinction of the two known things indicates the multiplicity of the two aspects and the two states. And those are two real known things to which two distinct knowledges attach; one of them necessary, the other acquired. And that is not like relations and additions, for they reduce to mere words in which there is no real knowledge attached to a real known.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objection claims that states (ahwal) are merely mental considerations (wujuh wa i'tibarat). The author responds that these aspects are not arbitrary but attached specifically to essences. They are real, intelligible, though not independently existent. They are attributes of essences. The author equates 'wujuh' with 'ahwal' but insists they are real. He gives evidence: two distinct knowledges (one necessary, one acquired) attach to a single essence, indicating multiple aspects. This is unlike mere relations (nisab) which are only words without real knowledge.

Key Terms
وجوه (aspects)
اعتبارات (considerations)
أحوال (states)
معلوم (known)
علم ضروري (necessary knowledge)
علم مكتسب (acquired knowledge)
نسب وإضافات (relations and additions)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Someone might think that if two knowledges relate to one essence, then the essence itself must be multiple.
Correction: Al-Shahrastani clarifies that the essence remains one; the multiplicity is in the aspects (وجوه) and states (أحوال), not in the essence itself.

Checkpoint
How does al-Shahrastani refute the claim that states are mere mental considerations?
A) By arguing that these aspects are specific to essences and correspond to real distinct knowledges.
B) By saying that mental considerations are the same as states.
C) By denying the reality of mental aspects.
D) By claiming that all knowledge is necessary.

Key Takeaway
States are real attributes of a single essence, evidenced by two distinct knowledges, and are not mere mental relations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your knowledge of Allah, do you find that your understanding of His attributes as real states (ahwal) presses you to deepen your worship, or do you often remain content with the idea that they are mere labels? Identify one attribute you have been treating as a 'relation' rather than a real state, and commit to studying it as a real known attached to His essence.
?
The distinction between necessary knowledge (that Allah exists) and acquired knowledge (His specific attributes) is grounded in the reality of states. Do you actively seek acquired knowledge of Allah's attributes, or do you rely solely on the necessary recognition of His existence without striving for deeper understanding? What stops you from pursuing that acquired knowledge?
?
Al-Shahrastani argues that states are not like mere words. Consider your own speech about Allah: are your words about His attributes backed by real knowledge that distinguishes between necessary and acquired, or are they empty repetitions? How can you ensure that your profession of faith is grounded in real knowns rather than mental aspects?

Defending the Universality and Particularity of States
Al-Shahrastani defends the generality and particularity of states against objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Shahrastani defends the reality of states against the objection that a single essence cannot have a shared attribute, and why he argues that states are not reducible to mere words.

Core Idea
States are real because they allow both particularity and universality, and are stable across languages.

Al-Shahrastani here confronts an objection that attacks the very foundation of states (ahwal). The objection claims that a single essence cannot have a shared state, and that what is shared has no existence. In response, the author distinguishes between the specific thing itself and the attributes by which particularization and generalization occur. He argues that these attributes are necessary truths, like the mental aspects accepted by his opponents. Furthermore, he notes that expressions vary but the underlying realities do not, so reducing states to mere words is mistaken.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of objection and response.
Notice the objection starts with 'وقولهم' and the author's reply begins with 'باطل'.
2
Identify the objection and the author's key distinction
The objection says a single thing cannot have sharing. The author responds by distinguishing the specific thing (which has no sharing) from the attributes (states) which are not specific but are the basis for particularization and generalization.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the reality of states.
3
Understand the analogy to 'aspects' (wujuh)
The author calls this a necessary proposition, likening it to accepted mental concepts. Think about how aspects are stable even if expressions differ.
4
Reflect on the argument about words vs. mental realities
Expressions change across languages and times, but mental aspects do not. If states were mere words, they would change, but they don't. This shows states are real.
This argument strengthens the case for the ontological status of states.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولهم (4) : الشيء الواحد لا شركة فيه ، والمشترك لا وجود له باطل، فإن الشيء المعين من حيث هو معين لا شركة فيه وهذه الصفات التي أثبتناها ليست معينة مخصصة، بل هي صفات يقع بها التخصيص والتعيين، ويقع فيها الشمول والعموم، وهو من القضايا الضرورية كالوجوه عندكم ، ومن رد التعميم والتخصيص إلى مجرد الألفاظ فقد أبطل الوجوه والاعتبارات العقلية، أليست العبارات تتبدل لغة فلغة وحالة فحالة ؟ وهذه الوجوه العقلية لا تتبدل بل الذوات ثابتة عليها قبل التعبير عنها بلفظ عام وخاص على السواء، ومن ردها إلى الاعتبارات أفقد ناقض وردها إلى العبارات فإن الاعتبارات لا تتبدل ولا تتغير بعقل وعقل والعبارات تتبدل بلسان ولسان وزمان وزمان.
And their saying: The one thing has no sharing in it, and the shared has no existence—is false. For the specific thing insofar as it is specific has no sharing in it. And these attributes which we have affirmed are not specific and particularized; rather they are attributes by which particularization and specification occur, and in them occur inclusiveness and universality. And this is among necessary propositions like the aspects (wujuh) with you. And whoever reduces generalization and specification to mere words has nullified the mental aspects and considerations. Do not expressions change from language to language and from state to state? But these mental aspects do not change; rather the essences are established upon them before expression by a general or specific term alike. And whoever reduces them to considerations contradicted, and whoever reduces them to expressions—for considerations do not change or alter from one intellect to another, while expressions change from one tongue to another and from one time to another.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objection claims that the one thing (the substance) has no sharing (i.e., no common attribute) and that what is shared has no existence. The author responds: the specific thing as specific indeed has no sharing, but the attributes he posits (states) are not specific; they are attributes by which specification and generalization occur. This is a necessary truth, like the aspects (wujuh) that objectors accept. He further argues that expressions vary, but the mental realities do not. So reducing generalization/specification to mere words is false. The states (or wujuh) are stable across languages and intellects.

Key Terms
شركة (sharing)
تخصيص (particularization)
تعميم (generalization)
وجوه عقلية (mental aspects)
عبارات (expressions)
اعتبارات (considerations)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think states are specific to each essence and cannot be shared.
Correction: The author says states are attributes that allow both particularity and universality; they are not themselves specific.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that if generalization and specification were mere words, it would nullify mental aspects?
A) Because mental aspects are stable while words change.
B) Because words are more real than aspects.
C) Because generalization and specification are not related to intellect.
D) Because the objectors only accept words.

Key Takeaway
Al-Shahrastani defends the reality of states by showing that they are attributes that allow both particularity and universality, and are stable across languages and intellects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the attributes of God in Arabic or in your own language, do you treat them as stable mental realities that transcend the words you use, or do you feel that changing the language changes the truth? The passage warns that reducing meanings to mere words nullifies mental aspects. How often do you reflect on the actual realities behind your religious vocabulary?
?
Consider a core belief you hold about God's oneness. Is it anchored in an unchanging mental aspect that would remain true even if all human languages and terms were different? The lesson shows that mental considerations do not change from intellect to intellect. Where in your faith are you most vulnerable to confusing cultural or temporal expressions with eternal truths?
?
The author argues that the specific thing as such has no sharing, but states allow both particularity and universality. This implies that the attributes of God are not His essence but are real in a way that allows them to be shared. How does this challenge your understanding of God's attributes being unique to Him? Are you willing to accept that some truths about God are universal and can be known by reason, even if they seem to imply similarity?

The First Refutation: Attributes of Attributes
Opponent refutes the proponents' claim that attributes are not described by showing they affirm attributes for attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against the theory of states: that it implies attributes of attributes and therefore a regress of states.

Core Idea
If attributes can have states, then states can have states, leading to an infinite regress.

The opponent begins by attacking the consistency of the proponents' position. The proponents claim that attributes themselves are not described, yet they affirm that attributes have states. This contradiction allows the opponent to demand that if attributes can have states, then states can have states, opening the door to regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's opening objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that the opponent is responding to a claim by the proponents.
The proponents had previously argued that attributes are not described, meaning attributes do not have attributes.
2
Identify the contradiction
The opponent points out that the proponents themselves have already affirmed an attribute for an attribute: they say existence, accidentality, colorness, and blackness are states of blackness. This means they have already described an attribute (blackness) with other attributes (states).
3
Understand the regress argument
The opponent then asks: if attributes can have attributes (states), then why not states have states? This would lead to an infinite regress, which is unacceptable.
This is a classic argument against the necessity of states.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يغني عن هذا الإلزام قولكم الصفة لا توصف، فإنكم أول من أثبت للصفة صفة حيث جعلتم الوجود والعرضية واللونية والسوادية أحوالاً للسواد، فإذا أثبتم للصفات صفات فهلا أثبتم للأحوال أحوالاً. وأما الوجوه والاعتبارات فقد تتحقق في الأحوال أيضا فإن الحال العام غير والحال الخاص غير وهما اعتباران في الحال، وحال يوجب أحوالا غير، وحال هي موجبة الحال غير أليس قد أثبت أبو هاشم حالا للباري سبحانه توجب كونه عالما قادرًا، والعالمية والقادرية حالتان فحال توجب وحال هي غير موجبة مختلفتان في الاعتبار، واختلاف الاعتبارين لا يوجب اختلاف الحالين للحال. هذا من الإلزامات المفحمة ومن جملتها أخرى وهي أن الوجود في القديم والحادث والجوهر والعرض عندهم حال لا يختلف البتة، بل وجود الجوهر في حكم وجود العرض على السواء، فيلزمهم على ذلك أمران منكران؛ إبهام شيء واحد في شيئين مختلفين، أو شيئان مختلفان في شيء واحد وواحد في اثنين واثنان في واحد محال، أما أحدهما أن شيئًا واحدا كيف يتصور في شيئين، فإن حال الوجود من حيث هو وجود واحد، ومن بديهة العقول أن الشيء الواحد لا يكون في شيئين.
And your saying 'the attribute is not described' does not avail against this objection, for you are the first to affirm an attribute for an attribute, when you made existence, accidentality, colorness, and blackness states (ahwal) of black. So if you affirm attributes for attributes, why do you not affirm states for states? As for aspects and considerations, they are also realized in states. For the general state is different and the particular state is different, and they are two considerations in the state. And a state necessitates other states, and a state is the necessitator of the state, different. Has not Abu Hashim affirmed a state for the Exalted Creator that necessitates His being knowing and powerful? And knowledgeability and powerability are two states. So a state that necessitates and a state that is not necessitating are different in consideration. And the difference of the two considerations does not necessitate the difference of the two states for the state. This is among the overwhelming objections. And among them is another: that existence in the eternal and the originated, and in substance and accident, in their view is a state that does not differ at all. Rather, the existence of substance is in the same ruling as the existence of accident. So two objectionable things follow from that: either one single thing is in two different things, or two different things are in one thing. And one in two and two in one is impossible. As for one of them: how can a single thing be conceived in two things? For the state of existence insofar as it is existence is one, and it is a self-evident truth that a single thing is not in two things.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage contains the opponent's further refutations against the proponents of states (ahwal). First, the opponent argues that the proponents' claim 'the attribute is not described' does not save them because they themselves affirm an attribute for an attribute: they say existence, accidentality, colorness, and blackness are states of blackness. So if attributes can have attributes (states), then why not states have states? This indicates a regress. Second, concerning 'aspects and considerations' (wujuh wa i'tibarat), the opponent shows they apply to states as well: a general state and a particular state are two considerations of the state; a state that necessitates other states and a state that is the necessitator are different in consideration, but this difference does not imply different states for the state itself. The opponent mentions Abu Hashim's theory that God has a state necessitating His being knowing and powerful, and that knowledgeability and powerability are two states. This shows the complexity and potential regress. Finally, the opponent raises a decisive objection: in their view, existence (wujud) as a state is identical in eternal and originated beings, and in substance and accident. So existence is one and the same, yet it is found in different entities. This leads to absurdity: either a single thing (existence) is in two different things (impossible), or two different things are in one thing (also impossible). The opponent concludes that it is self-evident that one thing cannot exist in two distinct subjects. This argument against the reality of states (or common existence) is a powerful attack on the theory.

Key Terms
حال (state)
صفة (attribute)
وجود (existence)
عرضية (accidentality)
لونية (colorness)
سوادية (blackness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the opponent is denying the reality of attributes altogether.
Correction: The opponent is specifically attacking the theory of states (ahwal), not attributes in general.

Checkpoint
What is the proponents' claim that the opponent says does not avail them?
A) The attribute is not described
B) The state is not described
C) Attributes are eternal
D) States are necessary

Key Takeaway
The opponent argues that the proponents' theory of states implies attributes can have attributes, leading to an infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim that you do not describe Allah's attributes, do you find yourself inadvertently describing them through your explanations? This lesson shows that even denying description can lead to describing. Examine your own words: have you ever spoken about Allah's attributes in a way that implies they are like created things? How can you guard against that?
?
The proponents invented states to avoid describing attributes, but the opponent caught them in a contradiction. Reflect: Are there areas in your understanding of Allah where you try to protect His transcendence but end up making subtle comparisons? What one specific precaution can you take today to avoid such errors?
?
This argument forces consistency in theological language. How often do you accept inconsistencies in your own beliefs because they are comfortable? Commit to one area where you will pursue conceptual consistency even if it is difficult.

The Multiplicity of States: Aspects and Considerations
Opponent argues that aspects and considerations apply to states themselves, creating internal multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the opponent shows that the theory of states leads to internal multiplicity and complexity that undermines its coherence.

Core Idea
If states can have aspects, then they are not simple but admit differentiation.

The opponent continues by examining the proponent's concept of 'aspects and considerations' (wujuh wa i'tibarat). He argues that these aspects also apply to states themselves, leading to a differentiation of states into general and particular, necessitating and necessitated, which ultimately opens the door to further complications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on aspects and considerations
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice how the opponent applies the proponents' own concept to states.
2
Identify the two distinctions within states
The opponent points out that states can be general vs particular, and also a state can be necessitating or necessitated. These are two different considerations of the same state.
These distinctions show that states themselves have a kind of complexity.
3
Analyze the example of Abu Hashim
Abu Hashim's theory posits a single state for God that necessitates His being knowing and powerful. But then knowledgeability and powerability are also states. This creates a situation where a state has different roles (necessitating vs being necessitated), further showing complexity.
4
Understand the conclusion
The opponent concludes that difference in consideration does not necessitate difference in the state itself. This undermines the simplicity and necessity of states.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يغني عن هذا الإلزام قولكم الصفة لا توصف، فإنكم أول من أثبت للصفة صفة حيث جعلتم الوجود والعرضية واللونية والسوادية أحوالاً للسواد، فإذا أثبتم للصفات صفات فهلا أثبتم للأحوال أحوالاً. وأما الوجوه والاعتبارات فقد تتحقق في الأحوال أيضا فإن الحال العام غير والحال الخاص غير وهما اعتباران في الحال، وحال يوجب أحوالا غير، وحال هي موجبة الحال غير أليس قد أثبت أبو هاشم حالا للباري سبحانه توجب كونه عالما قادرًا، والعالمية والقادرية حالتان فحال توجب وحال هي غير موجبة مختلفتان في الاعتبار، واختلاف الاعتبارين لا يوجب اختلاف الحالين للحال. هذا من الإلزامات المفحمة ومن جملتها أخرى وهي أن الوجود في القديم والحادث والجوهر والعرض عندهم حال لا يختلف البتة، بل وجود الجوهر في حكم وجود العرض على السواء، فيلزمهم على ذلك أمران منكران؛ إبهام شيء واحد في شيئين مختلفين، أو شيئان مختلفان في شيء واحد وواحد في اثنين واثنان في واحد محال، أما أحدهما أن شيئًا واحدا كيف يتصور في شيئين، فإن حال الوجود من حيث هو وجود واحد، ومن بديهة العقول أن الشيء الواحد لا يكون في شيئين.
And your saying 'the attribute is not described' does not avail against this objection, for you are the first to affirm an attribute for an attribute, when you made existence, accidentality, colorness, and blackness states (ahwal) of black. So if you affirm attributes for attributes, why do you not affirm states for states? As for aspects and considerations, they are also realized in states. For the general state is different and the particular state is different, and they are two considerations in the state. And a state necessitates other states, and a state is the necessitator of the state, different. Has not Abu Hashim affirmed a state for the Exalted Creator that necessitates His being knowing and powerful? And knowledgeability and powerability are two states. So a state that necessitates and a state that is not necessitating are different in consideration. And the difference of the two considerations does not necessitate the difference of the two states for the state. This is among the overwhelming objections. And among them is another: that existence in the eternal and the originated, and in substance and accident, in their view is a state that does not differ at all. Rather, the existence of substance is in the same ruling as the existence of accident. So two objectionable things follow from that: either one single thing is in two different things, or two different things are in one thing. And one in two and two in one is impossible. As for one of them: how can a single thing be conceived in two things? For the state of existence insofar as it is existence is one, and it is a self-evident truth that a single thing is not in two things.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage contains the opponent's further refutations against the proponents of states (ahwal). First, the opponent argues that the proponents' claim 'the attribute is not described' does not save them because they themselves affirm an attribute for an attribute: they say existence, accidentality, colorness, and blackness are states of blackness. So if attributes can have attributes (states), then why not states have states? This indicates a regress. Second, concerning 'aspects and considerations' (wujuh wa i'tibarat), the opponent shows they apply to states as well: a general state and a particular state are two considerations of the state; a state that necessitates other states and a state that is the necessitator are different in consideration, but this difference does not imply different states for the state itself. The opponent mentions Abu Hashim's theory that God has a state necessitating His being knowing and powerful, and that knowledgeability and powerability are two states. This shows the complexity and potential regress. Finally, the opponent raises a decisive objection: in their view, existence (wujud) as a state is identical in eternal and originated beings, and in substance and accident. So existence is one and the same, yet it is found in different entities. This leads to absurdity: either a single thing (existence) is in two different things (impossible), or two different things are in one thing (also impossible). The opponent concludes that it is self-evident that one thing cannot exist in two distinct subjects. This argument against the reality of states (or common existence) is a powerful attack on the theory.

Key Terms
حال (state)
اعتبار (consideration)
وجه (aspect)
أبو هاشم (Abu Hashim)
عالمية (knowledgeability)
قادرية (powerability)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the opponent is denying the possibility of distinctions within states.
Correction: The opponent actually uses the existence of distinctions to argue against the necessity of states as separate realities.

Checkpoint
What example does the opponent give to show that states have considerations?
A) General and particular state
B) Eternal and originated
C) Knowing and powerful
D) Substance and accident

Key Takeaway
The opponent argues that the inner complexity of states (general/specific, necessitating/necessitated) reveals inconsistencies in the theory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The opponent argues that considerations do not create separate states. When you think about God's attributes, do you ever inadvertently treat them as separate 'parts' of God? How does this passage expose that subtle mistake in your own thinking? Commit to one way you will train yourself to see the attributes as aspects of a single essence.
?
Abu Hashim's example involves a state that necessitates other states. In your life, do you tend to think of your own qualities (like knowledge or power) as distinct, separate selves? How might the insight that considerations don't multiply reality change how you view your own character? Be specific: name one quality you treat as a 'separate part' and describe what it would mean to see it as a mere consideration.

The Absurdity of a Single State of Existence
Opponent argues that a single state of existence common to all entities leads to absurd contradictions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's strongest objection: that a single state of existence cannot be common to different entities without contradiction.

Core Idea
A single state of existence cannot be in both the eternal and originated, or in substance and accident, without absurdity.

The opponent presents a powerful refutation: the proponents consider existence (wujud) as a single state that is identical in all things, from the eternal to the originated, from substance to accident. This leads to an impossible situation: either one thing is in many different things, or many different things are in one thing. Both are rationally absurd.

Guided Path
1
Read the final objection passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation. This is the climax of the opponent's refutation.
2
Understand the premise: existence as a single state
The opponent states that in the proponents' view, existence is a single state that does not differ whether in the eternal or originated, substance or accident.
3
Grasp the two impossible consequences
From the premise, two impossible things follow: either one thing (existence) is in two different things (e.g., eternal and originated), or two different things are in one thing (existence). Both are rationally impossible.
The opponent emphasizes that it is a self-evident truth that a single thing cannot be in two different subjects.

Objection: States Would Require States (Infinite Regress)
Objection against the theory of states using the threat of infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that if attributes have attributes, states would have states, leading to infinite regress, and how the theory of states is defended by distinguishing between considerations and states.

Core Idea
If attributes need attributes, states would need states — a regress that challenges the whole theory.

This passage presents a powerful logical objection against the Ash'ari theory of states (ahwal). The objector argues that the very logic used to posit attributes for God would also require states for states, leading to an infinite regress. We will examine this objection and see how it targets the coherence of the theory, and then see the defense based on the distinction between considerations and states.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, noting the initial analogy from blackness to illustrate the problem.
The objector uses blackness and its states as a concrete example.
2
Understand the regress argument
Notice how the objector extends the logic from attributes to states, and then into aspects and considerations, aiming to show infinite regress.
Pay attention to the references to general vs specific states and necessitating states.
3
See the defense
The passage ends with a response that differing considerations do not necessitate differing states, which blocks the regress.
This hinges on the distinction between ontological states and conceptual considerations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعرضية واللونية والسوادية أحوالاً للسواد، فإذا أثبتم للصفات صفات فهلا أثبتم للأحوال أحوالاً. وأما الوجوه والاعتبارات فقد تتحقق في الأحوال أيضا فإن الحال العام غير والحال الخاص غير وهما اعتباران في الحال، وحال يوجب أحوالا غير، وحال هي وجبة الحال غير أليس قد أثبت أبو هاشم حالا للباري سبحانه توجب كونه عالما قادرًا، والعالمية والقادرية حالتان فحال توجب وحال هي غير موجبة مختلفتان في الاعتبار، واختلاف الاعتبارين لا يوجب اختلاف الحالين للحال.
And accidentalness and blackness and blackness-ness are states of blackness. So if you affirm attributes for attributes, why do you not affirm states for states? As for aspects and considerations, they can also occur in states. For the general state is other than the specific state, and they are two considerations in the state. And a state necessitates other states, and a state is [؟] the state's necessitation. Did not Abu Hashim affirm a state for the Exalted Creator that necessitates His being knowing and powerful? And knowership and powerfulness are two states. So a state necessitates and a state is not necessitating, differing in consideration. And the difference of the two considerations does not necessitate the difference of the two states for the state.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the Ash'ari theory of states (ahwal). The objector argues that if attributes can have attributes, then states should also have states, leading to infinite regress. The example uses blackness and its states (accidentalness, blackness-ness). Then the discussion turns to aspects and considerations within states, referencing Abu Hashim's view that God has a state which necessitates His attributes. The author points out that even if two considerations differ, that does not mean the states themselves differ, thus challenging the opponents' reasoning.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage denies the existence of states altogether.
Correction: This passage is a logical objection against the theory of states, not a denial of their existence. It argues that if the theory is applied consistently, it leads to infinite regress, but the defense offered distinguishes between considerations and states.

Checkpoint

C) Abu Hashim's theory is correct and needs no modification.

Key Takeaway
The infinite regress objection is countered by distinguishing between the multiplicity of considerations and the unity of the state itself.

Objection: Existence as One State Leads to Absurdities
Objection that existence as one state leads to two absurd consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two absurd consequences of claiming that existence is a single state common to all beings, and why this renders the theory untenable.

Core Idea
One existence in many beings leads to logical impossibilities.

This lesson presents a powerful objection against the theory that existence is a single state shared by all things. The argument shows that if existence were truly one and the same in substances and accidents, eternal and temporal, then absurdities follow: either one thing exists in multiple distinct entities, or distinct entities collapse into one. The passage also refutes a philosophical analogy and questions the very locus of such an existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection's premise
The objector states that if existence is a single state common to all beings, then two objectionable matters follow.
The premise is that existence (al-wujud) is a state (hal) that does not differ at all between beings.
2
Identify the first absurd consequence
The first consequence: one and the same existence cannot be in two different things (e.g., substance and accident). This is rationally impossible.
The objector emphasizes that from the beginning of intellects, one thing cannot be in two things.
3
Identify the second absurd consequence
The second consequence: if existence is one state, then different genera, species, and individuals would become united in it, causing genera to change without any change in existence itself.
This would imply that the distinctness of beings collapses.
4
Consider the refutation of the philosophical analogy
The objector compares this to the philosophical idea of matter receiving different forms, but refutes it: matter always has some form, and some forms are necessary, so the analogy fails.
The analogy is unsound because matter is never devoid of form.
5
Reflect on the final challenge
The objector asks: how can a pure, absolute, universal existence exist? If it has a locus (substance or accident), it loses universality; if it has no locus, it is impossible.
This final point seals the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا من الإلزامات المفحمة ومن جملتها أخرى وهي أن الوجود في القديم والحادث والجوهر والعرض عندهم حال لا يختلف البتة، بل وجود الجوهر في حكم وجود العرض على السواء، فيلزمهم على ذلك أمران منكران : إبهام شيء واحد في شيئين مختلفين، أو شيئان مختلفان في شيء واحد وواحد في اثنين واثنان في واحد محال، أما أحدهما أن شيئًا واحدا كيف يتصور في شيئين، فإن حال الوجود من حيث هو وجود واحد، ومن بدايه العقول أن الشيء الواحد لا يكون في شيئين معابل في جميع الموجودات على السواء، وفي ما لم يوجد بعد، لكنه سيوجد على السواء وهو من أمحل المحال. والمنكر الثاني إذا كان الوجود حالا واحدا واجتمعت فيه أجناس وأنواع وأصناف فيلزم أن تتحد المتكثرات المختلفة فيه، ولزم تبدل الأجناس من غير تبدل وتغير في الوجود أصلا، وذلك كتيدل الصورة بعضها ببعضه في الهيولي عند الفلاسفة من غير تبدل في الهيولي، وذلك التمثيل أيضا غير سديد، فإن الهيولي لا تعري عن الصورة إلا أن من الصور ما هو لازم للهيولي كالأبعاد الثلاثة، ومنها ما يتبدل ; كالأشكال والمقادير المختلفة والأوضاع والكيفيات، وبالجملة وجود مجرد مطلق عام غير مختلف كيف يتصور وجوده وما محله؟ فإن كان الجوهر محلا له فقد بطل عمومه العرض، فإن عنيتم العرض فقد بطل عمومه الجوهر، وإن لم يكن ذا محل وهو من أمحل المحال.
This is one of the overwhelming necessary implications. And among them is another: that existence in the eternal and the temporal, the substance and the accident, according to them is a state that does not differ at all. Rather, the existence of the substance is in the same ruling as the existence of the accident. So two objectionable matters follow from that: either one thing being in two different things, or two different things being in one thing; and one in two and two in one are impossible. As for the first: how can one thing be in two things? For the state of existence insofar as it is existence is one, and from the beginning of intellects it is that one thing cannot be in two things [؟] in all existents equally, and in what has not yet existed but will exist equally, and that is among the most absurd of absurdities. And the second objection: if existence is one state and genera, species, and individuals are combined in it, then many different things must become united in it, and genera would change without any change or alteration in existence at all. That is like the transformation of forms into each other in matter according to the philosophers without change in matter. But that analogy is also unsound, for matter is never devoid of form, except that some forms are necessary for matter like the three dimensions, and some change like shapes, different magnitudes, positions, and qualities. In summary: a pure, absolute, universal existence that is not different—how can its existence be conceived and what is its locus? If substance is its locus, then its universality over accidents is invalid. If you mean accident, then its universality over substance is invalid. And if it has no locus, that is among the most absurd of absurdities.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection against the theory of states, focusing on the state of existence (al-wujud). The objector argues that if existence is a single state common to all beings (eternal/temporal, substance/accident), then two absurd consequences follow. First, one and the same existence would be in different things, which is impossible. Second, if existence is one, then all differences would collapse and genera would change without any change in existence itself. The objector also refutes an analogy from philosophy (matter receiving forms) by noting that matter always has forms. He then questions how a purely absolute existence can exist: if it has a locus (substance or accident), it loses universality; if it has no locus, it is impossible. Thus, the theory of a single state of existence is untenable.

حال
جوهر
عرض
محال
إلزام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the objection denies the reality of existence itself.
Correction: The objection is not about existence per se, but about the theory that existence is a single, undifferentiated state common to all beings.

Checkpoint
What is the second absurd consequence of claiming existence is one state?
A) It leads to the necessity of eternal attributes.
B) Genera would change without any change in existence.
C) Existence would be a substance.
D) Existence would be an accident.

Key Takeaway
The theory that existence is a single state leads to logical absurdities and is therefore untenable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This objection shows that a seemingly plausible premise can lead to logical contradictions. In your own life, what belief or assumption have you held that, upon honest reflection, leads to inconsistencies? Are you willing to abandon it for the sake of truth?
?

?
