Refuting Infinite Regress in the Doctrine of States
The affirmers argue that the objection of infinite regress does not apply to states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the affirmers of states (ahwal) refute the charge of infinite regress using analogies from genus and species, and why the deniers themselves rely on universals.

Core Idea
State-ness is not a state, just as genus-ness is not a genus—so no regress.

This passage presents the affirmers' key defense against the claim that positing states leads to infinite regress. By drawing a careful analogy with genus and species, they show that the objection is based on a confusion. They then turn the tables on the deniers, pointing out that their own argument uses universals.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the analogy between states and genera/species.
2
Identify the key terms
Find terms like الحال, الجنس, النوع, التسلسل, التماثل. Understand their meanings in context.
These are technical kalam terms.
3
Understand the structure
The passage has two main parts: first, the analogy refuting infinite regress; second, a counter-charge against the deniers.
4
Reflect on the logical force
Ask yourself why the analogy holds and what it implies for the existence of states. Then consider how the deniers cannot avoid using universals.
This connects to the broader debate on states.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال المثبتون : إلزام الحال علينا نقضا غير متوجه، فإن العموم والخصوص في الحال كالجنسية والنوعية في الأجناس والأنواع، فإن الجنسية في الأجناس ليس جنساً حتى يستدعي كل جنس جنساً ويودي إلى التسلسل، وكذلك النوعية في الأنواع ليست نوعًا حتى تستدعي كل نوع نوعًا، فكذلك الحالية للأحوال لا حالا فيؤدي إلى التسلسل، وليس يلزم على من يقول: الوجود عام أن يقول للعام عام، وكذلك لو قال : العرضية جنس، فلا يلزمه أن يقول للجنس جنس، وكذلك لو فرق فارق بين حقيقة الجنس والنوع وفصل أحدهما عن الثاني بأخص وصف لم يلزمه أن يثبت اعتبارًا عقليًا في الجنس هو كالجنس، ووجها عقليا هو كالنوع فلا يلزم الحال علينا بوجه لا من حيث العموم والخصوص، ولا من حيث الاعتبار والوجه. وأنتم معاشر النفاة لا يمكنكم أن تتكلموا بكلمة واحدة إلا وإن تدرجوا فيها عموماً وخصوصاً، ألستم تنفون الحال ولم تعينوا حالا بعينها هي صفة مخصوصة مشار إليها ؟ بل أطلقتم القول وعممتم النفي وأقمتم الدليل على جنس شمل أنواعًا أو على نوع عم أشخاصاً حتى استمر دليلكم، ومن نفى الحال لا يمكنه إثبات التماثل في المتماثلات، وإجراء حكم واحد فيها جميعا، فلو كانت الأشياء تتمايز بذواتها المعينة بطل التماثل وبطل الاختلاف، وذلك خروج عن قضايا العقول.
And the affirmers said: The imposition of [the doctrine of] state (al-hal) upon us is an untenable objection. For universality and particularity in the state are like genus-ness and species-ness in genera and species. For genus-ness in genera is not a genus such that every genus would require a genus and lead to infinite regress; likewise species-ness in species is not a species such that every species would require a species. Thus state-ness (al-haliyya) for states (al-ahwal) is not a state such that it would lead to infinite regress. And it is not incumbent upon one who says 'existence is universal' to say that the universal has a universal; likewise if he said 'accidentality is a genus', it is not incumbent upon him to say that the genus has a genus. And likewise if someone distinguished between the reality of genus and species and separated one from the other by the most specific description, it would not be incumbent upon him to posit an intellectual consideration (i'tibar 'aqli) in the genus that is like the genus, and an intellectual aspect (wajh 'aqli) that is like the species. So the state is not incumbent upon us in any way, neither from the perspective of universality and particularity, nor from the perspective of consideration and aspect. And you, O deniers (al-nufat), cannot utter a single word without involving universality and particularity in it. Do you not deny the state without specifying a particular state that is a specific indicated attribute? Rather, you spoke absolutely and universalized the negation and established the proof upon a genus that includes species, or upon a species that encompasses individuals, so that your proof continued. And one who denies the state cannot establish similarity (al-tamathul) among similar things, nor apply a single judgment to all of them. For if things were distinguished by their particular essences (dhawatiha al-mu'ayyana), similarity would be nullified and difference would be nullified, and that is a departure from the dictates of intellects.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the doctrine of states (ahwal) by the affirmers (al-muthbitun) against the objection that positing states leads to infinite regress. The author begins by stating that the objection is not valid. Then he draws an analogy: the relationship of universality and particularity in states is like the relationship of genus-ness and species-ness in genera and species. Just as genus-ness is not itself a genus, so it does not require another genus ad infinitum; likewise, state-ness is not itself a state, so it does not require another state. The author then extends the analogy: if someone says 'existence is universal,' he is not obliged to say that the universal has a universal; similarly, if someone says 'accidentality is a genus,' he is not obliged to say that genus has a genus. The same applies if someone distinguishes genus from species by an intrinsic description—he need not posit an intellectual consideration or aspect that is like the genus or species itself. So the objection fails on all counts. Next, the author turns to the deniers (al-nufat) and points out a contradiction: they themselves cannot utter a single word without employing universality and particularity. They deny the state without specifying a particular state—they universalize their negation and base their proof on a genus that includes species or a species that encompasses individuals. If states did not exist, one could not establish similarity among similar things or apply a single judgment to all of them. If things were distinguished only by their particular essences, similarity and difference would both be impossible, which contradicts the clear verdict of reason.

الحال (al-hal)
الأحوال (al-ahwal)
الجنس (al-jins)
النوع (al-naw')
التسلسل (al-tasalsul)
العموم والخصوص (al-umum wa al-khusus)
المثبتون (al-muthbitun)
النفاة (al-nufat)
التماثل (al-tamathul)
الاختلاف (al-ikhtilaf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Positing states (ahwal) leads to infinite regress because for every state there is another state of that state.
Correction: State-ness (al-haliyya) is not itself a state, so no regress occurs, just as genus-ness is not a genus.

Checkpoint
According to the affirmers, why does the doctrine of states (ahwal) not lead to infinite regress?
A) Because states are not real entities.
B) Because the state-ness of a state is not itself a state, just as genus-ness is not a genus.
C) Because infinite regress is impossible in any case.
D) Because the deniers have not specified a particular state.

Key Takeaway
The doctrine of states is not vulnerable to infinite regress because the property of being a state is not itself a state, analogous to genus-ness not being a genus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own beliefs, do you ever speak in sweeping generalities—'I am a believer,' 'Islam is true'—without examining the particular states of your heart? What does this passage reveal about the necessity of distinguishing universal claims from particular realities in your own soul?
?
If the deniers of states cannot utter a word without implicitly using universality and particularity, can you? Consider the last time you defended a theological position—did you rely on general concepts that require something like 'states' to be coherent? How does that accountability press you to be more precise in your speech about Allah?
?
The passage argues that without states, similarity and difference collapse. Think of a person you consider a righteous believer. What specific state (attribute) makes you see them as similar to other righteous people? If that state were just a label, would your judgment hold? What does this tell you about the reality of virtue and vice in your own character?

The Objection of One Thing in Two Answered by Symmetry
Objection and initial refutation by symmetry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection against the unity of existence and the author's initial response that the same problem applies to intellectual considerations.

Core Idea
The problem of 'one in two' is symmetrical between existence and intellectual considerations.

The objector claims that if existence is one, then one thing would be in two things. The author responds by pointing out that the same issue arises in the objector's own framework of intellectual considerations (i'tibarat). This lesson examines this exchange and shows how the author grounds existence as a rational meaning encompassing substance and accident.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the objector's statement is followed immediately by the author's counter: 'So we say: What applies to you... applies to us.'
The author does not deny the seeming problem but shows it is mutual.
2
Understand the symmetry
The author says 'the aspect is like the state and the state is like the aspect.' This means that the objector's 'intellectual considerations' (aspects) are analogous to the author's 'states' (ahwal). The same logical issue appears in both frameworks.
3
See the grounding in rational meaning
The author asserts that existence encompasses substance and accident 'as a rational meaning' (ma'nan ma'qul), not just a word. This sets up the idea that existence is a universal concept, which is necessary for further argument.
This prepares the ground for the next lesson on universals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولكم الوجود لو كان واحدا متشابها في جميع الموجودات لزم حصول شيء في شيئين أو شيئين في شيء. فنقول : يلزمكم في الاعتبارات والوجوه العقلية ما لزمنا في الوجود والحال، فإن الوجه كالحال والحال كالوجه، ولا ينكر منكر أن الوجود يعم الجوهر والعرض لا لفظا مجردًا، بل معنى معقولا، ومَن قال : كل موجود إنما يمايز موجودًا آخر بوجوده لم يمكنه أن يجري حكم موجود في موجود آخر، حتى أن من أثبت الحدوث لجوهر معين لم يتيسر له إثبات الحدوث لجوهر آخر بذلك الدليل بعينه، بل لزمه على كل جوهر دليلاً خاصا، وعلى كل عرض دليلا خاصا، ولا يمكنه أن يقسم تقسيما في المعقولات أصلا ؛ لأن التقسيم إنما يتحقق بعد الاشتراك في شيء، والاشتراك إنما يتصور بعد الاختصاص بشيء. ومن قال : هذا جسم مؤلف فقد حكم على جسم معين بالتأليف، لم يلزمه جريان هذا الحكم في كل جسم ما لم يقل كل جسم مؤلف، ولو اقتصر على هذا أيضًا لم يفض إلى العلم بحدوث كل جسم ما لم يقل وكل مؤلف محدث حتى يحصل العلم بأن كل جسم محدث، فأخذ الجسمية عامة والتأليف عاما، واستدعا التأليف للحدوث عاما حتى لزمه الحكم بحدوث كل جسم مؤلف عاما، فمن قال : الأشياء تتمايز بذواتها المعينة كيف يمكنه أن يجري حكمًا في محكوم خاص، فألزمتمونا قلب الأجناس والزمناكم رفع الأجناس، ودعوتمونا إلى المحسوس، ودعوناكم إلى المعقول وأفحتمونا بإثبات واحد في اثنين واثنين في
And as for your saying: If existence were one and similar in all existents, it would necessitate that a thing be in two [things] or two [things] in one thing. So we say: What applies to you in intellectual considerations (al-i'tibarat) and rational aspects (al-wujuh al-'aqliyya) applies to us in existence and state, for the aspect is like the state and the state is like the aspect. And no denier denies that existence encompasses substance and accident not as a mere word, but as a rational meaning. And whoever says that every existent is only distinguished from another existent by its existence cannot apply the judgment of one existent to another existent, such that if someone establishes temporality (al-huduth) for a particular substance, he would not be able to establish temporality for another substance by that same proof; rather, he would need a specific proof for every substance and a specific proof for every accident. And he cannot make any division in the intelligibles at all, because division is only realized after sharing in something, and sharing is only conceivable after specification by something. And whoever says 'this body is composed' has judged a particular body as composed, but it does not necessitate that this judgment apply to every body unless he says 'every body is composed.' And if he restricts himself to this, it would not lead to knowledge of the temporality of every body unless he says 'and every composed thing is temporally originated' so that knowledge is obtained that every body is temporally originated. So he took body-ness (al-jismiyya) as universal and composition (al-ta'lif) as universal, and composition required temporality universally, so that the judgment of the temporality of every composed body became necessary universally. So whoever says that things are distinguished by their particular essences, how can he apply a judgment to a particular subject? So you required us to invert genera and we required you to abolish genera. You called us to the sensible and we called you to the intelligible, and you confounded us with the affirmation of one in two and two in one... [text breaks off]
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection against the doctrine of states. The objector argued that if existence is one and similar in all existents, then one thing would be in two things or two things would be in one thing. The author replies that the same problem applies to the objector's own intellectual considerations and rational aspects: the aspect is like the state. Then the author asserts that existence encompasses substance and accident as a rational meaning, not just a word. He then demonstrates the logical consequences of denying the universality of existence: if each existent were distinguished only by its existence, one could not apply a judgment from one existent to another. For example, establishing temporality for one substance would require a separate proof for each substance and each accident. Moreover, no division of intelligibles would be possible because division presupposes sharing in something, and sharing presupposes specification by something. The author gives an example: saying 'this body is composed' does not imply that every body is composed; to reach a universal judgment, one must assert 'every body is composed' and 'every composed thing is temporally originated'. This requires taking body-ness and composition as universal concepts. Therefore, if things were distinguished only by their particular essences, no universal judgment could be made. The author concludes by contrasting their positions: the objector required them to 'invert genera' (i.e., admit one thing in two), while they require the objector to 'abolish genera' (i.e., deny universals). The objector calls to the sensible, while they call to the intelligible. Finally, the objector confounds them with the affirmation of one in two and two in one, but the text breaks off.

Key Terms
الوجود (al-wujud)
الاعتبارات (al-i'tibarat)
الوجوه العقلية (al-wujuh al-'aqliyya)
الجوهر (al-jawhar)
العرض (al-'arad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does existence encompass?
A) Only substances, not accidents
B) Substance and accident as a mere word
C) Substance and accident as a rational meaning
D) Only accidents

Key Takeaway
The objection of 'one thing in two' is symmetrical: the objector's own intellectual considerations face the same issue, and existence is a rational meaning that applies universally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief, do you apply the same standard of consistency to your own framework as you do to others? The objection in this passage is turned back on the objector. In your own life, where might you be guilty of demanding an explanation from others that your own worldview cannot provide? Commit to one concrete change in how you evaluate arguments.
?
The author asserts that existence is a rational meaning that applies to all things. Do you live as if existence is a mere word or as a meaningful reality that connects you to every person and thing? How would your daily interactions change if you truly internalized that shared existence? What is one step you can take today to live this truth?
?
The symmetry argument shows that the same problem applies to both sides. Can you think of a disagreement you have with someone where you could use this method to reach common ground rather than escalating? What would it cost you to admit that your own position might face a similar difficulty? Will you take that risk?

The Necessity of Universals for Judgment and Division
Logical argument on universals and division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying the unity of existence makes universal judgments and logical divisions impossible, and how the author uses this to refute the objector.

Core Idea
Without shared existence, no universal judgment or division of intelligibles is possible.

The author now deepens his argument. If each existent were distinguished only by its own existence, then no judgment could be transferred from one to another, and no logical division (taqsim) could occur. He uses the example of proving the temporality of bodies to show that universals like 'body-ness' and 'composition' are necessary. This lesson unpacks that logical chain.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument about temporality
Read from 'Man qala...' to '...kullu jismin muhdath'. Notice how the author shows that to prove every body is temporally originated, you need universal statements: 'every body is composed' and 'every composed thing is temporally originated'.
This illustrates the need for shared concepts.
2
Understand the logic of division
The author explains that division (taqsim) requires sharing (ishtirak) in something, and sharing requires specification (ikhtisas) by something. This is a basic logical principle.
Without sharing, you cannot divide cats from dogs, for example.
3
See the conclusion and the final contrast
The author concludes that the objector's position inverts genera (by making one thing in two) while they require abolishing genera (denying universals). The objector calls to the sensible, but the author calls to the intelligible.
The passage breaks off but the main argument is complete.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Objection and the Ash'ari Response
Presenting the objection and the Ash'ari counter-argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari responds to the objection that a single existence would entail absurdities, by showing the same issue applies to the opponent's own concepts.

Core Idea
The objection against unity of existence boomerangs onto the opponent.

The objection seems powerful: if existence is one, then either the same thing is in two distinct things, or two things are in one. But the Ash'ari masterfully turns the table: the opponent's own 'rational aspects' are also universals, and the same problem would apply to them. Moreover, existence is not a mere word but a real meaning that applies to both substances and accidents.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the first sentence carefully: it states the problem that would arise if existence were one and the same in all existents.
The objector assumes that if existence is a single universal, it must be literally the same in every thing, leading to absurdities.
2
Analyze the Ash'ari response
Notice how the response uses the phrase 'considerations and rational aspects' to show that the opponent's own conceptual tools are also universal. The Ash'ari argues that no one denies that existence is a real meaning encompassing substance and accident.
The key move is to find a parallel: the objector's own ideas of 'aspects' are just as universal.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this response is effective: it undercuts the objection by showing that the critic would have to reject their own foundational concepts.
This sets up the next part about the necessity of universals for knowledge.

وأما قولكم الوجود لو كان واحدا متشابها في جميع الموجودات لزم حصول شيء في شيئين أو شيئين في شيء. فنقول: يلزمكم في الاعتبارات والوجوه العقلية ما لزمنا في الوجود والحال، فإن الوجه كالحال والحال كالوجه، ولا ينكر منكر أن الوجود يعم الجوهر والعرض لا لفظا مجردًا، بل معنى معقولاً. ومَن قال: كل موجود إنما يمايز موجودًا آخر بوجوده لم يمكنه أن يجري حكم موجود في موجود آخر، حتى أن من أثبت الحدوث لجوهر معين لم يتيسر له إثبات الحدوث لجوهر آخر بذلك الدليل بعينه، بل لزمه أن على كل جوهر دليلاً خاصاً، وعلى كل عرض دليلا خاصا، ولا يمكنه أن يقسم تقسيما في المعقولات أصلا؛ لأن التقسيم إنما يتحقق بعد الاشتراك في شيء، والاشتراك إنما يتصور بعد الاختصاص بشيء. ومن قال: هذا جسم مؤلف فقد حكم على جسم معين بالتأليف، لم يلزمه جريان هذا الحكم في كل جسم ما لم يقل كل جسم مؤلف، ولو اقتصر على هذا أيضًا لم يفض إلى العلم بحدوث كل جسم ما لم يقل وكل مؤلف محدث حتى يحصل العلم بأن كل جسم محدث، فأخذ الجسمية عامة والتأليف عاما، واستدعا التأليف للحدوث عاماً حتى لزمه الحكم بحدوث كل جسم مؤلف عاما، فمن قال: الأشياء تتمايز بذواتها المعينة كيف يمكنه أن يجري حكمًا في محكوم خاص، فألزمتمونا قلب الأجناس والزمناكم رفع الأجناس، ودعوتمونا إلى المحسوس، ودعوناكم إلى المعقول وأفحتمونا بإثبات واحد في اثنين واثنين في واحد، وأفحمناكم بنفي الواحد في الاثنين والدليل متعارض، والدست قايم فمن الحاكم؟
And as for your saying: If existence were one and similar in all existents, then it would necessitate the occurrence of a thing in two things or two things in one thing. We say: What you object to us regarding existence and state applies to you regarding the considerations and rational aspects, for the aspect is like the state and the state is like the aspect. And no one denies that existence encompasses substance and accident, not as a mere word, but as a rationally understood meaning. Whoever says that every existent is only differentiated from another existent by its existence cannot apply a judgment of one existent to another existent, such that if someone proves the temporal origination of a particular substance, he cannot manage to prove the temporal origination of another substance by that same proof; rather, he must have a particular proof for every substance and a particular proof for every accident. And he cannot make a classification in the intelligibles at all, because classification is only realized after commonality in something, and commonality is only conceived after particularity in something. And whoever says: 'This is a composite body' has judged a particular body to be composite; he is not compelled to apply this judgment to every body unless he says 'Every body is composite'. And if he limits himself to this, it does not lead to knowledge of the temporal origination of every body unless he says 'Everything composite is temporally originated' so that knowledge is obtained that every body is temporally originated. Thus he takes 'body-ness' as universal and 'composition' as universal, and he demands composition for temporal origination as universal until the judgment that every composite body is temporally originated becomes necessary for him. So whoever says that things are differentiated by their particular essences, how can he apply a judgment to a particular subject? So you have compelled us to invert genera and we have compelled you to elevate genera; you call us to the sensible and we call you to the intelligible; you have silenced us by affirming one in two and two in one, and we have silenced you by negating the one in the two, while the proof is contradictory and the rule is established, so who is the judge?
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection against the Ash'ari position that existence is a universal concept applying to all existents. The objector argues that if existence were one and the same in all things, then either the same thing would be in two distinct things, or two things would be in one thing, both absurd. The Ash'ari replies that the same problem applies to the opponent's own concepts of 'considerations' and 'rational aspects' (اعتبارات ووجوه عقلية), which are also universals. He then argues that existence is a real meaning, not merely a word, and that it applies to both substances and accidents. The core of the response is a defense of the necessity of universals for reasoning: without commonalities, no judgment can be extended from one particular to another, making knowledge impossible. For example, proving the temporal origination of one substance requires a separate proof for each, and classification (تقسيم) requires prior commonality. The author then uses the example of 'body' and 'composition' to show how universals are necessary for syllogistic reasoning to yield knowledge that all bodies are temporally originated. He accuses the opponent of forcing them into absurdities ('inverting genera') while the opponent ends up in worse absurdities ('raising genera'). The passage concludes by stating that the opponent's proof is contradictory, and the rational principle (الدست) stands, leaving the question of who is the judge unresolved.

Key Terms
وجود
اعتبار
وجه عقلي
حال
جوهر
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Ash'ari view of existence as one universal entails literal identity of existence in all things.
Correction: The Ash'ari does not claim that existence is numerically one; it is a universal concept that applies to all existents without implying that existence is a single physical thing. The objector's 'one in two' inference is based on a misunderstanding.

Checkpoint
What is the Ash'ari's initial response to the objection?
A) He denies that existence is one.
B) He shows that the same problem applies to the opponent's rational aspects.
C) He argues that existence is a mere word.
D) He affirms that absurdities are acceptable.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari response to the objection is to point out that the opponent's own conceptual framework relies on the same kind of universals they criticize.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you object to someone else's position, do you ever stop to check whether your own beliefs suffer from the same inconsistency? This lesson shows how an objection can boomerang back. In what area of your life are you currently holding a double standard that you need to address?
?
The Ash'ari says existence is a rationally understood meaning that encompasses everything. Does your belief in the oneness of God (tawḥīd) remain a mere intellectual concept, or does it shape how you actually see every person, object, and event as dependent on Him? What would change if you truly lived as if all existence is one in its dependence on God?
?
If you discovered that an argument you often use against others applies equally to your own worldview, would you have the courage to revise your stance? Or would you rationalize it away? This lesson demands intellectual honesty. Will you commit to examining your own arguments with the same rigor you apply to others?

The Necessity of Universals for Knowledge
Demonstrating the necessity of universals for reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why universals are necessary for making judgments and classifications in reasoning, and how the Ash'ari uses this to defend the concept of a single existence.

Core Idea
Without universals, no judgment can extend beyond a single instance, making knowledge impossible.

The Ash'ari now deepens the argument: if every existent is unique in its essence, then no universal judgment can be made. Classification requires commonality, and commonality requires a universal aspect. Through the example of bodies and composition, he shows that universals are indispensable for syllogistic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument against particularism
Start from 'Whoever says' and follow the reasoning: if each existent is differentiated only by its own existence, then you cannot apply a judgment from one to another. This leads to an infinite regress of proofs.
The Ash'ari is arguing against the position that everything is completely unique.
2
Understand the example of body and composition
Notice the step-by-step reasoning: to prove that all bodies are temporally originated, you need universal statements about body and composition. Without universals, you cannot make such inferences.
This illustrates the role of universals in syllogistic reasoning.
3
Reflect on the final accusation
The passage ends with a mutual criticism: the opponent 'inverts genera' while the Ash'ari 'raises genera'. The Ash'ari claims to be on the side of the intelligible, while the opponent is stuck with the sensible.
This summarizes the dialectical stalemate.

The Judge's Critique of the Rejecters of Universals
Al-Hakim criticizes the rejecters for two errors and uses animal instinct as evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Hakim argues against those who reduce universals to mere words, using the animal instinct example to show that universals are grounded in reality.

Core Idea
Universals are not just words; even animals recognize them by instinct.

In this passage, the judge (al-Hakim) delivers a sharp critique of those who deny the reality of universals, reducing them to mere linguistic conventions. He identifies two errors in their position and then offers a powerful argument: even animals, which lack language and reason, recognize universals such as edibility. This reveals that universals are grounded in reality, not mere words.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the judge's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the direct address to the rejecters.
2
Identify the two errors
The passage begins by stating the rejecters erred in two respects. Find the two errors and understand the judge's criticism of each.
The first error is reducing generality/particularity to words; the second is about differing essences.
3
Analyze the animal argument
The judge uses the example of animals recognizing edibility by instinct. Ask yourself: why does this example work? How does it prove universals are not just linguistic?
Animals lack language but still generalize from one herb to another.
4
Consider the implications
The judge concludes that the rejecters have cut themselves off from definition (ḥadd) and rational consideration (naẓar). Reflect on why these two are essential for knowledge.
This connects the critique to broader epistemological consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الحاكم المشرف على نهاية إقدام الفريقين أنتم معاشر النفاة أخطأتم من وجهين؛ من حيث رددتم العموم والخصوص إلى الألفاظ المجردة، وحكمتم بأن الموجودات المختلفة تختلف بذواتها ووجودها فكلامكم هذا ينقض بعضه بعضا، ويدفع آخره أوله، وهذا إنكار لأخص أوصاف العقل، أليس هذا الرد حكما عاما على كل عام وخاص بأنه راجع إلى اللفظ المجرد؟ أليست الألفاظ لو رفعت من البين لم ترتفع القضايا العقلية حتى البهائم التي لا نطق لها ولا عقل لم تعدم هذه الهداية، فإنها تعلم بالفطرة ما ينفعها من العشب فتأكل، ثم إذا رأت عشبا آخر يماثل ذلك الأول ما اعتراها ريب في أنه مأكول كالأول، فلولا أنها تخيلت من الثاني عين حكم الأول وهو كونه مأكولا، وإلا لما أكلت وتعرف جنسها به وتعرف ضدها فتهرب منه ولقد صدق المثبتون عليكم أنكم حسمتم على أنفسكم باب الحد وشموله للمحدودات، وباب النظر وتضمنه للعلم.
The judge overseeing the final limit of the zeal of the two groups said: 'You, community of rejecters, have erred in two respects: by reducing generality and particularity to mere words, and by judging that different existents differ by their essences and existences. So your speech contradicts itself, its latter part repels its former. This is a denial of the most specific attributes of reason. Is not this rejection a general judgment about every general and particular that it refers to mere words? If words were removed from the situation, would not rational propositions remain? Even animals, which have no speech or reason, are not deprived of this guidance; for they know by instinct what benefits them of the herbage and eat, then if they see another herb similar to that first one, no doubt befalls them that it is edible like the first. If they did not imagine in the second the exact same judgment of the first, namely its being edible, they would not eat. And they recognize their kind by it, and recognize their enemy, so they flee from it. And the affirmers have spoken truth against you: that you have cut off from yourselves the door of definition and its coverage of defined things, and the door of consideration and its inclusion of knowledge.'
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage presents the judgment of the 'hakim' (judge), who is presumably the author or an authority, criticizing the rejecters (النفاة) — likely those who deny universals, such as the Mu'tazila or certain philosophers. The judge identifies two errors: (1) reducing generality (العموم) and particularity (الخصوص) to mere words, and (2) judging that different existents differ by their essences and existences. He points out that their own claim is contradictory because their rejection of universals is itself a general judgment. He then argues that even animals, which lack language and reason, recognize universals such as edibility by instinct. When an animal sees a second herb similar to the first, it does not doubt that it is edible, demonstrating that it grasps the universal judgment of edibility. This proves that universals exist independently of language. The judge concludes by affirming that the 'affirmers' (المثبتون, i.e., the Ash'aris) are correct, and that the rejecters have closed off for themselves the doors of definition (الحد) and rational consideration (النظر), which are necessary for knowledge.

Key Terms
عموم
خصوص
لفظ مجرد
قضية عقلية
فطرة
حد
نظر
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Universals are merely linguistic conventions with no real existence.
Correction: The judge demonstrates that universals are recognized even by animals without language, proving they are real and grounded in the nature of things.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the behavior of animals demonstrate?
A) Animals have language and reason.
B) Animals recognize universals like edibility by instinct.
C) Animals do not have any form of reasoning.
D) Animals only rely on sense perception without any generalization.

Key Takeaway
The judge shows that universals are not mere words but are recognized by all creatures, even animals, through instinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a new situation, do you instinctively apply a universal principle (like justice or fairness) or treat it as completely unique? What does your answer reveal about your belief in universals?
?
The judge says even animals recognize the same ‘edibility’ across similar herbs. In your daily life, are you consistent in applying the same moral or rational judgments to similar cases, or do you make exceptions? How would you justify those exceptions?
?
You have just read that denying universals cuts off the door to definition and rational inquiry. Is there any area of your life where you have been reluctant to define or categorize something out of fear of oversimplification? How will you now approach that area with the confidence that universals are real?

The Mental Reality of Universal Concepts
Al-Shahrastani affirms universals as mental meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that universal concepts are real meanings that exist in the human mind, not mere words or external attributes.

Core Idea
Universals are mental realities, not words or things.

Al-Shahrastani begins his resolution by observing the common human experience of conceiving universal ideas without regard to specific language or particular objects. He argues that these concepts cannot be reduced to expressions or external entities, so they must be genuine meanings realized in the intellect. This passage lays the foundation for the mental reality of universals, a key concept in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the translation. Pay attention to how the author builds his argument from experience to conclusion.
Notice the phrase 'فالحق في المسألة إذًا' which signals the author's definitive position.
2
Identify the two refuted options
The author mentions that rational considerations either go back to expressions or external objects. Both have been rejected. Find where he says this and understand why.
The refutation of these two extremes is crucial for the positive conclusion.
3
Grasp the positive claim
The only remaining option is that universals are 'meanings existing and realized in the human mind'. Notice how the author emphasizes that they are perceived by the intellect and independent of language.
The phrase 'حتى لو طاحت العبارات أو تبدلت لم تبطل المعنى' underscores their independence from expressions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالحق في المسألة إذًا أن الإنسان يجد من نفسه تصور أشياء كلية عامة مطلقة دون ملاحظة جانب الألفاظ، ولا ملاحظة جانب الأعيان، ويجد من نفسه اعتبارات عقلية لشيء واحد، وهي إما أن ترجع إلى الألفاظ المحددة وقد أبطلناه، وإما أن ترجع إلى الأعيان الموجودة المشار إليها وقد زيفناه، فلم يبق إلا أن يقال : معان هي موجودة محققة في ذهن الإنسان، والعقل الإنساني هو المدرك لها، ومن حيث هي كلية عامة لا وجود لها في الأعيان فلا موجود مطلقا في الأعيان، ولا عرض مطلقا، ولا لون مطلقا، بل هي الأعيان ؛ بحيث يتصور العقل منها معنى كليا عاما فتصاغ له عبارة لتطابقه وتنص عليه، ويعتبر العقل منها معنى ووجها فتصاغ له عبارة حتى لو طاحت العبارات أو تبدلت لم تبطل المعنى المقدر في الذهن المتصور في العقل. فنفاة الأحوال أخطئوا من حيث ردها إلى العبارات المجردة، وأصابوا حيث قالوا ما ثبت وجوده معينًا لا عموم فيه ولا اعتبار ومثبتو الأحوال أخطئوا من حيث ردوها إلى صفات في الأعيان، وأصابوا من حيث قالوا : هي معان معقولة وراء العبارات، وكان من حقهم أن يقولوا: هي موجودة متصورة في الأذهان بدل قولهم : لا موجودة ولا معدومة، وهذه المعاني مما لا ينكرها عاقل من نفسه، غير أن بعضهم يعبر عنها بالتصور في الأذهان، وبعضهم يعبر عنها بالتقدير في العقل، وبعضهم يعبر عنها بالحقائق والمعاني التي هي مدلولات العبارات والألفاظ وبعضهم يعبر عنها بصفات الأجناس والأنواع والمعاني إذا لاحت للعقول واتضحت فليعبر المعبر عنها بما يتيسر له.
The truth in the issue then is that man finds in himself the conception of universal and absolute general things without observing the aspect of expressions, nor observing the aspect of external objects, and he finds in himself rational considerations for one thing, and they either go back to defined expressions—and we have refuted this—or go back to particular existing external objects—and we have shown this to be false. So nothing remains except to say: they are meanings that exist and are realized in the human mind, and the human intellect is the perceiver of them. Insofar as they are universal and general, they have no existence in external objects. So there is no absolute existent in external objects, no absolute accident, no absolute color. Rather, they are the external objects in such a way that the intellect conceives from them a universal, general meaning, so an expression is coined to correspond to it and designate it. And the intellect considers from them a meaning and an aspect, so an expression is coined for it. Even if the expressions disappear or change, the meaning estimated in the mind, conceived in the intellect, does not become void. Those who deny states (ahwal) erred when they reduced them to mere expressions, and they were correct when they said that what is established as being particular has no generality or consideration. And those who affirm states erred when they reduced them to attributes in external objects, and they were correct when they said: they are intelligible meanings beyond expressions. It was right for them to say: they are existent and conceived in minds, instead of their saying: they are neither existent nor non-existent. These meanings are such that no rational person denies them from himself. However, some express them as conception in minds, some express them as estimation in the intellect, some express them as the realities and meanings that are the denotations of expressions and words, and some express them as the attributes of genera and species and meanings when they become clear to the intellects. So let the expresser express them in whatever way is easy for him.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents the author's resolution of the debate over the reality of universal concepts (al-ahwal). It begins by stating that humans naturally conceive universal ideas without regard to language or particular objects. Then it refutes two extreme positions: those who reduce universals to mere verbal expressions and those who treat them as real attributes existing in external things. The correct view is that they are meanings (ma'ani) actually existing in the mind. The author then criticizes those who deny states (nufat al-ahwal) for reducing them to mere words, while acknowledging they are correct in noting that particular existents have no generality. Similarly, those who affirm states (muthbit al-ahwal) err by making them extra-mental attributes, but are correct that they are intelligibles beyond expressions. The ideal formulation is that they are 'existing and conceived in minds' rather than 'neither existent nor non-existent.' Finally, the author notes that different thinkers call these mental realities by various names (conceptions, estimations, denotations, etc.), and any acceptable term suffices.

Key Terms
ma'ani
'ibarat
'ayan
al-adhhan

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the author denies the reality of universals entirely or reduces them to mere words.
Correction: The author strongly affirms that universals are real meanings in the mind, not mere words or external attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the correct way to characterize universal concepts (ahwal)?
A) They are mere expressions or words.
B) They are attributes existing in external objects.
C) They are meanings existent and conceived in minds.
D) They are neither existent nor non-existent.

Key Takeaway
Universal concepts are real meanings in the mind, independent of language and external objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the thoughts that fill your mind right now—are you more aware of the words you use to express them or the meanings themselves? By al-Shahrastani's argument, those meanings are real in your mind. Are you living as though your mental concepts correspond to truth, or have you allowed expressions to distract you from the reality of what you hold in your intellect?
?
If universal concepts like 'justice' or 'mercy' are not just names but real meanings in your mind, how does that affect your responsibility to seek true understanding? Are you content to parrot words, or do you labor to ensure the meanings in your mind align with God's truth?
?
The author says even if all expressions vanished, the mental meaning would remain. What is one concept you hold that would survive the collapse of language? Are you investing in that meaning, or in its surface expression? Commit today to deepen one universal meaning that draws you closer to your Creator.

Correcting the Extremes: Deniers and Affirmers of States
Al-Shahrastani corrects both positions on ahwal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Shahrastani's evaluation of both extremes in the debate on ahwal and his recommended formulation that they are existent in minds.

Core Idea
Both extremes have partial truth but err in their reductions.

Having established that universal concepts are mental realities, al-Shahrastani now examines the two main schools of thought on al-ahwal. He points out where each went wrong and where they were correct, then proposes a precise formulation that avoids their errors. He also notes the variety of terms used for these mental meanings, emphasizing that the reality is universal and undeniable.

Guided Path
1
Read the evaluation of those who deny states
The author identifies what the nufat al-ahwal got wrong and what they got right. Note the distinction between their error and their insight.
Their error is reducing states to mere expressions; their accuracy is recognizing that particular objects have no generality.
2
Read the evaluation of those who affirm states
Similarly, the muthbit al-ahwal erred by treating states as extra-mental attributes, but were correct in seeing them as intelligible meanings beyond expressions.
The author suggests a better formulation: 'existent and conceived in minds' instead of 'neither existent nor non-existent'.
3
Understand the terminology and conclusion
The author notes that different thinkers use different terms for these mental realities, and concludes that any convenient expression is acceptable.
This shows that the debate is about reality, not just words.

?

The Three Considerations of Realities
The author enumerates the three considerations and their properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three aspects under which realities and meanings are considered: in themselves, in external objects, and in minds.

Core Idea
Realities have three faces: pure essence, external particularity, and mental generality.

The author introduces a foundational classification in kalam: the three considerations (i'tibarat) of realities. He explains that every reality can be viewed from three distinct perspectives, each revealing a different property. Understanding this framework is crucial for resolving many theological puzzles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins by stating the three considerations before explaining their properties.
2
Identify the three considerations
Find the three phrases that name the considerations: in their essences and themselves, in relation to external objects, and in relation to minds.
These three considerations correspond to dhat, 'ayan, and adhhan.
3
Examine the properties of each
Note what the author says about the second and third considerations: external objects cause determination and specialization, minds cause generality and inclusiveness. The first consideration is pure reality without generality or particularity.
4
Reflect on the importance
Consider why this classification is important. The author hints that it resolves difficulties, which will be explored in the next lesson.
This lesson sets the stage for the next on the non-existent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالحقائق والمعاني إذا ذات اعتبارات ثلاث ؛ اعتبارها في ذواتها وأنفسها، واعتبارها بالنسبة إلى الأعيان واعتبارها بالنسبة إلى الأذهان، وهي من حيث هي موجودة في الأعيان يعرض لها أن تتعين وتتخصص، وهي من حيث هي متصورة في الأذهان يعرض لها أن تعم وتشمل ، فهي باعتبار ذواتها في أنفسها حقائق محضة لا عموم فيها ولا خصوص، ومن عرف الاعتبارات الثلاث زال إشكاله في مسألة ، ويبين له الحق في مسألة المعدوم ، هل هو شيء أم لا ؟ والله الموفق.
So realities and meanings have three considerations: consideration of them in their essences and themselves, consideration of them in relation to external objects, and consideration of them in relation to minds. Insofar as they exist in external objects, they are subject to being determined and specialized. Insofar as they are conceived in minds, they are subject to being general and inclusive. So by virtue of their essences in themselves, they are pure realities with no generality or particularity. Whoever knows the three considerations will have his difficulty removed in the issue, and the truth in the issue of the non-existent—whether it is a thing or not—will become clear to him. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage classifies the three considerations (i'tibarat) under which realities and meanings can be examined: (1) in themselves (fi dhatiha wa anfusiha), (2) in relation to external objects (bi al-nisba ila al-a'yan), and (3) in relation to minds (bi al-nisba ila al-adhhan). The author explains that when considered as existing in external objects, they become particularized and specialized. When considered as conceived in minds, they become general and inclusive. In themselves, they are pure realities without generality or particularity. The author concludes that understanding these three aspects resolves theological difficulties, particularly the long-standing question of whether the non-existent (al-ma'dum) is or is not a thing (shay'). This framework provides a clear solution: the non-existent is not a thing in external objects but is conceivable in the mind. The passage ends with a pious invocation.

Key Terms
haqa'iq
ma'ani
i'tibarat
dhat
'ayan
adhhan

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the three considerations refer to three different realities or that all considerations apply equally to every aspect.
Correction: The three considerations are aspects of the same reality, not separate realities. Each reveals a different property depending on the perspective.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what property do realities have when considered in relation to external objects?
A) They are pure realities with no generality or particularity.
B) They become determined and specialized.
C) They become general and inclusive.
D) They cease to exist.

Key Takeaway
Realities can be viewed from three perspectives: in themselves (pure), in external objects (determined), and in minds (general).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of a concept like 'justice', do you treat it as if it exists perfectly as a general idea in your mind, or do you recognize that in external reality, justice is always particular and imperfect? How does this lesson challenge your usual way of thinking about abstract ideals? Commit to one concrete situation today where you will test your mental concept against external reality.
?
In what areas of your life do you confuse the mental image you have of a person (e.g., a family member, a leader) with who they actually are in their specific external existence? How might this confusion lead to unfair judgments or unmet expectations? Write down one relationship where you will consciously separate your mental concept from the person's reality, and act accordingly today.
?
You carry mental models of yourself—your strengths, weaknesses, and potential. How often do you mistake these models for your actual self in action? This lesson teaches that pure realities have no generality or particularity in themselves. How can you use this insight to stop over-identifying with your mental self-image and instead engage with your actual, particular actions? Choose one habitual thought about yourself that you will discard today, and replace it with attention to a specific external action.

Applying the Three Considerations: The Non-Existent
The author applies the three considerations to resolve the issue of the non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the three considerations resolve the classic kalam debate over whether the non-existent (ma'dum) is considered a thing.

Core Idea
The non-existent has mental reality but no external existence.

The author now applies the three considerations to a specific theological problem: Is the non-existent a thing? By distinguishing between mental and external existence, the framework provides a clear answer. This demonstrates the practical value of the classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation. Notice that it is a concluding remark that ties the three considerations to the issue of the non-existent.
2
Recall the three considerations
Remember the three aspects from the previous lesson: in themselves, in external objects, and in minds. Think about how each might apply to the concept of the non-existent.
The non-existent (ma'dum) is not a thing in external objects but is conceivable in the mind.
3
Understand the resolution
The author says the truth becomes clear. The three considerations reveal that the non-existent is not a thing in external reality (since it does not exist) but it is a thing in the mind (since it can be conceived). This resolves the debate.
The phrase 'هل هو شيء أم لا؟' refers to the debate over whether the non-existent is a thing at all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

So realities and meanings have three considerations: consideration of them in their essences and themselves, consideration of them in relation to external objects, and consideration of them in relation to minds. Insofar as they exist in external objects, they are subject to being determined and specialized. Insofar as they are conceived in minds, they are subject to being general and inclusive. So by virtue of their essences in themselves, they are pure realities with no generality or particularity. Whoever knows the three considerations will have his difficulty removed in the issue, and the truth in the issue of the non-existent—whether it is a thing or not—will become clear to him. And God is the granter of success.

Commentary

This framework provides a clear solution: the non-existent is not a thing in external objects but is conceivable in the mind. The passage ends with a pious invocation.

Key Terms
ma'dum
shay'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the non-existent is absolutely nothing with no reality at all.
Correction: The non-existent has no existence in external objects but is a known concept in the mind, so it is a thing in mental consideration.

Checkpoint
What does the author say will become clear to one who knows the three considerations?
A) The nature of God's attributes.
B) The truth about the non-existent and whether it is a thing.
C) The difference between substance and accident.
D) The meaning of prophethood.

Key Takeaway
Understanding the three considerations resolves the debate: the non-existent is a thing in the mind but not in external reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You were once non-existent, and now you exist by God's will. Are you truly living with the gratitude that this shift from non-being to being demands, or do you take your existence for granted?
?
Your past sins are non-existent in the external world now, but they remain as mental realities in your mind and in God's knowledge. How does this truth compel you to repent sincerely and change your present actions?
?
Your future death is currently a non-existent event that you conceive in your mind. Does your daily life reflect the certainty of that death, or do you push the thought away? What commitment will you make today because of this reality?

Proof from Contradiction: Non-Existent Is Not a Thing
Deniers argue from the principle of contradiction against thingness of the non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument from the principle of contradiction that the non-existent cannot be a thing, and how it refutes the Mu'tazilite position.

Core Idea
Non-existence cannot be a thing because that would violate the principle of non-contradiction.

This passage presents a key argument in kalam against the view that the non-existent is a thing (a position held by the Mu'tazila). The deniers ground their proof in the bedrock logical principle of non-contradiction. As you read, notice how they derive a syllogism from this principle to show that the non-existent cannot be established (thabit).

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow.
2
Identify the logical principle
Find where the text invokes the opposition of negation and affirmation. This is the principle of non-contradiction.
3
Understand the syllogism
The conclusion is 'every non-existent is not established.' Trace the two premises: 'every non-existent is negated' and 'every negated is not established.'
This directly refutes the idea that the non-existent is a thing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال نفاة الشيئية عن العدم: قد تقرر في أوائل العقول أن النفي والإثبات يتقابلان، والمنفي والمثبت يتقابلان تقابل التناقض، حتى إذا نفيت شيئا معينا في حال مخصوص بجهة مخصوصة لم يمكنك إثباته على تلك الحال وتلك الجهة المخصوصة، ومن أنكر هذه القضية فقد أنكر هذه الحقايق كلها، وإذا كان المنفي ثابتا على أصل من قال: إن المعدوم شيء فقد رفع هذه القضية أصلا، وكان كمن قال: لا معقول ولا معلوم إلا الثبوت فحسب، وذلك خروج عن القضايا الأولية، وتحديد ذلك أن كل معدوم منفي وكل منفي ليس بثابت، فكل معدوم ليس بثابت.
The deniers of thingness for the non-existent said: It has been established in the first principles of intellects that negation and affirmation are opposites, and the negated and the affirmed are opposites by the opposition of contradiction, so that if you negate a specific thing in a specific state with a specific respect, you cannot affirm it in that same state and that specific respect. Whoever denies this principle has denied all these realities. And if the negated is established (thabit) according to the principle of those who say that the non-existent is a thing, then he has completely removed this principle, and he is like one who says: there is nothing intelligible or known except affirmation (thubut) alone. And that is a departure from primary propositions. The specification of that is: every non-existent is negated, and every negated is not established (thabit), so every non-existent is not established.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage presents the argument of those who deny that the non-existent (المعدوم) is a thing. They base their proof on the fundamental logical principle of contradiction (تقابل التناقض). The principle states that negation and affirmation are mutually exclusive: if something is negated in a particular state and respect, it cannot be affirmed in that same state and respect. The deniers argue that if someone claims the non-existent is a thing (i.e., established/ثابت), then they are affirming what is negated, violating the principle. This would lead to the absurd consequence that only affirmation (ثبوت) exists, rendering all negation meaningless. They conclude with a syllogism: every non-existent is negated (by definition), every negated is not established, therefore every non-existent is not established. This directly refutes the Mu'tazilite position.

Key Terms
النفي
الإثبات
التقابل
التناقض
المعدوم
المنفي
الثبوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is just wordplay and doesn't engage with reality.
Correction: The argument rigorously applies the logical principle of non-contradiction, which is foundational to rational thought.

Checkpoint
What is the core logical principle used by deniers of thingness for the non-existent?
A) The principle of causality
B) The principle of non-contradiction
C) The principle of sufficient reason
D) The principle of identity

Key Takeaway
The deniers prove that the non-existent cannot be a thing by showing that it would violate the principle of non-contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an area in your life where you verbally negate something (e.g., 'I don't rely on my own strength') but your actions affirm it (e.g., you act anxiously as if outcomes depend on you). How does the principle that negation and affirmation cannot coexist in the same respect expose this contradiction? What concrete step will you take today to align your actions with your words?
?
You spend time worrying about future events that do not yet exist—non-existents. If the non-existent is not a thing, such worries are based on nothing real. How can this truth free you from anxiety this week? Identify one specific worry about a future possibility and replace it with a conscious tawakkul (reliance on Allah) in the next 24 hours.
?
The lesson warns that denying the principle of non-contradiction leads to a worldview where only affirmation is meaningful, erasing all negation. In your own thinking, do you ever reject a truth because it contradicts a comfortable falsehood? Commit to examining one belief today that might contradict a clear teaching of the Qur'an and Sunnah, and ask Allah for guidance to accept the truth even if it is hard.

Distinguishing Existence from Being Established: The Affirmers' Response
The affirmers distinguish existence from being established.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Mu'tazilite theologians distinguish between existence (wujud) and being established (thubut) to argue that the non-existent can be considered a 'thing' without contradiction.

Core Idea
Existence and thingness are not the same; the non-existent can be established without existing.

This passage presents the key argument of those who affirm that the non-existent is a thing (al-mu'tazila). They accept the basic rational principle of contradiction but argue that the deniers have conflated two distinct conceptual pairs. By distinguishing existence from being established, they carve out a logical space for the non-existent as an affirmable entity. Let us examine their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English carefully. Notice the claim about two oppositions.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the two pairs
The affirmers refer to two pairs: negation/affirmation and existence/non-existence. They claim existence and thubut are not synonyms.
This is the core of their distinction.
3
Understand the conclusion
The non-existent can be called a 'thing' because thingness pertains to thubut, not wujud.
Thus they avoid contradiction by assigning each rational division its proper domain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال من أثبت المعدوم شيئًا: كما تقرر في العقل تقابل النفي والإثبات، فقد تقرر أيضًا تقابل الوجود والعدم، فنحن وفزنا على كل تقسيم عقلي حظه، وقلنا: إن الوجود والثبوت لا يترادفان على معنى واحد والمعدوم والمنفي كذلك.
Those who affirmed that the non-existent is a thing said: Just as it has been established in the intellect that negation and affirmation are opposites, so too it has been established that existence and non-existence are opposites. We have given each rational division its due, and we said: existence (al-wujud) and being established (al-thubut) are not synonymous in meaning, and similarly the non-existent and the negated are not synonymous.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is the rejoinder of those (mainly Mu'tazilites) who affirm that the non-existent is a thing. They accept the principle of contradiction but argue that the deniers have conflated two distinct pairs: (1) negation/affirmation and (2) existence/non-existence. The affirmers claim that existence (الوجود) and being established (الثبوت) are not synonyms; 'being established' has a broader scope that includes both existents and non-existents (as things). Similarly, 'non-existent' (المعدوم) is not the same as 'negated' (المنفي); the non-existent can be affirmed as a 'thing' without being an existent. By making these distinctions, they avoid the contradiction: the non-existent is affirmable as a 'thing' (established in thingness) but negated as an existent. Thus each rational division is assigned its proper domain.

Key Terms

العدم
الثبوت
النفي
الإثبات
المعدوم
المنفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the affirmers reject the principle of contradiction.
Correction: They instead refine terminology to preserve logical consistency.

Checkpoint

A) By denying the principle of contradiction
B) By distinguishing between existence and being established
C) By claiming the non-existent is an existent
D) By rejecting all rational divisions

Key Takeaway
The affirmers distinguish existence from being established, allowing the non-existent to be a thing without existing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that the non-existent can be established as a thing in the mind without existing outwardly. Is there an idea, a memory, or a hope that you treat as real in your heart even though it does not exist yet in the world? Are you willing to examine it with the same clarity the affirmers demand—giving each rational division its due?
?
The passage argues that 'existence' and 'being established' are not synonyms. Consider your own concepts of Allah: do you sometimes confuse your mental image or idea of Him with His actual existence? How might distinguishing between the two protect you from reducing the Creator to a concept?
?
If the non-existent can be affirmed as a thing, then the Day of Judgment is real even now. Does that truth weigh on your choices today? Will you act as though the non-existent future is already established, or will you let its absence cloud your accountability?

Objection to the Affirmers' Distinction
Deniers argue for symmetry in conceptual broadening.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the deniers' argument for symmetry between the pairs 'existence/being established' and 'negated/non-existent'.

Core Idea
If being established is broader than existence, then negated should be broader than non-existent.

The deniers concede the affirmers' distinction between existence and being established, but then turn the logic around. They argue that consistency demands a similar broadening on the negative side. This passage records their attempt to push the affirmers to admit that the non-existent is ultimately a subset of what is negated, challenging the affirmers' original claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how the deniers begin by conceding a point before pressing their objection.
The passage is cut off; focus on the logic up to the break.
2
Identify the concession and the argument
The deniers first agree that the affirmers have distinguished between existence and being established, and that being established is broader. Then they ask why the same logic does not apply to 'negated' and 'non-existent'.
3
Reflect on the symmetry
Consider why the deniers demand symmetry. What would it mean for the affirmers if 'negated' is broader than 'non-existent'? How does this challenge the claim that the non-existent is affirmed as a thing?
This connects to the broader debate about the reality of non-existent things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت النفاة: الآن صرحتم بالحق حيث ميزتم بين قسم وقسم، فالثبوت عندكم أعم من الوجود، فإن الثبوت يشمل الموجود والمعدوم، فهلا قلتم في المقابل كذلك: وإن المنفي أعم من المعدوم حتى يكون صفة عمومة حالا أو وجها للمنفي
The deniers said: Now you have stated the truth when you distinguished between one division and another, for being established (al-thubut) according to you is more general than existence (al-wujud), since being established includes both the existent and the non-existent. So why do you not say similarly in the opposite: that the negated (al-manfi) is more general than the non-existent (al-ma'dum), so that it is a universal attribute...
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage records a further objection from the deniers against the affirmers' distinction. The deniers concede that the affirmers have drawn a valid distinction (existence vs. being established), with 'being established' being a broader category that encompasses both existents and non-existents. But they then apply the same logic to the opposite pair: if 'being established' is broader than 'existence', then by the same token, 'negated' (المنفي) should be broader than 'non-existent' (المعدوم). This would imply that the non-existent is a subset of what is negated, and that being negated is a universal property that can apply to things in some states or aspects (as the text says 'so that it is a universal attribute state or aspect'). The argument is cut off, but the deniers are pressing for symmetry: if the affirmers allow a broader concept for the positive side, consistency demands a broader concept for the negative side as well. This objection aims to force the affirmers to admit that the non-existent is ultimately negated under some aspect, undermining their claim that it is affirmed as a thing.

B) That being established is broader than existence
C) That the deniers were wrong
D) That existence is broader than being established

Key Takeaway
The deniers force the affirmers to consider symmetry in their conceptual distinctions, threatening the claim that the non-existent is an affirmed thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The deniers use the affirmers' own logic to challenge them. Are there beliefs you hold where you accept a distinction in one area but resist applying the same logic in another? What is that area, and what would consistency demand?
?
The tension here is between affirmation and negation: the non-existent is affirmed under one concept but negated under another. In your own life, do you hold onto a claim about yourself that is both true and false depending on the aspect? Can you name one such tension and commit to resolving it?

First Argument of the Affirmers: Negation and Affirmation
Muthbitun argue that if every non-existent is fixed, then every negation is fixed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the muthbitun's argument that the nufat's position on non-existence leads to a contradiction regarding negation and affirmation.

Core Idea
If every non-existent is fixed, then every negation is also fixed—destroying the distinction between negation and affirmation.

In this passage, the muthbitun (affirmers of fixed non-existents) press their opponents, the nufat, to be consistent. The nufat claim that non-existent things have a fixed reality, but they also distinguish between negated things and non-existent things. The muthbitun argue that this distinction collapses, forcing an absurd conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the logical structure.
The argument is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the key claim and its consequence
Notice that the muthbitun start from the nufat's claim 'every non-existent is a fixed thing' and deduce that 'every negated thing is a fixed thing'. Trace how they connect these.
The phrase 'وإن لم يثبتوا فرقاً بين المنفي والمعدوم' is crucial.
3
Understand the conclusion
The consequence is that the opposition between negation and affirmation is removed. Why is this problematic? Reflect on what it means for logical distinctions.
This is a reductio ad absurdum against the nufat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثابتا، كما كان صفة خصوص المعدوم حالا للمعدوم أو وجها ثابتا، فيتحقق أمر ثابت، ويرتفع المقابل العقلي، وإن لم يثبتوا فرقاً بين المنفي والمعدوم، ثم قالوا: كل معدوم شيء ثابت لزمهم أن يقولوا: كل منفي شيء ثابت، فرجع الإلزام عليهم رجوعاً ظاهرًا، وهو رفع التقابل بين النفي والإثبات.
It is fixed, just as the attribute of particularity of the non-existent is a state for the non-existent or a fixed aspect, so a fixed matter is realized and the rational opposition is lifted, even if they did not establish a difference between the negated and the non-existent. Then they said: every non-existent is a fixed thing, so it is necessary for them to say: every negated thing is a fixed thing, so the obligation returns to them manifestly, which is the removal of the opposition between negation and affirmation.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument of the muthbitun (those who affirm that non-existent things have a fixed reality) against the nufat (those who deny it). They assume the nufat have already conceded that every non-existent is a fixed thing. The muthbitun then point out that the nufat also admit that a negated thing (manfi) is not the same as a non-existent thing (ma'dum) in their view, but in fact they have not established a real difference. Therefore, if they hold that every non-existent is fixed, consistency demands that they also say that every negated thing is fixed. This would eliminate the fundamental opposition between negation and affirmation, which is absurd. Thus the nufat's position leads to a self-contradiction.

Key Terms

معدوم
منفي
نفي
إثبات
تقابل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The nufat think they can accept that non-existents are fixed without being forced to say that negated things are also fixed, because they distinguish between 'non-existent' and 'negated'.
Correction: The muthbitun argue that the nufat have not actually established a real difference, so consistency forces them to accept the consequence.

Checkpoint
What do the muthbitun argue the nufat must conclude from their claim that every non-existent is a fixed thing?
A) That every negated thing is also fixed.
B) That negation and affirmation are the same.
C) That non-existence has no reality.
D) That the nufat are correct.

Key Takeaway
The muthbitun show that the nufat's position leads to the absurdity that every negation is a fixed thing, eliminating the logical distinction between negation and affirmation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there beliefs you hold that, when followed to their logical conclusion, lead to an absurdity? What would it mean for your faith if you refused to follow the logical implications of your own statements?
?
How does your commitment to truth and logical consistency affect your daily speech? Do you ever affirm something that, if taken to its logical end, contradicts your core beliefs?
?
If you claim that something is true but deny its necessary implications, are you being honest with yourself? What would it take for you to examine your own beliefs for hidden contradictions?
