Second Argument of the Affirmers: Generality and Specificity
Muthbitun argue that nufat admit generality/specificity in non-existent, so it must be fixed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the muthbitun use the nufat's own classification of non-existents to argue that non-existence must be a fixed reality.

Core Idea
The nufat's own division of non-existence proves it is something real.

The muthbitun (affirmers of the reality of non-existence) now turn the nufat's own methodology against them. They point out that the nufat, when discussing non-existence, routinely divide it into categories like 'necessary', 'possible', and 'impossible'. This very act of categorization, the muthbitun argue, implies that non-existence has some sort of fixed reality—otherwise such divisions would be meaningless. Let's examine their reasoning closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the English translation. Notice the direct address to the nufat and the list of categories of non-existence.
The passage is the first part of a two-part argument; the second aspect will come in the next lesson.
2
Identify the four categories of non-existence
The nufat are quoted as saying that among non-existents, there is what is necessary (like the impossible), what is permissible (like the possible), what is impossible in itself, and what is impossible due to another. List them and understand each example.
These categories are drawn from rational judgment.
3
Understand the logical implication
Ask: Why does dividing non-existence into categories require it to be something fixed? The muthbitun argue that if non-existence were absolutely nothing, it could not have 'particularity' or 'generality'—it could not be distinguished into types.
4
Reflect on the force of the argument
Consider how this argument pressures the nufat: they claim non-existence is mere negation, yet they treat it as a subject of classification. This inconsistency is the core of the muthbitun's challenge.
This argument is an example of 'reductio ad absurdum' or turning the opponent's own statements against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المثبتون: إذا حققتم الكلام في النفي والإثبات والموجود والمعدوم، وميزتم بين الخصوص والعموم فيه عاد الإلزام عليكم متوجها من وجهين؛ أحدهما: أنكم أثبتم في المعدوم خصوصاً وعموما أيضا حتى قلتم: منه ما هو واجب كالمستحيل، ومنه ما هو جائز كالممكن، ومنه ما يستحيل لذاته كالجمع بين المتضادين، ومنه ما يستحيل لغيره كخلاف المعلوم، فهذه التقسيمات قد وردت على المعدوم، فأخذتم المعدومية عامة، وخصصتم بهذه الخصايص، فلولا أن المعدوم شيء ثابت وإلا لما تحقق فيه العموم والخصوص ولما تحقق التميز بين قسم وقسم.
The affirmers said: When you examine speech concerning negation and affirmation, and the existent and the non-existent, and you distinguish between particularity and generality in that, the obligation returns to you directed from two aspects: One of them is that you have established in the non-existent particularity and generality as well, until you said: Among it is what is necessary like the impossible, and among it is what is permissible like the possible, and among it is what is impossible in itself like the conjunction of opposites, and among it is what is impossible due to another like contradicting the known. These divisions have been applied to the non-existent, so you took non-existence as general and specified it with these characteristics. If the non-existent were not a fixed thing, then generality and particularity would not be realized in it, nor would differentiation between one division and another be realized.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage continues the muthbitun's argument, presenting the first of two points (the second is in the next lesson). They address the nufat directly: 'you have established in the non-existent particularity and generality' because you speak of different types of non-existents: necessary (like the impossible), possible, impossible in itself (like a conjunction of opposites), and impossible due to another (like contradicting divine knowledge). These classifications imply that non-existence has some sort of reality—otherwise you could not categorize it. If non-existence were completely unreal, it would have no divisions. Thus, the nufat's own practice of dividing the non-existent into categories proves that it must be something fixed.

Key Terms
خصوص
عموم
واجب
جائز
مستحيل
تقسيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the nufat completely deny any reality to non-existence, including the ability to categorize it.
Correction: The nufat do categorize non-existence (e.g., necessary, possible, impossible), which implies that in their own framework, non-existence has some sort of conceptual reality—otherwise these categories would be meaningless. This is precisely what the muthbitun use against them.

Checkpoint
What do the muthbitun claim the nufat have implicitly admitted by dividing non-existence into categories?
A) That non-existence is a fixed thing.
B) That non-existence is completely unreal.
C) That existence and non-existence are the same.
D) That categories are purely verbal.

Key Takeaway
The nufat's own classification of non-existence forces them to admit it is something fixed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself making fine distinctions about something you claim is 'nothing' or 'unimportant'? What does that inconsistency reveal about your actual belief? Take a moment today to honestly assess an area where your words and your actions are not aligned.
?
The passage shows that even the nufat could not avoid treating non-existence as a category with sub-types. When you think about impossibilities related to God's attributes (like the impossibility of contradicting His knowledge), do you recognize that these impossibilities have a certain fixedness? How does this affect your trust in divine perfection? Commit to reflecting on one attribute of God that you have not fully acknowledged as necessarily real in this way.

The Knowledge Argument for the Reality of Non-Existence
Muthbitun argue that knowing non-existent implies a fixed object of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the muthbitun argue that knowledge of the non-existent requires a fixed object, and what this implies for the nature of non-existence.

Core Idea
Knowledge cannot attach to nothing; the known must have some reality.

The muthbitun present a powerful argument: if you can know and talk about the non-existent, then that non-existent must be something fixed in itself. This lesson unpacks their reasoning from the admission that the negated and non-existent are known, to the conclusion that they possess a fixed reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how the muthbitun build on the nufat's own admission that the non-existent is known.
Focus on the question: 'What is the object of knowledge?'
2
Identify the two premises
Break down the argument: (1) The nufat admit the non-existent is known. (2) Knowledge must have an object. From this they conclude a fixed object is necessary.
Consider what 'attachment' (ta'alluq) means in the context of knowledge.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself: Does this argument prove that the non-existent exists externally? Or does it only establish some kind of mental or essential reality? Use the misconception note to clarify.
The muthbitun are arguing for a fixed object, not necessarily external existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثاني: أنكم اعترفتم بأن المنفي والمعدوم معلوم، وقد أخبرتم عنه، وتصرفتم بأفكاركم فيه، فما متعلق العلم؟ وما معنى التعلق إذا لم يكن شيئًا ثابتا أصلا؟
And the second aspect: that you have admitted that the negated and the non-existent are known, and you have informed about them, and you have exercised your thoughts concerning them. So what is the object of knowledge? And what is the meaning of 'attachment' if it is not a fixed thing at all?
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This is the second of the two points the muthbitun present against the nufat. They argue from the fact that the nufat admit that the negated and the non-existent are known (ma'lum), that people speak about them and think about them. For knowledge to have an object (muta'allaq), that object must be something real—otherwise the 'attachment' (ta'alluq) of knowledge would be meaningless. Since the nufat agree that the non-existent is known, they must concede that it has some fixed reality as the object of that knowledge.

Key Terms
علم
معلوم
تعلق
معدوم
منفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument proves that non-existent things exist in the external world.
Correction: The muthbitun only argue that non-existents have some fixed reality (thubut) as objects of knowledge, not external existence. This thubut could be mental or essential.

Checkpoint
What do the muthbitun claim is necessary for knowledge of the non-existent to be possible?
A) That the non-existent has no reality.
B) That the non-existent must be a fixed thing.
C) That the non-existent must exist externally.
D) That knowledge is purely subjective.

Key Takeaway
The muthbitun use the necessity of a fixed object for knowledge to argue that non-existence has a reality (thubut) grounding our knowledge of it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about something that does not exist, like a past sin you regret, do you consider that thought to have a reality? If so, what does that reality demand from you in terms of repentance or change?
?
If knowledge requires a fixed object, then what does your knowledge of Allah's attributes (like His mercy or justice) say about their reality? Are they merely mental concepts, or do they have a fixed reality that commands your worship and obedience?
?
The nufat admitted that non-existents are known. Is there any area of your life where you ignore the reality of something non-existent—like the consequences of a hidden sin—because you think it has no bearing? How does this lesson challenge that negligence?

How Knowledge of Non-Existence Requires Estimation
Deniers explain that non-existence has no real particularity or generality; knowledge depends on estimation of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the deniers (nufat) argue that knowledge of non-existence is only possible through an estimation of existence, and how this answers the objection that knowledge requires a fixed object.

Core Idea
Non-existence is known only through existence—actual or estimated.

In the previous lessons, the muthbitun argued that knowledge of non-existence requires a fixed object. Here, the deniers respond by clarifying that knowledge of the non-existent is indirect: it depends on an estimation of existence. This passage explains how we can speak of non-existence without positing any real object in the state of non-existence itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the technical terms like 'تقدير' (estimation).
2
Identify the two ways non-existence is known
The deniers present two modes: pointing to an actual existent or mentally estimating something. Find these in the passage.
The example of the Resurrection illustrates the second mode.
3
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that knowledge attaches to the existent, not to non-existence directly. Reflect on how this refutes the opposing view.
This is the key doctrinal point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت النفاة: نحن لا نثبت في العدم خصوصاً وعموما، بل الخصوص والعموم فيه راجع إلى اللفظ المجرد، وإلى التقدير في العقل، بل العلم لا يتعلق بالمعدوم من حيث هو معدوم إلا على تقدير الوجود، فالعدم المطلق يعلم ويعقل على تقدير الوجود المطلق في مقابلة العدم المخصوص؛ أعني عدم شيء بعينه، فأما أن يشار إلى موجود محقق فيقال: عدم هذا الشيء، وإما أن يقدر في العقل فيقال: ذلك المقدر، كالقيامة تقدر في العقل ثم تنفى في الحال أو تثبت في المآل. فالعدم إذا لا يخص ولا يعم ولا يعلم من غير وجود أو تقدير وجود، فكان العلم يتعلق بالموجود ثم من ضرورته أن يصير عدم ذلك الشيء معلوما فيخبر عنه بأنه منفي، ويعبر عنه أنه ليس بشيء في الحال وإن كان شيئًا في ثاني الحال.
The deniers said: We do not establish in non-existence particularity and generality; rather, particularity and generality in it refer to bare wording and to estimation in the mind. Rather, knowledge does not attach to the non-existent insofar as it is non-existent except on an estimation of existence. Absolute non-existence is known and understood on the estimation of absolute existence in contrast to particular non-existence—I mean the non-existence of a specific thing. Either it points to an existing thing actually existent, and one says 'the non-existence of this thing,' or it is estimated in the mind and one says 'that estimated thing,' like the Resurrection is estimated in the mind, then negated in the present or affirmed in the future. So non-existence therefore does not particularize, nor generalize, nor is it known without existence or an estimation of existence. Thus, knowledge attaches to the existent, then necessarily the non-existence of that thing becomes known, and one informs about it as negated, and one expresses that it is not a thing in the present, although it is a thing in the second state.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
In this passage, the nufat (deniers) respond to the muthbitun's arguments. They deny that non-existence itself has any real particularity or generality; those are merely a result of wording or mental estimation. Knowledge of the non-existent is only possible when we presuppose existence: either by pointing to an actual existent and saying 'the non-existence of this thing' (so the non-existence is known through that existent), or by mentally estimating something (like the resurrection) and then negating or affirming it at another time. Therefore, non-existence per se is not directly known—it is known derivatively through the existence that is negated. This answers the muthbitun's charge that knowledge requires a fixed object: the object is the existent thing, not the non-existence itself.

تقدير
وجود
لفظ

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-existence can be known directly without any reference to existence.
Correction: The deniers argue that knowledge of non-existence always depends on an estimation of existence, either actual or mental.

Checkpoint
According to the nufat, how is the non-existence of a specific thing known?
A) Directly as a fixed reality.
B) Through an estimation of existence, either actual or mental.
C) By pure intuition without any reference to existence.
D) Through revelation.

Key Takeaway
Non-existence is not directly knowable; it is known through the existence that is negated or estimated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your knowledge of your own non-existence before birth comes only through your current existence. How does that reality make you more urgently grateful for every moment you exist today, rather than taking existence for granted?
?
If you only know non-existence (like death) through estimation of existence, are you treating your own death as a mere mental exercise or as a certainty that must change how you live? What would it mean to live today as if your non-existence is already known?
?
The deniers say non-existence has no inherent particularity—it is just the absence of something. How does this thought expose your attachment to worldly things? Are you clinging to things whose non-existence is merely a negation of existence, not a reality in itself?

The Negators' Case Against Inherent Properties of Non-Existence
The negators argue that non-existence lacks inherent properties and knowledge of it depends on mental estimation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the negators' argument that non-existence has no inherent particularity or generality, and that knowledge of non-existence depends on mental estimation of existence.

Core Idea
Non-existence has no reality of its own; it only gains specificity from our minds and words.

This passage presents the argument of the negators (nufat) who deny that non-existence (al-‘adam) possesses any intrinsic attributes like particularity or generality. They contend that such distinctions arise only from verbal expression or mental estimation. Further, they argue that knowledge of a non-existent thing requires an estimation of its existence. To reinforce their position, they impose a dilemma on the affirmers (muthbitūn) regarding the status of the world's atoms, forcing them to either concede pre-eternity or accept that non-existence has reality—a conclusion the affirmers reject.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the negators use a very precise and technical style.
2
Identify the two parts of the argument
The passage has two main moves: first, the negators deny that non-existence has inherent particularity/generality; second, they impose a dilemma on the affirmers regarding the atoms of the world.
Look for the transition word 'على أنا نلزمكم' (moreover, we impose upon you).
3
Understand the denial of particularity/generality
The negators argue that non-existence (al-‘adam) does not itself have particularity (khusus) or generality (umum). These are introduced by verbal expression (lafz) or mental estimation (taqdir fi al-aql). Knowledge of a non-existent thing requires an estimation of existence.
For example, non-existence of a specific thing is known by referencing an existent and negating it, or by estimating a concept in the mind.
4
Analyze the dilemma on existence of atoms
The negators then ask: Is existence (wujud) affirmed or negated from the atoms (jawahir)? If affirmed, it implies the world is pre-eternal (qadim), negating creation. If negated, then according to the affirmers, every negated thing is non-existent, and every non-existent is something established (thabit), which would contradict their own denial of reality in non-existence.
This dilemma is meant to expose inconsistency in the affirmers' position.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this argument challenges the affirmers' view that non-existence has no reality. If the affirmers deny that non-existence is something, they must accept pre-eternity; if they affirm it, they break their own principle.
This sets up a key debate in Islamic theology about the nature of non-existence and the world's createdness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت النفاة: نحن لا نثبت في العدم خصوصاً وعموماً، بل الخصوص والعموم فيه راجع إلى اللفظ المجرد، وإلى التقدير في العقل، بل العلم لا يتعلق بالمعدوم من حيث هو معدوم إلا على تقدير الوجود، فالعدم المطلق يعلم ويعقل على تقدير الوجود المطلق في مقابلة العدم المخصوص؛ أعني عدم شيء بعينه، أن يشار إلى موجود محقق فيقال: عدم هذا الشيء، وإما أن يقدر في العقل فيقال: ذلك المقدر، كالقيامة تقدر في العقل ثم تنفى في الحال أو تثبت في المآل. فالعدم إذا لا يخص ولا يعم ولا يعلم من غير وجود أو تقدير وجود، فكان العلم يتعلق بالموجود ثم من ضرورته أن يصير عدم ذلك الشيء معلوماً فيخبر عنه بأنه منفي، ويعبر عنه أنه ليس بشيء في الحال وإن كان شيئاً في ثاني الحال، على أنا نلزمكم الوجود نفسه والصفات التابعة للوجود عندكم مثل: التحيز للجوهر وقبوله للعرض وقيامه بالذات، ومثل احتياج العرض إلى الجوهر وقيامه به، فنقول: الوجود منفي عن الجواهر أم ثابت؟ فإن كان ثابتاً فهو تصريح بقِدَم العالم، وتقرير أن لا إبداع ولا اختراع، ولا أثر لقادرية الباري سبحانه وتعالى أصلاً، وإن كان منفياً، فكل منفي عندكم معدوم، وكل معدوم ثابت، فعاد الإلزام عليكم جزعاً.
The negators said: We do not affirm in non-existence particularity and generality; rather particularity and generality in it refer back to bare verbal expression and to mental estimation. Rather, knowledge does not attach to the non-existent insofar as it is non-existent except upon an estimation of existence. Thus absolute non-existence is known and understood upon an estimation of absolute existence in contrast to specific non-existence – I mean the non-existence of a specific thing – either by pointing to an actual existent and saying 'the non-existence of this thing', or by estimating in the mind and saying 'that estimated thing', like the Resurrection is estimated in the mind and then negated in the present or affirmed in the future. So non-existence has neither particularity nor generality, and is not known without existence or estimation of existence. Thus knowledge attaches to the existent, then necessarily that thing's non-existence becomes known, so it is reported as negated and described as not a thing in the present even if it is a thing in the second state. Moreover, we impose upon you existence itself and the attributes dependent upon existence according to you, such as: occupying space (tahayyuz) for the substance (jawhar), its receptivity to accidents (ard), its subsisting by itself, and such as the accident's need for the substance and its subsistence therein. Then we say: Is existence negated from the atoms (jawahir) or affirmed? If it is affirmed, then that is an assertion of the world's pre-eternity (qidam) and a confirmation that there is no act of bringing into existence (ibda') nor creation (ikhitira'), and no effect of the power (qadiriyya) of the Creator at all. And if it is negated, then every negated thing according to you is non-existent, and every non-existent is established (thabit); so the implication returns upon you in distress.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage presents the argument of the negators (those who deny that non-existence has any inherent reality). They begin by stating their position: they do not affirm that non-existence in itself has any particularity (khusus) or generality (umum). Rather, these are introduced by verbal expression or mental estimation. Knowledge of a non-existent thing requires an estimation of its existence; for example, absolute non-existence is known only by opposing it to absolute existence, and specific non-existence is known either by reference to an actual existent that is absent or by a mental conception that is then negated or affirmed. They conclude that non-existence cannot be known apart from existence or its estimation. They then apply this to the question of the world's atoms: if existence is affirmed of them, it implies they are eternal (thus denying creation), and if it is negated, then according to the affirmers every negated thing is non-existent, and every non-existent is something established (thabit), which would contradict their own denial of reality in non-existence. Thus they charge the affirmers with inconsistency. The argument is meant to force the affirmers into either accepting pre-eternity of the world or accepting that non-existence has reality.

Correction: The negators only deny that non-existence has intrinsic attributes; they affirm that it is known through mental estimation of existence.

Checkpoint

B) Existence or mental estimation of existence
C) A divine attribute of knowledge
D) Nothing at all

Key Takeaway
Non-existence has no inherent properties; all attributions to it depend on mental estimation, and this leads to a dilemma about the existence of the world's atoms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah's power of creation, do you ever mentally reduce it to a mere concept, like the negators' 'estimation'? How does this lesson warn you against letting your mind's categories limit your belief in His absolute ability to bring things from nothing?
?
The negators' dilemma forces a stark choice: either the atoms are eternal or non-existence has reality. In your own life, do you sometimes treat the absence of something (like a blessing or a person) as if it has its own power or reality, rather than seeing it as entirely dependent on Allah's will? How can you correct this in your heart?
?
If non-existence has no intrinsic properties, then your own past and future are only real through Allah’s knowledge and power. Does this truth change how you view your sins (which are non-existent in the present) or your hopes? Will you today repent for something you did in the past with the awareness that only Allah’s knowledge and mercy give it weight?

Refutation of Non-Existents as Things
Author argues that infinite non-existents cannot be considered fixed things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari author refutes the Mu'tazili doctrine that non-existents are fixed things, and the absurd consequences that follow.

Core Idea
Treating non-existents as things leads to infinite absurdities.

The Mu'tazilis argued that non-existents have fixed essences and are 'things' known to God. The Ash'ari author here strongly refutes this, showing that it leads to an infinite regress of 'things' in non-existence, which is irrational. This passage is a key argument in the debate over the ontological status of non-existents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice the forceful rhetorical style.
The author uses logical consequences and reductio ad absurdum.
2
Identify the central argument
The author argues that if every non-existence (e.g., Zayd not being in a place) is a fixed thing, then there would be infinite fixed things in non-existence, which is absurd.
This is the core refutation.
3
Examine the objection and response
The author addresses the counterargument that attributes like occupying space apply only to existents. He shows that the Mu'tazili's own criterion—being knowable and reportable—makes impossibilities also 'things', leading to further absurdities.
This strengthens the refutation.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author ends with a plea for protection from such a doctrine, emphasizing the theological stakes.
Consider why this matters for Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عرف أنه ليس بموضع آخر سواها، ولا يستدعي ذلك أن يتعدد علمه بأنه ليس في دار عمر وبكر وخالد، بل يقع ذلك معلومًا لزوماً ضرورة، ومثل هذه المعلومات لا تتناهى ويستحيل أن يقال : هذه المعلومات أشياء ثابتة حتى يكون عدم زيد في مكان كذا، أو عدم كل جوهر في حيز كذا أو عدم كل عرض في محل كذا إلى ما لا يتناهى أشياء ثابتة في العدم، فإن ذلك خروج عن قضايا العقل وتيه في مفازة الجهل. وأما إلزام الوجود من حيث هو وجود، والتحيز وقبول العرض وجميع الصفات التابعة للحدوث فمتوجه والاعتذار بأنه منفي وليس بثابت ولا شيء نقض صريح لمذهبهم، فإن كل معلوم أمكن الإخبار عنه وتعلق العلم به فهو شيء ثابت عندهم ويا ليت شعري إذا لم يمكن الإخبار عن الوجود، ولم يمكن تعلق العلم به فعن أي شيء يخبر وعن أي شيء يعلم، وليس ذلك كالمحال الذي تمثلوا به على أن المحالات مما يعلم ويخبر عنها، فهلا كانت أشياء حتى يلزم أن يكون عدم الإلهية أشياء ما والعالم بما فيه من الجواهر والأعراض أشياء ثابتة في الأزل، وكما لا تتناهى المعلومات لا تتناهى الأشياء بأجناسها وأنواعها وأصنافها، والعياذ بالله من مذهب هذا مآله.
It is known that it is not in another place besides it, and that does not necessitate that His knowledge be multiple that He is not in the house of Amr, Bakr, and Khalid; rather that occurs as known necessarily. And such pieces of information are infinite, and it is impossible to say: these pieces of information are fixed things, such that the non-existence of Zayd in a certain place, or the non-existence of every substance in a certain location, or the non-existence of every accident in a certain substrate, ad infinitum, are fixed things in non-existence. For that is a departure from the dictates of reason and a wandering in the desert of ignorance. As for the implication of existence qua existence, and occupying space, and receptivity to accident, and all attributes that follow from temporality, it is valid. And the excuse that it is negated and not fixed and not a thing is an explicit refutation of their doctrine. For every known thing that can be reported about and to which knowledge attaches is a thing in their view. Would that I knew if reporting about existence is impossible, and knowledge of it is impossible, then about what thing does one report and about what thing does one know? And that is not like the impossible that they used as an example. On the contrary, impossibilities are known and reported about; so why are they not things, such that it would follow that the non-existence of divinity are things, and the world including its substances and accidents are things fixed in pre-eternity. And just as pieces of knowledge are infinite, so things are infinite in their genera, species, and varieties. And God forbid from a doctrine whose end is this.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazili doctrine that non-existents (ma'dūmāt) are 'things' (ashyā') with fixed essences in a state of non-existence. The author begins by noting that God's knowledge that something is not in a place does not imply multiple acts of knowledge; rather it is a single necessary concomitant knowledge. He then argues that if we take each instance of non-existence (e.g., Zayd not being here, every substance not in a particular location) as a fixed thing, then we would have infinite things in non-existence, which is an absurd conclusion and a departure from reason. He then addresses the objection that the attributes following from temporality (tahayyuz, qabūl al-‘araḍ) properly apply to existence, and the excuse that these are negated and not fixed is a clear refutation of their own position because if something can be known and reported about, it is a 'thing' in their view. He presses the point: if existence cannot be reported about or known, then what do they report and know? Impossibilities (muḥālāt) are known and reported, so why are they not things? This would force the absurdity that the non-existence of divinity and the world in pre-eternity are things, leading to infinite things. He concludes with a plea for divine protection from such a doctrine.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the author is denying that non-existents can be known.
Correction: Actually, he is refuting the idea that they are fixed things, not that they are unknown. He affirms that non-existents are known and reported about, but they are not 'things' with fixed essences.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does the Mu'tazili view lead to if non-existents are considered fixed things?
A) That God's knowledge becomes multiple.
B) That infinite things exist in non-existence, which is absurd.
C) That existence is created by God.
D) That attributes are eternal.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari author demonstrates that considering non-existents as fixed things leads to infinite absurdities, thus refuting the Mu'tazili doctrine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Dilemma of Specific vs Universal Substance in Pre-Existence
Shahrastani presents a logical dilemma against the fixed reality of non-existents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Shahrastani's logical dilemma refuting the Mu'tazilite claim that non-existents have fixed identities.

Core Idea
A non-existent cannot be both fixed and come to exist — it's a logical contradiction.

Shahrastani presents a powerful logical argument against the idea that non-existents have a fixed reality. He poses a dilemma: was a substance before existence already specific or universal? Both options lead to contradiction, showing that what is fixed in non-existence cannot become existent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the structure of the dilemma.
Notice how the author sets up two possibilities.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Find the two options: either the substance was already specific, or it was absolute. What does each lead to?
3
Understand the logical consequences
If specific, then non-existence would have an identity. If absolute, then it is not 'this,' so the existent is not the same as the non-existent.
Both lead to a contradiction.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The argument concludes that what is fixed in non-existence cannot realize existence, and what realizes existence was not fixed.
This refutes the fixed identity of non-existents.
5
Consider the common misunderstanding
This argument does not deny God's knowledge; it denies that non-existents have essences independent of creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : إذا أشار مشير إلى جوهر بعينه فنسألكم : هل كان هذا الجوهر قبل وجوده شيئاً ثابتاً جوهراً جسميًا من حيث هو هذا أم كان جوهرًا مطلقا شيئًا عام غير متخصص بهذا ؟ فإن قلتم : كان بعينه جوهراً فيجب أن تحقق الإشارة إليه بهذا، ويكون ذلك المشار إليه هو هذا؛ لأن هذا لا يشاركه فيه غير هذا، وإن كان قبل وجوده جوهراً مطلقاً لا هذا فلم يكن ذلك هذا، فلم يكن هذا شيئًا والمطلق من حيث هو مطلق لا هذا، ولم يكن هذا ذاك ولا ذاك هذا، فما هو ثابت في العدم لم يتحقق له وجود، وما تحقق له وجود لم يكن ثابتا.
We say: If a pointer points to a specific substance, we ask you: Was this substance before its existence a fixed thing, a corporeal substance inasmuch as it is 'this,' or was it an absolute substance, a general thing, not particularized by this? For if you say: It was that specific substance, then the pointing to it as 'this' must be verified, and that which is pointed to is 'this'; because 'this' is not shared by other than 'this.' And if before its existence it was an absolute substance, not 'this,' then that was not 'this,' so 'this' was not a thing, and the absolute inasmuch as it is absolute is not 'this,' and 'this' was not that, nor was that 'this.' So what is established in non-existence does not realize existence for itself, and what has realized existence for itself was not established.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage presents a logical argument against the claim that non-existents have a fixed identity (i.e., the Mu'tazila position). The author sets up a dilemma: when someone points to a specific substance, was that substance before its existence already a fixed, specific substance, or was it a universal, absolute substance? If it was specific, then the pointing to it as 'this' must be verifiable, but that would mean the non-existent already had an identity, which is problematic. If it was absolute, then it is not 'this,' so the substance that exists is not the same as what was in non-existence. Both options lead to contradiction: what is fixed in non-existence cannot come into existence, and what comes into existence was not fixed. This refutes the idea that non-existents have a definite reality.

Key Terms
جوهر
ثابت
مطلق
متخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument implies that God does not know non-existents because they have no reality.
Correction: No, the argument only denies that non-existents have a fixed essence independent of God; God knows them as future creations, but they are not fixed things.

Checkpoint
What does the argument conclude about a non-existent substance?
A) It has a fixed essence that becomes existent
B) If it is fixed in non-existence, it cannot realize existence
C) It is always absolute and universal
D) It is the same as the existent substance

Key Takeaway
A non-existent cannot have a fixed identity because if it did, it would be both fixed and not realized, which is contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you assume that your future plans have a fixed reality in God's knowledge, as if they already exist? This passage shows that thinking so leads to a logical contradiction. Will you instead trust that God creates each moment anew, without pre-existing essences?
?
Consider a desire or fear you have about something that does not yet exist. Do you treat it as if it already has a fixed identity? How does the conclusion 'what is established in non-existence does not realize existence' change your attitude toward the unknown future? Are you willing to let go of the illusion of control and rely on God's creative power alone?
?
If nothing that will exist has a fixed essence before God brings it into being, then your own identity is entirely dependent on God's act of creation. Does that thought humble you? How does it affect your gratitude for your existence right now, given that you were nothing fixed before?

Critique of the Proponents of States (Ahwal)
Critique of Ash'ari theory of states for contradictory treatment of genera.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific contradiction in the Ash'ari theory of states (ahwal) as critiqued in this passage, and how the author distinguishes between intellectual concepts and sensory perceptions.

Core Idea
Treating genera as real in non-existence but mere states in existence is a contradiction.

This passage takes aim at a key internal debate within Ash'ari theology: the theory of states (ahwal). The proponents of states held that attributes like substantiality exist as real 'things' in non-existence so that God's knowledge can attach to them. Yet in existence, they call these same attributes mere 'states' that are not independently known. The author finds this deeply contradictory and proposes a distinction between intellectual concepts and sensory realities.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the emotional tone: 'And the wonder, all wonder...' signals strong critique.
The passage is a single continuous argument, not a list.
2
Identify the key contradiction
The author says proponents treat genera (e.g., substantiality) as 'things fixed in non-existence' so knowledge can lean on them, but then in existence, they are 'states not known in themselves.' Pinpoint this inconsistency.
This is the heart of the critique.
3
Understand the author's proposed solution
The author argues that intellectual concepts (quiddities) do not require external existence; they are mental constructs with intrinsic constituents. He contrasts this with sensory perception, which does require existence. Note the distinction between 'causes of existence' and 'causes of quiddity.'
This solves the supposed need for states in non-existence.
4
Reflect on the misconception
A common mistake is thinking the author rejects the whole notion of states. In fact, he only rejects the specific contradiction about genera. Consider how this distinction preserves God's knowledge without positing real states in non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعجب كل العجب من مثبتي الأحوال أنهم جعلوا الأنواع مثل الجوهرية والجسمية والعرضية واللونية أشياء ثابتة في العدم؛ لأن العلم قد تعلق بها، والمعلوم يجب أن يكون شيئًا حتى يتوكأ عليه العلم، ثم هي بأعيانها - أعني: الجوهرية والعرضية واللونية والسوادية - أحوال في الوجود ليست معلومة على حيالها، ولا موجودة بانفرادها، فيا له من معلوم في العدم يتوكأ عليه العلم وغير معلوم في الوجود. ولو أنهم اهتدوا إلى مناهج العقول في تصورها الأشياء بأجناسها وأنواعها لعلموا أن تصورات العقول ماهيات الأشياء بأجناسها وأنواعها لا تستدعي كونها موجودة محققة، أو كونها أشياء ثابتة خارجة عن العقول، أو ما لها بحسب ذواتها وأجناسها وأنواعها في الذهن من المقومات الذاتية التي تتحقق ذواتها بها لا تتوقف على فعل الفاعل حتى يمكن أن تعرف هي والوجود لا يخطر بالبال، فإن أسباب الوجود غير وأسباب الماهية غير، ولعلموا أن إدراكات الحواس ذوات الأشياء بأعيانها وأعلامها تستدعي كونها موجودة محققة، وأشياء ثابتة خارجة
And the wonder, all wonder, at the proponents of states (ahwal) that they made the genera, such as substantiality, corporeality, accidentalness, and colorness, as things fixed in non-existence, because knowledge has attached to them, and the known must be a thing so that knowledge may lean upon it. Then these very same things—I mean substantiality, accidentalness, colorness, and blackness—are states in existence that are not known in themselves, nor do they exist independently. So what a [strange] known thing in non-existence upon which knowledge leans, and not known in existence! And if they had been guided to the methods of intellects in their conceptualization of things by their genera and species, they would know that the concepts of intellects—the quiddities of things by their genera and species—do not require them to be realized existent things, nor to be things fixed external to intellects, nor what belongs to them according to their essences and genera and species in the mind of the intrinsic constituents by which their essences are realized, which do not depend on the act of an agent so that they can be known together with existence without causing thought. For the causes of existence are different from the causes of quiddity. And they would know that the perceptions of the senses of particular things by their very selves and their signs require them to be realized existent things and fixed things external.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a direct critique of the proponents of states (ahwal) within the Ash'ari school, who held that attributes like substantiality and colorness exist as real 'states' in non-existence and are known by God. The author points out a contradiction: they treat these genera as fixed things in non-existence (so that God's knowledge can attach to them), but then in existence they call them 'states' that are not independently known or existent. The author argues that intellectual conceptualization of genera and species does not require them to be externally real; they are mental constructs with intrinsic constituents that do not depend on an agent's act. The causes of existence and the causes of quiddity (essence) are distinct. Finally, he distinguishes sensory perception of specific individuals, which does require external existence, from intellectual conception of genera.

Correction: The author only rejects the specific contradiction of treating genera as fixed in non-existence yet as mere states in existence. He does not deny that states can be conceptualized mentally; rather, he argues they need not be externally real.

D) They equate existence with quiddity.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari proponents of states contradict themselves by treating genera as real in non-existence but as mere states in existence; the author resolves this by distinguishing intellectual concepts from sensory objects.

?

What step will you take this week to move from mere conceptual knowledge to existential certainty?
?
If the causes of existence and the causes of quiddity are different, are you confusing knowing about something with truly experiencing it? Where in your life have you settled for 'knowing about' God instead of 'knowing' Him through worship and obedience?

Judgments Are Necessary Relative to Their Causes
Clarifies the necessity of judgments relative to their causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why judgments (أحكام) are considered necessary relative to their causes and how possibility applies only to the subject, not to the judgment itself.

Core Idea
Judgments are necessary by their causes; possibility belongs to the subject, not the judgment.

This passage begins by clarifying the application of the terms 'possibility' and 'necessity' to judgments. The author argues that judgments are more accurately described as necessary because they are bound to their causes. Through the example of knowledge and being knowledgeable, he illustrates why the judgment itself escapes possibility while the subject retains it. Let's examine this foundational distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author first says that necessity is closer to judgments than possibility.
The author uses the example of knowledge to illustrate.
2
Examine the example of knowledge
Understand why the world being knowledgeable is necessary after knowledge resides. The author says the judgment has no essence, so possibility does not apply.
3
Distinguish between the judgment and the subject
The subject (possessor of knowledge) does have possibility; it can be knowledgeable or not. This distinction is crucial.
This sets up the later discussion about possibility and agents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يطلق عليها الجواز والوجوب من حيث هي أحكام، بل الوجوب إليها أقرب فإنها بالنسبة إلى عللها واجبة سواء قدرت قديمة أو حادثة، فكون العالم عالما بعد قيام العلم بالمحل ليس بجائز، بل واجب ؛ لأنه من حيث هو حكم العلم لا ذات له فلا يتطرق إليه الجواز والإمكان ومن حيث هو ذو العلم يتطرق إلى ذي العلم الجواز ؛ إذ يجوز أن يكون ذا علم، ويجوز أن لا يكون. والجواز من هذا الوجه لا يعلل البتة، بل ينسب إلى الفاعل حتى يخصص بأحد طرفي الجواز، فهو إذا من حيث إنه جائز لا يعلل بعلة، ولو وجب تعليل كل جائز بعلة، وتلك العلة أيضا تعلل بعلة فيؤدي إلى ما لا نهاية له ولست أقول : لا ينسب إلى فاعل، وأن نسبة الجائز في أن يوجد إلى فاعل ليس كنسبة العالمية في أن توجب إلى علم، فليفرق الفارق بين تعليل الأحكام بالعلل وبين تعلق المقدور بالقدرة، فإن بينهما بونا عظيما وأمدًا بعيدا، فلا يجوز أن يقتبس حكم من الآخر، أولا أن يطرد حكم أحدهما في الثاني إلا أن يمنع مانع أصل التعليل ويرفع العلة والمعلول ولا نقول بهما في الشاهد والغائب، وذلك كلام آخر.
[The term] possibility and necessity are applied to them insofar as they are judgments, rather necessity is closer to them because they are necessary in relation to their causes, whether they are considered eternal or originated. So the world being knowing after the residing of knowledge in the subject is not possible, but necessary; because in terms of its being a judgment of knowledge it has no essence, so possibility and contingency do not enter into it. And in terms of its being the possessor of knowledge, possibility enters into the possessor of knowledge, for it is possible that it be knowledgeable and possible that it not be. And possibility from this aspect is not caused at all, but is attributed to the agent so that it is specified with one of the two sides of possibility. So it, insofar as it is possible, is not caused by a cause. And if it were necessary to cause every possible by a cause, and that cause also is caused by a cause, it would lead to what has no end. And I do not say: it is not attributed to an agent; indeed the attribution of the possible in its coming to be to an agent is not like the attribution of being-knowing in its necessitating to knowledge. So let the distinguisher distinguish between the attribution of judgments to causes and between the relation of the [thing] measured by power to power. For between them is a great distance and a far extent. So it is not permissible to borrow a judgment from the other, nor that the judgment of one be applied in the second unless a preventer prevents the principle of causation and removes the cause and the effect. And we do not affirm them in the witnessed and the unseen. And that is another discussion.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument about possibility and necessity in the context of judgments (أحكام) and their relation to causes. The author makes several key points in sequence. First, he states that terms like 'possibility' and 'necessity' are applied to judgments (such as 'being knowing') not in themselves, but because they are considered relative to their causes. In fact, necessity is closer to them because judgments are necessary given their causes, regardless of whether those causes are eternal or originated. For example, once knowledge resides in a subject, the subject's being knowledgeable is not merely possible but necessary – it cannot fail to have that qualification as long as the cause (knowledge) is present. Then he distinguishes between the judgment itself and the subject that possesses it: the judgment (being knowledgeable) as a consequence of knowledge has no independent essence, so possibility does not apply to it; but the subject (the person) does have possibility – he can be knowledgeable or not. Next, the author turns to possibility from the agent's perspective. He argues that possibility itself is not caused – it is simply attributed to the agent (فاعل) who chooses one of the two options. If we were to say every possible thing must have a cause, and that cause itself is also caused, we would get an infinite regress. He then clarifies: he is not denying that the possible is attributed to an agent; rather, the relationship between a possible thing and its agent is different from the relationship between being-knowing and knowledge. The latter is a necessary causal relationship (knowledge causes being-knowing), while the former is a free attribution by an agent. Therefore, one cannot analogize from one domain (causal relations among judgments) to the other (the relation of possible acts to divine power). They are fundamentally different, and no inference from one to the other is valid unless one denies causation altogether. The author concludes that this is a separate topic.

Key Terms
الحكم
العلة
الجواز
الوجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the world's being knowledgeable necessary after knowledge resides?
A) Because the judgment of knowledge has no essence, so possibility does not enter into it.
B) Because the world is necessarily knowledgeable by itself.
C) Because possibility is always attributed to an agent.
D) Because knowledge is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Judgments are necessary relative to their causes and do not admit possibility in themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What judgment about yourself do you treat as merely possible (e.g., 'I might be patient') when in reality it is a necessary consequence of a cause within your control? How will you take ownership of that cause starting today?
?
If every judgment of Allah is necessary relative to His knowledge and will, why do you sometimes doubt the certainty of His promise or threat? What cause in your heart needs strengthening to align your certainty with His decree?
?
You often say 'I could have done otherwise' as an excuse. But if the cause (your intention) existed, was your action truly possible? Will you now hold yourself accountable for the causes you set?

Possibility Is Not Caused: The Role of the Agent
Argues that possibility is not caused and distinguishes agentive attribution from causal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why possibility as such is not caused and how attributing it to an agent avoids infinite regress, and you will distinguish between causal necessity and agentive choice.

Core Idea
Possibility itself is not caused; only its actualization by an agent avoids infinite regress.

The author now turns to possibility from the perspective of the agent. He argues that possibility in itself does not require a cause; requiring a cause for every possible leads to an infinite regress. Instead, possibility is attributed to an agent who freely chooses one side. This contrasts sharply with the necessary causal relationships among judgments and their causes. Let's unpack this argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim that possibility is not caused
Read the passage where the author says 'الجواز من هذا الوجه لا يعلل البتة' (possibility from this aspect is not caused at all).
This is a key point distinguishing possibility from judgments.
2
Follow the infinite regress argument
If every possible required a cause, and that cause also needed a cause, we would have an endless chain. This is impossible.
The author uses this to show that possibility itself cannot be caused.
3
Compare two types of attribution
The author contrasts the attribution of a possible thing to an agent (free choice) with the attribution of being-knowing to knowledge (necessity). They are fundamentally different.
This prevents false analogies between causal necessity and divine power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

?
If God’s power is not like a natural cause that compels its effect, then your prayers are not a mechanism that forces a response. They are an appeal to a free Agent who can choose otherwise. Does that make you more earnest in supplication, or more hesitant? What does your prayer life reveal about your true belief in God’s freedom and your own need?

Rejection of the Negativity of Necessity and Defense of Existence's Equality
Author rebuts philosophers on terminology, defends equal generality of existence, and critiques negative necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that necessity (wujub) is a positive, essential attribute emphasizing existence, and why calling it negative is contradictory.

Core Idea
Necessity is not a negation; it emphasizes existence itself.

The philosophers claim that necessity (wujub) is a negative concept. The author finds this absurd. He argues that necessity is an emphasis on existence—it cannot be understood apart from existence. In this passage, he exposes the logical flaw in reducing necessity to negation and contingency to affirmation, showing that such terminology leads to confusion. Let's read the author's rebuttal and follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Notice the author's accusatory tone and rhetorical questions.
The passage is a single, sustained argument.
2
Identify the author's accusation
The author begins by accusing the philosophers of inventing terminologies when existence constrained them. Understand what 'generality with equality' means for existence.
He defends that existence applies equally to the Necessary and contingent in terms of mental concept.
3
Grasp the argument on 'more prior'
The author explains that calling the Necessary 'more prior' is just a definition, not a property of existence itself. Even if we replace 'wujub' with that definition, the division of existence remains correct.
This undermines the philosophers' claim that existence is not univocal.
4
Understand the dependence of wujub on existence
The author states that wujub cannot be conceived without existence; it is an essential meaning. Therefore existence is essential to the Necessary.
This shows that necessity is not a negation but an emphasis on existence.
5
Analyze the rhetorical critique
The author poses several rhetorical questions to show the absurdity of calling wujub negative. Reflect on why he finds it ridiculous.
He concludes that such reasoning is confusion and whispers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل لهم : أنتم وضعتم هذه الاصطلاحات حيث ضاق بكم التزام الوجود وشموله. فنقول : العموم إذا حصل معنى مفهوم من لفظ متصور في ذهن كان شموله بالسوية لست أقول : شموله بالنسبة إلى سائر الموجودات، بل أقول : شموله بالنسبة إلى قسمية الأخصين به وهو الوجوب والجواز والقول بأنه في الواجب أولى وأول تفسير لمعنى الواجب ؛ أي هو ما يكون الوجود له أولى وأول حتى لو تركنا لفظ الواجب جانبًا، وقلنا : الوجود ينقسم إلى ما يكون الوجود له أولى وأول، وإلى ما يكون الوجود له لا أولى ولا أول كان التقسيم صحيحًا مفيدا لفائدة الأولى، ثم الوجوب لا يفهم إلا وأن يفهم الوجود أولا حتى لو رفع الوجود في الوهم ارتفع الوجوب بارتفاعه، وهو معنى ذاتي، فالوجود ذاتي للواجب بهذا المعنى وبمعنى أنه أولى به ، وأنه لذاته، وبذاته، وأنه لغيره على خلاف ذلك. ومن العجب أنهم قالوا : الوجوب معنى سلبي حتى لزمهم أن يقولوا: الجواز الذي في مقابلته معنى إيجابي وليت شعري كيف يستجيز العاقل من عقله أن يرد معنى الوجوب إلى السلب والجواز إلى الإيجاب؟ أليس الوجود أولى بالواجب، فكيف صار أولى بالجائز؟ أليس الوجوب تأكيدًا للوجود فكيف تأكد الوجوب بالسلب؟ وهل هذا كله إلا تحير العقل ودوار في الرأس وتطرق الوسواس إلى صدور الناس؟
It was said to them: You have invented these terminologies when the comprehension and inclusiveness of existence constrained you. So we say: Generality, when it obtains a conceptual meaning from a word imagined in the mind, its inclusiveness is with equality – I do not mean its inclusiveness with respect to all existents, but I mean its inclusiveness with respect to the two divisions of the two more specific to it, which are wujub (necessity) and jawaz (contingency). And the statement that in the Necessary it is more prior and prior is an explanation of the meaning of the Necessary; that is, it is that which existence is for it more prior and prior. So if we disregard the word 'the Necessary' and say: Existence is divided into that for which existence is more prior and prior, and into that for which existence is neither prior nor prior, the division would be correct and beneficial for the first benefit. Then wujub is not understood unless existence is understood first, so that if existence is removed in the imagination, wujub is removed by its removal. And it is an essential meaning. So existence is essential to the Necessary in this sense and in the sense that it is more prior to it, and that it is by itself and in itself, and that it is for another contrary to that. And it is a wonder that they said: Wujub is a negative meaning, such that they are obliged to say that jawaz, which is opposite it, is a positive meaning. Would that I knew how a rational person can permit his reason to reduce the meaning of wujub to negation and jawaz to affirmation? Is not existence more prior to the Necessary? So how did it become more prior to the contingent? Is not wujub an emphasis of existence? So how is wujub emphasized by negation? Is all of this nothing but confusion of the mind, dizziness of the head, and the penetration of whispers into the hearts of people?
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is the author's rebuttal to the philosophers' earlier argument. The author accuses the philosophers of inventing terminologies when the reality of existence constrained them. He then presents his own position: generality in the mind applies equally to all specific terms, meaning that existence covers necessity and contingency equally in terms of mental concept. He argues that the notion of 'more prior and prior' is simply an explanation of what 'Necessary' means, not a property of existence itself. If we define the Necessary as that for which existence is more prior, the division is still based on existence equally understood. Moreover, necessity (wujub) cannot be conceived without existence; it is an essential meaning that depends on existence. Therefore existence is essential to the Necessary. The author then criticizes the philosophers for claiming that wujub is a negative meaning while contingency (jawaz) is positive. He finds this absurd: if existence is more prior to the Necessary, how can necessity be negative? He argues that wujub is actually an emphasis of existence, so calling it negative is contradictory. He concludes that such reasoning reflects confusion and intellectual dizziness, dismissing it as whispers (waswasa) entering people's hearts.

Key Terms
عموم
وجود
وجوب
جواز
ذاتي
معنى سلبي
تأكيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessity (wujub) is a negative or privative concept.
Correction: The author argues that wujub is an essential positive meaning that emphasizes existence; it cannot be understood without existence and is not a negation.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the claim that wujub is a negative meaning absurd?
A) Because wujub is the same as existence.
B) Because existence is more prior to the Necessary, so wujub must be positive.
C) Because the philosophers admitted it.
D) Because wujub is an accident.

Key Takeaway
The author demonstrates that necessity (wujub) is a positive, essential attribute that emphasizes existence, not a negation—any claim otherwise leads to logical confusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever treated God's attributes—like His power or life—as mere negations (e.g., 'He is not weak') rather than as positive, overwhelming realities? What would change in your worship if you focused on the affirmative aspect?
?
The author says existence is 'more prior' to the Necessary. Do you live as though your own existence is a gift from a Necessarily Existent One, or do you take it for granted? How can you cultivate that awareness today?
?
If necessity is an 'emphasis of existence', are you living a life that emphasizes the reality of your Creator? In what specific area are you acting as if His existence is secondary or merely conceptual?

Existence is Essential to the Necessary Being
Refutation of an objection concerning existence and necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how existence is essential to the necessary being and why necessity cannot be understood apart from existence.

Core Idea
Existence comes first; necessity depends on it.

This passage addresses a common objection: that the theologian's terminology of existence and necessity is arbitrary. The author clarifies that existence's generality applies specifically to the divisions of necessity and possibility, and that necessity inherently presupposes existence. This shows that existence is essential to the necessary being—it belongs to it by itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the objection that the author's terminology is forced, and then the author's initial reply.
The objection is presented as 'qīla lahum' (it was said to them).
2
Understand the clarification of generality
The author specifies that the generality of existence is not over all existents but over the two more specific meanings: necessity and possibility.
This is a key move: existence's extension is equal across these categories.
3
See how necessity is defined by existence
The author argues that saying 'existence is more appropriate to the necessary being' is actually a definition of necessity itself. Even if we drop the word 'necessary', the division still works.
This shows that necessity is not an independent concept.
4
Grasp the priority of existence over necessity
Necessity cannot be conceived without existence; if existence is removed in the mind, necessity disappears. Hence existence is essential (dhati) to the necessary being.
This conclusion reinforces the ontological dependence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل لهم : أنتم وضعتم هذه الاصطلاحات حيث ضاق بكم التزام الوجود وشموله. فنقول : العموم إذا حصل معنى مفهوم من لفظ متصور في ذهن كان شموله بالسوية لست أقول : شموله بالنسبة إلى سائر الموجودات، بل أقول : شموله بالنسبة إلى قسمية الأخصين به وهو الوجوب والجواز والقول بأنه في الواجب أولى وأول تفسير لمعنى الواجب ؛ أي هو ما يكون الوجود له أولى وأول حتى لو تركنا لفظ الواجب جانبًا، وقلنا : الوجود ينقسم إلى ما يكون الوجود له أولى وأول، وإلى ما يكون الوجود له لا أولى ولا أول كان التقسيم صحيحًا مفيدا لفائدة الأولى، ثم الوجوب لا يفهم إلا وأن يفهم الوجود أولا حتى لو رفع الوجود في الوهم ارتفع الوجوب بارتفاعه، وهو معنى ذاتي، فالوجود ذاتي للواجب بهذا المعنى ويمعنى أنه أولى به ، وأنه لذاته ،وبذاته، وأنه لغيره على خلاف ذلك.
It was said to them: You have placed these terminologies where the commitment of existence and its comprehensiveness constrained you. So we say: Generality, when it obtains a meaning understood from a word conceived in the mind, its comprehensiveness is equal—I do not mean its comprehensiveness with respect to all existents, but rather I mean its comprehensiveness with respect to the division of the two more specific [meanings] to it, which are necessity and possibility. And the statement that it is in the Necessary Being more appropriate and prior is an interpretation of the meaning of the necessary: that is, it is that for which existence is more appropriate and prior, so that even if we set aside the word 'necessary' and say: existence is divided into that for which existence is more appropriate and prior, and into that for which existence is neither more appropriate nor prior, the division would be correct and beneficial with the benefit of the [notion of] priority. Then necessity is not understood except that existence is understood first, so that even if existence were removed in supposition, necessity would be removed by its removal, and it is an essential meaning. So existence is essential to the necessary being in this sense and in the sense that it is more appropriate to it, and that it is for itself and by itself, and that it is for other than it in contrary to that.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objectors accuse the author of arbitrarily setting up terminologies (such as existence and necessity) out of a limited perspective. The author responds by clarifying the nature of generality: when a meaning is understood from a word, its extension is equal across all instances. He specifies that he does not mean extension with respect to all existents, but extension with respect to the two more specific meanings that fall under existence: necessity and possibility. He then explains that saying 'existence is more appropriate to the necessary being' is actually a definition of necessity itself: the necessary is that for which existence is more appropriate and prior. Even if we dropped the term 'necessary' and simply described the two divisions, the division would still be valid. Moreover, necessity cannot be conceived without first conceiving existence; if existence were removed in the mind, necessity would vanish. This shows that existence is essential (dhati) to the necessary being: it belongs to it by itself and for itself, whereas for contingent beings existence is received from another.

Key Terms
وجود (wujud)
وجوب (wujub)
جواز (jawaz)
عموم (umum)
ذاتي (dhati)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessity and possibility are independent categories that do not presuppose existence.
Correction: The author shows that necessity cannot be conceived without first conceiving existence; necessity is inherently tied to existence.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is existence essential (dhati) to the necessary being?
A) Because the necessary being does not exist
B) Because necessity cannot be conceived without existence, and existence belongs to it by itself
C) Because existence is more general than the necessary being
D) Because the necessary being is possible

Key Takeaway
Existence is essential to the necessary being; necessity depends on existence and cannot be understood without it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if your existence depends entirely on the Necessary Being? Or do you act as if you own your own existence? Reflect on a moment today when you felt self-sufficient and pride yourself.
?
If necessity is inconceivable without existence, then your very existence is a sign that God's existence is necessary. How often do you pause to let your own being remind you of His necessity? What one practice can you start to turn your thoughts from your own existence to His?
?
The passage says that for the Necessary Being, existence is essential and for others it is received. Have you ever thanked God for the gift of existence itself, not just the gifts within it? Write down a prayer of thanks for your very being.

Necessity is Not a Negative Attribute
Critique of those who claim necessity is a negative attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why necessity (wujub) cannot be considered a negative attribute and why it must be seen as a positive affirmation of existence.

Core Idea
Necessity is a positive affirmation of existence, not a negation.

In this passage from Sanusi's Nihayat al-Aqdam, he delivers a sharp critique of a logical inconsistency: the claim that necessity is a negative attribute while possibility is positive. As you read, notice how Sanusi uses rhetorical questions to expose the absurdity of this position. We'll unpack why necessity must be understood as a positive attribute that affirms existence, not negates it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's rhetorical style come through before you interpret.
Notice the strong language: 'astonishing', 'would that I knew', 'confusion of the mind'.
2
Identify the claim being critiqued
The passage targets those who define necessity (wujub) as a negative meaning (salbi) and possibility (jawaz) as positive (ijabi). Identify this claim and the logical consequence Sanusi draws from it.
3
Understand the counter-argument
Sanusi argues that necessity is more appropriate to existence and is an affirmation of existence. Ask yourself: How can necessity, which emphasizes existence, be described by negation? The author concludes such a view is pure confusion.
This connects to the learning objective: necessity is positive, not negative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن العجب أنهم قالوا : الوجوب معنى سلبي حتى لزمهم أن يقولوا: الجواز الذي في مقابلته معنى إيجابي وليت شعري كيف يستجيز العاقل من عقله أن يرد معنى الوجوب إلى السلب والجواز إلى الإيجاب؟ أليس الوجود أولى بالواجب، فكيف صار أولى بالجائز؟ أليس الوجوب تأكيدًا للوجود فكيف تأكد الوجوب بالسلب؟ وهل هذا كله إلا تحير العقل ودوار في الرأس وتطرق الوسواس إلى صدور الناس؟
And it is astonishing that they said: Necessity is a negative meaning, so that they were obliged to say: Possibility, which is opposite to it, is a positive meaning. Would that I knew how a rational person can permit his intellect to reduce the meaning of necessity to negation and possibility to affirmation? Is not existence more appropriate to the necessary? So how did it become more appropriate to the possible? Is not necessity an affirmation of existence? So how can necessity be confirmed by negation? Is all this anything but confusion of the mind, dizziness in the head, and the penetration of whispers into the hearts of people?
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a sharp critique directed against those who define necessity (wujub) as a negative attribute and possibility (jawaz) as a positive one. The author finds this contradictory: if anything, necessity adds an affirmation of existence, while possibility is the lack of necessity. He asks rhetorically: existence is more appropriate (awla) to the necessary being—so how can they claim that this superiority is expressed through negation? How can something that is an emphasis (ta'kid) of existence be explained by its absence? The author concludes that such a view is nothing but intellectual confusion and a sign of disturbed thinking.

Key Terms
وجوب (wujub)
جواز (jawaz)
سلبي (salbi)
إيجابي (ijabi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that because necessity involves a mode of being that cannot be otherwise, it should be defined negatively (e.g., 'not possible not to exist').
Correction: The author insists that necessity is inherently positive because it affirms existence, not negates it.

Checkpoint
Why does the author find it 'astonishing' that some define necessity as negative and possibility as positive?
A) Because necessity is more appropriate to the necessary being
B) Because necessity is an affirmation of existence, so it cannot be negative
C) Because possibility is also negative
D) Because existence is not relevant

Key Takeaway
Necessity is a positive attribute because it affirms existence, not negates it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author finds it astonishing that someone would call necessity negative. Have you ever, even unwittingly, treated your belief in God as a mere rejection of false ideas, rather than a positive commitment to the truth of His existence? Reflect on your own mindset: when you say 'La ilaha illa Allah,' do you feel it as a negation (no gods) or as a positive affirmation (Allah alone exists)? Write down one concrete change you can make today to turn a negation into an affirmation in your worship.
?
The passage ends by warning against 'confusion of mind and whispers.' Is there an area of your faith where you have allowed intellectual confusion to create doubts? How can you, like the author, clarify the positive foundation of your belief in Allah's attributes? Describe a step you will take tonight to strengthen your certainty.
?
Do you sometimes define your faith merely by what you reject (e.g., shirk, innovation) rather than by a positive affirmation of tawhid? Like the opponents in this passage, do you treat 'necessity' (or the fundamental truth of God's existence) as a mere absence of falsehood? How can you actively affirm God's existence in your daily awareness?

The Problem of Distinguishing the Necessary Being
Raises the problem of distinguishing the Necessary Being from contingent beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the problem of distinguishing the Necessary Being from contingent beings when they share common attributes like existence and self-subsistence.

Core Idea
The Necessary Being cannot be distinguished by existence alone; something beyond essence is needed.

The author challenges a potential opponent's view by pointing out that if existence and self-subsistence are common to both necessary and contingent, then we need a further property to uniquely identify the Necessary Being. This lesson unpacks that problem and sets the stage for the subsequent discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the rhetorical question at the end.
The author first mentions that he will discuss the opponent's derivation later, then immediately presents the problem.
2
Identify the common ground
The passage states that existence (al-wujud) as a general concept applies to both the Necessary and the possible. Similarly, self-subsistent essence (al-dhat al-qaim bi-nafsih) also applies to both. Understand that these are shared predicates.
Self-subsistence is a property of both necessary and contingent beings in different ways.
3
Grasp the question
The author asks: what extra thing (amr wara' dhatihi) characterizes the Necessary Being so that it is distinguished from all other essences? This is the core problem.
The question implies that if only essence and existence are considered, there is no distinction.
4
Reflect on the importance
Consider why this question is crucial for theism: without a distinguishing property, the Necessary Being would not be unique. This sets up the need for additional attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكرتموه من تفريع مذهبكم على هذه القاعدة نتكلم عليها بعد هذه المسألة إن شاء الله تعالى ونقول: إذا كان الوجود من حيث هو وجود يعم الواجب والممكن فالذات القائم بالنفس من حيث إنه ذات قائم بالنفس يعم الواجب والممكن، فما الذي يخص الواجب من أمر وراء ذاته حتى يتميز عن سائر الذوات
And what you have mentioned of the derivation of your doctrine upon this principle, we will discuss it after this issue, God willing, and say: If existence, insofar as it is existence, encompasses the Necessary and the possible, and the essence subsisting by itself, insofar as it is an essence subsisting by itself, encompasses the Necessary and the possible, then what characterizes the Necessary Being in terms of something beyond its essence such that it is distinguished from other essences?
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage introduces a new theological question. The author addresses an opponent's derivation of their doctrine based on a certain principle. He says he will discuss it after this issue, but then immediately begins: if existence (الوجود) as a general concept applies to both the Necessary Being (الواجب) and the possible being (الممكن), and if the essence subsisting by itself (الذات القائم بالنفس) also applies to both (since the Necessary Being is self-subsistent and possible beings are also essences subsisting by themselves in a sense?), then what extra thing (أمر وراء ذاته) characterizes the Necessary Being so that it is distinguished from all other essences? The author implies that the opponent's view leads to a difficulty in differentiating the Necessary Being from contingent beings if they share the same general predicates. This passage sets up a problem that the author intends to resolve.

Key Terms
وجود
واجب
ممكن
ذات قائم بالنفس
تمييز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that existence alone is sufficient to distinguish the Necessary Being.
Correction: The passage shows that existence is common to both necessary and contingent, so an additional property is needed.

Checkpoint
What question does the passage raise about the Necessary Being?
A) Whether it has a body.
B) What distinguishes it from contingent beings beyond its essence.
C) Whether it knows particulars.
D) Whether it can be seen in the afterlife.

Key Takeaway
The passage teaches that the Necessary Being cannot be distinguished merely by existence or self-subsistence; an additional distinguishing property is required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been content with merely believing that God exists, without seeking to know His unique attributes that set Him apart from everything else? If so, what will you do today to deepen your understanding of who He truly is?
?
The passage shows that common concepts like 'existence' and 'self-subsistence' cannot define the Creator uniquely. In your worship, is there any area where you treat God as just 'one more being' rather than the Absolutely Distinct? What specific change can you make to single Him out in your heart?
?
If distinguishing the Necessary Being requires something beyond essence, then your own recognition of God must go beyond a mere mental acknowledgment. What extra step do you need to take—whether in knowledge, devotion, or action—to ensure your tawhid is not just a shared concept but a transformative reality?
