How Not to Argue from Human Intellect
Refutation of an argument from human intellect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why appealing to human rational faculty fails as evidence for God's intellect when the opponent disputes the nature of intellection itself.

Core Idea
Human intellect proofs only work if you already agree on how intellection works.

This short but dense passage is a classic kalam argument about the limits of analogy. The author, likely Ash'ari, warns that using human rational capacity to prove that God is an 'intellect' and 'intelligent' only convinces those who already accept a specific theory of self-intellection. If the opponent denies the very attributes you're trying to prove, the analogy is useless.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Note the disputational style: 'your appeal... only benefits you with one who does not dispute...' This is a response to an argument.
The passage is one long sentence in Arabic, so read it as a unit.
2
Identify the key questions about intellection
The passage highlights two contested questions: (1) Does the intellect intellect itself by itself? (2) If it intellects, does it do so by an intellect other than itself or by an intellect that is itself? These questions are presupposed by the analogy.
These are not trivial; they were debated among theologians and philosophers.
3
Understand the refutation's logic
The author argues that the analogy only works for someone who agrees on the answers to those questions. For someone who denies that God is an intellect and intelligent at all, the analogy is irrelevant.
This is a common kalam tactic: showing that an argument assumes what it wants to prove.
4
Reflect on the broader lesson
Consider why this matters for Islamic theology. It shows that not all analogies from creation to Creator are valid; they must not depend on controversial premises.
This passage guards against a superficial use of human faculties as evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما استشهادك بالقوة العاقلة منا فإنما ينفعك مع من لا ينازعك في أن العقل هل يعقل بذاته ذاته؟ وإن عقل أفبعقل غير ذاته أم بعقل هو ذاته؟ أما من نازعك في إثبات كونه عقلاً وعاقلا -أي: علمًا وعالمًا فلا ينفعه هذا الاستشهاد.
And as for your appeal to the rational faculty in us, it only benefits you with one who does not dispute with you about whether the intellect intellects itself by itself? And if it intellects, does it intellect by an intellect other than itself or by an intellect that is itself? As for one who disputes with you in establishing that He is an intellect and intelligent—i.e., knowledge and knowing—this appeal does not benefit him.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument that uses human rational faculty as evidence for God being an intellect (عقل) and intelligent (عاقل). The objector points out that this reasoning only works for someone who already agrees with a particular theory of intellection: whether the intellect intellects itself by itself, and if so whether it does so by an intellect identical to or distinct from itself. These are contested issues. For someone who denies that God is an intellect and intelligent in the first place, the human example is irrelevant and proves nothing.

Key Terms
قوة عاقلة
عقل
عاقل
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The analogy from human intellect to God's intellect is straightforward and universally persuasive.
Correction: The passage shows that the analogy only works if the opponent already accepts specific answers to how intellection works, making it ineffective against those who deny the attributes entirely.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the appeal to human rational faculty is only persuasive to:
A) Those who already accept that God is knowing
B) Those who agree about how the intellect intellects itself
C) Those who deny the existence of God
D) Those who are experts in logic

Key Takeaway
Arguments from human attributes to God's attributes can fail if they depend on contested theories about how those attributes operate in humans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever clung to an argument about Allah that assumed something about your own mind without questioning whether that assumption is even true? What would it cost you to admit you don't fully understand how you yourself think?
?
When you try to describe Allah's knowledge, do you unconsciously picture your own way of knowing and just enlarge it? Are you willing to let go of that picture if it's not from revelation?
?
If a proof for one of Allah's attributes relies on a controversial theory of human intellection that you never examined, how solid is your faith built on that proof? Will you seek a firmer foundation?

Refuting the Inference from Ma'qūl to 'Āqil
Refutation of the inference that being ma'qūl implies being 'āqil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why inferring God's intellection from His being intelligible is logically flawed according to the Ashʿarī argument.

Core Idea
Being an object of knowledge does not imply being a knower.

This passage tackles a specific inference: because God is intelligible (maʿqūl), He must be intelligent (ʿāqil). The author dismisses this as a weak analogy, pointing out that accidents are intelligible yet not intelligent. This lesson unpacks the refutation and its logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author uses a comparison and a counterexample to refute the inference.
The phrase 'لا يمتنع عليه أن يعلم' may be garbled; focus on the overall argument.
2
Identify the three refutation points
The refutation has three parts: (1) it is like a tautology, (2) accidents are intelligible but not intelligent, (3) granting knowledge does not necessitate knowledge. Identify each in the text.
3
Reflect on the logical fallacy
Ask yourself why moving from 'possible' to 'necessary' is invalid here. The commentary explains this as 'taking what is necessary from what is not impossible'.
This connects to the core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما اقتباس كونه عاقلا من كونه معقولا فهو من أمحل المطلوبات، وهو كمن قال: لا يمتنع عليه أن يعلم فلا يمتنع عليه أن يعلم، والعرض في ذاته ممتنع أن يعقل ويعلم عند القوم، فكيف يصح القياس؟ وهب أنه يعلم فلم ينبغي أن يعلم، ثم أخذ ما يجب مما لا يمتنع من أبعد القياسات وأمحل المحالات.
And as for your borrowing His being intelligent (ʿāqil) from His being intelligible (maʿqūl), it is among the most unsound of sought conclusions. It is like one who says: It is not impossible for Him to know, so it is not impossible for Him to know. But an accident, in its essence, is impossible for it to be intellected and known according to the people (the theologians). So how can the analogy be valid? Even if we grant that He knows, why is it necessary that He knows? Then taking what is necessary from what is not impossible is among the most remote of analogies and the most unsound of impossibilities.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the inference that because God is maʿqūl (intelligible) He must be ʿāqil (intelligent). The objector compares this to a trivial tautology: saying 'it is not impossible that He knows, so it is not impossible that He knows' is empty. Moreover, the objector gives a counterexample: an accident (ʿaraḍ) is in its essence impossible to be an intellect or a knower, yet it is intelligible. Thus the analogy from intelligibility to intellection fails. Even if the opponent grants that God knows, that does not necessitate that He knows—the inference from possibility to necessity is invalid. The argument is dismissed as an extremely weak analogy.

Key Terms
اقتباس
عاقل
معقول
عرض
قياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is an object of knowledge (maʿqūl), it must itself be a knower (ʿāqil).
Correction: Accidents are intelligible but are not knowers; thus the inference fails.

Checkpoint
What counterexample does the objector use to refute the inference from being ma'qūl to being 'āqil?
A) A body
B) An accident (ʿaraḍ)
C) Another intellect
D) The divine essence itself

Key Takeaway
Being intelligible does not entail being intelligent; the argument from maʿqūl to ʿāqil is logically invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often assume that because you understand something about God, you can call Him by the same attribute you experience. How does this lesson expose the error in that assumption? What attribute have you assumed about God based on your own experience that you must now re-examine?
?
This lesson warns against deriving necessity from possibility. In your own life, where have you treated a possibility as a necessity, especially in matters of faith? What is one step you will take to differentiate between what is necessary to believe and what is merely possible?
?
The example of the accident shows that something can be known without being a knower. How does this challenge your reliance on human analogies when thinking about God? What specific analogy have you used that this lesson calls into question?

Three Types of Voluntary Human Movements
Enumeration of three voluntary movement types with their respective contents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and describe the three categories of voluntary human movements and their characteristic contents.

Core Idea
Human actions are classified into mental, verbal, and physical movements, each containing moral and cognitive opposites.

This passage from the Nihayat al-Aqdam provides a foundational classification of human voluntary actions. Understanding this division is essential before studying how God's judgment applies to them. Here, the author lists three types and the kinds of good and evil, truth and falsehood they encompass.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification
Read the Arabic passage once, focusing on the three movement types and their definitions.
2
List the contents for each movement
For each movement type, note the pairs of opposites mentioned (e.g., mental includes argument/doubt).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن وجه آخر نقول : الحركات الاختيارية التي اختصت بتصرفات الإنسان ثلاث ؛ حركة فكرية وهي من تصرفات عقله وفكره وحركة قولية وهي من تصرفات نفسه ولسانه وحركة فعلية وهي من تصرفات بدنه والقوى البدنية، وما من حركة من هذه الحركات إلا والله فيها حكم بالأمر والنهي، فإن الفكرية تشتمل على حجة وشبهة وصواب وخطأ وحق وباطل والقولية تشتمل على صدق وكذب، وإقرار وإنكار وحكمة وسفه والفعلية تشتمل على خير وشر، وطاعة ومعصية، وصلاح وفساد، وقد قام الدليل على أن الباري تعالى حكم (۳) عدل، وله حكم وقضية في كل حركة من هذه الحركات، وذلك الحكم إما أن يكون فعلا الأفعال، وإما أن يكون قولاً من الأقوال، واستحال أن يكون فعلا يفعله، فإن كل فعل فهو مسبوق بحكمه (۳) ، بل ذلك الفعل دليل على حكمه)، وحكمه مدلول فعله.
And from another aspect we say: The voluntary movements that are specific to human actions are three: a mental movement, which is from the actions of his intellect and thought; a verbal movement, which is from the actions of his soul and tongue; and a physical movement, which is from the actions of his body and physical powers. There is no movement among these movements except that God has a judgment (حكم) in it of command and prohibition. For the mental movement includes argument, doubt, correctness, error, truth, and falsehood; the verbal movement includes truth, falsehood, affirmation, denial, wisdom, and foolishness; and the physical movement includes good and evil, obedience and disobedience, righteousness and corruption. The evidence has established that the Creator, exalted, is a wise judge, and He has a judgment and decree in every movement of these movements. That judgment is either an act of acts or a word of words, and it is impossible that it be an act that He does, for every act is preceded by His judgment; rather, that act is a proof of His judgment, and His judgment is the meaning of His act.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage classifies voluntary human movements into three categories: mental, verbal, and physical. Each category has specific contents: mental includes argument and doubt, verbal includes truth and falsehood, physical includes good and evil. The author then argues that God has a judgment concerning each movement, expressed as command or prohibition. The judgment must be either an act or a word. It cannot be an act because every act is preceded by judgment; the act itself is a proof of the judgment, and the judgment is the meaning of the act. Therefore, the judgment must be a word.

Key Terms
حركات اختيارية
حركة فكرية
حركة قولية
حركة فعلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What are the three types of voluntary movements mentioned in the passage?
A) Mental, verbal, physical
B) Necessary, voluntary, accidental
C) Good, evil, neutral
D) Thought, desire, action

Key Takeaway
Human voluntary actions are divided into mental, verbal, and physical movements, each containing morally relevant opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your day, which of the three movements—mental, verbal, or physical—do you tend to overlook in terms of accountability to God? How can you begin to take them more seriously starting today?
?
The passage says every voluntary movement contains opposites like truth/falsehood or good/evil. Which area (mental, verbal, or physical) do you struggle with most in avoiding the negative side? What specific step will you take this week to turn that around?
?
If you knew that your thoughts are just as accountable before God as your actions, how would you change what you allow yourself to dwell on? Commit to one practical habit to monitor your mental movements.

Divine Judgment is a Word, Not an Act
Argument that God's judgment is a word, not an act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that God's judgment regarding human voluntary movements is a divine word, not a created act.

Core Idea
God's judgment over actions must be a word because every act is preceded by judgment.

Having classified voluntary movements, the author now addresses the nature of God's judgment upon them. He argues that the divine command or prohibition cannot be an act of creation; rather, it is God's eternal speech. This argument protects the distinction between God's essence and His actions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Notice the claim that God's judgment is either an act or a word.
2
Follow the reasoning why not an act
Understand why it cannot be an act: every act is preceded by its judgment.
3
Conclude it must be a word
By elimination, the judgment is a divine word.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن وجه آخر نقول : الحركات الاختيارية التي اختصت بتصرفات الإنسان ثلاث ؛ حركة فكرية وهي من تصرفات عقله وفكره وحركة قولية وهي من تصرفات نفسه ولسانه وحركة فعلية وهي من تصرفات بدنه والقوى البدنية، وما من حركة من هذه الحركات إلا والله فيها حكم بالأمر والنهي، فإن الفكرية تشتمل على حجة وشبهة وصواب وخطأ وحق وباطل والقولية تشتمل على صدق وكذب، وإقرار وإنكار وحكمة وسفه والفعلية تشتمل على خير وشر، وطاعة ومعصية، وصلاح وفساد، وقد قام الدليل على أن الباري تعالى حكم (۳) عدل، وله حكم وقضية في كل حركة من هذه الحركات، وذلك الحكم إما أن يكون فعلا الأفعال، وإما أن يكون قولاً من الأقوال، واستحال أن يكون فعلا يفعله، فإن كل فعل فهو مسبوق بحكمه (۳) ، بل ذلك الفعل دليل على حكمه)، وحكمه مدلول فعله.
And from another aspect we say: The voluntary movements that are specific to human actions are three: a mental movement, which is from the actions of his intellect and thought; a verbal movement, which is from the actions of his soul and tongue; and a physical movement, which is from the actions of his body and physical powers. There is no movement among these movements except that God has a judgment (حكم) in it of command and prohibition. For the mental movement includes argument, doubt, correctness, error, truth, and falsehood; the verbal movement includes truth, falsehood, affirmation, denial, wisdom, and foolishness; and the physical movement includes good and evil, obedience and disobedience, righteousness and corruption. The evidence has established that the Creator, exalted, is a wise judge, and He has a judgment and decree in every movement of these movements. That judgment is either an act of acts or a word of words, and it is impossible that it be an act that He does, for every act is preceded by His judgment; rather, that act is a proof of His judgment, and His judgment is the meaning of His act.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage classifies voluntary human movements into three categories: mental, verbal, and physical. Each category has specific contents: mental includes argument and doubt, verbal includes truth and falsehood, physical includes good and evil. The author then argues that God has a judgment concerning each movement, expressed as command or prohibition. The judgment must be either an act or a word. It cannot be an act because every act is preceded by judgment; the act itself is a proof of the judgment, and the judgment is the meaning of the act. Therefore, the judgment must be a word.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's judgment (command/prohibition) is a created act.
Correction: The author argues it must be a word because every act is preceded by judgment.

What is the author's conclusion about the nature of God's judgment?
A) It is an act
B) It is a word
C) It is both
D) It is neither

God's judgment over human actions is a divine word, not a created act, because every act is evidence of a prior judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's judgment over every one of your movements is a word that He speaks eternally, does that move you to pause before each action and ask: 'What word of God precedes this? And am I living in obedience to that word?'
?
You may have thought of God's decrees as distant events, but this lesson shows they are intimate words attached to each of your movements. How will you let this truth reshape your awareness of God's presence in your daily choices?
?
The argument proves that judgment cannot be an act because that would lead to an infinite regression. But more personally: if every act you do is preceded by God's word, then your actions are not just physical—they are responses to a divine command. What would change in your life if you truly believed that before you act, God has already spoken?

Voluntary Movements Indicate God's Judgment
Analogy linking necessary and voluntary movements to God’s attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how voluntary movements, like necessary ones, indicate specific attributes of God—namely His judgment, command, and decree.

Core Idea
Voluntary actions point to God’s judgment just as necessary actions point to His knowledge.

The author extends the argument from necessary movements (which demonstrate God’s knowledge, will, and power) to voluntary movements. He claims that if natural motions require a divine cause, then deliberate human acts also require a divine ruling. This analogy is central to proving that God’s command and decree are manifest in all actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Note the parallel structure between necessary and voluntary movements.
The analogy is the core of the argument.
2
Identify the two sides of the analogy
Compare how necessary movements indicate knowledge, will, power and voluntary movements indicate judgment, command, decree.
3
Examine the objection and response
Note the opponent’s claim that 'disposal by act' equals 'obligation by speech'. See how the author concedes for non‑voluntary but not for voluntary actions.
This sharpens the argument.
4
Conclude the author’s purpose
Understand that every act of the Creator points to His judgment, which is the intended conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكما أن الحركات الضرورية دلت باختصاصها () ببعض (1) الجائزات دون البعض على علمه (۷) وإرادته وقدرته ، كذلك الحركات الاختيارية دلت باختصاصها ببعض الأحكام دون البعض على حكمه وأمره وقضيته، وقولكم: إن التصريف بالفعل نازل منزلة التكليف بالقول مسلم فيما يرجع إلى أفعال غير اختيارية ،للإنسان لكن الأفعال الاختيارية لما استدعت ممن له الخلق والأمر حكما واقتضاء وطلبًا فكل فعل وتصريف من الخالق (۱) يدل على حكمه واقتضائه (۲) ، وذلك بعينه هو غرضنا ومرادنا.
And just as necessary movements (الحركات الضرورية) indicated by their specification to some of the possible things without others His knowledge (علم), will (إرادة), and power (قدرة), likewise voluntary movements (الحركات الاختيارية) indicate by their specification to some rulings without others His judgment (حكم), command (أمر), and decree (قضية). And your saying: 'The disposal by act takes the place of the obligation by speech' is accepted regarding non-voluntary actions of man, but voluntary actions, since they require from Him who has creation and command a judgment, a requirement, and a demand, so every act and disposal from the Creator indicates His judgment and requirement, and that is precisely our purpose and intention.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage uses an analogy to argue that just as necessary movements (like natural motions) indicate God's attributes of knowledge, will, and power, voluntary movements also indicate His attributes of judgment, command, and decree. The author addresses an opponent's objection that disposal by act is equivalent to obligation by speech. He concedes this for non-voluntary actions, but maintains that for voluntary actions, they require a specific judgment and demand from God. Therefore, every act of the Creator indicates His judgment and requirement, which is the author's goal.

أمر
قضية
تصريف بالفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only necessary movements indicate God's attributes; voluntary movements are irrelevant.
Correction: The author argues that voluntary movements also indicate God's judgment, command, and decree through their specification to particular rulings.

Checkpoint
What do voluntary movements indicate according to the analogy in this passage?
A) God's knowledge, will, and power
B) God's judgment, command, and decree
C) God's mercy and wrath
D) God's essence

Key Takeaway
Voluntary movements, like necessary ones, are evidence of God's judgment, command, and decree.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a voluntary choice today, do you recognize that this specific action is a sign of God’s judgment over you? How might that awareness change the way you decide?
?
If every voluntary movement you make points to God’s command and decree, what does that mean for the choices you habitually make without thinking? Is there one area of your life where you need to stop treating your actions as neutral and start seeing them as responses to His judgment?
?
The author argues that your voluntary actions are not separate from God’s authority. Which decision are you currently avoiding because you prefer to act as if it is merely your own private choice? Will you submit that decision to God’s judgment today and ask Him to guide your will?

Indications of an Act for God's Attributes
Details how an act indicates God's knowledge, power, will, and judgment, concluding opponents conceded.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a single act can indicate multiple divine attributes from different aspects, leading to the conclusion that God has a judgment and decree regarding actions.

Core Idea
An act reveals God's attributes from multiple angles.

In this passage, the author explains how every act of creation points to different attributes of God depending on which aspect you consider. The precision, occurrence, particularity, and fluctuation of an act each reveal a different divine quality, culminating in the inference that God has a judgment and decree. This argument is used to counter opponents who deny divine speech or command.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the four aspects
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the four 'aspects' (وجوه) mentioned: precision, occurrence, particularity, and fluctuation.
Each aspect corresponds to a different attribute of the agent.
2
Match each aspect to an attribute
For each aspect, note which attribute it indicates: precision indicates knowledge, occurrence indicates power, particularity indicates will, fluctuation indicates judgment.
3
Understand the conclusion about judgment
The fluctuation between permissibility, prohibition, and permission implies a judgment (حكم) and decree (قضية). The judgment can be a command, prohibition, or permission.
4
Reflect on the opponents' concession
The author concludes that the opponents have conceded the issue because they admit the fluctuation which necessarily implies a judgment.
This is a key dialectical point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : ذلك الفعل من وجه إحكامه دل على علم الفاعل، ومن وجه وقوعه دل على قدرته، ومن وجه اختصاصه دل على إرادته ومن وجه تردده بين الجواز (۳) والحظر والإباحة دل على أن للباري تعالى فيها حكمًا وقضية، ثم ذلك الحكم قد يكون أمراً وقد يكون نهيًا وقد يكون إباحة، فقد سلمتم المسألة من حيث منعتموها.
So we say: That act, from the aspect of its precision, indicates the knowledge of the agent; from the aspect of its occurrence, indicates His power; from the aspect of its particularity, indicates His will; and from the aspect of its fluctuation between permissibility, prohibition, and permission, indicates that the Creator, exalted, has in it a judgment and decree. Then that judgment may be a command, or a prohibition, or a permission. So you have conceded the issue from the very point where you denied it.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed argument that a single act can indicate multiple divine attributes depending on the aspect considered. The act's precision indicates God's knowledge; its occurrence indicates His power; its particularity indicates His will; and its fluctuation between categories of permissibility, prohibition, and permission indicates that God has a judgment and decree regarding it. The judgment can be command, prohibition, or permission. The author concludes that the opponents have effectively conceded the issue (the existence of divine speech/command) because they admit that the act has this fluctuation which implies a judgment.

حظر

نهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think an act only indicates one attribute (e.g., power) rather than multiple.
Correction: The author shows that from different aspects, the same act can indicate knowledge, power, will, and judgment.

Checkpoint
What does the fluctuation of an act between permissibility, prohibition, and permission indicate?
A) God's knowledge
B) God's power
C) God's will
D) God's judgment and decree

Key Takeaway
The act's fluctuation between permissibility, prohibition, and permission proves that God has a judgment and decree regarding actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at any action you perform today, do you see it as merely a movement or do you perceive in its precision, occurrence, and particularity the knowledge, power, and will of your Creator? Will you pause before each action to recognize His attributes manifest in it?
?
Your actions fluctuate between what is permissible, prohibited, and commanded. Do you truly live as though God has a specific judgment and decree on every single one of them? When did you last change a behavior because you realized it fell under a divine prohibition?
?
The opponents conceded the very point they denied. What about you? Are there areas of your life where you theoretically affirm God's judgment but practically act as if it does not apply? What will you do today to align your actions with His command, prohibition, and permission?

True Capability Requires Power Over Opposites
Author establishes the principle of capability over opposites and applies it to the rival definition of speaker.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why true capability necessitates the ability to perform opposites and how this principle undermines a rival definition of 'speaker'.

Core Idea
True capability means being able to do or not do—power over opposites.

Before we dive into the details of divine speech, the author establishes a fundamental principle: genuine capability (qudra) is the power to perform opposites. This principle is then used to challenge the opponent's simplistic equation of 'speaker' with 'creator of speech'. You'll see how this forces an unwanted conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle of capability
The passage states that a truly capable agent must be capable of performing opposites (e.g., speech and silence). This is a key axiom in kalam reasoning.
This principle is used against the opponent.
2
Apply the principle to the opponent's definition
The opponent says the speaker is the one who creates speech. By the principle, consistency requires that the silent one creates silence, etc. This leads to attributing all types of speech (commands, news, good, evil, true, false) to God, which is impossible.
The absurdity lies in attributing evil and falsehood to God.
3
Recognize the weight of the objection
The author then mentions two other problems (pre-eternity and unity of speech) but defers them, focusing here on the logical inconsistency of the opponent's position on capability.
This sets up the need for a correct understanding of human speech in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما نلزمهم أن القادر على الحقيقة من يكون قادرًا على الضدين والكلام معنى له أضداد، فإذا قالوا المتكلم من فعل الكلام يلزمهم أن يقولوا: الساكت من فعل السكوت حتى لو خلق سكوت في محل كان ساكنا، ولو خلق أمرًا في محل كان أمرًا، ولو خلق خبرًا في محل كان مخبرًا، ثم من الأوامر ما يكون خيرًا، ومنه ما يكون شرا، ومن الأخبار ما يكون صدقا ومنه ما يكون كذبًا، فيلزمهم إضافة الكل إلى الله تعالى وهو محال، وأما ما أورده على قدم الكلام واتحاده نفرد لهذين الإشكالين مسألتين ونتكلم عليهما بما فيه مقنع - إن شاء الله تعالى لكنا نعارضهم ها هنا بما التزموه مذهبًا. فنقول: من المعلوم الذي لا مرية فيه أن النطق اللساني مركب من حروف، والحروف مقطعات من أصوات، وما من حرف يتفوه به الإنسان وينطق به اللسان إلا ويفنى عقيب ما وجد وينعدم، يتجدد ويعقبه حرف آخر إلى أن يصير مجموع الحرفين والثلاث وأكثر كلمة ويصير مجموع الكلمتين والثلاث وأكثر كلامًا مفهوما مشتملا على معنى من المعاني معلوم لولا ذلك المعنى لم يسم الحروف والكلمات كلامًا، فإذا كل الحروف والكلمات محالها اللسان، وكل المعاني والمفهومات محالها الجنان، وبمجموع الأمرين سمي الإنسان ناطقا ومتكلما، حتى لو وجدت الحروف اللسانية منه دون المعاني الجنانية سمي مجنونا لا متكلما إلا بالمجاز، ولو وجدت المعاني الجنانية منه دون الألفاظ اللسانية سمي مفكرًا لا متكلما إلا بالمجاز، وإن كان بين الموضعين فرق وهو كالفرق بين المؤمن بلسانه دون قلبه، وبين المؤمن بقلبه دون لسانه، غير أنا نسامحهم ها هنا حتى يتضح الحق من السقيم، ثم نعطي كل قسم حقه.
And among what we impose upon them is that the one truly capable (al-qadir ‘ala al-haqiqa) is the one who is capable of opposites, and speech is a meaning (ma‘na) that has opposites. So if they say that the speaker is the one who creates speech, it would be necessary for them to say: the silent one is the one who creates silence, so that if He created silence in a locus, He would be silent; and if He created a command in a locus, He would be a commander; and if He created news in a locus, He would be an informant. Then among commands are what is good and among them what is evil, and among news are what is true and among them what is false. So it would be necessary to attribute all of that to God the Exalted, and that is impossible. As for what was raised concerning the pre-eternity (qidam) of speech and its unity, we set aside two issues for these two problems and will speak about them in a manner that is convincing—God willing—but we oppose them here with what they have committed themselves to as a doctrine. We say: It is known without doubt that linguistic speech (al-nutq al-lisani) is composed of letters, and letters are segments of sounds. No letter is uttered by a human and spoken by the tongue except that it ceases after it comes to be and becomes nonexistent; it is renewed, and another letter follows it, until the sum of two, three, or more letters becomes a word, and the sum of two, three, or more words becomes intelligible speech including a meaning from among known meanings. Were it not for that meaning, the letters and words would not be called speech. So all letters and words have their locus (mahall) as the tongue, and all meanings and concepts have their locus as the heart. By the combination of the two things is the human called a speaker (natiq) and mutakallim, so that if the tongue's letters are present without the heart's meanings, he is called a madman (majnun), not a speaker except metaphorically; and if the heart's meanings are present without the tongue's words, he is called a thinker (mufakkir), not a speaker except metaphorically. Although there is a difference between the two cases, like the difference between one who believes with his tongue without his heart and one who believes with his heart without his tongue. Nevertheless, we are lenient with them here so that the truth becomes clear from the defective, and then we give each part its due.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage contains two related arguments. First, the author presses an objection based on the nature of capability: a truly capable agent must be able to perform opposites (e.g., to create or not create). Speech is a meaning that has opposites (e.g., silence, commands, prohibitions, true/false news). If the opponent defines the speaker as the one who creates speech, consistency demands that the silent one be defined as the one who creates silence, etc. This would force them to attribute all types of speech (commands, news, good, evil, true, false) to God, which is absurd—especially attributing evil and falsehood to God. The author then mentions two other problems (pre-eternity and unity of speech) but defers them to later issues. Second, the author presents his own positive analysis of human speech: linguistic speech (nutq lisani) is composed of letters, which are segmented sounds. Letters are temporal and perish immediately. They combine into words, and words into meaningful speech. The locus of letters/words is the tongue; the locus of meanings is the heart (jinan). A human is called a speaker only when both are present. If only letters exist without meaning, he is mad; if only meaning without words, he is a thinker. This analysis undermines the opponent's simplistic definition of speaker as mere creator of speech, since human speech requires both external material and internal meaning, and it is composed and temporal. The author says he is being lenient to clarify the truth, then they will rightly evaluate each position.

Key Terms
القادر
الضدين
الساكت
الأمر
الخبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that simply creating speech suffices to be a speaker.
Correction: True capability requires power over opposites; the opponent's definition leads to attributing evil and falsehood to God, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does it mean for someone to be truly capable (al-qadir ‘ala al-haqiqa)?
A) To be able to create one thing.
B) To be able to perform opposites.
C) To be able to create speech.
D) To be able to create silence.

Key Takeaway
True capability requires power over opposites; defining a speaker merely as a creator of speech leads to absurd consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim to be 'capable' of something, do you honestly include the ability to refrain from it? In what area of your life does your sense of power actually mask a failure to exercise restraint?
?
If your words (commands, news) were judged by the same logic — that the speaker is defined by what he creates — would you be willing to stand by every command you have given and every statement you have spread, knowing that some may be evil or false?
?
The passage shows that God is not attributed with evil because He is truly capable over opposites. How does this truth expose your own need to confront not just the good you do, but the evil you allow? What one step will you take today to ensure your capability is not used for what displeases Him?

Human Speech is Composite: Letters and Meanings
Author analyzes human speech as composite of letters and meanings, refuting the opponent's simplistic view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the composite nature of human speech: it consists of physical letters (tongue) and mental meanings (heart), and both are necessary for actual speech.

Core Idea
Human speech is a composite of sounds and meanings; neither alone suffices.

Having exposed the problems with the rival definition of speech, the author now presents his own correct analysis of human speech. He shows that linguistic speech (nutq lisani) is composed of letters that are temporal and combine into words, and that meaningful speech requires both external utterances and internal meanings. This analysis directly opposes the idea that speech is a single eternal entity.

Guided Path
1
Examine the composition of linguistic speech
The author states that speech (nutq lisani) is composed of letters, which are segments of sounds. Each letter perishes as soon as it is uttered, and they combine into words and sentences.
This temporal and composite nature is key.
2
Identify the two loci
Letters/words have their locus in the tongue; meanings have their locus in the heart. A human is called a speaker only when both are present. If only letters exist, he is mad; if only meanings, he is a thinker.
3
Understand the metaphorical qualification
The author notes that if only letters without meaning, the person is called a speaker only metaphorically (majaz). Similarly, if only meanings, he is called a thinker. This reinforces that true speech requires both components.

Which of these three categories describes you most accurately right now—and what needs to change so that you are a complete mutakallim of faith?

The Physics and Cognition of Sound
Explains sound production and cognitive processing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how sound is physically produced by striking or tearing, transmitted through the ear, and processed through imaginative, psychic, and rational faculties to become intelligible.

Core Idea
Sound travels from physical vibration to intellectual understanding through a chain of psychological powers.

This passage from Sanusi's al-Aqdam explains the journey of sound from its physical origins to its intellectual interpretation. Before exploring the ascent of meaning, we must understand how sound itself becomes intelligible. Sanusi details the mechanics of sound creation and the cognitive powers that process it, setting the stage for the metaphysical discussion that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on sound production and perception
Read the complete passage once to get an overview of the physical and cognitive process described.
Notice the two methods of sound production: striking and tearing.
2
Trace the sound wave's journey
Follow the wave from the air through the ear canal, to the nerve in the ear, and then to the imaginative, psychic, and rational powers.
The ear is compared to a drum with a stretched nerve.
3
Identify the three cognitive powers
Notice how the sound is processed sequentially: imagination estimates, soul thinks, intellect discriminates.
Each power adds a layer of meaning.
4
Reflect on the sensible vs. intelligible
Consider how sound starts as a sensible phenomenon (letter and sound in air) and ends as an intelligible meaning in the intellect.
This prepares for the discussion of ascent of meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منتجة أو غير منتجة، وإن اعتبرت بمجرد الخيال كانت تقديرا أو تصويراً، فتارة تصوير المحسوس بالمعقول، وتارة تقدير المعقول في المحسوس. ويلزمها أن تكون من جانب المحسوس عربيًا أو عجميا أو هنديا أو روميا أو سريانيا أو عبرانيا، ومن جانب المعقول أن يكون بسيطًا في صورة مركبة ومركبا على نعت بسيط، وذاتياً مع عرضي، ويقينا مع وهم، وأول ما يرد على السمع حرف وصوت يحصل في الهوى بسبب تموج يقع بحركة شديدة تحدث من قرع بعنف أو قلع بحدة، فإن كان من قرع اصطك الجسمان وانضغط الهوى بشدة وحدث الصوت، وإن كان من قلع انقطع الجسمان وانفلت الهوى بشدة وحدث الصوت، ووصل الموج إلى الهوى الراكد الذي في الصماخ وانفعل به وهو مجاور للعصبة المفروشة في أقصى الصماخ الممدودة مد الجلد على الطبل، فيحصل فيه طنين فتشعر به القوة المودعة في تلك العصبة، فيصل إلى القوة الخيالية فيتصرف الخيال فيه تقديرا، فيصل إلى القوة النفسانية فتتصرف النفس فيه تفكيرا، فيصل إلى القوة العقلية فيتصرف العقل فيه تمييزاً وللمعنى صعود من المحسوس المسموع إلى المعقول المعلوم صعودًا من الكثرة إلى الوحدة ونزول من المعقول المعلوم إلى المحسوس المسموع، نزولا من الوحدة إلى الكثرة، والعرفان مبتدئ من تفريق ونفض وترك ورفض ممعن في جمع هو جمع صفات الحق للذات المريدة للصدق، ثم انتهى إلى الوحدة ثم وقوف، وهذا من حيث الصعود والعرفان مبتدئ من توحيد وتفكير وتمييز وتصوير ممعن في معرفة هي معرفة صفات الخلق، ثم انتهى إلى الكثرة ثم وقوف، وهذا من حيث النزول.
Productive or non-productive, and if considered according to mere imagination, it becomes estimation or depiction; sometimes depiction of the sensible by the intelligible, and sometimes estimation of the intelligible in the sensible. And it is necessary that on the side of the sensible it be Arabic, or non-Arab, or Indian, or Roman, or Syriac, or Hebrew, and on the side of the intelligible that it be simple in composite form and composite in simple description, essential with accidental, certain with illusory. And the first thing that reaches hearing is a letter and sound that occurs in the air due to an undulation caused by a violent motion arising from violent striking or forceful tearing. If from striking, the two bodies collide and the air is compressed violently and sound occurs. If from tearing, the two bodies separate and the air is released violently and sound occurs. The wave reaches the still air in the ear canal and is affected by it, being adjacent to the nerve spread at the far end of the ear canal stretched like the stretching of skin over a drum. There occurs in it a ringing, and the power deposited in that nerve perceives it. It reaches the imaginative power, and the imagination acts upon it by estimation. It reaches the psychic power, and the soul acts upon it by thinking. It reaches the rational power, and the intellect acts upon it by discrimination. And for meaning there is an ascent from the heard sensible to the known intelligible, an ascent from multiplicity to unity, and a descent from the known intelligible to the heard sensible, a descent from unity to multiplicity. And gnosis begins from differentiation, shaking off, leaving, and rejecting, being absorbed in a gathering which is the gathering of the attributes of truth for the essence that wills truth, then it ends at unity, then stopping. This is from the perspective of ascent. And gnosis begins from tawhid, thinking, discrimination, and depiction, being absorbed in knowledge (which is knowledge of the attributes of creation), then it ends at multiplicity, then stopping. This is from the perspective of descent.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage describes the process of sound perception and the cognitive ascent and descent of meaning, along with the path of gnosis. It begins by discussing how sounds are produced—either by striking or tearing—and how the resulting wave travels to the ear, causing a ringing in the nerve, which is then processed by the imaginative, psychic, and rational faculties. The author then introduces the concept of ascent (ṣuʿūd) and descent (nuzūl): meaning ascends from the sensory multiplicity to intellectual unity, and descends from unity to multiplicity. Finally, the passage associates gnosis ('irfān) with these movements: in ascent, it starts from differentiation and leads to unity; in descent, it starts from tawḥīd and leads to multiplicity. This explanation serves as a foundation for understanding how sensory perception relates to intellectual knowledge in the kalam tradition.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sounds are immediately understood by the intellect without any intermediary cognitive processing.
Correction: The passage shows that sound passes through imagination (estimation) and soul (thinking) before reaching the intellect (discrimination), indicating a layered chain of cognitive processing.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens after sound reaches the still air in the ear canal?
A) It is immediately processed by the rational power.
B) It causes a ringing in the nerve, then reaches the imaginative power, then the psychic power, then the rational power.
C) It is stored in the ear without further processing.
D) It becomes a letter without sound.

Key Takeaway
Sound perception involves a sequential process from physical production to intellectual discrimination through imagination, soul, and intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the Qur'an recited, do you consciously engage your imaginative, psychic, and rational powers to move from the physical sound to deep understanding, or does the sound often remain at the surface? What one change will you make in your listening habits today to ensure your intellect is actively discriminating the message?
?
The passage describes sound as either productive (meaningful) or non-productive. Think about the sounds you allow into your ears daily—conversations, media, reminders. Which of these are truly productive for your soul? Which are merely noise? What can you remove or reduce starting this week?
?
Your hearing involves a chain of internal powers that can be trained. Have you ever considered that your imagination plays a role in how you process Islamic knowledge? For instance, when you hear a description of Paradise, do you actively imagine it? What step will you take this week to strengthen the connection between your hearing and your intellect?

The Ascent and Descent of Meaning and Gnosis
Explains the ascent and descent of meaning and the two paths of gnosis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concepts of ascent (ṣuʿūd) and descent (nuzūl) of meaning as described by Sanusi, and how gnosis ('irfān) follows these two paths.

Core Idea
Meaning ascends from sensory multiplicity to intellectual unity and descends back; gnosis mirrors this ascent and descent.

After detailing how sound is physically and cognitively processed, Sanusi elevates the discussion to the movement of meaning itself. He describes how meaning ascends from the heard sensible to the known intelligible—from multiplicity to unity—and descends in reverse. Then he connects this to the two paths of gnosis: one starting from differentiation and leading to unity (ascent), and another starting from tawḥīd and leading to multiplicity (descent). This passage integrates epistemology with spiritual journey.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on ascent and descent of meaning
Read the passage once to understand the two directions: ascent from sensible to intelligible (multiplicity to unity) and descent from intelligible to sensible (unity to multiplicity).
Contrast the two movements.
2
Identify the two paths of gnosis
Notice how gnosis begins differently depending on direction: in ascent, it starts with differentiation (tafrīq) and ends in unity; in descent, it starts with tawḥīd and ends in multiplicity.
Each path involves a 'gathering' (jamʿ) or 'knowledge' (maʿrifa).
3
Connect to the earlier sound example
Consider how the cognitive processing of sound (from sensible to intelligible) exemplifies the ascent, while teaching or expressing meaning exemplifies descent.
This bridges the physical and metaphysical.
4
Reflect on the spiritual implications
Think about how these two movements correspond to the seeker's journey: one from creation to Creator, another from Creator to creation.
This is a key concept in Sufi and kalam traditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منتجة أو غير منتجة، وإن اعتبرت بمجرد الخيال كانت تقديرا أو تصويراً، فتارة تصوير المحسوس بالمعقول، وتارة تقدير المعقول في المحسوس. ويلزمها أن تكون من جانب المحسوس عربيًا أو عجميا أو هنديا أو روميا أو سريانيا أو عبرانيا، ومن جانب المعقول أن يكون بسيطًا في صورة مركبة ومركبا على نعت بسيط، وذاتياً مع عرضي، ويقينا مع وهم، وأول ما يرد على السمع حرف وصوت يحصل في الهوى بسبب تموج يقع بحركة شديدة تحدث من قرع بعنف أو قلع بحدة، فإن كان من قرع اصطك الجسمان وانضغط الهوى بشدة وحدث الصوت، وإن كان من قلع انقطع الجسمان وانفلت الهوى بشدة وحدث الصوت، ووصل الموج إلى الهوى الراكد الذي في الصماخ وانفعل به وهو مجاور للعصبة المفروشة في أقصى الصماخ الممدودة مد الجلد على الطبل، فيحصل فيه طنين فتشعر به القوة المودعة في تلك العصبة، فيصل إلى القوة الخيالية فيتصرف الخيال فيه تقديرا، فيصل إلى القوة النفسانية فتتصرف النفس فيه تفكيرا، فيصل إلى القوة العقلية فيتصرف العقل فيه تمييزاً وللمعنى صعود من المحسوس المسموع إلى المعقول المعلوم صعودًا من الكثرة إلى الوحدة ونزول من المعقول المعلوم إلى المحسوس المسموع، نزولا من الوحدة إلى الكثرة، والعرفان مبتدئ من تفريق ونفض وترك ورفض ممعن في جمع هو جمع صفات الحق للذات المريدة للصدق، ثم انتهى إلى الوحدة ثم وقوف، وهذا من حيث الصعود والعرفان مبتدئ من توحيد وتفكير وتمييز وتصوير ممعن في معرفة هي معرفة صفات الخلق، ثم انتهى إلى الكثرة ثم وقوف، وهذا من حيث النزول.
Productive or non-productive, and if considered according to mere imagination, it becomes estimation or depiction; sometimes depiction of the sensible by the intelligible, and sometimes estimation of the intelligible in the sensible. And it is necessary that on the side of the sensible it be Arabic, or non-Arab, or Indian, or Roman, or Syriac, or Hebrew, and on the side of the intelligible that it be simple in composite form and composite in simple description, essential with accidental, certain with illusory. And the first thing that reaches hearing is a letter and sound that occurs in the air due to an undulation caused by a violent motion arising from violent striking or forceful tearing. If from striking, the two bodies collide and the air is compressed violently and sound occurs. If from tearing, the two bodies separate and the air is released violently and sound occurs. The wave reaches the still air in the ear canal and is affected by it, being adjacent to the nerve spread at the far end of the ear canal stretched like the stretching of skin over a drum. There occurs in it a ringing, and the power deposited in that nerve perceives it. It reaches the imaginative power, and the imagination acts upon it by estimation. It reaches the psychic power, and the soul acts upon it by thinking. It reaches the rational power, and the intellect acts upon it by discrimination. And for meaning there is an ascent from the heard sensible to the known intelligible, an ascent from multiplicity to unity, and a descent from the known intelligible to the heard sensible, a descent from unity to multiplicity. And gnosis begins from differentiation, shaking off, leaving, and rejecting, being absorbed in a gathering which is the gathering of the attributes of truth for the essence that wills truth, then it ends at unity, then stopping. This is from the perspective of ascent. And gnosis begins from tawhid, thinking, discrimination, and depiction, being absorbed in knowledge (which is knowledge of the attributes of creation), then it ends at multiplicity, then stopping. This is from the perspective of descent.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage describes the process of sound perception and the cognitive ascent and descent of meaning, along with the path of gnosis. It begins by discussing how sounds are produced—either by striking or tearing—and how the resulting wave travels to the ear, causing a ringing in the nerve, which is then processed by the imaginative, psychic, and rational faculties. The author then introduces the concept of ascent (ṣuʿūd) and descent (nuzūl): meaning ascends from the sensory multiplicity to intellectual unity, and descends from unity to multiplicity. Finally, the passage associates gnosis ('irfān) with these movements: in ascent, it starts from differentiation and leads to unity; in descent, it starts from tawḥīd and leads to multiplicity. This explanation serves as a foundation for understanding how sensory perception relates to intellectual knowledge in the kalam tradition.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ascent and descent of meaning are literal physical movements in space.
Correction: The passage describes these as cognitive and spiritual movements from multiplicity to unity and vice versa, not spatial changes.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the ascent of meaning (ṣuʿūd) involve?
A) From the known intelligible to the heard sensible
B) From multiplicity to unity
C) From tawḥīd to multiplicity
D) From differentiation to gathering of creation's attributes

Key Takeaway
Meaning and gnosis move in two opposite directions: ascent from multiplicity to unity and descent from unity to multiplicity, each with a distinct spiritual path.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own journey of seeking truth, do you find yourself more often ascending towards unity through separation and rejection, or descending into multiplicity through thinking and depiction? Which direction are you currently neglecting, and what step will you take this week to address that?
?
The passage says gnosis from the perspective of ascent ends at unity and then stops. Have you ever reached a point of spiritual unity where you felt you had 'arrived', and then stopped? What might that stopping mean in your life—is it contentment or complacency?
?
The descent path ends at multiplicity and then stops. Are you someone who gets lost in the multiplicity of creation (knowledge, distinctions) without returning to unity? How can you ensure your engagement with multiplicity serves your ascent rather than becoming an end in itself?

The Core Syllogism: Abstract Knowledge and the Soul
Al-Ash'ari states the main syllogism of the proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core deductive argument that abstract universal knowledge cannot inhere in a body, proving the soul is non-corporeal.

Core Idea
Abstract universal knowledge cannot dwell in a divisible, composite, physical body.

In this lesson, we examine the first part of a decisive proof (burhan qati) that the rational soul is not a body. The argument hinges on a simple logical premise: abstract universal knowledge cannot inhere in a body. Understanding this foundational claim is essential for grasping the full proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the syllogism. Notice the logical structure: if A cannot inhere in B, then if A inheres in C, C must not be B.
The author states the conclusion first, then the premise.
2
Identify the premise and conclusion
The premise is that abstract universal knowledge cannot inhere in a body. The conclusion is that it inheres in a non-body.
3
Reflect on implications
Why does this prove the soul is not a body? Because the soul is the subject of abstract knowledge, so it must be non-corporeal.
This is the core of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم البرهان القاطع على أن النفس ليس بجسم ولا قوة في جسم أن العلم المجرد الكلي لا يجوز أن يحل في جسم، وكل ما لا يجوز أن يحل في جسم فإذا حلَّ حل في غير جسم، فالعلم المجرد الكلي إذا حل حل في غير جسم. أما المقدمة فنثبتها بناءً على أن كل جسم منقسم، وما حلَّ في منقسم يجب أن يكون منقسماً، ونثبتها بناءً على أن كل جسم فمركب من مادة وصورة، والمعنى الوحداني الكلي بالذات لا يحل في مركب، ونثبتها بناءً على أن كل جسم فهو ذو وضع وقدر وشكل وحيز، وكل ما حل في ذي قدر ووضع فيكون له نسبة وقدر وهيئة ووضع والمعنى المعقول المجرد في نفس الإنسان مجرد عن القدر والوضع، فتجرده إما أن يكون باعتبار محله أو باعتبار ما منه حصل، وباطل أن يكون تجرده بما منه حصل، فإن الإنسان يتلقى حد الإنسان وحقيقته من إنسان شخصي له قدر مخصوص، لكن الحس تجرده نوع تجريد عن المادة، فإن المادة لا تدخل في الحس، بل التماثل الذي يطابقه يرتسم فيه، والخيال يجرده تجريدا أشد، فإن الحس يناله وهو حاضر، والخيال يناله وهو غائب، والفكر يجرده تجريدا أشد، فإن الخيال يناله مع غيبوبته شخصيا جزئيا، والفكر العقلي يتصوره مجردا فيتصوره العقل كليًّا واحدا مبرأ من الوضع، والقدر مجردا عن المادة، والشكل فتجرده بما هو مجرد، وتفرده عن العلائق المادية بما هو مفرد، فدل إدراكه المعقول على هذا الوجه المذكور على أن ذات النفس الناطقة العاقلة لا تنقسم إلى جزء، وجزء ولا تتركب من مادة وصورة ولا توصف بوضع ومقدار وشكل وحيز، وذلك ما أردنا بيانه.
Then the decisive proof that the soul is not a body nor a power in a body is that abstract universal knowledge cannot inhere in a body, and whatever cannot inhere in a body, if it inheres, it inheres in a non-body; so abstract universal knowledge, if it inheres, inheres in a non-body. As for the premise, we establish it based on the fact that every body is divisible, and whatever inheres in a divisible thing must be divisible; and we establish it based on the fact that every body is composed of matter and form, and the unitary universal meaning by essence cannot inhere in a composite; and we establish it based on the fact that every body has position, magnitude, shape, and location, and whatever inheres in something with magnitude and position will have relation, magnitude, configuration, and position, while the abstract intelligible meaning in the human soul is abstract from magnitude and position. Thus its abstraction is either by virtue of its locus or by virtue of that from which it is obtained. It is false that its abstraction is by virtue of that from which it is obtained, for man receives the definition and reality of man from a particular human being having a specific magnitude, but sensation abstracts it in a kind of abstraction from matter, for matter does not enter into sensation; rather, the likeness that corresponds to it is imprinted in it. Imagination abstracts it more intensely, for sensation attains it while it is present, while imagination attains it while it is absent. Intellect abstracts it even more intensely, for imagination attains it in its absence as a particular individual, while rational intellect conceives it as abstract, so the intellect conceives it as a single universal, free from position, magnitude, abstract from matter and shape. Thus its abstraction is by virtue of what is abstract, and its separation from material attachments is by virtue of what is separate. So the perception of the intelligible in this mentioned manner indicates that the essence of the rational, intelligent soul is not divided into part and part, nor composed of matter and form, nor described by position, quantity, shape, and location. And that is what we intended to explain.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage provides a deductive proof (burhan qati) that the soul is neither a body nor a power in a body. The core argument is: abstract universal knowledge cannot inhere in a body; therefore, if such knowledge inheres in something, that something must be non-corporeal. The author then justifies the premise (that abstract universal knowledge cannot inhere in a body) with three supporting arguments: (1) Every body is divisible; what inheres in a divisible thing must be divisible, but abstract knowledge is indivisible. (2) Every body is composed of matter and form; the unitary universal meaning cannot inhere in a composite. (3) Every body has position, magnitude, shape, and location; whatever inheres in such a thing acquires those attributes, but abstract knowledge is free from them. The author then examines how human knowledge arises through abstraction. Sensation abstracts from matter by receiving a likeness. Imagination abstracts further by retaining the form in the absence of the object. Intellect abstracts most fully, conceiving a universal free from particularity, position, magnitude, and shape. This process shows that the intellect—and thus the rational soul—is indivisible, non-composite, and not subject to physical attributes. The conclusion is that the soul's essence is not divisible, not composed of matter and form, and not describable by physical categories.

Key Terms
البرهان القاطع
العلم المجرد الكلي
النفس
جسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that abstract knowledge can be located in the brain or in a physical organ.
Correction: The proof shows that because bodies are divisible, composite, and have position, abstract knowledge—which is indivisible and universal—cannot inhere in them.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence if abstract knowledge cannot inhere in a body?
A) It must inhere in a non-body
B) It cannot inhere at all
C) It inheres in the brain
D) It inheres in the imagination

Key Takeaway
The decisive proof starts with the premise that abstract universal knowledge cannot be hosted by a body, leading to the conclusion that the soul—its locus—is non-corporeal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat your self as merely a biological machine, or do you truly believe your soul is a non-corporeal reality that can grasp God and truth? How would your daily priorities shift if you lived as though your soul were not a body?
?
When you encounter abstract concepts like justice, goodness, or the oneness of God, do you realize that these cannot be produced by your brain alone? Does that realization humble you and draw you to seek their source in a non-material realm?
?
If abstract knowledge cannot inhere in a body, then your truest self is not your physical form. Are you willing to detach from sensory pleasures and invest in the knowledge that defines your soul's essence? What concrete step will you take today to prioritize your soul's nourishment over your body's desires?

Three Justifications for the Premise
The author provides three arguments and an analysis of abstraction to support the premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three detailed justifications for why abstract universal knowledge cannot inhere in a body, and how the abstraction process supports this.

Core Idea
Bodies are divisible, composite, and have physical attributes—all incompatible with abstract, universal, indivisible knowledge.

In this lesson, we delve into the justifications for the key premise. The author provides three arguments based on the nature of bodies: divisibility, composition, and physical attributes. He then illustrates through the hierarchy of abstraction (sensation, imagination, intellect) that the soul's knowledge is abstract, confirming its non-corporeality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic from 'أما المقدمة' to the end. Note the three justifications and the abstraction process.
2
Identify the three justifications
Note the three reasons: (a) bodies are divisible, (b) bodies are composed of matter and form, (c) bodies have position, magnitude, shape.
Each is introduced with 'ونثبتها بناءً على'.
3
Understand the abstraction process
Follow how knowledge becomes more abstract from sensation to imagination to intellect, culminating in a universal concept free of physical attributes.
4
Connect to the conclusion
See how this abstraction shows the soul is not divisible, not composite, and not physical.

Denying the Necessity of Division of Accidents
Author denies necessity and gives counterexamples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mutakallim denies that every accident necessarily divides with its subject and how counterexamples undermine that claim.

Core Idea
Not all accidents divide with their subject; some cease, others persist in a part.

The third objection targets the claim that accidents always divide with their subject. The author counters by denying the necessity of this proposition and providing examples of accidents that behave differently, thus weakening the opponent's foundation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opponent's claim carefully: every accident that inheres in a subject divides when the subject divides.
This is presented as a necessary truth by the opponent.
2
Identify the denial
The author asserts that this proposition is not self-evident (daruri) but was generalized from limited examples like colors and shapes.
3
Examine the counterexamples
Notice the two types of accidents: those that cease (like contact and composition) and those that do not cease (like knowledge).
This shows the claim is not universal.
4
Reflect on the mutakallim's stance
Understand that for the mutakallim, a single knowledge only subsists in a single part, not in the whole.
This sets up the later reversal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض الثالث فنقول: أولا أنتم مطالبون بأن كل عرض حل في محل انقسم بانقسام المحل ضرورة، فإن هذه القضية عندنا ليست ضرورية، بل لما توهم في الألوان والأشكال أنها تنقسم بانقسام المحل أجري حكم ذلك في الكل، فمن الأعراض ما ينعدم بانقسام المحال (كالمماسة والتأليف) ومنها ما لا ينعدم فيقوم بجزء منه قيامه بالكل وعند المتكلم العلم الواحد لا يقوم إلا بجزء واحد، وانعكس الأمر عليكم فنقول: إن كان انقسام المحل يوجب انقسام العرض، فاتحاد العرض يستدعي اتحاد المحل، وأنتم إذا سلمتم معنى وحداني الذات لا ينقسم بوجه فيلزمكم أن تثبتوا محلا له وحداني الذات لا ينقسم، أليست النقطة عرض وهو شيء ما لا ينقسم بوجوه، فيلزمكم أن تثبتوا محلا له وحداني الذات لا ينقسم من الجسم؟ ومحلها من الجسم، وجب أن لا ينقسم. على أنا نلزمهم أمرًا لا جواب لهم عنه، وهو أن هذا المعنى الكلي الواحد الذي يدركه العقل أيبقى مع العقل محفوظا مذكورًا بالفعل أبد الدهر، أو يجوز أن يغفل عنه العقل، ولا حاصل للقسم الأول، فإن الإنسان يجد من نفسه غفلته عن المعقولات المحصلة فيبقى متحفظا خزانة الحفظ وهي قوى جسمانية، إن جاز أن يحفظها كلية أفلم لا يجوز أن يكون قوة.
The third objection: so we say: first, you are required [to accept] that every accident (arad) that inheres in a subject divides necessarily by the division of the subject. But this proposition is not necessary for us. Rather, because it was imagined of colors and shapes that they divide by the division of the subject, the ruling of that was applied to everything. Among accidents are those that cease to exist with the division of the subject (like contact and composition), and among them are those that do not cease to exist, so they subsist in a part of it as they subsist in the whole. According to the mutakallim, a single knowledge does not subsist except in a single part. The matter is reversed upon you, so we say: If the division of the subject necessitates the division of the accident, then the unity of the accident necessitates the unity of the subject. And if you accept a meaning that has unity of essence (wahdaniyy al-dhat) that is not divisible in any way, then it is necessary for you to affirm a subject for it that has unity of essence and is not divisible. Is not the point (al-nuqta) an accident? It is something that is not divisible in various ways. So it is necessary for you to affirm a subject for it that has unity of essence and is not divisible from the body. And its subject from the body must not be divisible. Moreover, we impose upon them something for which they have no answer: that this single universal meaning (al-ma'na al-kulli al-wahid) which the intellect perceives — does it remain with the intellect preserved and actually remembered forever, or is it permissible for the intellect to be heedless of it? The first division is not realized, for man finds in himself his heedlessness of acquired intelligibles. So it remains preserved in a storehouse of preservation, which are bodily powers. If it is permissible for it to preserve them as universals, then why is it not permissible for it to be a power [that perceives universals?]
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector claims that every accident (arad) that inheres in a subject necessarily divides with the division of that subject. The author refutes this by stating that the proposition is not self-evident (daruri), but was mistakenly generalized from colors and shapes. He then distinguishes two types of accidents: those that cease to exist upon division (e.g., contact and composition) and those that continue to exist in a part as they did in the whole. For the latter, a single accident can subsist in a single part. The argument is then turned against the opponent: if division of the subject necessitates division of the accident, then by similar logic, unity of the accident necessitates unity of the subject. Since the opponent admits a meaning (like the soul) that has unity of essence (wahdaniyy al-dhat) and is indivisible, they must affirm an indivisible subject for it. The example of the point (al-nuqta) — an accident that is not physically divisible — forces the opponent to posit an indivisible subject for it as well. Finally, the author raises a dilemma about the universal concept perceived by the intellect: either it remains constantly in the intellect, which is contradicted by human forgetfulness, or it is temporarily forgotten, implying storage in a bodily faculty (memory). This challenges the opponent's assumption that the intellect itself can preserve universals without an indivisible substrate. The commentary follows the author's argumentative structure: first stating the objection, then denying its necessity, providing counterexamples, turning the logic around, using the point as a proof, and ending with the dilemma about memory.

Key Terms
عرض
محل
انقسام
مماسة
تأليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All accidents necessarily divide with their subject because colors and shapes do.
Correction: The author shows that some accidents (e.g., contact) cease to exist upon division, while others (e.g., knowledge) can remain in a part, so the claim is not universal.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the proposition that every accident divides with its subject not necessary?
A) Because it was revealed by divine scripture
B) Because it was mistakenly generalized from colors and shapes
C) Because the mutakallim rejects all necessary truths
D) Because accidents are not real entities

Key Takeaway
The mutakallim denies the necessity of the division claim by offering counterexamples of accidents that behave differently.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever generalized a rule from a few examples, only to find it false later? How does this passage warn you against such intellectual shortcuts in matters of faith?
?
What assumptions do you hold about Allah's attributes or actions that might be based on weak analogies? Will you examine them in light of this lesson's method?
?

Reversing the Argument: Unity Implies Unity
Author reverses argument and uses point as example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallim reverses the opponent's logic: if subject division implies accident division, then accident unity implies subject unity, leading to the necessity of an indivisible subject for indivisible accidents like the point.

Core Idea
Unity of accident demands unity of subject: a point requires an indivisible subject.

Having refuted the necessity of the division claim, the author now turns the tables. He argues that if division of the subject necessarily divides the accident, then by the same token, the unity of an accident should require unity of the subject. This forces the opponent to admit an indivisible subject for indivisible accidents like the point, which they cannot deny.

Guided Path
1
Read the reversal argument
Read carefully: 'If division of subject implies division of accident, then union of accident implies union of subject.'
This is a logical contrapositive.
2
Apply to the opponent's commitments
The opponent admits a meaning with unity of essence (like the soul) that is indivisible. They must then affirm an indivisible subject.
3
Consider the point example
A point is an accident that is not divisible in any way. It therefore requires an indivisible subject, but its subject (the body) is divisible—a contradiction.
This puts the opponent in a difficult position.
4
Reflect on the implication
Understand how this reversal strengthens the author's position by forcing the opponent to accept indivisible subjects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض الثالث فنقول: أولا أنتم مطالبون بأن كل عرض حل في محل انقسم بانقسام المحل ضرورة، فإن هذه القضية عندنا ليست ضرورية، بل لما توهم في الألوان والأشكال أنها تنقسم بانقسام المحل أجري حكم ذلك في الكل، فمن الأعراض ما ينعدم بانقسام المحال (كالمماسة والتأليف) ومنها ما لا ينعدم فيقوم بجزء منه قيامه بالكل وعند المتكلم العلم الواحد لا يقوم إلا بجزء واحد، وانعكس الأمر عليكم فنقول: إن كان انقسام المحل يوجب انقسام العرض، فاتحاد العرض يستدعي اتحاد المحل، وأنتم إذا سلمتم معنى وحداني الذات لا ينقسم بوجه فيلزمكم أن تثبتوا محلا له وحداني الذات لا ينقسم، أليست النقطة عرض وهو شيء ما لا ينقسم بوجوه، فيلزمكم أن تثبتوا محلا له وحداني الذات لا ينقسم من الجسم؟ ومحلها من الجسم، وجب أن لا ينقسم. على أنا نلزمهم أمرًا لا جواب لهم عنه، وهو أن هذا المعنى الكلي الواحد الذي يدركه العقل أيبقى مع العقل محفوظا مذكورًا بالفعل أبد الدهر، أو يجوز أن يغفل عنه العقل، ولا حاصل للقسم الأول، فإن الإنسان يجد من نفسه غفلته عن المعقولات المحصلة فيبقى متحفظا خزانة الحفظ وهي قوى جسمانية، إن جاز أن يحفظها كلية أفلم لا يجوز أن يكون قوة.
The third objection: so we say: first, you are required [to accept] that every accident (arad) that inheres in a subject divides necessarily by the division of the subject. But this proposition is not necessary for us. Rather, because it was imagined of colors and shapes that they divide by the division of the subject, the ruling of that was applied to everything. Among accidents are those that cease to exist with the division of the subject (like contact and composition), and among them are those that do not cease to exist, so they subsist in a part of it as they subsist in the whole. According to the mutakallim, a single knowledge does not subsist except in a single part. The matter is reversed upon you, so we say: If the division of the subject necessitates the division of the accident, then the unity of the accident necessitates the unity of the subject. And if you accept a meaning that has unity of essence (wahdaniyy al-dhat) that is not divisible in any way, then it is necessary for you to affirm a subject for it that has unity of essence and is not divisible. The first division is not realized, for man finds in himself his heedlessness of acquired intelligibles. So it remains preserved in a storehouse of preservation, which are bodily powers. Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector claims that every accident (arad) that inheres in a subject necessarily divides with the division of that subject. The Dilemma of the Universal Meaning
Author presents dilemma about preservation of universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dilemma about the universal meaning: if the intellect perceives a universal, it must either remember it always or be able to forget it, but forgetfulness implies storage in bodily memory, which challenges the opponent's view.

Core Idea
The universal meaning's persistence forces a dilemma about memory, not the intellect.

The author concludes with a powerful dilemma that leaves the opponent with no answer. If a universal concept is perceived by the intellect, does it stay there forever or can the intellect be heedless of it? Since we experience forgetfulness, the concept must be stored elsewhere, in bodily powers. This undermines the opponent's position on the nature of universals.

Guided Path
1
Read the final dilemma
Read the two possibilities: either the universal remains constantly in the intellect, or the intellect can be heedless of it.
2
Evaluate the first possibility
The author rejects the first because humans experience forgetfulness of acquired intelligibles.
This is an empirical observation.
3
Understand the second possibility
If forgetful, the universal must be preserved in a bodily faculty (memory), not in the intellect itself.
4
Reflect on the opponent's position
Realize how this dilemma undermines the opponent's assumption that the intellect alone can handle universals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
