Refutation of Perception by an Additional Perception
Refutes perception by an additional attribute via contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a proof by contradiction refutes the claim that perception requires a separate attribute of idrak, and why we know necessarily that perception works uniformly.

Core Idea
The necessity of uniform perception refutes the need for an additional perception attribute.

In this passage, the author directly challenges a key claim about perception. He does not merely assert his view; he shows that the opposing view leads to absurd consequences that contradict what we know by necessity. This lesson examines his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic and English to get the overall argument. Notice the conditional structure starting with 'if'.
The passage is compact; read slowly.
2
Identify the conditional claim
The author assumes for contradiction that perception is by an additional idrak. Look for the 'if...then' pattern.
3
Analyze the village example
Consider the vivid example: a person with sound senses in a village with children playing, cattle grazing, drums beating, and statues being made. If perception required a separate idrak, he might not perceive them because he was not created with that specific perception. Ask: is that plausible?
This example highlights the absurdity.
4
Consider the near vs. distant example
The second example: it would be possible to see distant objects but not nearby ones, again due to selective perception. This further shows the consequences are absurd.
5
Understand the conclusion from necessary knowledge
The author appeals to necessary knowledge: we know that in reality, a healthy person with sound senses perceives whatever is present. Therefore, the original assumption must be false.
This is the core of the proof by contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ولو كان المدرك مدركًا بإدراك للزم أن يحضر عند الحاسة السليمة [؟] قرية تلعب وإنعام تسرح وطبول تضرب وصور تنفخ فيه وهو لا يراها ولا يسمعها إذ لم يخلق له إدراك ذلك وكذلك يجوز أن يرى الشخص البعيد ولا يرى القريب لأنه خلق له إدراك البعيد دون القريب وقد علمنا ضرورة أن الأمر على خلاف ذلك.
He said: If the perceiver were perceiving by means of a perception, then it would be necessary that when the sound sense is present, [if] a village plays, cattle graze, drums beat, and statues are blown into, he does not see them nor hear them because he was not created with perception of that; and likewise it would be permissible that a person see the distant and not see the near, because he was created with perception of the distant but not of the near – but we know necessarily that the matter is contrary to that.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is a proof by contradiction refuting the claim that perception occurs through a separate attribute called idrak. The author argues that if perception required a distinct faculty or attribute, then when the senses are sound, we would not necessarily perceive everything present; rather, we could lack perception of some things because we were not created with that specific perception. The example given is a person in a village where children play, cattle graze, drums beat, and statues are produced – yet he might not see or hear them because he was not given that particular perception. Also, it would be theoretically possible for someone to see distant objects but not nearby ones, if he were created with perception for the distant only. However, we know by necessity that this is not how perception works: a healthy sense perceives whatever is present to it, not selectively based on an arbitrary attribute. Thus the conclusion is that perception is not by an additional idrak.

Key Terms
المدرك
الإدراك
الحاسة السليمة
خلق له إدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Perception requires a separate attribute of idrak that can be independently created.
Correction: The argument shows that if that were true, perception would be arbitrary, but we know necessarily that perception of present objects is uniform; therefore, perception is not by an additional idrak.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does the author draw from the example of a person in a village not seeing or hearing things despite sound senses?
A) Perception is possible without any physical conditions.
B) Perception cannot be due to an additional attribute because we know necessarily that it works differently.
C) Perception of distant objects is easier than near ones.
D) A person can choose not to perceive something.

Key Takeaway
Perception does not require a separate attribute of idrak because the uniform functioning of the senses would be contradicted by such a theory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that your inability to perceive something—like a spiritual truth or a sign of God—was because you simply lacked a special 'capacity' or 'perception' for it? How does this lesson challenge you to reconsider that assumption, and what might you be missing by not trusting your innate senses and reason?
?
Think of a moment when you doubted the evidence before your eyes because you thought you weren't 'created' to perceive it. Did that doubt protect your faith or weaken it? After this lesson, will you trust that God has given you sufficient faculties to see the truth when it is present?
?
This argument uses 'necessary knowledge' (we know necessarily that the matter is contrary). Reflect on a core belief of yours that you accept as necessary. Are you living as if it is necessary, or do you allow intellectual doubts to overshadow what you inherently know to be true? What step will you take today to act on that necessary knowledge?

Hearing and Its Cognitive Chain
Describes hearing and its cognitive sequence, with comparison to sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how hearing involves a sequence of cognitive acts and how it differs from the other senses in that perception is not merely an imprint on the sense organ.

Core Idea
Hearing is not just sound; it is a chain of understanding, discrimination, judgment, and acceptance.

The passage begins with a description of hearing that emphasizes the cognitive steps beyond mere sound reception. It compares the auditory nerve to the eye's crystalline humor and points out that the two superior senses (hearing and sight) are special because their perception is not of what is imprinted on the sense organ itself. This lesson unpacks that process and distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the hearing description
Read the Arabic text describing how sound is perceived and the cognitive steps that follow (hearing, understanding, etc.).
Notice the sequence: hearing, then understanding, then discrimination, then judgment, then acceptance.
2
Compare with sight analogy
The text compares the auditory nerve to the eye's crystalline humor. Note the difference: the image in a mirror appears all at once, but in the nerve it is sequential.
This highlights the temporal nature of hearing.
3
Identify the distinction
The passage says these two senses are distinct because their perception is not of what is imprinted in the sense organs. Think about what this means conceptually.
This distinction is key to understanding Ash'ari epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطبل ، والوتر على الصنج حصل منه طنين فيها فتشعر به القوة المودعة في تلك العضبة على رأي أو أدركه الحس المشترك على رأي ، فحصل السماع ثم الفهم ثم التمييز ثم الحكم ثم القبول ، فتلك العصبة بالنسبة إلى الصوت والكلام نازل منزلة الجليدية في العين غير أن الصورة في المرآة تحصل دفعة تامة، والصورة في العصبة تحصل متعاقبة في لحظة ، ويحصل الإدراك به وينتقش الخيال منه انتقاش اللوح من القلم فيحصل فيه الكلام مكتوبًا معاينا فيقرأ كما يقرأ من الصحيفة، وهاتان الحاستان ممتازتان عن سائر الحواس بأن إدراكهما غير ما ارتسم في الحاستين. وأما الشم فإنه بقوة في زائدتي الدماغ المشبهتين بحلمتي الثدي، وإنما يدرك بواسطة جسم ينفعل من الروائح، وذلك بانفعال الهوى من ذي الرائحة، لا بانتقال أجزاء تنفصل منها، أو رائحة تنتقل عنها، فيصل إلى الحلمتين فتدركه القوة المركوزة فيها ، والذوق هو قوة مودعة في العصبة المفروشة على ظاهر اللسان بواسطة الرطوبة العذبة التي لا طعم لها المنبسطة على ظاهر اللسان، فإنها تأخذ طعم ذي الطعم وتستحيل إليه وتتصل بتلك العصبة فتدركها العصبة، واللمس هو قوة مبثوثة في جميع البشرة واللحم المتصل بها يدرك بها الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة والصلابة واللين والخشونة والملاسة، والخفة والثقل، والذي يحمل هذه القوة جسم لطيف يجري في شباك العصب ويستمد من القلب والدماغ، وإنما يدرك إذا استحال كيفية البشرة إلى شبه المدرك بشرط أن يتفاوتا في الكيفية، وهذه القوى كلها معدات لقبول الفيض من واهب الصور، وإلا فالحاسة لا تحدث إدراكا. والأشعري فصل بين السمع والبصر وبين الثلاثة الباقية ؛ حيث شرط لها هنا اتصالا جسمانيا، وأثبت إحساسا جسمانيا ولم يثبتها في صفات الباري تعالى، ولما علم أن لهما اختصاصا بالباطن ردد قوله في أن الإدراك علم مخصوص أو في الحاسة المشتركة، ولا يستبعد من مذهبه قيام الإدراك السمعي معنى آخر بالبصر والبصر بالسمع ، فإن القوم حكموا باتحادهما عند الحاسة المشتركة حتى يصير المرئي مقروءاً والمقروء مرئيا، فإنهما في حقيقة الإدراك لا يختلفان، ومحلهما
The drum and the string upon the cymbal produced a ringing in them, so the power deposited in that nerve feels it according to one opinion, or the common sense perceives it according to another opinion, so hearing occurs, then understanding, then discrimination, then judgment, then acceptance. So that nerve with respect to sound and speech is analogous to the crystalline humor in the eye, except that the image in the mirror is obtained at once completely, while the image in the nerve is obtained successively in an instant. Perception occurs through it, and the imagination is engraved from it as the tablet is engraved from the pen, so speech comes to be written and visible in it, and it is read as one reads from a page. These two senses are distinct from the other senses in that their perception is not of what is imprinted in the two senses. As for smell, it is by a power in the two protuberances of the brain resembling the nipples of the breast, and it perceives only through a body that receives the effect of odors, and that is by the air being affected by the odorous thing, not by the transfer of particles separating from it, or an odor moving from it, until it reaches the nipples and the power lodged in them perceives it. Taste is a power deposited in the nerve spread over the surface of the tongue by means of the sweet moisture that has no taste spread over the surface of the tongue, for it takes the taste of the flavored thing and changes into it and connects to that nerve, and the nerve perceives it. Touch is a power diffused over all the skin and the flesh connected to it; through it one perceives heat, cold, moisture, dryness, hardness, softness, roughness, smoothness, lightness, and heaviness. That which carries this power is a subtle body flowing in the network of nerves and deriving from the heart and brain. It perceives only when the quality of the skin changes to resemble the perceived object, provided that they differ in quality. All these powers are preparations for receiving the emanation from the Bestower of Forms; otherwise the sense does not produce perception. Al-Ash'ari differentiated between hearing and sight and the remaining three, in that he required for these here physical contact, and he affirmed physical sensation, and he did not affirm them in the attributes of God the Exalted. And since he knew that they have a special connection to the inner [sense], he wavered in his statement as to whether perception is a particular knowledge or in the common sense. It is not remote from his school that there can occur auditory perception as another meaning by sight, and visual perception by hearing, for the people [of theology] ruled that they unite at the common sense, so that the visible becomes readable and the readable becomes visible, for they do not differ in the reality of perception, and their locus...
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical description of the four senses (hearing, smell, taste, touch) and concludes with a theological discussion of Ash'ari's position on perception. The author first continues an explanation of hearing: sound waves strike the ear, causing a perception that involves a chain of cognitive acts (hearing, understanding, discrimination, judgment, acceptance). The auditory nerve is compared to the eye's crystalline humor, but with a sequential process. The two superior senses (hearing and sight) are distinguished from others because their perception is not of what is imprinted on the sense organ itself. Then the author describes smell: it is mediated by air affected by odors, not by particle transfer. Taste is explained as a nerve on the tongue that absorbs flavors via a neutral moisture. Touch is a power throughout the skin perceiving opposites like hot/cold, etc., carried by a subtle body from heart and brain. The author then makes a key philosophical point: all these senses are merely preparations to receive the emanation from the Bestower of Forms (Wahib al-Suwar); the sense itself does not produce perception. Then Ash'ari's view is presented: he distinguishes hearing and sight from the other three senses by requiring physical contact for the latter, and he does not affirm these three as attributes of God. Ash'ari wavers whether perception is a specific knowledge or a function of the common sense. The passage ends by stating that, in Ash'ari's school, it is possible for hearing to occur through sight and vice versa, since at the level of the common sense they unite—the visible becomes readable and the readable visible, as they do not differ in the reality of perception. The text breaks off at 'and their locus'.

Key Terms
قوة مودعة
الحس المشترك
الحاسة المشتركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many cognitive steps follow the initial hearing?
A) Three
B) Four
C) Five
D) Six

Key Takeaway
Hearing involves a sequential cognitive process (understanding, discrimination, judgment, acceptance) and is distinct because its perception is not merely a sensory imprint.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you listen to the Qur'an being recited, do you merely hear the sound, or do you actively move through the cognitive chain of understanding, discrimination, judgment, and acceptance? Which step do you most often skip, and what would it take to engage it honestly?
?
The passage teaches that hearing and sight are distinct because their perception is not simply a physical imprint—your mind actively constructs meaning. In your daily life, how often do you react to what you hear as if it were raw fact, without recognizing the role of your own understanding and judgment? Will you commit to pausing after hearing something important to consciously move through the chain before accepting it?
?
Your hearing is compared to a page being written—every sound leaves an impression on your imagination and soul. Are you guarding what you allow to be inscribed on that page? What sounds from today will remain written in your heart, and are they worthy of the cognitive process Allah has gifted you?

Rational Judgments

Smell, Taste, and Touch: Senses and Their Mechanisms
Explains the mechanisms of smell, taste, and touch, and states they are preparations for divine emanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how each of the senses of smell, taste, and touch works, and how they all serve as mere preparations for receiving perception from the Bestower of Forms.

Core Idea
Smell, taste, and touch are physical processes that prepare the soul for divine emanation.

After discussing hearing and sight, the passage moves to the three 'lower' senses: smell, taste, and touch. Each is explained in terms of its physical mechanism. The author then makes a crucial philosophical point: these senses do not produce perception themselves; they are merely preparations for receiving an emanation from the Bestower of Forms. This lesson breaks down each sense and that metaphysical conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Examine smell
Read how smell works: a power in the brain protuberances perceives odors via the air being affected, not by particle transfer.
Note the rejection of particle transfer—this is a key philosophical position.
2
Examine taste
Taste is a power in the nerve on the tongue, mediated by a neutral moisture that absorbs flavor.
3
Examine touch
Touch is a power diffused over the skin and flesh, perceiving qualities like heat, cold, etc., through a subtle body from heart and brain.
Touch requires a change in the skin's quality to resemble the object perceived.
4
Understand the metaphysical conclusion
The passage states that all these powers are 'preparations for receiving the emanation from the Bestower of Forms.' The sense itself does not produce perception.
This is a key Ash'ari principle: perception is a divine gift.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطبل ، والوتر على الصنج حصل منه طنين فيها فتشعر به القوة المودعة في تلك العضبة على رأي أو أدركه الحس المشترك على رأي ، فحصل السماع ثم الفهم ثم التمييز ثم الحكم ثم القبول ، فتلك العصبة بالنسبة إلى الصوت والكلام نازل منزلة الجليدية في العين غير أن الصورة في المرآة تحصل دفعة تامة، والصورة في العصبة تحصل متعاقبة في لحظة ، ويحصل الإدراك به وينتقش الخيال منه انتقاش اللوح من القلم فيحصل فيه الكلام مكتوبًا معاينا فيقرأ كما يقرأ من الصحيفة، وهاتان الحاستان ممتازتان عن سائر الحواس بأن إدراكهما غير ما ارتسم في الحاستين. وأما الشم فإنه بقوة في زائدتي الدماغ المشبهتين بحلمتي الثدي، وإنما يدرك بواسطة جسم ينفعل من الروائح، وذلك بانفعال الهوى من ذي الرائحة، لا بانتقال أجزاء تنفصل منها، أو رائحة تنتقل عنها، فيصل إلى الحلمتين فتدركه القوة المركوزة فيها ، والذوق هو قوة مودعة في العصبة المفروشة على ظاهر اللسان بواسطة الرطوبة العذبة التي لا طعم لها المنبسطة على ظاهر اللسان، فإنها تأخذ طعم ذي الطعم وتستحيل إليه وتتصل بتلك العصبة فتدركها العصبة، واللمس هو قوة مبثوثة في جميع البشرة واللحم المتصل بها يدرك بها الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة والصلابة واللين والخشونة والملاسة، والخفة والثقل، والذي يحمل هذه القوة جسم لطيف يجري في شباك العصب ويستمد من القلب والدماغ، وإنما يدرك إذا استحال كيفية البشرة إلى شبه المدرك بشرط أن يتفاوتا في الكيفية، وهذه القوى كلها معدات لقبول الفيض من واهب الصور، وإلا فالحاسة لا تحدث إدراكا. والأشعري فصل بين السمع والبصر وبين الثلاثة الباقية ؛ حيث شرط لها هنا اتصالا جسمانيا، وأثبت إحساسا جسمانيا ولم يثبتها في صفات الباري تعالى، ولما علم أن لهما اختصاصا بالباطن ردد قوله في أن الإدراك علم مخصوص أو في الحاسة المشتركة، ولا يستبعد من مذهبه قيام الإدراك السمعي معنى آخر بالبصر والبصر بالسمع ، فإن القوم حكموا باتحادهما عند الحاسة المشتركة حتى يصير المرئي مقروءاً والمقروء مرئيا، فإنهما في حقيقة الإدراك لا يختلفان، ومحلهما
The drum and the string upon the cymbal produced a ringing in them, so the power deposited in that nerve feels it according to one opinion, or the common sense perceives it according to another opinion, so hearing occurs, then understanding, then discrimination, then judgment, then acceptance. Commentary
This passage is a philosophical description of the four senses (hearing, smell, taste, touch) and concludes with a theological discussion of Ash'ari's position on perception. The author first continues an explanation of hearing: sound waves strike the ear, causing a perception that involves a chain of cognitive acts (hearing, understanding, discrimination, judgment, acceptance). The text breaks off at 'and their locus'.

واهب الصور

إحساس جسماني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Smell occurs by particles separating from the odorous object and traveling to the nose.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that smell is by the air being affected, not by transfer of particles.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does smell occur?
A) By particles transferring from the odor source
B) By the air being affected by the odor
C) By direct contact with the odor
D) By a power in the tongue

Key Takeaway
Smell, taste, and touch each have specific physical mechanisms, but they are only preparations; true perception is from the Bestower of Forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your senses are merely preparations and true perception comes only from the Bestower of Forms, how does that change the way you experience the world today? Will you consciously acknowledge that every sight, sound, and touch is a direct gift from Him?
?
The passage says that without the divine emanation, the sense does not produce perception. Do you live as if your senses give you reality on their own? What would it take for you to stop trusting your senses as independent and instead see them as channels for God's constant giving?
?
When you taste something delicious or feel a gentle touch, do you ever pause to thank the One who grants the perception? Commit to one moment today where you will intentionally recognize the preparation of your sense and the emanation from the Bestower.

Rational Judgments
intermediate
6 min
Ash'ari's Distinction and the Unity of Perception
Presents Ash'ari's distinction between hearing/sight and other senses, his wavering on the nature of perception, and the unity at common sense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ash'ari's differentiation between hearing/sight and the other three senses, his position on physical contact and divine attributes, and the doctrine that all perceptions unite at the common sense.

Core Idea
Ash'ari separates the two 'spiritual' senses from the three 'physical' ones, yet allows them to interchange at the common sense.

The passage concludes with a summary of Ash'ari's distinctive views on the senses. He differentiates hearing and sight from smell, taste, and touch by requiring physical contact for the latter and not affirming them as divine attributes. He also wavers on whether perception is a specific knowledge or resides in the common sense. The passage then notes that, for Ash'ari's school, it is possible for hearing to occur through sight and vice versa, because at the level of the common sense they unite. This lesson unpacks these nuanced positions.

Guided Path
1
Understand Ash'ari's distinction
Ash'ari separates hearing and sight from the other three senses: he requires physical contact for the latter and does not count them as divine attributes.
This distinction is theological: hearing and sight are attributes of God, but smell, taste, touch are not.
2
Note his hesitation on perception
Ash'ari's statement wavers: is perception a specific knowledge (ʿilm makhṣūṣ) or a function of the common sense (al-ḥāssa al-mushtaraka)?
This shows a deep philosophical question about the nature of perception.
3
Grasp the unity at common sense
The passage says that hearing and sight can 'swap' at the common sense: the visible becomes readable and the readable visible, because they do not differ in the reality of perception.
This implies a higher-level unity of sensory experience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطبل ، والوتر على الصنج حصل منه طنين فيها فتشعر به القوة المودعة في تلك العضبة على رأي أو أدركه الحس المشترك على رأي ، فحصل السماع ثم الفهم ثم التمييز ثم الحكم ثم القبول ، فتلك العصبة بالنسبة إلى الصوت والكلام نازل منزلة الجليدية في العين غير أن الصورة في المرآة تحصل دفعة تامة، والصورة في العصبة تحصل متعاقبة في لحظة ، ويحصل الإدراك به وينتقش الخيال منه انتقاش اللوح من القلم فيحصل فيه الكلام مكتوبًا معاينا فيقرأ كما يقرأ من الصحيفة، وهاتان الحاستان ممتازتان عن سائر الحواس بأن إدراكهما غير ما ارتسم في الحاستين. وأما الشم فإنه بقوة في زائدتي الدماغ المشبهتين بحلمتي الثدي، وإنما يدرك بواسطة جسم ينفعل من الروائح، وذلك بانفعال الهوى من ذي الرائحة، لا بانتقال أجزاء تنفصل منها، أو رائحة تنتقل عنها، فيصل إلى الحلمتين فتدركه القوة المركوزة فيها ، والذوق هو قوة مودعة في العصبة المفروشة على ظاهر اللسان بواسطة الرطوبة العذبة التي لا طعم لها المنبسطة على ظاهر اللسان، فإنها تأخذ طعم ذي الطعم وتستحيل إليه وتتصل بتلك العصبة فتدركها العصبة، واللمس هو قوة مبثوثة في جميع البشرة واللحم المتصل بها يدرك بها الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة والصلابة واللين والخشونة والملاسة، والخفة والثقل، والذي يحمل هذه القوة جسم لطيف يجري في شباك العصب ويستمد من القلب والدماغ، وإنما يدرك إذا استحال كيفية البشرة إلى شبه المدرك بشرط أن يتفاوتا في الكيفية، وهذه القوى كلها معدات لقبول الفيض من واهب الصور، وإلا فالحاسة لا تحدث إدراكا. والأشعري فصل بين السمع والبصر وبين الثلاثة الباقية ؛ حيث شرط لها هنا اتصالا جسمانيا، وأثبت إحساسا جسمانيا ولم يثبتها في صفات الباري تعالى، ولما علم أن لهما اختصاصا بالباطن ردد قوله في أن الإدراك علم مخصوص أو في الحاسة المشتركة، ولا يستبعد من مذهبه قيام الإدراك السمعي معنى آخر بالبصر والبصر بالسمع ، فإن القوم حكموا باتحادهما عند الحاسة المشتركة حتى يصير المرئي مقروءاً والمقروء مرئيا، فإنهما في حقيقة الإدراك لا يختلفان، ومحلهما
The drum and the string upon the cymbal produced a ringing in them, so the power deposited in that nerve feels it according to one opinion, or the common sense perceives it according to another opinion, so hearing occurs, then understanding, then discrimination, then judgment, then acceptance. So that nerve with respect to sound and speech is analogous to the crystalline humor in the eye, except that the image in the mirror is obtained at once completely, while the image in the nerve is obtained successively in an instant. Perception occurs through it, and the imagination is engraved from it as the tablet is engraved from the pen, so speech comes to be written and visible in it, and it is read as one reads from a page. These two senses are distinct from the other senses in that their perception is not of what is imprinted in the two senses. As for smell, it is by a power in the two protuberances of the brain resembling the nipples of the breast, and it perceives only through a body that receives the effect of odors, and that is by the air being affected by the odorous thing, not by the transfer of particles separating from it, or an odor moving from it, until it reaches the nipples and the power lodged in them perceives it. Taste is a power deposited in the nerve spread over the surface of the tongue by means of the sweet moisture that has no taste spread over the surface of the tongue, for it takes the taste of the flavored thing and changes into it and connects to that nerve, and the nerve perceives it. Touch is a power diffused over all the skin and the flesh connected to it; through it one perceives heat, cold, moisture, dryness, hardness, softness, roughness, smoothness, lightness, and heaviness. That which carries this power is a subtle body flowing in the network of nerves and deriving from the heart and brain. It perceives only when the quality of the skin changes to resemble the perceived object, provided that they differ in quality. All these powers are preparations for receiving the emanation from the Bestower of Forms; otherwise the sense does not produce perception. Al-Ash'ari differentiated between hearing and sight and the remaining three, in that he required for these here physical contact, and he affirmed physical sensation, and he did not affirm them in the attributes of God the Exalted. And since he knew that they have a special connection to the inner [sense], he wavered in his statement as to whether perception is a particular knowledge or in the common sense. It is not remote from his school that there can occur auditory perception as another meaning by sight, and visual perception by hearing, for the people [of theology] ruled that they unite at the common sense, so that the visible becomes readable and the readable becomes visible, for they do not differ in the reality of perception, and their locus...
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical description of the four senses (hearing, smell, taste, touch) and concludes with a theological discussion of Ash'ari's position on perception. The author first continues an explanation of hearing: sound waves strike the ear, causing a perception that involves a chain of cognitive acts (hearing, understanding, discrimination, judgment, acceptance). The auditory nerve is compared to the eye's crystalline humor, but with a sequential process. The two superior senses (hearing and sight) are distinguished from others because their perception is not of what is imprinted on the sense organ itself. Then the author describes smell: it is mediated by air affected by odors, not by particle transfer. Taste is explained as a nerve on the tongue that absorbs flavors via a neutral moisture. Touch is a power throughout the skin perceiving opposites like hot/cold, etc., carried by a subtle body from heart and brain. The author then makes a key philosophical point: all these senses are merely preparations to receive the emanation from the Bestower of Forms (Wahib al-Suwar); the sense itself does not produce perception. Then Ash'ari's view is presented: he distinguishes hearing and sight from the other three senses by requiring physical contact for the latter, and he does not affirm these three as attributes of God. Ash'ari wavers whether perception is a specific knowledge or a function of the common sense. The passage ends by stating that, in Ash'ari's school, it is possible for hearing to occur through sight and vice versa, since at the level of the common sense they unite—the visible becomes readable and the readable visible, as they do not differ in the reality of perception. The text breaks off at 'and their locus'.

Key Terms
الحاسة المشتركة
علم مخصوص
اتصال جسماني
إحساس جسماني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'ari denies any sensory perception for the lower senses (smell, taste, touch) because he does not count them as divine attributes.
Correction: Ash'ari does affirm physical sensation for them; he simply does not consider them attributes of God.

Checkpoint
According to Ash'ari, why do hearing and sight differ from the other three senses?
A) They require physical contact
B) They are not affirmed as divine attributes
C) They require physical contact and are not divine attributes
D) They are the only real senses

Key Takeaway
Ash'ari distinguishes hearing and sight as divine attributes from the three physical senses, but holds that all perception unites at the common sense, allowing sensory interchange.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran, do you ever pause to realize that your sight (seeing the words) and your hearing (reciting or listening) are not separate gifts but part of a unified perception that should lead you to the One who created both? How can you train yourself to experience this unity as a reminder of tawhid?
?
Ash'ari wavers on whether perception is a specific knowledge or located in the common sense. Have you ever felt uncertainty about how you know Allah? Do you rely more on intellectual proofs or spiritual experiences? What would it take for you to unify your knowledge of Him into a single, unwavering certainty?
?
If at the common sense, the visible becomes readable and the readable becomes visible, then every sensory experience can point to the same truth. Are you living as if your senses are separate distractions, or are you intentionally using each sense to draw closer to Allah? What practical step can you take today to make one of your sensory experiences a conscious act of worship?

First Mu'tazila Objection: The Need for a Correct Cause for Vision
The Mu'tazila object that vision requires a correct cause which is existence, but such causes are not always required.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that vision should not require a correct cause if it is like knowledge, and the importance of consistency in theological reasoning.

Core Idea
Vision, like knowledge, needs no cause.

The Mu'tazila challenge the Ash'ari requirement of a correct cause for vision by pointing out that knowledge does not require such a cause, even though it relates to all things. This objection forces the Ash'aris to either admit inconsistency or explain why vision is fundamentally different from knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the Mu'tazila present their objection in a question form, asking for consistency.
Pay attention to the examples of uncaused rulings.
2
Identify the key analogy
The Mu'tazila compare vision to knowledge. Understand why they think this analogy undermines the need for a correct cause.
Knowledge relates to the necessary, possible, and impossible without a cause.
3
Distinguish caused vs. uncaused rulings
The passage lists examples: caused rulings like 'being knowing'; uncaused like 'occupying space'. See where vision might fit.
The Mu'tazila argue that permissibility of vision might be uncaused.
4
Reflect on the challenge
Ask yourself: If vision is a type of knowledge or similar to it, should it need a cause? How would an Ash'ari respond?
This objection sets up the need for a defense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا قالت المعتزلة : قد طلبتم للرؤية علة مصححة وتعين لكم الوجود، فما أنكرتم أن جواز الرؤية من الأحكام التي لا تعلل، فإن من الأحكام ما يعلل كالعالمية والقادرية، ومنها ما لا يعلل كالتحيز وقبول العرض وجميع الصفات النفسية، أليس تعلق العلم بالمعلومات لا يستدعي مصححا إذ تعلق بالواجب والجائز والمستحيل، ولم توجد قضية هي أعم من الأقسام الثلاثة حتى تكون تلك القضية هي المصححة لتعلق العلم بها وكونها معلومة هي نفس تعلق العلم بها فتكون العلة والمعلول واحدا، وإذا قررتم أن الرؤية إما علم مخصوص أو معنى في حكم العلم، ثم لم تطلبوا مصححا للعلم فلم طلبتم مصححا للرؤية، فقولوا: تعلقت الرؤية بما تعلق به العلم أو لا علة ولا مصحح.
And when the Mu'tazila said: You have sought for vision a correct cause and existence was determined for you, so why do you not deny that the permissibility of vision is among the rulings that are not caused? For among rulings are those that are caused, like being knowing and being powerful, and among them are those that are not caused, like occupying space, receiving accidents, and all the essential attributes. Does not knowledge's relation to known things not require a correct cause, since it relates to the necessary, possible, and impossible? And there is no proposition more general than the three divisions such that that proposition would be the correct cause for knowledge's relation to them, and their being known is the very relation of knowledge to them, so the cause and effect would be one. And since you have established that vision is either a specific knowledge or a meaning in the ruling of knowledge, then you do not seek a correct cause for knowledge, so why do you seek a correct cause for vision? Say: vision relates to what knowledge relates to, or there is no cause and no correct cause.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage presents the first Mu'tazila objection to the Ash'ari argument for the possibility of vision. The Mu'tazila point out that the Ash'aris have demanded a correct cause (illa musahhiha) for vision, and they have identified it as existence (al-wujud). However, the Mu'tazila argue that permissibility of vision might be among those rulings that do not require a cause, like the essential attributes. They draw an analogy with knowledge: knowledge relates to all things (necessary, possible, impossible) without needing a correct cause. If the Ash'aris claim that vision is either a specific knowledge or like knowledge, then they should be consistent and not require a cause for vision. Thus, the Mu'tazila demand consistency: either admit that vision, like knowledge, needs no cause, or explain the difference.

Key Terms
علة مصححة (correct cause)
جواز الرؤية (permissibility of vision)
أحكام لا تعلل (uncaused rulings)
علم (knowledge)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Mu'tazila are denying the permissibility of vision entirely.
Correction: They are not denying permissibility; they are challenging the logical consistency of the Ash'ari argument by showing that if vision is like knowledge, it should not require a cause.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the first Mu'tazila objection?
A) Vision is impossible because it requires a cause
B) The Ash'aris should not require a cause for vision if they compare it to knowledge
C) Existence is not a correct cause
D) There are no uncaused rulings

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that consistency demands vision be treated like knowledge, without a cause, so the Ash'aris must either accept this or explain the difference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there an area of your life where you demand a justification for something from others—or from Allah—that you do not demand of yourself? The Mu'tazila called out the Ash'aris for inconsistency. Where are you inconsistent in your standards of belief or practice?
?
The passage argues that some rulings are uncaused—they simply are. Do you treat your faith as something that requires constant external validation, or do you accept its fundamentals as self-evident? What would it mean for you to trust in a truth that needs no cause?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that knowledge needs no cause. Have you ever felt your certainty about Allah shaken because you lacked a proof? What if that uncertainty stems from demanding of faith what it never required?

Second Objection: Ash'ari Inconsistency Regarding States (Ahwal)
Second objection against Ash'ari consistency on states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against the Ash'ari argument for the possibility of seeing God, which highlights an internal inconsistency regarding their denial of states while relying on state-based classifications.

Core Idea
The Ash'aris deny states but use them in their proof— an internal contradiction.

This passage presents a second objection targeting the Ash'ari use of the division of states (commonality and difference) in their argument for seeing God. The author argues that the Ash'aris have rejected the reality of states, reducing such divisions to mere verbal expressions. Yet, in their proof, they rely on the very same classification, creating an inconsistency that undermines their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Note the key terms introduced.
Focus on understanding the flow of the objection.
2
Identify the key terms
Identify and understand the terms: al-hal (state), qadiyyat al-ishtirak (case of commonality), qadiyyat al-iftiraq (case of difference), al-wujud (existence), musahhih (corrector).
These terms are central to the objection.
3
Analyze the logical structure
Break down the objection: 1) The classification of states is valid only for those who affirm states. 2) Ash'aris deny states. 3) Yet they use this classification in their proof. 4) Conclusion: inconsistency.
This reveals the internal contradiction.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this objection is significant for the debate on seeing God and the Ash'ari doctrine of states.
It challenges the coherence of the Ash'ari position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعتراض ثان : أن تقسيم الحال إلى قضية الاشتراك والافتراق إنما يصح على مذهب مثبتي الحال، وأنتم معاشر الأشعرية قد نفيتم الحال ورددتم قضايا الخصوص والعموم والافتراق والاشتراك إلى محض العبارات، فكيف صرتم إلى إلزام أحكام الحال حتى أبطلتم قضية الافتراق، وعينتم قضية الاشتراك ثم حصرتم ذلك في قضية واحدة وهو الوجود على أن الموجودات إنما تختلف بوجودها عندكم، والوجود في الجوهر هو نفس الجوهر، وكما يتمايز الجوهر والعرض بالجوهرية والعرضية تمايزا في الوجود، وكما تمايز القديم والحادث في القدم والحدوث تمايزا في الوجود، فإن الوجود العام الذي شمل الموجودات حتى يصح أن يكون مصححا، وحتى يصح الجمع بين الشاهد والغائب بذلك الجامع.
A second objection: That the division of the state (al-hal) into the case of commonality and the case of difference is only valid according to the school of the affirmers of states. And you, the Ash'ari community, have denied states and reduced the cases of particularity, generality, difference, and commonality to mere expressions. So how have you come to impose the rulings of states, such that you invalidated the case of difference, specified the case of commonality, and then confined that to a single case, namely existence (al-wujud), given that existents, according to you, only differ in their existence? And existence in the substance is the substance itself. And just as substance and accident are distinguished by substantiality and accidentality in existence, and just as the eternal and the temporal are distinguished by eternity and temporality in existence, thus the universal existence which encompasses existents, so that it may be a corrector (musahhih), and so that the analogy between the witnessed and the unseen may be valid by that common factor.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage is an objection. The author points out that the Ash'ari position is internally inconsistent. The division of the state (al-hal) into a case of commonality (qadiyyat al-ishtirak) and a case of difference (qadiyyat al-iftiraq) is only a valid classification for those who affirm the reality of states (muthbiti al-hal). However, the Ash'aris (ma'ashir al-Ash'ariyya) have denied states (nafaytum al-hal) and reduced these categories to mere verbal expressions (muhd al-'ibarat). Yet, in their proof for the possibility of seeing God, they rely on the notion of a shared attribute (jami')—specifically existence (al-wujud)—to analogize from the witnessed to the unseen. The objection is that by doing so, they are implicitly using the rulings (ahkam) of states, such as invalidating the case of difference and specifying the case of commonality. Moreover, according to Ash'ari metaphysics, existence in a substance is identical to the substance itself, and distinctions between substance and accident or between eternal and temporal occur within existence itself. Thus, the universal existence they posit as a corrector (musahhih) and as the basis for analogy is a concept borrowed from the very doctrine of states they reject. This inconsistency is the heart of the objection.

Key Terms
الحال (al-hal) - state
قضية الاشتراك (case of commonality)
قضية الافتراق (case of difference)
الوجود (existence)
المصحح (corrector)
الجمع بين الشاهد والغائب (analogy between the witnessed and the unseen)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Ash'ari denial of states prevents them from using any classification of states in their proofs.
Correction: The objection shows that the Ash'aris do use such classifications (e.g., commonality and difference) in their proof for seeing God, creating an internal contradiction.

Checkpoint
What contradiction does the second objection point out in the Ash'ari position?
A) They affirm states and then deny them.
B) They deny states but then use the classification of states in their proof for vision.
C) They deny the possibility of seeing God.
D) They affirm the existence of a single state.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari denial of states is internally inconsistent with their use of state classifications in their proof for seeing God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever defended a theological position while unknowingly relying on a concept you officially reject? How does that challenge your intellectual honesty?
?
When you argue for God's attributes, do you use common terms like 'existence' as mere words, or do they carry real weight? What does your answer reveal about your consistency?
?
If you discovered a contradiction in your own beliefs—like denying states but using state-like reasoning—would you be willing to abandon the argument, or would you rationalize it? Which choice brings you closer to truth?

Third Objection: The Scope of Vision's Attachment
The author presents a third objection regarding the universality of vision's attachment to substance and accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against the Ash'ari proof for seeing God, which challenges the universality of vision's attachment to substance and accident.

Core Idea
Vision does not attach to all substances and accidents, so the Ash'ari proof fails.

The Ash'ari argument for the possibility of seeing God relies on the claim that vision attaches broadly to substance and accident. In this passage, an objector challenges that universality, pointing out that abstract substances are not seen and not every accident is visible. This lesson unpacks that objection and its logical conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Get a sense of the objection's flow.
2
Understand the critique on abstract substance
The objection argues that abstract substances and bodies are not seen because they are never free of color. Identify why this undermines the universality claim.
The opponent assumed vision attaches to substance qua substance, but the objector says it only attaches to substances with color.
3
Understand the critique on accident
Not every accident is seen; only some like color. Therefore, vision's attachment is not universal across accidents either.
4
Grasp the logical conclusion
Since vision attaches only to some things, the opponent must either specify which or generalize—and that very issue is the point of dispute.
This shows the proof begs the question.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعتراض ثالث : قد أجملتم القول إجمالا في أن الرؤية تعلقت بالجوهر والعرض، أما الجوهر المجرد والجسم المجرد فغير مسلم تعلق الرؤية به؛ إذ قد استحال تجرده عن اللون فلم تتعلق الرؤية به مجردًا، وأما العرض فليس كل عرض قد تعلقت الرؤية به، وإنما يستقيم دليلكم هذا أن لو تعلقت الرؤية بالجوهر والعرض على الإطلاق والتعميم حتى يصح منكم ربط الحكم بجامع بينهما، وإذا تعلقت ببعض الأعراض دون البعض فلم يكن الحكم معلقا بالعرض من حيث إنه عرض، ولا بالجوهر من حيث إنه جوهر، ولا يكون معلقا بما يجمع بينهما وهو الوجود أو الحدوث، فلا بد إذا من إثبات التعلق وحصره في شيء دون شيء، أو تعميمه بكل شيء، وذلك نفس المتنازع فيه.
A third objection: You have spoken summarily that vision is attached to substance and accident. As for the abstract substance (al-jawhar al-mujarrad) and the abstract body (al-jism al-mujarrad), it is not granted that vision is attached to them, because it is impossible for them to be free of color, so vision is not attached to them as abstract. As for the accident, not every accident is attached by vision. This proof of yours would only be sound if vision were attached to substance and accident absolutely and universally, so that you could validly link the ruling to a common factor (jami') between them. But if it is attached to some accidents and not others, then the ruling is not dependent on the accident qua accident, nor on the substance qua substance, nor is it dependent on what brings them together, namely existence (al-wujud) or contingency (al-huduth). Therefore, it is necessary either to specify the attachment to some things and not others, or to generalize it to everything, and that is the very point of dispute.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage is an objection. The author criticizes the Ash'ari proof for the possibility of seeing God, which relies on the claim that vision attaches generally to substance and accident. The opponent (the Ash'ari) has spoken broadly (ajmaltum al-qawl ijmalan) that vision is attached to substance and accident. The objection challenges this on two fronts. First, regarding substance: an abstract substance (al-jawhar al-mujarrad) or an abstract body (al-jism al-mujarrad) is not admitted to be seen, because it is impossible for them to be free of color (istihala tajarruduhu 'an al-lawn). In the physical world, bodies always have color, so vision cannot attach to them in abstraction from color. Second, regarding accident: not every accident is seen (laysa kull 'arad qad ta'allaqat al-ru'ya bihi). Only certain accidents like color are visible. Therefore, the proof would only be valid if vision attached to all substances and all accidents universally, so that a common factor (jami') like existence or contingency could link them. Since vision attaches only to some, the common factor is not established, and the ruling is not tied to the nature of substance or accident. The objection concludes that the opponent must either specify which things are visible (which is the very point of dispute) or generalize it, and that is the exact issue under debate (dhalika nafs al-mutanaza' fihi).

Key Terms
الجوهر المجرد (abstract substance)
الجسم المجرد (abstract body)
الرؤية (vision)
الجامع (common factor)
الوجود (existence)
الحدوث (contingency)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari proof for vision relies on a universal attachment of vision to all substances and accidents without exception.
Correction: The objection shows that abstract substances and many accidents are not seen, so the claimed common factor does not hold.

Checkpoint
Why does the third objection reject the Ash'ari proof for vision?
A) Because vision does not attach to abstract substances or all accidents.
B) Because vision attaches only to accidents.
C) Because substance is not visible at all.
D) Because the common factor is not shared between substance and accident.

Key Takeaway
The third objection exposes that vision attaches only to some substances and accidents, preventing the Ash'ari from establishing a universal common factor, thus making their proof question-begging.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the objector says 'it is impossible for abstract substance to be free of color in this world,' does that challenge your assumption that you can reason about God's essence from physical experience? How does it feel to realize that your own reasoning about the divine may be limited by the conditions of this world?
?
The Ash'ari proof fails because it made a universal claim without evidence. In your own faith, are there beliefs you hold about Allah that you treat as self-evident but have never examined their premises? Would you be willing to reconsider them if a logical flaw were pointed out?
?
This objection shows that even respected theologians can make logical mistakes. How does that affect your trust in human reasoning about religion? Do you tend to rely on arguments or on revelation? What concrete step can you take today to ensure your belief in seeing Allah in the afterlife is based on the Quran and Sunnah, not on flawed analogies?

Contingency as a Corrector for Vision: The Fourth Objection
The author presents a fourth objection arguing that contingency (huduth) can serve as the corrector (musahhih) for vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the fourth objection argues that contingency (huduth) can serve as a corrector (musahhih) for vision, and how it distinguishes between existence qualified by descriptions and the composite nature of rulings.

Core Idea
Contingency is not a mere negation but existence under a description, enabling it to be a corrector for vision.

The theologian claims that contingency cannot be the corrector for vision because it is a negative attribute. The fourth objection challenges this, arguing that contingency is existence qualified by a description, and that rational rulings can attach to such composites. This passage unpacks that argument with an analogy between the visible and the known, and a logical principle about negating composites.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the objection's structure: a challenge, a refutation of a counterargument, an analogy, and a logical principle.
Pay attention to the technical terms: huduth, musahhih, wujud, mar'i, ma'lum, munqasim, murakkab.
2
Identify the core claim
The objection asks: why deny contingency as a corrector when there are rational rulings attached to contingency? Identify this challenge and the anticipated counterargument.
The counterargument is that 'being preceded by non-existence' is not effective.
3
Analyze the analogy
The objection draws an analogy: the visible is more specific than the known, yet vision attaches to the known under existence. Similarly, vision can attach to existence under contingency. Understand why this supports the objection.
This shows that a corrector does not have to be a simple property like existence; it can be a qualified property.
4
Unpack the logical principle
The objection states that when a divided gives divisions, a ruling may be attached to a composite of divisions, not to individuals alone. Therefore, one must negate the composite as well. See how this principle is often overlooked by theologians.
This principle is crucial for understanding how multiple attributes can combine to form a corrector.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this objection challenges the Ash'ari denial of contingency as a corrector. If accepted, it opens the door for God's contingency to be a condition for vision, which has implications for the vision of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعتراض رابع : لم قلتم : إن الحدوث لا يكون مصححا وقد عرفتم أن من الأحكام العقلية ما يتعلق بالحدوث دون الوجود؟ وليس لقائل أن يقول : إن كونه مسبوقا بعدم ليس بمؤثر فإن معناه أنه وجود على صفة واعتبار، وكما أن المرئي أخص من المعلوم، والرؤية تتعلق بالمعلوم على صفة الوجود، كذلك نقول : تتعلق بالوجود على صفة الحدوث، وبالحادث على صفة المقابلة أو التكون، أو غير ذلك من الصفات والاعتبارات، وهذا السر وهو أن المنقسم ربما يورد أقسامًا وينفي بعض الأقسام مفردًا، وربما يكون الحكم معلقًا بمركب من القسمين لا بمفرد منهما فكما يجب نفي الأفراد من حيث هي أفراد فكذلك يجـ نفي المركب من حيث هو مركب، وهذا مما أغفله المتكلم كثيرًا في مجاري تقسيماته، وهو واجب الرعاية جدا.
A fourth objection: Why do you say that contingency (al-huduth) cannot be a corrector (musahhih), when you know that among the rational rulings (al-ahkam al-'aqliyya) there are some that are attached to contingency rather than existence? And no speaker may say: 'Its being preceded by non-existence is not effective,' for its meaning is that it is existence qualified by a description and consideration. And just as the visible (al-mar'i) is more specific than the known (al-ma'lum), and vision attaches to the known under the description of existence, similarly we say: It attaches to existence under the description of contingency, and to the contingent under the description of alignment (al-muqabala) or becoming (al-takawwun), or other descriptions and considerations. And this is the secret: that the divided (al-munqasim) sometimes yields divisions, and some divisions are negated individually, and sometimes the ruling is attached to a composite of the two divisions, not to either singly. So just as it is necessary to negate individuals as individuals, so it is necessary to negate the composite as composite. This is something the theologian (al-mutakallim) often overlooks in his divisions, and it is very important to observe.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage is an objection. The author challenges the Ash'ari claim that contingency (huduth) cannot be the corrector (musahhih) for vision. He asks why they deny this when there are rational rulings (ahkam 'aqliyya) that depend on contingency rather than existence. For example, the fact that something is preceded by non-existence (mashuq bi-'adam) is not merely a negative attribute; rather, it signifies existence under a specific description (wujud 'ala sifa wa i'tibar). The objector draws an analogy: the visible (al-mar'i) is more specific than the known (al-ma'lum), yet vision attaches to the known under the description of existence. Similarly, vision can attach to existence under the description of contingency, or to the contingent under descriptions like alignment (muqabala) or becoming (takawwun), etc. The objector then explains a logical principle: the divided (al-munqasim) sometimes yields individual divisions that are negated separately, but sometimes the ruling is attached to a composite (murakkab) of two divisions, not to either alone. In such cases, one must negate both the individuals and the composite. This principle, he says, is often neglected by theologians in their divisions, and it is crucial to observe. The objection thus argues that the corrector for vision does not have to be a simple property like existence; it can be a composite property like existence qualified by contingency.

Key Terms
الحدوث (contingency)
المصحح (corrector)
الوجود (existence)
المرئي (the visible)
المعلوم (the known)
المنقسم (the divided)
المركب (the composite)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Contingency cannot serve as a corrector because it is a mere negative attribute (being preceded by non-existence) without any positive content.
Correction: The objection clarifies that contingency is existence qualified by a description, i.e., it has a positive aspect (existence) combined with a specific attribute, making it a valid corrector.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth objection, what does contingency (huduth) essentially mean?
A) Non-existence preceding existence.
B) Existence qualified by a description.
C) A purely negative attribute.
D) The absence of existence.

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection argues that contingency can be a corrector for vision because it is existence under a description, and rational rulings can attach to composites, not just simple properties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a concept as a mere lack or negation (like a failure or absence) only later to realize it could be reframed as existence under a description? How might rethinking your own shortcomings as 'qualified existence' change your response to them? Will you commit to identifying one area where you have overlooked the positive content of a negative attribute?
?
The theologian often overlooks the logical principle that rulings can attach to composites. In your own decision-making, do you tend to reduce complex issues to simple either/or choices, ignoring the composite nature of reality? Where has this led you to a flawed judgment? Take a moment to identify one situation in your life where you need to consider the composite rather than the individual parts.
?
The objection uses an analogy to defend a subtle point about vision and contingency. Are there analogies you rely on to defend your beliefs that might be flawed because they miss the nuances this passage highlights? How can you ensure your reasoning respects the complexity of the subject matter, especially in matters of faith?

Rational Judgments
intermediate
5 min
Ash'ari Response: The Demand for a Validating Factor is Not Unique
The Ash'ari setup: opponent's cause and the two types of visible things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Ash'ari demand for a cause of vision mirrors the opponent's own method, and that the validity of vision for both substances and accidents establishes the need for a common cause.

Core Idea
The opponent's own use of a cause justifies the Ash'ari search.

The Ash'aris begin their response by turning the tables on their critics. They point out that the opponent, who objects to the demand for a validating factor, himself uses such a factor (color and the colored) for vision. This common ground then leads to the observation that vision pertains to both substances and accidents, both of which are validly seen. This sets the stage for the central question: what common cause makes both visible?

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: first a rebuttal, then a factual observation.
The opponent's definition of musahhih is color/colored.
2
Identify the opponent's own musahhih
The Ash'aris say 'you have made color and the colored a validating factor'. This shows that the opponent also relies on a cause.
3
Note the two types of visible objects
The passage states that vision pertains to substances and accidents, and both are validly visible.
This is a neutral observation accepted by both sides.
4
Appreciate the setup for the argument
By establishing that both substances and accidents are visible, the Ash'aris prepare the ground for the next step: finding a common cause.
This lesson covers only the setup; the argument follows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الأشعرية : الجواب عن الاعتراض الأول : أن طلب المصحح لم يختص بنا، فإنكم جعلتم اللون والمتلون مصححا، وذلك أن الرؤية لما تعلقت ببعض الموجودات وذلك نوعان؛ نوع من الأعراض ونوع من الجواهر، فصح كون الجوهر والعرض مرئيا، [فقلنا: ما المصحح لهذه الصحة أهوا (المكون الجوهر جوهرا، فلزم أن لا يكون العرض مرئيا، أو كون العرض عرضا فلزم أن لا يكون الجوهر مرئيا، فلا بد إذا من قضية واحدة جامعة بينهما شاملة لهما حتى تتكون الصحة معللة لها؛ إذ الصحة حكم هو قضية واحدة فلا يكون مصححها علتان مختلفتان فإن اختلاف العلتين يوجب اختلاف الحكمين في العقليات ولما تعلقت الرؤية بمختلفات وجب علينا طلب العلة التي لأجلها صح تعلقها بها، ولا بد أن يكون السبب المصحح قضية تجمعهما لا كاللون والمتلون، فإنهما قضيتان مختلفتان اختلاف الجنس والنوع، ومن المحال ربط حكم واحد بعلتين مختلفتين؛ فإن العلة العقلية لا بد أن تستقل بإفادة الحكم، فإن استقل أحد المختلفين استغنى الحكم عن الثاني، فطلبنا العلة؛ لأن الرؤية تعلقت بمختلفين، وجعلنا العلة قضية واحدة تشملها لاتحاد الحكم وهو الصحة، فصح الطلب وحصل المطلوب.
The Ash'aris said: The answer to the first objection is that the demand for a validating factor is not specific to us; for you have made color and the colored a validating factor. That is because vision, when it pertains to some existents, and these are two types: a type of accidents and a type of substances, it is valid that substance and accident are visible. [We said: What is the validating factor for this validity? Is it that the substance is substance, then it would follow that accident is not visible; or that accident is accident, then it would follow that substance is not visible? So there must be a single proposition combining them, encompassing them, so that validity is caused by it. For validity is a judgment that is a single proposition; so its validating factor cannot be two different causes, for the difference of two causes necessitates difference of two judgments in rational matters. And since vision pertains to different things, we must seek the cause for which its pertaining is valid. And the validating cause must be a proposition that combines them, not like color and the colored, for they are two different propositions differing in genus and species. And it is impossible to link a single judgment to two different causes, for a rational cause must be independent in producing the judgment; if one of the different things is independent, the judgment dispenses with the second. So we sought the cause because vision pertained to different things, and we made the cause a single proposition encompassing them due to the unity of the judgment, which is validity. Thus the demand is valid and the desired is obtained.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage is the Ash'ari response to the first objection raised against their view on vision. The response defends the Ash'ari method of seeking a single cause (musahhih) for the validity of vision. The Ash'aris argue that the opponent himself uses a validating factor (color and colored) for vision, so the demand for a cause is not unique to them. They then elaborate: vision pertains to both substances and accidents; if the cause were specific to one type (e.g., being a substance), the other type would not be visible. Hence there must be a single proposition (qadiyya wahida) common to both that explains why vision is valid for them. This is because a single judgment (validity) cannot have two different causes, as that would rationally lead to different judgments. The cause must be a unitary rational ground that combines the diverse objects of vision. Thus, the Ash'aris conclude that their search for a single cause (such as existence or some other common attribute) is justified and successful.

Key Terms
مصحح
لون
متلون
جوهر
عرض
رؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What do the Ash'aris claim about the opponent's view regarding a cause for vision?
A) The opponent denies any cause for vision.
B) The opponent also uses a cause (color and colored).
C) The opponent requires a single cause for both substances and accidents.
D) The opponent agrees with the Ash'aris on the need for a single cause.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'aris establish common ground by noting the opponent's own use of a cause for vision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own reasoning about faith, do you ever demand evidence from others that you do not demand from yourself? When have you held someone to a standard you were unwilling to apply to your own beliefs?
?
The Ash'aris point out that the opponent's own use of a cause justifies the search for a cause. Are you consistent in your intellectual standards, or do you change the rules depending on which position you are defending?
?
After reading this passage, what is one area of your life where you need to stop being selective in your reasoning and instead be honest about the principles you apply to yourself and to others?
